\section{Definitions}

Everything we know about the Euclidean geometry of $\mathbb{R}^n$ can be derived from its \textbf{dot product}, which is defined for $v = (v^1, \ldots, v^n)$ and $w = (w^1, \ldots, w^n)$ by
\[
v \cdot w = \sum_{i=1}^{n} v^i w^i.
\]

The dot product has a natural generalization to arbitrary vector spaces. Given a vector space $V$ (which we always assume to be real), an \textbf{inner product on $V$} is a map $V \times V \to \mathbb{R}$, typically written $(v, w) \mapsto \langle v, w \rangle$, that satisfies the following properties for all $u, v, x \in V$ and $a, b \in \mathbb{R}$:

\begin{enumerate}
\item[\textmd{(i)}] \textbf{Symmetry:} $\langle v, w \rangle = \langle w, v \rangle$.
\item[\textmd{(ii)}] \textbf{Bilinearity:} $\langle av + bw, x \rangle = a\langle v, x \rangle + b\langle w, x \rangle = \langle x, av + bw \rangle$.
\item[\textmd{(iii)}] \textbf{Positive Definiteness:} $\langle v,v \rangle \geq 0$, with equality if and only if $v = 0$.
\end{enumerate}

A vector space endowed with a specific inner product is called an \textit{inner product space}.

An inner product on $V$ allows us to make sense of geometric quantities such as lengths of vectors and angles between vectors. First, we define the \textit{length} or \textit{norm} of a vector $v \in V$ as
$$|v| = \langle v,v \rangle^{1/2}.$$

The following identity shows that an inner product is completely determined by knowledge of the lengths of all vectors.

\begin{lemma}[Polarization Identity]
\label{lem:polarization-identity}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.real_inner_eq_inner_add_add_self_sub_inner_sub_sub_self_div_four}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.1}
Suppose $\langle \cdot,\cdot \rangle$ is an inner product on a vector space $V$. Then for all $v, w \in V$,
$$\langle v,w \rangle = \frac{1}{4}\bigl[\langle v+w, v+w \rangle - \langle v-w, v-w \rangle\bigr].$$
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
By bilinearity and symmetry,
\[
\langle v+w, v+w \rangle = \langle v,v \rangle + 2\langle v,w \rangle + \langle w,w \rangle,
\qquad
\langle v-w, v-w \rangle = \langle v,v \rangle - 2\langle v,w \rangle + \langle w,w \rangle .
\]
Subtracting the second identity from the first cancels $\langle v,v\rangle$ and $\langle w,w\rangle$ and leaves $4\langle v,w\rangle$. Dividing by $4$ gives the stated formula.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:polarization-proof}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.real_inner_eq_inner_add_add_self_sub_inner_sub_sub_self_div_four}
\leanok
\uses{lem:polarization-identity}
\dcref{ch2:2.2}
Prove the preceding lemma.
\end{exercise}

The \textit{angle} between two nonzero vectors $v, w \in V$ is defined as the unique $\theta \in [0,\pi]$ satisfying
$$\cos \theta = \frac{\langle v,w \rangle}{|v||w|}.$$

Two vectors $v, w \in V$ are said to be \textit{orthogonal} if $\langle v,w \rangle = 0$, which means that either their angle is $\pi/2$ or one of the vectors is zero. If $S \subseteq V$ is a linear subspace, the set $S^\perp \subseteq V$, consisting of all vectors in $V$ that are orthogonal to every vector in $S$, is also a linear subspace, called the \textit{orthogonal complement} of $S$. Vectors $v_1,\ldots,v_k$ are called \textit{orthonormal} if they are of length $1$ and pairwise orthogonal, or equivalently if $\langle v_i, v_j \rangle = \delta_{ij}$ (where $\delta_{ij}$ is the \textit{Kronecker delta symbol} defined in Appendix B; see (B.1)). The following well-known proposition shows that every finite-dimensional inner product space has an orthonormal basis.

\begin{proposition}[Gram-Schmidt Algorithm]
\label{prop:gram-schmidt}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_orthonormalBasis_span_image_Iic_eq}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.3}
Let $V$ be an $n$-dimensional inner product space, and suppose $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ is any ordered basis for $V$. Then there is an orthonormal ordered basis $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ satisfying the following conditions:
$$\text{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_k) = \text{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_k) \quad \text{for each } k = 1,\ldots,n.$$
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
The basis vectors $b_1,\ldots,b_n$ are defined recursively by
$$b_1 = \frac{v_1}{|v_1|},$$
$$b_j = \frac{v_j - \sum_{i=1}^{j-1}\langle v_j, b_i \rangle b_i}{\left|v_j - \sum_{i=1}^{j-1}\langle v_j, b_i \rangle b_i\right|}, \quad 2 \le j \le n.$$

Because $v_1 \ne 0$ and $v_j \notin \text{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_{j-1})$ for each $j \ge 2$, the denominators are all nonzero. These vectors satisfy the span conditions by construction, and are orthonormal by direct computation.
\end{proof}

If two vector spaces $V$ and $W$ are both equipped with inner products, denoted by $\langle \cdot, \cdot \rangle_V$ and $\langle \cdot, \cdot \rangle_W$, respectively, then a map $F : V \to W$ is called a \emph{linear isometry} if it is a vector space isomorphism that preserves inner products: $\langle F(v), F(v') \rangle_W = \langle v, v' \rangle_V$.

\begin{proposition}[Classification of Inner Product Spaces]
\label{prop:inner-product-spaces-linearly-isometric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.nonempty_linearIsometryEquiv_of_finrank_eq}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt}
All inner product spaces of the same finite dimension are linearly isometric to each other.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Let $V$ and $W$ be inner product spaces of dimension $n$. Each has an orthonormal basis: apply Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt} to any ordered basis. Choose orthonormal bases $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ for $V$ and $(w_1,\ldots,w_n)$ for $W$, and let $F : V \to W$ be the unique linear map with $F(v_i) = w_i$; it is an isomorphism, since it carries a basis to a basis. Writing $v = \sum_i a^i v_i$ and $v' = \sum_j b^j v_j$, orthonormality of the two bases gives
\[
\langle F(v), F(v') \rangle_W = \Big\langle \sum_i a^i w_i, \sum_j b^j w_j \Big\rangle_W = \sum_i a^i b^i = \Big\langle \sum_i a^i v_i, \sum_j b^j v_j \Big\rangle_V = \langle v, v' \rangle_V ,
\]
so $F$ preserves inner products and is therefore a linear isometry.
\end{proof}

\section{Riemannian Metrics}

To extend these geometric ideas to abstract smooth manifolds, we define a structure that amounts to a smoothly varying choice of inner product on each tangent space.

\begin{definition}[Riemannian Metric]
\label{def:riemannian-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric}
\leanok
Let $M$ be a smooth manifold. A \textbf{Riemannian metric} on $M$ is a smooth covariant $2$-tensor field $g$ on $M$ whose value $g_p$ at each $p \in M$ is an inner product on $T_p M$; thus $g$ is a symmetric $2$-tensor field that is positive definite, in the sense that $g_p(v,v) \geq 0$ for each $p \in M$ and each $v \in T_p M$, with equality if and only if $v = 0$. A \textbf{Riemannian manifold} is a pair $(M,g)$ consisting of a smooth manifold $M$ together with a Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$.
\end{definition}

If $M$ is understood to be endowed with a specific Riemannian metric, we sometimes say ``$M$ is a Riemannian manifold.''

The next proposition shows that Riemannian metrics exist in great abundance.

\begin{lemma}[Convexity of the set of inner products]
\label{lem:convex-inner-products}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.convex_symm_posDef}
\leanok
Let $M$ be a smooth manifold and let $p \in M$. The set of inner products on $T_p M$ is a convex subset of the space of bilinear forms on $T_p M$: if $q_1, q_2$ are inner products and $a, b \geq 0$ satisfy $a + b = 1$, then $a q_1 + b q_2$ is an inner product.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Symmetry is immediate from the symmetry of $q_1$ and $q_2$, since
\[
(a q_1 + b q_2)(u,v) = a q_1(u,v) + b q_2(u,v) = a q_1(v,u) + b q_2(v,u) = (a q_1 + b q_2)(v,u).
\]
For positive definiteness, let $v \neq 0$. Since $a + b = 1$ and $a, b \geq 0$, at least one of $a, b$ is strictly positive; say $a > 0$ (the case $b > 0$ is symmetric). Then $a q_1(v,v) > 0$ because $q_1$ is positive definite, and $b q_2(v,v) \geq 0$ because $b \geq 0$ and $q_2(v,v) > 0$. Hence
\[
(a q_1 + b q_2)(v,v) = a q_1(v,v) + b q_2(v,v) > 0. \qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Positive definite forms are coercive]
\label{lem:posdef-coercive}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isVonNBounded_of_posDef}
\leanok
Let $V$ be a finite-dimensional normed real vector space and let $q$ be a bilinear form on $V$ with $q(v,v) > 0$ for every $v \neq 0$. Then the sublevel set $\{v \in V : q(v,v) < 1\}$ is bounded. The reason is that $q$ is coercive: there is a constant $c > 0$ with $q(v,v) \geq c |v|^2$ for all $v \in V$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
If $V = \{0\}$ the statement is vacuous, so assume $V \neq \{0\}$. The unit sphere $S = \{v : |v| = 1\}$ is compact, because $V$ is finite dimensional, and nonempty. The function $v \mapsto q(v,v)$ is continuous, so it attains a minimum $c = q(v_0, v_0)$ at some $v_0 \in S$. Since $|v_0| = 1$ we have $v_0 \neq 0$, so $c > 0$ by positive definiteness.

Now let $v \in V$. If $v = 0$ both sides vanish. Otherwise $u = |v|^{-1} v$ lies in $S$, so $q(u,u) \geq c$, and bilinearity gives
\[
q(v,v) = q(|v| u, |v| u) = |v|^2 q(u,u) \geq c |v|^2 .
\]
Finally, if $q(v,v) < 1$ then $c |v|^2 < 1$, so $|v| < c^{-1/2}$; thus the sublevel set is contained in the ball of radius $c^{-1/2}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Every smooth manifold admits a Riemannian metric]
\label{prop:existence-riemannian-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_riemannianMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.4}
Every smooth manifold admits a Riemannian metric.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,lem:convex-inner-products,lem:posdef-coercive}
Let $M$ be a smooth $n$-manifold, and let $\{U_\alpha\}$ be the cover of $M$ by chart domains, with charts $\varphi_\alpha : U_\alpha \to \mathbb{R}^n$.

On each $U_\alpha$ define a Riemannian metric $g_\alpha$ by pulling back the Euclidean metric $\bar{g}$ of Example \ref{ex:euclidean-metric} along the chart:
\[
g_\alpha = \varphi_\alpha^* \bar{g}, \qquad\text{that is}\qquad
(g_\alpha)_p(v,w) = \langle d(\varphi_\alpha)_p (v), d(\varphi_\alpha)_p (w)\rangle
\]
for $p \in U_\alpha$ and $v, w \in T_p M$. This is a smooth symmetric $2$-tensor field on $U_\alpha$, and it is positive definite because $d(\varphi_\alpha)_p$ is a linear isomorphism, so $d(\varphi_\alpha)_p(v) = 0$ only for $v = 0$.

Let $\{\psi_\alpha\}$ be a smooth partition of unity subordinate to $\{U_\alpha\}$, which exists because $M$ is a smooth manifold, hence second countable and Hausdorff. Define
\[
g = \sum_\alpha \psi_\alpha \, g_\alpha ,
\]
where each summand $\psi_\alpha g_\alpha$ is extended by zero outside $\operatorname{supp}\psi_\alpha \subseteq U_\alpha$. The family $\{\operatorname{supp}\psi_\alpha\}$ is locally finite, so the sum has finitely many nonzero terms near each point and $g$ is a smooth $2$-tensor field.

It remains to check that $g_p$ is an inner product on $T_p M$ for each $p$. Fix $p$ and let $A = \{\alpha : \psi_\alpha(p) \neq 0\}$, a finite nonempty set. The weights $\{\psi_\alpha(p)\}_{\alpha \in A}$ are nonnegative and sum to $1$, and for $\alpha \in A$ we have $p \in U_\alpha$, so $(g_\alpha)_p$ is an inner product on $T_p M$. Hence $g_p = \sum_{\alpha \in A} \psi_\alpha(p) (g_\alpha)_p$ is a convex combination of inner products on $T_p M$, and is therefore an inner product by Lemma \ref{lem:convex-inner-products}. By Lemma \ref{lem:posdef-coercive} the inner product $g_p$ induces the given topology on $T_p M$. Thus $g$ is a Riemannian metric on $M$.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}[Use a partition of unity to prove the preceding proposition]
\label{exer:partition-unity-proof}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_riemannianMetric}
\leanok
\uses{prop:existence-riemannian-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.5}
Use a partition of unity to prove the preceding proposition.
\end{exercise}

We will give a number of examples of Riemannian metrics, along with several systematic methods for constructing them, later in this chapter and in the next.

If $M$ is a smooth manifold with boundary, a Riemannian metric on $M$ is defined in exactly the same way: a smooth symmetric $2$-tensor field $g$ that is positive definite everywhere. A \textbf{Riemannian manifold with boundary} is a pair $(M, g)$, where $M$ is a smooth manifold with boundary and $g$ is a Riemannian metric on $M$. Many of the results we will discuss in this book work equally well for manifolds with or without boundary, with the same proofs, and in such cases we will state them in that generality. But when the treatment of a boundary would involve additional difficulties, we will generally restrict attention to the case of manifolds without boundary, since that is our primary interest. Many problems involving Riemannian manifolds with boundary can be addressed by embedding into a larger manifold without boundary and extending the Riemannian metric arbitrarily to the larger manifold; see Proposition A.31 in Appendix A.

A Riemannian metric is not the same as a metric in the sense of metric spaces (though, as we will see later in this chapter, the two concepts are related). In this book, when we use the word ``metric'' without further qualification, it always refers to a Riemannian metric.

Let $g$ be a Riemannian metric on a smooth manifold $M$ with or without boundary. Because $g_p$ is an inner product on $T_p M$ for each $p \in M$, we often use the following angle-bracket notation for $v, w \in T_p M$:
\[
\langle v, w \rangle_g = g_p(v, w).
\]

Using this inner product, we can define lengths of tangent vectors, angles between nonzero tangent vectors, and orthogonality of tangent vectors as described above. The length of a vector $v \in T_p M$ is denoted by $|v|_g = \langle v, v \rangle_g^{1/2}$. If the metric is understood, we sometimes omit it from the notation, and write $\langle v, w \rangle$ and $|v|$ in place of $\langle v, w \rangle_g$ and $|v|_g$, respectively.

The starting point for Riemannian geometry is the following fundamental example.

\begin{definition}[The Euclidean Metric]
\label{ex:euclidean-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.euclideanMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.6}
The \emph{Euclidean metric} is the Riemannian metric $\bar{g}$ on $\mathbb{R}^n$ whose value at each $x \in \mathbb{R}^n$ is the usual dot product on $T_x\mathbb{R}^n$ under the natural identification $T_x \mathbb{R}^n \cong \mathbb{R}^n$. This means that for $v,w \in T_x \mathbb{R}^n$ written in standard coordinates $(x^1, \ldots, x^n)$ as $v = \sum_i v^i \partial_i|_x$, $w = \sum_j w^j \partial_j|_x$, we have
\[
\langle v, w \rangle_{\bar{g}} = \sum_{i=1}^n v^i w^i.
\]
More generally, any inner product space carries the Riemannian metric whose value at each point is that inner product; the Euclidean metric on $\mathbb{R}^n$ is the case of the dot product.
\end{definition}

When working with $\mathbb{R}^n$ as a Riemannian manifold, we always assume we are using the Euclidean metric unless otherwise specified.

\section{Isometries}

Suppose $(M, g)$ and $(\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{g})$ are Riemannian manifolds with or without boundary. An \emph{isometry from} $(M,g)$ \emph{to} $(\widetilde{M},\widetilde{g})$ is a diffeomorphism $\varphi: M \to \widetilde{M}$ such that $\varphi^* \widetilde{g} = g$. Unwinding the definitions shows that this is equivalent to the requirement that $\varphi$ be a smooth bijection and each differential $d\varphi_p: T_p M \to T_{\varphi(p)} \widetilde{M}$ be a linear isometry. We say $(M, g)$ and $(\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{g})$ are \emph{isometric} if there exists an isometry between them.

\begin{definition}[Metric-Preserving Maps]
\label{def:metric-preserving}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsMetricPreserving}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(M,g)$ and $(\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{g})$ be Riemannian manifolds with or without boundary. A map $\varphi: M \to \widetilde{M}$ \emph{preserves the metric} if $\varphi^* \widetilde{g} = g$, that is, if
\[
  \widetilde{g}_{\varphi(p)}\bigl(d\varphi_p(v), d\varphi_p(w)\bigr) \;=\; g_p(v,w)
  \qquad \text{for all } p \in M \text{ and } v, w \in T_p M.
\]
Equivalently, each differential $d\varphi_p : T_p M \to T_{\varphi(p)} \widetilde{M}$ is a linear isometry onto its image.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Isometry]
\label{def:isometry}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsIsometry}
\leanok
\uses{def:metric-preserving,def:riemannian-metric}
An \emph{isometry} from $(M,g)$ to $(\widetilde{M},\widetilde{g})$ is a diffeomorphism $\varphi: M \to \widetilde{M}$ that preserves the metric.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[Metric-Preserving Maps Are Immersions]
\label{lem:metric-preserving-immersion}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsMetricPreserving.injective_mfderiv}
\leanok
\uses{def:metric-preserving,lem:pullback-immersion-metric}
If $\varphi: M \to \widetilde{M}$ preserves the metric, then $d\varphi_p$ is injective for every $p \in M$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pullback-immersion-metric}
Since $\varphi^* \widetilde{g} = g$ and $g$ is positive definite, the pullback $\varphi^* \widetilde{g}$ is positive definite. By Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-immersion-metric} this holds if and only if $d\varphi_p$ is injective at every $p$.
\end{proof}

A composition of isometries and the inverse of an isometry are again isometries, so being isometric is an equivalence relation on the class of Riemannian manifolds with or without boundary. Our subject, \emph{Riemannian geometry}, is concerned primarily with properties of Riemannian manifolds that are preserved by isometries.

\begin{lemma}[The Inverse of an Isometry]
\label{lem:isometry-symm}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsIsometry.symm}
\leanok
\uses{def:isometry}
If $\varphi$ is an isometry from $(M,g)$ to $(\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{g})$, then $\varphi^{-1}$ is an isometry from $(\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{g})$ to $(M,g)$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:isometry,def:metric-preserving}
The inverse of a diffeomorphism is a diffeomorphism, so only metric preservation is at issue. Fix $q \in \widetilde{M}$ and put $p = \varphi^{-1}(q)$. Differentiating $\varphi \circ \varphi^{-1} = \mathrm{id}_{\widetilde{M}}$ at $q$ gives $d\varphi_p \circ d(\varphi^{-1})_q = \mathrm{id}_{T_q \widetilde{M}}$. Hence for $v, w \in T_q\widetilde{M}$, applying $\varphi^* \widetilde{g} = g$ at $p$ to the vectors $d(\varphi^{-1})_q(v)$ and $d(\varphi^{-1})_q(w)$ yields
\[
  g_p\bigl(d(\varphi^{-1})_q v,\, d(\varphi^{-1})_q w\bigr)
  = \widetilde{g}_{\varphi(p)}\bigl(d\varphi_p d(\varphi^{-1})_q v,\, d\varphi_p d(\varphi^{-1})_q w\bigr)
  = \widetilde{g}_q(v, w),
\]
using $\varphi(p) = q$. This is exactly $(\varphi^{-1})^* g = \widetilde{g}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Local Isometry]
\label{def:local-isometry}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsLocalIsometry}
\leanok
\uses{def:isometry,def:metric-preserving,def:riemannian-metric}
If $(M, g)$ and $(\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{g})$ are Riemannian manifolds, a map $\varphi: M \to \widetilde{M}$ is a \emph{local isometry} if each point $p \in M$ has a neighborhood $U$ such that $\varphi|_U$ is an isometry onto an open subset of $\widetilde{M}$.

Equivalently, $\varphi$ is a local diffeomorphism that preserves the metric: the two conditions agree because preserving the metric is a condition on the differentials $d\varphi_p$ separately, so it holds on a neighborhood of every point if and only if it holds at every point.
\end{definition}

The substance of the following characterization is its converse half, which produces the local diffeomorphism out of nothing but the metric condition and a count of dimensions.

\begin{lemma}[Metric-Preserving Maps in Equal Dimensions Are Local Diffeomorphisms]
\label{lem:metric-preserving-equidim-local-diffeomorphism}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsMetricPreserving.isLocalDiffeomorph}
\leanok
\uses{def:metric-preserving,lem:metric-preserving-immersion,thm:inverse-function-theorem-manifolds}
Let $(M,g)$ and $(\widetilde{M},\widetilde{g})$ be Riemannian manifolds of the same dimension and let $\varphi: M \to \widetilde{M}$ be a smooth map with $\varphi^* \widetilde{g} = g$. Then $\varphi$ is a local diffeomorphism.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:metric-preserving-immersion,thm:inverse-function-theorem-manifolds}
Fix $p \in M$. By Lemma \ref{lem:metric-preserving-immersion} the differential $d\varphi_p : T_p M \to T_{\varphi(p)}\widetilde{M}$ is injective. Since $\dim M = \dim \widetilde{M}$, these tangent spaces are vector spaces of the same finite dimension, so an injective linear map between them is bijective, and $d\varphi_p$ is therefore invertible. By the inverse function theorem for manifolds, Theorem \ref{thm:inverse-function-theorem-manifolds}, $\varphi$ restricts to a diffeomorphism from a neighborhood of $p$ onto an open subset of $\widetilde{M}$. As $p$ was arbitrary, $\varphi$ is a local diffeomorphism.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:characterize-local-isometry}
\dcref{ch2:2.7}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isLocalIsometry_iff_isMetricPreserving}
\leanok
\uses{def:local-isometry,def:metric-preserving,def:isometry,lem:metric-preserving-equidim-local-diffeomorphism}
Prove that if $(M,g)$ and $(\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{g})$ are Riemannian manifolds of the same dimension, a smooth map $\varphi: M \to \widetilde{M}$ is a local isometry if and only if $\varphi^* \widetilde{g} = g$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:local-isometry,lem:metric-preserving-equidim-local-diffeomorphism}
Suppose first that $\varphi$ is a local isometry, and let $p \in M$. Then $\varphi$ restricts, on some neighborhood $U$ of $p$, to an isometry onto an open subset of $\widetilde{M}$, so in particular $\widetilde{g}_{\varphi(p)}(d\varphi_p v, d\varphi_p w) = g_p(v,w)$ for all $v,w \in T_pM$. Since every point of $M$ lies in such a $U$, this identity holds at every point, which is $\varphi^*\widetilde{g} = g$. This half uses neither the smoothness hypothesis nor the equality of dimensions.

Conversely, suppose $\varphi^* \widetilde{g} = g$. By Lemma \ref{lem:metric-preserving-equidim-local-diffeomorphism}, $\varphi$ is a local diffeomorphism, so each $p \in M$ has a neighborhood $U$ on which $\varphi$ restricts to a diffeomorphism onto an open subset of $\widetilde{M}$. That restriction preserves the metric, because $\varphi$ does at every point of $M$ and metric preservation is a pointwise condition on the differential. Hence $\varphi|_U$ is an isometry onto an open subset, and $\varphi$ is a local isometry.
\end{proof}

A Riemannian $n$-manifold is said to be \emph{flat} if it is locally isometric to a Euclidean space, that is, if every point has a neighborhood that is isometric to an open set in $\mathbb{R}^n$ with its Euclidean metric. Problem 2-1 shows that all Riemannian 1-manifolds are flat; but we will see later that this is far from the case in higher dimensions.

An isometry from $(M,g)$ to itself is called an \emph{isometry of $(M,g)$}. The set of all isometries of $(M,g)$ is a group under composition, called the \emph{isometry group of $(M,g)$}; it is denoted by $\text{Iso}(M,g)$, or sometimes just $\text{Iso}(M)$ if the metric is understood.

A deep theorem of Sumner B. Myers and Norman E. Steenrod [MS39] shows that if $M$ has finitely many components, then $\text{Iso}(M,g)$ has a topology and smooth structure making it into a finite-dimensional Lie group acting smoothly on $M$. We will neither prove nor use the Myers--Steenrod theorem, but if you are interested, a good source for the proof is [Kob72].

\section{Local Representations for Metrics}

Suppose $(M,g)$ is a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary. If $(x^1,\ldots,x^n)$ are any smooth local coordinates on an open subset $U \subseteq M$, then $g$ can be written locally in $U$ as
\begin{equation*}
g = g_{ij} dx^i \otimes dx^j \tag{2.7}
\end{equation*}
for some collection of $n^2$ smooth functions $g_{ij}$ for $i,j = 1,\ldots,n$. (Here and throughout the book, we use the Einstein summation convention; see p. 375.) The component functions of this tensor field constitute a matrix-valued function $(g_{ij})$, characterized by $g_{ij}(p) = \langle \partial_i|_p, \partial_j|_p \rangle$, where $\partial_i = \partial/\partial x^i$ is the $i$th coordinate vector field; this matrix is symmetric in $i$ and $j$ and depends smoothly on $p \in U$. If $v = v^i \partial_i|_p$ is a vector in $T_p M$ such that $g_{ij}(p)v^i = 0$, it follows that $\langle v,v \rangle = g_{ij}(p) v^i v^j = 0$, which implies $v = 0$; thus the matrix $(g_{ij}(p))$ is always nonsingular. The notation for $g$ can be shortened by expressing it in terms of the symmetric product (see Appendix B): using the symmetry of $g_{ij}$, we compute
\begin{align*}
g &= g_{ij} dx^i \otimes dx^j \\
  &= \tfrac{1}{2}(g_{ij} dx^i \otimes dx^j + g_{ji} dx^i \otimes dx^j) \\
  &= \tfrac{1}{2}(g_{ij} dx^i \otimes dx^j + g_{ij} dx^j \otimes dx^i) \\
  &= g_{ij} dx^i dx^j.
\end{align*}

For example, the Euclidean metric on $\mathbb{R}^n$ (Example 2.6) can be expressed in standard coordinates in several ways:
\begin{equation*}
\bar{g} = \sum_i dx^i dx^i = \sum_i (dx^i)^2 = \delta_{ij} dx^i dx^j. \tag{2.8}
\end{equation*}

The matrix of $\bar{g}$ in these coordinates is thus $\bar{g}_{ij} = \delta_{ij}$.

More generally, if $(E_1,\ldots,E_n)$ is any smooth local frame for $TM$ on an open subset $U \subseteq M$ and $(e^1,\ldots,e^n)$ is its dual coframe, we can write $g$ locally in $U$ as
\begin{equation*}
g = g_{ij} e^i \otimes e^j, \tag{2.9}
\end{equation*}
where $g_{ij}(p) = \langle E_i|_p, E_j \rangle_p$, and the matrix-valued function $(g_{ij})$ is symmetric and smooth as before.

A Riemannian metric $g$ acts on smooth vector fields $X, Y \in \mathfrak{X}(M)$ to yield a real-valued function $\langle X,Y \rangle$. In terms of any smooth local frame, this function is expressed locally by $\langle X,Y \rangle = g_{ij} X^i Y^j$ and therefore is smooth. Similarly, we obtain a nonnegative real-valued function $|X| = \langle X,X \rangle^{1/2}$, which is continuous everywhere and smooth on the open subset where $X \neq 0$.

\begin{lemma}[Smoothness of the pairing of vector fields]
\label{lem:inner-product-vector-fields-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric.contMDiff_innerAt}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.2}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary. If $X, Y \in \mathfrak{X}(M)$ are smooth vector fields, then $\langle X, Y \rangle : M \to \mathbb{R}$ is smooth.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Smoothness is local, so it suffices to check it on the domain $U$ of an arbitrary smooth local frame $(E_i)$. Write $X = X^i E_i$ and $Y = Y^j E_j$ on $U$; the component functions $X^i, Y^j$ are smooth because $X$ and $Y$ are smooth sections and $(E_i)$ is a smooth frame. By bilinearity,
\[
\langle X, Y\rangle = g_{ij} X^i Y^j , \qquad g_{ij} = \langle E_i, E_j\rangle ,
\]
and the $g_{ij}$ are smooth on $U$ because $g$ is a smooth $2$-tensor field and the $E_i$ are smooth. Thus $\langle X, Y\rangle$ is a finite sum of products of smooth real-valued functions on $U$, hence smooth on $U$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Continuity of the length of a vector field]
\label{lem:length-vector-field-continuous}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric.continuous_normAt}
\leanok
\uses{lem:inner-product-vector-fields-smooth}
\dcref{ch2:2.2}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary. If $X \in \mathfrak{X}(M)$ is a smooth vector field, then $|X| = \langle X, X\rangle^{1/2} : M \to \mathbb{R}$ is continuous.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:inner-product-vector-fields-smooth}
By Lemma \ref{lem:inner-product-vector-fields-smooth} the function $\langle X, X\rangle$ is smooth, hence continuous, and it is nonnegative by positive semidefiniteness of $g$. The square root $t \mapsto \sqrt{t}$ is continuous on $[0,\infty)$ — including at $t = 0$, where it fails only to be differentiable. So $|X| = \sqrt{\cdot} \circ \langle X, X\rangle$ is a composition of continuous functions, hence continuous.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Smoothness of the length away from the zero set]
\label{lem:length-vector-field-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric.contMDiffAt_normAt}
\leanok
\uses{lem:inner-product-vector-fields-smooth}
\dcref{ch2:2.2}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary and let $X \in \mathfrak{X}(M)$ be a smooth vector field. Then $|X|$ is smooth at every $p \in M$ with $X_p \neq 0$; that is, $|X|$ is smooth on the open subset of $M$ where $X$ does not vanish.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:inner-product-vector-fields-smooth}
Let $p \in M$ with $X_p \neq 0$. By positive definiteness of $g_p$ we have $\langle X_p, X_p\rangle > 0$, and by Lemma \ref{lem:inner-product-vector-fields-smooth} the function $\langle X, X\rangle$ is smooth. Since $t \mapsto \sqrt{t}$ is smooth on $(0,\infty)$, it is smooth at $\langle X_p, X_p \rangle$, so the composition $|X| = \sqrt{\cdot} \circ \langle X, X\rangle$ is smooth at $p$. As this holds at every point of the set $\{q : X_q \neq 0\}$, which is open by continuity of $X$, the function $|X|$ is smooth there.
\end{proof}

A local frame $\{E_i\}$ for $M$ on an open set $U$ is said to be an \textbf{orthonormal frame} if the vectors $E_1|_p, \ldots, E_n|_p$ are an orthonormal basis for $T_p M$ at each $p \in U$. Equivalently, $(E_i)$ is an orthonormal frame if and only if
\[
\langle E_i, E_j \rangle = \delta_{ij},
\]
in which case $g$ has the local expression
\[
g = (e^1)^2 + \cdots + (e^n)^2,
\]
where $(e^i)^2$ denotes the symmetric product $e^i e^i = e^i \otimes e^i$.

\begin{proposition}[Existence of Orthonormal Frames]
\label{prop:existence-orthonormal-frames}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_orthonormalFrame}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.8}
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt,def:riemannian-metric}

Let $(M, g)$ be a Riemannian $n$-manifold with or without boundary. If $(X_i)$ is any smooth local frame for $TM$ over an open subset $U \subseteq M$, then there is a smooth orthonormal frame $(E_j)$ over $U$ such that $\text{span}\{E_1|_p, \ldots, E_k|_p\} = \text{span}\{X_1|_p, \ldots, X_k|_p\}$ for each $k = 1, \ldots, n$ and each $p \in U$. In particular, for every $p \in M$, there is a smooth orthonormal frame $(E_j)$ defined on some neighborhood of $p$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt}
Applying the Gram–Schmidt algorithm to the vectors $(X_1|_p, \ldots, X_n|_p)$ at each $p \in U$, we obtain an ordered $n$-tuple of rough orthonormal vector fields $(E_1, \ldots, E_n)$ over $U$ satisfying the span conditions. Because the vectors whose norms appear in the denominators of (2.5)–(2.6) are nowhere vanishing, those formulas show that each vector field $E_j$ is smooth. The last statement of the proposition follows by applying this construction to any smooth local frame in a neighborhood of $p$.

In more detail, write $u_j$ for the unnormalized Gram–Schmidt vectors, defined recursively by
\[
u_j = X_j - \sum_{i < j} \frac{\langle u_i, X_j\rangle}{\langle u_i, u_i\rangle}\, u_i ,
\qquad E_j = \frac{u_j}{|u_j|} .
\]
Fix $p \in U$. Since $(X_1|_p, \ldots, X_n|_p)$ is a basis of $T_pM$, it is linearly independent, so $u_j|_p \neq 0$ for every $j$, and hence $\langle u_j|_p, u_j|_p\rangle > 0$ by positive definiteness of $g_p$. Therefore every denominator appearing above is nonvanishing on all of $U$.

Smoothness of $E_j$ now follows by induction on $j$. The function $p \mapsto \langle v|_p, w|_p\rangle_g$ is smooth whenever $v, w$ are smooth vector fields, because $g$ is a smooth $2$-tensor field. So, assuming inductively that $u_i$ is smooth for every $i < j$, each coefficient $\langle u_i, X_j\rangle / \langle u_i, u_i\rangle$ is a quotient of smooth functions with nowhere-vanishing denominator, hence smooth; therefore $u_j$, being $X_j$ minus a finite sum of smooth multiples of smooth vector fields, is smooth. Finally $|u_j| = \langle u_j, u_j\rangle^{1/2}$ is smooth and nonvanishing on $U$, because $t \mapsto \sqrt{t}$ is smooth away from $t = 0$ and $\langle u_j, u_j\rangle > 0$ there; so $E_j = |u_j|^{-1} u_j$ is smooth.

Orthonormality of $(E_j)$ and the span conditions $\text{span}\{E_1|_p, \ldots, E_k|_p\} = \text{span}\{X_1|_p, \ldots, X_k|_p\}$ are the standard properties of the Gram–Schmidt algorithm of Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt}, applied in the inner product space $(T_pM, g_p)$ at each $p \in U$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}
\label{rem:coordinate-frame-warning}
\uses{prop:existence-orthonormal-frames}

\textbf{Warning:} A common mistake made by beginners is to assume that one can find coordinates near $p$ such that the coordinate frame $(\partial_i)$ is orthonormal. Proposition 2.8 does not show this. In fact, as we will see in Chapter 7, this is possible only when the metric is flat, that is, locally isometric to the Euclidean metric.
\end{remark}

For a Riemannian manifold $(M, g)$ with or without boundary, we define the \textbf{unit tangent bundle} to be the subset $UTM \subseteq TM$ consisting of unit vectors:
\begin{equation}
UTM = \{(p, v) \in TM : |v|_g = 1\}.
\tag{2.10}
\end{equation}

\begin{proposition}[Properties of the Unit Tangent Bundle]
\label{prop:unit-tangent-bundle-properties}
\dcref{ch2:2.9}

If $(M, g)$ is a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary, its unit tangent bundle $UTM$ is a smooth, properly embedded codimension-1 submanifold with boundary in $TM$, with $\partial(UTM) = \pi^{-1}(\partial M)$ (where $\pi : UTM \to M$ is the canonical projection). The unit tangent bundle is connected if and only if $M$ is connected, and compact if and only if $M$ is compact.
\end{proposition}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:orthonormal-frames-proposition}
\dcref{ch2:2.10}
Use local orthonormal frames to prove the preceding proposition.
\end{exercise}

\section{Methods for Constructing Riemannian Metrics}

Many examples of Riemannian manifolds arise naturally as submanifolds, products, and quotients of other Riemannian manifolds. In this section, we introduce some of the tools for constructing such metrics.

\subsection*{Riemannian Submanifolds}

Every submanifold of a Riemannian manifold automatically inherits a Riemannian metric, and many interesting Riemannian metrics are defined in this way. The key fact is the following lemma.

\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:pullback-immersion-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pullbackForm_posDef_iff_immersion}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.11}
Suppose $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ is a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary, $M$ is a smooth manifold with or without boundary, and $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ is a smooth map. The smooth 2-tensor field $g = F^*\tilde{g}$ is a Riemannian metric on $M$ if and only if $F$ is an immersion.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
By definition the pullback is the $2$-tensor field
\[
(F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,w) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}(dF_p v,\, dF_p w), \qquad v, w \in T_p M .
\]
It is symmetric at every $p$, whatever $F$ is, because $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}$ is symmetric, and it is a smooth $2$-tensor field because $F$ is smooth and $\tilde{g}$ is a smooth $2$-tensor field. So $F^*\tilde{g}$ is a Riemannian metric if and only if it is positive definite at every point, and it suffices to prove that this is equivalent to injectivity of every $dF_p$.

Suppose first that $F$ is an immersion, and let $p \in M$ and $v \in T_p M$ with $v \neq 0$. Then $dF_p v \neq 0$, since $dF_p$ is injective, so positive definiteness of $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}$ gives
\[
(F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,v) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}(dF_p v,\, dF_p v) > 0 .
\]
Hence $F^*\tilde{g}$ is positive definite.

Conversely, suppose $F^*\tilde{g}$ is positive definite, and fix $p \in M$. Since $dF_p$ is linear, it suffices to show its kernel is trivial. Let $v \in T_p M$ with $dF_p v = 0$, and suppose for contradiction that $v \neq 0$. Then
\[
0 < (F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,v) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}(dF_p v,\, dF_p v) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}(0,0) = 0,
\]
a contradiction. So $v = 0$, and $dF_p$ is injective. As $p$ was arbitrary, $F$ is an immersion.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:prove-lemma-2-11}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pullbackForm_posDef_iff_immersion}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.12}
\uses{lem:pullback-immersion-metric}
Prove Lemma 2.11.
\end{exercise}

\begin{definition}[The metric induced by an immersion]
\label{def:induced-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pullbackMetric}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pullback-immersion-metric,def:riemannian-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.11}
Suppose $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ is a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary and $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ is a smooth immersion. The \emph{metric induced by $F$} is the Riemannian metric $F^*\tilde{g}$ on $M$, which is a Riemannian metric by Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-immersion-metric}.
\end{definition}

On the other hand, if $M$ is already endowed with a given Riemannian metric $g$, an immersion or embedding $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ satisfying $F^*\tilde{g} = g$ is called an \emph{isometric immersion} or \emph{isometric embedding}, respectively. Which terminology is used depends on whether the metric on $M$ is considered to be given independently of the immersion or not.

The most important examples of induced metrics occur on submanifolds. Suppose $M \subseteq \tilde{M}$ is an (immersed or embedded) submanifold, with or without boundary. The \emph{induced metric on $M$} is the metric $g = \iota^*\tilde{g}$ induced by the inclusion map $\iota : M \hookrightarrow \tilde{M}$. With this metric, $M$ is called a \emph{Riemannian submanifold} (or Riemannian submanifold with boundary) of $\tilde{M}$. We always consider submanifolds (with or without boundary) of Riemannian manifolds to be endowed with the induced metrics unless otherwise specified.

If $(M, g)$ is a Riemannian submanifold of $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$, then for every $p \in M$ and $v, w \in T_p M$, the definition of the induced metric reads
\[
g_p(v,w) = \tilde{g}_p(d\iota_p(v), d\iota_p(w)).
\]

Because we usually identify $T_p M$ with its image in $T_p \tilde{M}$ under $d\iota_p$, and think of $d\iota_p$ as an inclusion map, what this really amounts to is $g_p(v,w) = \tilde{g}(v,w)$ for $v,w \in T_p M$. In other words, the induced metric $g$ is just the restriction of $\tilde{g}$ to vectors tangent to $M$. Many of the examples of Riemannian metrics that we will encounter are obtained in this way, starting with the following.

\begin{definition}[Spheres]
\label{ex:spheres}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.roundMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:induced-metric,ex:euclidean-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.13}
For each positive integer $n$, the unit $n$-sphere $S^n \subseteq \mathbb{R}^{n+1}$ is an embedded $n$-dimensional submanifold. The Riemannian metric induced on $S^n$ by the Euclidean metric is denoted by $\bar{g}$ and known as the \textit{round metric} or \textit{standard metric} on $S^n$.

Concretely, the round metric is the pullback $\iota^* \bar{g}$ of the Euclidean metric along the inclusion $\iota : S^n \hookrightarrow \mathbb{R}^{n+1}$. This is a Riemannian metric by Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-immersion-metric}, because $\iota$ is a smooth immersion: it is smooth, and its differential $d\iota_p : T_p S^n \to T_p\mathbb{R}^{n+1}$ is injective at every $p$, with image the orthogonal complement $(\mathbb{R}p)^\perp$ of the radial direction.
\end{definition}

The next lemma describes one of the most important tools for studying Riemannian submanifolds. If $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ is an $m$-dimensional smooth Riemannian manifold and $M \subseteq \tilde{M}$ is an $n$-dimensional submanifold (both with or without boundary), a local frame $(E_1, \ldots, E_m)$ for $\tilde{M}$ on an open subset $U \subseteq \tilde{M}$ is said to be \textit{adapted to} $M$ if the first $n$ vector fields $(E_1, \ldots, E_n)$ are tangent to $M$. In case $\tilde{M}$ has empty boundary (so that slice coordinates are available), adapted local orthonormal frames are easy to find.

\begin{proposition}[Existence of Adapted Orthonormal Frames]
\label{prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames}
\dcref{ch2:2.14}
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,lem:partial-frame-extension,lem:pushforward-smooth,lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian,prop:existence-orthonormal-frames,lem:pullback-immersion-metric}
Let $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ be a Riemannian manifold (without boundary), and let $M \subseteq \tilde{M}$ be an embedded smooth submanifold with or without boundary. Given $p \in M$, there exist a neighborhood $\tilde{U}$ of $p$ in $\tilde{M}$ and a smooth orthonormal frame for $\tilde{M}$ on $\tilde{U}$ that is adapted to $M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:gram-schmidt-adapted-frame}
\dcref{ch2:2.15}
\uses{prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames,prop:slice-coordinates,prop:existence-orthonormal-frames}
Prove the preceding proposition. [Hint: Apply the Gram--Schmidt algorithm to a coordinate frame in slice coordinates (see Prop. A.22).]
\end{exercise}

Slice coordinates are only one way to produce a frame whose first $n$ members are tangent to $M$. Presenting the submanifold by an immersion $F : M \to \tilde M$ instead, the tangent directions are supplied by the pushforward of a frame of $TM$, and the frame lives on the ambient tangent bundle $F^* T\tilde M$ of Lemma \ref{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian}. The following four lemmas carry out that route; they use no slice coordinates and so apply to immersed as well as embedded submanifolds. Throughout, a \textit{local $k$-frame} for a vector bundle $V \to B$ over $u \subseteq B$ means a $k$-tuple of smooth sections of $V$ over $u$ that is linearly independent at every point of $u$ — not necessarily spanning.

\begin{lemma}[Ordered extension of a linearly independent family]
\label{lem:ordered-linear-extension}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_linearIndependent_castAdd}
\leanok
Let $V$ be a vector space with $\dim V = m$, let $k, d \geq 0$ satisfy $k + d \leq m$, and let $(c_1, \ldots, c_k)$ be a linearly independent family in $V$. Then there is a linearly independent family $(w_1, \ldots, w_{k+d})$ in $V$ with $w_i = c_i$ for $1 \leq i \leq k$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Induct on $d$. If $d = 0$, take $w = c$.

Suppose the claim holds for $d$, and let $k + d + 1 \leq m$. Then $\dim \mathrm{span}(c_1, \ldots, c_k) = k < m$, so $\mathrm{span}(c_1, \ldots, c_k) \neq V$ and there is $y \in V \setminus \mathrm{span}(c_1, \ldots, c_k)$. The family $(c_1, \ldots, c_k, y)$ is linearly independent: in a vanishing linear combination $\sum_i a_i c_i + b y = 0$, a nonzero $b$ would give $y = -b^{-1}\sum_i a_i c_i \in \mathrm{span}(c_1,\ldots,c_k)$, contrary to the choice of $y$; so $b = 0$, and then all $a_i$ vanish by independence of the $c_i$. Applying the inductive hypothesis to this family of $k+1$ vectors and to $d$ (note $(k+1) + d \le m$) yields a linearly independent family of $k + 1 + d = k + (d+1)$ vectors whose first $k+1$ members are $c_1, \ldots, c_k, y$; in particular its first $k$ members are $c_1, \ldots, c_k$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Linear independence of smooth sections is an open condition]
\label{lem:open-linear-independence}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isLocalIndepOn_nhds}
\leanok
Let $V \to B$ be a smooth vector bundle with model fibre $F$, let $u \subseteq B$ be open, and let $s_1, \ldots, s_k$ be smooth sections of $V$ over $u$. If $(s_1|_{x_0}, \ldots, s_k|_{x_0})$ is linearly independent in $V_{x_0}$ for some $x_0 \in u$, then there is an open set $v$ with $x_0 \in v \subseteq u$ such that $(s_1|_x, \ldots, s_k|_x)$ is linearly independent in $V_x$ for every $x \in v$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
First, the set of linearly independent $k$-tuples is open in $F^k$. Let $(v_1, \ldots, v_k)$ be linearly independent and let $L : \mathbb{R}^k \to F$ be the injective linear map $a \mapsto \sum_i a_i v_i$. As $\mathbb{R}^k$ is finite dimensional, injectivity of $L$ gives a $\kappa > 0$ with $\|La\| \geq \kappa \|a\|$ for all $a$. Let $(v_1', \ldots, v_k')$ satisfy $\delta := \sum_i \|v_i' - v_i\| < \kappa$, and let $L'$ be the corresponding map. Then
\[
\|L'a - La\| = \Big\| \sum_i a_i (v_i' - v_i) \Big\| \leq \max_i |a_i| \sum_i \|v_i' - v_i\| \leq \delta \|a\|,
\]
so $\|L'a\| \geq \|La\| - \delta\|a\| \geq (\kappa - \delta)\|a\| > 0$ for $a \neq 0$. Hence $L'$ is injective, i.e. $(v_i')$ is linearly independent.

Now choose a local trivialization $\Phi$ of $V$ over an open set containing $x_0$, and write $\Phi_x : V_x \to F$ for its fibrewise linear isomorphism. Put $c(x) = (\Phi_x(s_1|_x), \ldots, \Phi_x(s_k|_x)) \in F^k$. Each component is continuous at $x_0$ because $s_i$ is a smooth section, so $c$ is continuous at $x_0$; and $c(x_0)$ is linearly independent, being the image of a linearly independent tuple under the isomorphism $\Phi_{x_0}$. By the previous paragraph linear independence holds on a neighbourhood of $c(x_0)$, so by continuity there is an open $v$ with $x_0 \in v$, contained in $u$ and in the domain of $\Phi$, such that $c(x)$ is linearly independent for every $x \in v$. For such $x$, $(s_i|_x)_i$ is the image of $c(x)$ under the isomorphism $\Phi_x^{-1}$ and is therefore linearly independent.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Extension of a partial frame]
\label{lem:partial-frame-extension}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isLocalFrameOn_extend}
\leanok
\uses{lem:ordered-linear-extension,lem:open-linear-independence}
Let $V \to B$ be a smooth vector bundle of rank $m$, let $u \subseteq B$ be open, and let $(s_1, \ldots, s_k)$ be a local $k$-frame for $V$ over $u$. Then $k \leq m$, and for each $x_0 \in u$ there are an open set $v$ with $x_0 \in v \subseteq u$ and a smooth local frame $(E_1, \ldots, E_m)$ for $V$ over $v$ with $E_i = s_i$ on $v$ for $1 \leq i \leq k$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Fix a local trivialization $\Phi$ of $V$ over an open set containing $x_0$, with fibrewise isomorphisms $\Phi_x : V_x \to F$. The vectors $c_i := \Phi_{x_0}(s_i|_{x_0})$, $1 \leq i \leq k$, are linearly independent in $F$, whence $k \leq \dim F = m$. By Lemma \ref{lem:ordered-linear-extension} with $d = m - k$ they extend to a linearly independent family $(w_1, \ldots, w_m)$ in $F$ with $w_i = c_i$ for $i \leq k$; being $m$ independent vectors in the $m$-dimensional space $F$, it is a basis.

Over $u \cap \mathrm{dom}\,\Phi$ define
\[
E_i = s_i \quad (1 \leq i \leq k), \qquad E_j|_x = \Phi_x^{-1}(w_j) \quad (k < j \leq m).
\]
For $i \leq k$ the section $E_i$ is smooth by hypothesis; for $j > k$ it is smooth because it is constant in the trivialization $\Phi$.

At $x_0$ we have $E_j|_{x_0} = \Phi_{x_0}^{-1}(w_j)$ for \emph{every} $j$: for $j > k$ by definition, and for $j \leq k$ because $w_j = c_j = \Phi_{x_0}(s_j|_{x_0})$. So $(E_1|_{x_0}, \ldots, E_m|_{x_0})$ is the image of the basis $(w_j)_j$ under the isomorphism $\Phi_{x_0}^{-1}$, hence a basis of $V_{x_0}$; in particular it is linearly independent. By Lemma \ref{lem:open-linear-independence} there is an open $v$ with $x_0 \in v \subseteq u \cap \mathrm{dom}\,\Phi$ on which $(E_1|_x, \ldots, E_m|_x)$ is linearly independent at every point. Since $\dim V_x = m$, a linearly independent family of $m$ vectors in $V_x$ spans $V_x$; so $(E_1, \ldots, E_m)$ is a smooth local frame for $V$ over $v$, and it agrees with $s$ in its first $k$ members by construction.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Smoothness of the pushforward of a vector field]
\label{lem:pushforward-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffOn_pushforward}
\leanok
\uses{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian}
Let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be a smooth map and let $X$ be a smooth vector field on an open set $u \subseteq M$. Then $x \mapsto dF_x(X_x)$ is a smooth section of the ambient tangent bundle $F^* T\tilde{M}$ over $u$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Fix $x_0 \in u$, a trivialization $\Sigma$ of $TM$ around $x_0$ and a trivialization $\Theta$ of $T\tilde{M}$ around $F(x_0)$, with fibrewise isomorphisms $\Sigma_x$ and $\Theta_y$. By construction a trivialization of $F^* T\tilde M$ at $x_0$ is $\Theta$ read along $F$: its fibre map at $x$ is $\Theta_{F(x)}$. Hence, for $x$ in the neighbourhood of $x_0$ on which $x \in \mathrm{dom}\,\Sigma$ and $F(x) \in \mathrm{dom}\,\Theta$, the section $x \mapsto dF_x(X_x)$ read in that trivialization is
\[
x \longmapsto \Theta_{F(x)}\big(dF_x(X_x)\big) = D(x)\,\xi(x), \qquad
D(x) := \Theta_{F(x)} \circ dF_x \circ \Sigma_x^{-1}, \quad \xi(x) := \Sigma_x(X_x),
\]
the equality holding because $\Sigma_x^{-1}(\xi(x)) = X_x$.

The map $\xi$ is smooth because $X$ is a smooth section read in $\Sigma$, and $D$ — the differential of $F$ in tangent coordinates — is smooth because $F$ is smooth. Evaluating a smooth family of continuous linear maps on a smooth family of vectors is smooth, so the coordinate representation is smooth at $x_0$. As $x_0 \in u$ was arbitrary, the section is smooth on $u$.
\end{proof}

Suppose $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ is a Riemannian manifold and $M \subseteq \tilde{M}$ is a smooth submanifold with or without boundary in $\tilde{M}$. Given $p \in M$, a vector $v \in T_p \tilde M$ is said to be \textit{normal to} $M$ if $\langle v,w\rangle = 0$ for every $w \in T_p M$. The space of all vectors normal to $M$ at $p$ is a subspace of $T_p \tilde{M}$, called the \textit{normal space at} $p$ and denoted by $N_p M = (T_p M)^\perp$. At each $p \in M$, the ambient tangent space $T_p \tilde{M}$ splits as an orthogonal direct sum $T_p \tilde{M} = T_p M \oplus N_p M$. A section $N$ of the ambient tangent bundle $T\tilde{M}|_M$ is called a \textit{normal vector field along} $M$ if $N_p \in N_p M$ for each $p \in M$. The set
\[
NM = \bigsqcup_{p \in M} N_p M
\]
is called the \textit{normal bundle of} $M$.

\begin{lemma}[The Ambient Tangent Bundle Along a Submanifold]
\label{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian}
\lean{Bundle.ContMDiffRiemannianMetric.pullback}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,def:induced-metric}
Let $(\tilde M, \tilde g)$ be a Riemannian manifold and let $F : M \to \tilde M$ be a smooth map. Then the pullback bundle $F^* T\tilde M$ over $M$, whose fibre at $p$ is $T_{F(p)}\tilde M$, carries the ambient inner product $\tilde g_{F(p)}$ on each fibre, and this inner product varies smoothly with $p$; that is, $F^* T\tilde M$ is a Riemannian vector bundle over $M$.

When $F$ is the inclusion of a submanifold $M \subseteq \tilde M$, this bundle is the \emph{ambient tangent bundle along $M$}, written $T\tilde M|_M$: the bundle in which the splitting $T_p\tilde M = T_p M \oplus N_p M$ and the projections $\pi^\top, \pi^\perp$ of Proposition \ref{prop:normal-bundle} live.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
The fibre of $F^* T\tilde M$ at $p$ \emph{is} $T_{F(p)}\tilde M$, so it carries $\tilde g_{F(p)}$ with no choice involved; symmetry and positive definiteness are those of $\tilde g$ at the point $F(p)$. Only the smooth dependence on $p$ requires an argument.

Fix $p_0 \in M$. Smoothness of a metric on a vector bundle means that, read through a local trivialization of the bundle near $p_0$, the metric is a smooth map into the space of bilinear forms on the model fibre. By construction, a trivialization of $F^* T\tilde M$ over $p_0$ is the pullback along $F$ of a trivialization $\Phi$ of $T\tilde M$ over $F(p_0)$: its fibre map at $p$ is the fibre map of $\Phi$ at $F(p)$. Hence, for $p$ in the neighbourhood of $p_0$ on which $F(p)$ stays in the domain of $\Phi$, the metric of $F^* T\tilde M$ read through the pulled-back trivialization at $p$ equals the metric $\tilde g$ read through $\Phi$ at $F(p)$.

The map $p \mapsto (\tilde g \text{ read through } \Phi)(F(p))$ is a composition of $F$, which is smooth, with the coordinate representation of $\tilde g$, which is smooth because $\tilde g$ is a smooth metric. So the coordinate representation of the pulled-back metric is smooth at $p_0$, and $p_0$ was arbitrary.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Existence of Adapted Orthonormal Frames, immersed form]
\label{prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames-immersed}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_adapted_orthonormalFrame}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,lem:partial-frame-extension,lem:pushforward-smooth,lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian,prop:existence-orthonormal-frames,lem:pullback-immersion-metric}
Let $(\tilde M, \tilde g)$ be a Riemannian $m$-manifold, let $M$ be an $n$-manifold and let
$F : M \to \tilde M$ be a smooth immersion. Then $n \le m$, and every $p \in M$ has a
neighborhood $v$ carrying a smooth orthonormal frame $(E_1, \ldots, E_m)$ for the ambient
tangent bundle $F^* T\tilde M$ of Lemma \ref{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian} such that,
at every $x \in v$,
\[
  \operatorname{span}(E_1|_x, \ldots, E_n|_x) \;=\; \operatorname{im} dF_x .
\]
Since $\operatorname{im} dF_x$ is Lee's $T_x M$ viewed inside $T_{F(x)}\tilde M$, this says
exactly that the frame is adapted to $M$. It is Proposition \ref{prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames}
with the submanifold presented by an immersion rather than by an embedding, so it applies to
immersed submanifolds as well; the price is that the frame is a frame of $F^* T\tilde M$ over
$M$ rather than of $T\tilde M$ over an open subset of $\tilde M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:partial-frame-extension,lem:pushforward-smooth,lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian,prop:existence-orthonormal-frames}
Fix $p \in M$ and let $(X_1, \ldots, X_n)$ be any smooth local frame for $TM$ on an open
$u \ni p$ — for instance the frame induced on the base set of a trivialization of $TM$ at $p$ by
a basis of the model space. Put
\[
  Z_i|_x := dF_x(X_i|_x), \qquad i = 1, \ldots, n, \quad x \in u .
\]
By Lemma \ref{lem:pushforward-smooth} each $Z_i$ is a smooth section of $F^* T\tilde M$ over $u$.
At each $x \in u$ the family $(Z_i|_x)_i$ is linearly independent: $(X_i|_x)_i$ is, and $dF_x$ is
injective because $F$ is an immersion. So $(Z_1, \ldots, Z_n)$ is a local $n$-frame for
$F^* T\tilde M$ over $u$.

By Lemma \ref{lem:partial-frame-extension} applied to this $n$-frame — the rank of
$F^* T\tilde M$ is $m$ — we get $n \le m$, an open $v$ with $p \in v \subseteq u$, and a smooth
local frame $(Y_1, \ldots, Y_m)$ for $F^* T\tilde M$ over $v$ with $Y_i = Z_i$ on $v$ for
$i \le n$.

By Lemma \ref{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian} the ambient metric $\tilde g$ makes
$F^* T\tilde M$ a Riemannian bundle, so Proposition \ref{prop:existence-orthonormal-frames}
applies to it: the fibrewise Gram--Schmidt orthonormalization $(E_1, \ldots, E_m)$ of
$(Y_1, \ldots, Y_m)$ is again a smooth local frame over $v$, is orthonormal with respect to
$\tilde g$ at every point of $v$, and satisfies the flag condition
$\operatorname{span}(E_1|_x, \ldots, E_k|_x) = \operatorname{span}(Y_1|_x, \ldots, Y_k|_x)$ for
every $k$ and every $x \in v$.

It remains to identify the span of the first $n$ members. Fix $x \in v$ and $i \le n$. The flag
condition at $k = i$ gives
$E_i|_x \in \operatorname{span}(Y_1|_x, \ldots, Y_i|_x) = \operatorname{span}(Z_1|_x, \ldots, Z_i|_x)$,
and each $Z_j|_x = dF_x(X_j|_x)$ lies in $\operatorname{im} dF_x$, which is a subspace; hence
$E_i|_x \in \operatorname{im} dF_x$. Therefore
$\operatorname{span}(E_1|_x, \ldots, E_n|_x) \subseteq \operatorname{im} dF_x$. Both sides have
dimension $n$: the left because $(E_1|_x, \ldots, E_n|_x)$ is orthonormal, hence linearly
independent, and the right because $dF_x$ is injective on the $n$-dimensional $T_x M$. A subspace
of a space of the same finite dimension is the whole space, so the inclusion is an equality.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[The Normal Space]
\label{def:normal-space}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.normalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian}
Let $(\tilde M, \tilde g)$ be a Riemannian $m$-manifold and let $F : M \to \tilde M$ present an
$n$-manifold $M$ as a smooth immersion. For $x \in M$, the \emph{normal space to $M$ at $x$} is
the subspace of the ambient tangent space $T_{F(x)}\tilde M$ given by
\[
  N_x M = \bigl\{\, v \in T_{F(x)}\tilde M \;:\; \tilde g_{F(x)}(v, w) = 0
    \ \text{ for every } w \in T_x M \,\bigr\},
\]
where, as elsewhere in this chapter, $T_x M$ denotes the image $dF_x(T_x M) \subseteq
T_{F(x)}\tilde M$. It is a linear subspace, being the intersection of the kernels of the linear
functionals $\tilde g_{F(x)}(\cdot, w)$ for $w \in T_x M$.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[Local Orthonormal Frames for the Normal Space]
\label{lem:normal-space-orthonormal-frame}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_orthonormalFrame_normalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:normal-space,prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames-immersed}
Let $(\tilde M, \tilde g)$ be a Riemannian $m$-manifold and let $F : M \to \tilde M$ present an
$n$-manifold $M$ as a smooth immersion. Then $n \le m$, and every $p \in M$ has a neighbourhood
$v$ carrying smooth sections $N_1, \ldots, N_{m-n}$ of the ambient tangent bundle $F^*T\tilde M$
over $v$ whose values at each $x \in v$ form an orthonormal basis of $N_x M$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames-immersed}
By Proposition \ref{prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames-immersed} there are $n \le m$, a
neighbourhood $v$ of $p$, and a smooth local frame $(E_1, \ldots, E_m)$ for $F^*T\tilde M$ over
$v$ that is orthonormal with respect to $\tilde g$ at every $x \in v$ and whose first $n$ members
span $T_x M$ at every $x \in v$. Put $N_j = E_{n+j}$ for $1 \le j \le m-n$; these are smooth
sections over $v$, and they are orthonormal at each $x \in v$ because $(E_a)_a$ is.

Fix $x \in v$. Each $N_j|_x$ is normal: for $i \le n$ we have $n + j \ne i$, so
$\tilde g(N_j|_x, E_i|_x) = 0$ by orthonormality; since $E_1|_x, \ldots, E_n|_x$ span $T_x M$ and
$\tilde g$ is linear in its second argument, $\tilde g(N_j|_x, w) = 0$ for every $w \in T_x M$.
Hence $\operatorname{span}(N_1|_x, \ldots, N_{m-n}|_x) \subseteq N_x M$, as $N_x M$ is a subspace.

Conversely let $w \in N_x M$. Since $(E_a|_x)_{a=1}^m$ is a basis of $T_{F(x)}\tilde M$, write
$w = \sum_{a=1}^m c_a E_a|_x$. Pairing with $E_b|_x$ and using orthonormality gives
\[
  \tilde g(E_b|_x, w) = \sum_{a=1}^m c_a \, \tilde g(E_b|_x, E_a|_x) = c_b .
\]
For $b \le n$ we have $E_b|_x \in T_x M$, and $w$ is normal, so $c_b = \tilde g(E_b|_x, w) = 0$.
Therefore $w = \sum_{j=1}^{m-n} c_{n+j} N_j|_x$ lies in
$\operatorname{span}(N_1|_x, \ldots, N_{m-n}|_x)$.

The two inclusions give $\operatorname{span}(N_1|_x, \ldots, N_{m-n}|_x) = N_x M$; together with
orthonormality, $(N_j|_x)_j$ is an orthonormal basis of $N_x M$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[The Normal Bundle]
\label{prop:normal-bundle}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffVectorBundle_normalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian,def:normal-space,lem:normal-space-orthonormal-frame,lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles}
\dcref{ch2:2.16}
If $\tilde{M}$ is a Riemannian $m$-manifold and $M \subseteq \tilde{M}$ is an immersed or embedded $n$-dimensional submanifold with or without boundary, then $NM$ is a smooth rank-$(m-n)$ vector subbundle of the ambient tangent bundle $T\tilde{M}|_M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:normal-space-orthonormal-frame,lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles,lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian}
By Lemma \ref{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian} the ambient tangent bundle $T\tilde M|_M = F^*T\tilde M$ is a smooth vector bundle over $M$ carrying the fibre metric $\tilde g$, so Lemma \ref{lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles} applies to it. Take the family of subspaces $D_p = N_p M \subseteq T_p\tilde M$, each of dimension $m - n$.

Its hypothesis is Lemma \ref{lem:normal-space-orthonormal-frame}: every $p \in M$ has a neighbourhood $v$ carrying smooth sections $N_1, \ldots, N_{m-n}$ of $T\tilde M|_M$ whose values at each $x \in v$ form an orthonormal basis --- in particular a basis --- of $N_x M$. Lemma \ref{lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles} therefore makes $NM = \bigcup_{p \in M} N_p M$ a smooth rank-$(m-n)$ subbundle of $T\tilde M|_M$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}
\label{rem:normal-bundle-projections}
\uses{prop:normal-bundle}
Lee's statement of the preceding proposition also asserts the existence of smooth bundle homomorphisms
\[
\pi^\top : T\tilde{M}|_M \to TM, \quad \pi^\perp : T\tilde{M}|_M \to NM,
\]
the \emph{tangential and normal projections}, restricting at each $p \in M$ to the orthogonal projections of $T_p\tilde M$ onto $T_p M$ and $N_p M$. These are the fibrewise orthogonal projections of the splitting $T_p\tilde M = T_p M \oplus N_p M$; their smoothness follows from the same adapted orthonormal frame, in which $\pi^\top$ and $\pi^\perp$ are the truncations of the coordinate expansion to the first $n$ and the last $m-n$ members. They are not part of the node above.
\end{remark}

\begin{proof}
\uses{thm:local-embedding-theorem,lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles,prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames-immersed,lem:normal-space-orthonormal-frame}
Given any point $p \in M$, Theorem A.16 shows that there is a neighborhood $U$ of $p$ in $\widetilde{M}$ that is embedded in $\widetilde{M}$, and then Proposition 2.14 shows that there is a smooth orthonormal frame $(E_1, \ldots, E_m)$ that is adapted to $U$ on some neighborhood $\widetilde{U}$ of $p$ in $\widetilde{M}$. This means that the restrictions of $(E_1, \ldots, E_n)$ to $U \cap \widetilde{U}$ form a local orthonormal frame for $M$. Given such an adapted frame, the restrictions of the last $m - n$ vector fields $(E_{n+1}, \ldots, E_m)$ to $M$ form a smooth local frame for $NM$, so it follows from Lemma A.34 that $NM$ is a smooth subbundle.

The bundle homomorphisms $\pi^\top$ and $\pi^\perp$ are defined pointwise as orthogonal projections onto the tangent and normal spaces, respectively, which shows that they are uniquely defined. In terms of an adapted orthonormal frame, they can be written
\[
\pi^\top(X^1 E_1 + \cdots + X^m E_m) = X^1 E_1 + \cdots + X^n E_n,
\]
\[
\pi^\perp(X^1 E_1 + \cdots + X^m E_m) = X^{n+1} E_{n+1} + \cdots + X^m E_m,
\]
which shows that they are smooth.
\end{proof}

In case $\widetilde{M}$ is a manifold with boundary, the preceding constructions do not always work, because there is not a fully general construction of slice coordinates in that case. However, there is a satisfactory result in case the submanifold is the boundary itself, using boundary coordinates in place of slice coordinates.

Suppose $(M, g)$ is a Riemannian manifold with boundary. We will always consider $\partial M$ to be a Riemannian submanifold with the induced metric.

\begin{proposition}[Existence of Outward-Pointing Normal]
\label{prop:outward-pointing-normal}
\dcref{ch2:2.17}
If $(M, g)$ is a smooth Riemannian manifold with boundary, the normal bundle to $\partial M$ is a smooth rank-1 vector bundle over $\partial M$, and there is a unique smooth outward-pointing unit normal vector field along all of $\partial M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:prove-outward-normal}
\dcref{ch2:2.18}
\uses{prop:induced-orientation-boundary}
Prove this proposition. [Hint: Use the paragraph preceding Prop. B.17 as a starting point.]
\end{exercise}

Computations on a submanifold $M \subset \widetilde{M}$ are usually carried out most conveniently in terms of a \emph{smooth local parametrization}: this is a smooth map $X : U \to \widetilde{M}$, where $U$ is an open subset of $\mathbb{R}^n$ (or $\mathbb{R}^n_+$ in case $M$ has a boundary), such that $X(U)$ is an open subset of $M$, and such that $X$, regarded as a map from $U$ into $M$, is a diffeomorphism onto its image. Note that we can think of $X$ either as a map into $M$ or as a map into $\widetilde{M}$; both maps are typically denoted by the same symbol $X$. If we put $V = X(U) \subseteq M$ and $\varphi = X^{-1} : V \to U$, then $(V, \varphi)$ is a smooth coordinate chart on $M$.

Suppose $(M, g)$ is a Riemannian submanifold of $(\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{g})$ and $X : U \to \widetilde{M}$ is a smooth local parametrization of $M$. The coordinate representation of $g$ in these coordinates is given by the following 2-tensor field on $U$:
\[
(\varphi^{-1})^* g = X^* g = X^* \iota^* \widetilde{g} = (\iota \circ X)^* \widetilde{g}.
\]
Since $\iota \circ X$ is just the map $X$ itself, regarded as a map into $\tilde{M}$, this is really just $X^*\tilde{g}$. The simplicity of the formula for the pullback of a tensor field makes this expression exceedingly easy to compute, once a coordinate expression for $\tilde{g}$ is known. For example, if $M$ is an immersed $n$-dimensional Riemannian submanifold of $\mathbb{R}^m$ and $X : U \to \mathbb{R}^m$ is a smooth local parametrization of $M$, the induced metric on $U$ is just
\[
g = X^*\tilde{g} = \sum_{i=1}^m (dX^i)^2 = \sum_{i=1}^m \left(\sum_{j=1}^n \frac{\partial X^i}{\partial u^j} du^j\right)^2 = \sum_{i=1}^m \sum_{j,k=1}^n \frac{\partial X^i}{\partial u^j}\frac{\partial X^i}{\partial u^k} du^j du^k.
\]

\begin{example}[Metrics in Graph Coordinates]
\label{ex:metrics-in-graph-coordinates}
\dcref{ch2:2.19}

If $U \subseteq \mathbb{R}^n$ is an open set and $f : U \to \mathbb{R}$ is a smooth function, then the \textbf{graph of $f$} is the subset $\Gamma(f) = \{(x, f(x)) : x \in U\} \subseteq \mathbb{R}^{n+1}$, which is an embedded submanifold of dimension $n$. It has a global parametrization $X : U \to \mathbb{R}^{n+1}$ called a \textbf{graph parametrization}, given by $X(u) = (u, f(u))$; the corresponding coordinates $(u^1,\ldots,u^n)$ on $M$ are called \textbf{graph coordinates}. In graph coordinates, the induced metric of $\Gamma(f)$ is
\[
X^*\tilde{g} = X^*\left((dx^1)^2 + \cdots + (dx^{n+1})^2\right) = (du^1)^2 + \cdots + (du^n)^2 + df^2.
\]

Applying this to the upper hemisphere of $S^2$ with the parametrization $X : \mathbb{R}^2 \to \mathbb{R}^3$ given by
\[
X(u,v) = \left(u, v, \sqrt{1-u^2-v^2}\right),
\]
we see that the round metric on $S^2$ can be written locally as
\[
\hat{g} = X^*\bar{g} = du^2 + dv^2 + \left(\frac{u\,du + v\,dv}{\sqrt{1-u^2-v^2}}\right)^2
\]
\[
= \frac{(1-v^2) du^2 + (1-u^2) dv^2 + 2uv\,du\,dv}{1-u^2-v^2}.
\]
\end{example}

\begin{example}[Surfaces of Revolution]
\label{ex:surfaces-of-revolution}
\dcref{ch2:2.20}

Let $H$ be the half-plane $\{(r,z) : r > 0\}$, and suppose $C \subseteq H$ is an embedded 1-dimensional submanifold. The \textbf{surface of revolution} determined by $C$ is the subset $S_C \subseteq \mathbb{R}^3$ given by
\[
S_C = \left\{(x,y,z) : \left(\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}, z\right) \in C\right\}.
\]

The set $C$ is called its \textbf{generating curve} (see Fig. 2.1). Every smooth local parametrization $\gamma(t) = (a(t), b(t))$ for $C$ yields a smooth local parametrization for $S_C$ of the form
\begin{equation}
X(t, \theta) = (a(t) \cos \theta, a(t) \sin \theta, b(t)), \tag{2.11}
\end{equation}

provided that $(t, \theta)$ is restricted to a sufficiently small open set in the plane. The $t$-coordinate curves $t \mapsto X(t, \theta_0)$ are called \textbf{meridians}, and the $\theta$-coordinate curves $\theta \mapsto X(t_0, \theta)$ are called \textbf{latitude circles}. The induced metric on $S_C$ is
% [FIGURE: Two diagrams showing a surface of revolution — left diagram shows a curve C in the rz-plane, right diagram shows the 3D surface obtained by rotating C around the z-axis]
\begin{align*}
X^*\bar{g} &= d(a(t)\cos\theta)^2 + d(a(t)\sin\theta)^2 + d(b(t))^2 \\
&= (a'(t)\cos\theta \, dt - a(t)\sin\theta \, d\theta)^2 + (a'(t)\sin\theta \, dt + a(t)\cos\theta \, d\theta)^2 + (b'(t)dt)^2 \\
&= (a'(t)^2 + b'(t)^2)dt^2 + a(t)^2 d\theta^2.
\end{align*}

In particular, if $\gamma$ is a \emph{unit-speed curve} (meaning that $|\gamma'(t)|^2 = a'(t)^2 + b'(t)^2 \equiv 1$), this reduces to $dt^2 + a(t)^2 d\theta^2$.

Here are some examples of surfaces of revolution and their induced metrics.

\begin{itemize}
\item If $C$ is the semicircle $r^2 + z^2 = 1$, parametrized by $\gamma(\varphi)=(\sin\varphi, \cos\varphi)$ for $0 < \varphi < \pi$, then $S_C$ is the unit sphere (minus the north and south poles). The map $X(\varphi, \theta) = (\sin\varphi\cos\theta, \sin\varphi\sin\theta, \cos\varphi)$ constructed above is called the \emph{spherical coordinate parametrization}, and the induced metric is $d\varphi^2 + \sin^2\varphi \, d\theta^2$. (This example is the source of the terminology for meridians and latitude circles.)

\item If $C$ is the circle $(r-2)^2 + z^2 = 1$, parametrized by $\gamma(t) = (2+\cos t, \sin t)$, we obtain a torus of revolution, whose induced metric is $dt^2 + (2+\cos t)^2 d\theta^2$.

\item If $C$ is a vertical line parametrized by $\gamma(t) = (1,t)$, then $S_C$ is the unit cylinder $x^2 + y^2 = 1$, and the induced metric is $dt^2 + d\theta^2$. Note that this means that the parametrization $X: \mathbb{R}^2 \to \mathbb{R}^3$ is an isometric immersion. \quad //
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\begin{example}[The n-Torus as a Riemannian Submanifold]
\label{ex:n-torus-riemannian-submanifold}
\dcref{ch2:2.21}
\uses{ex:covering-n-torus}

The smooth covering map $X: \mathbb{R}^n \to \mathbb{T}^n$ described in Example A.52 restricts to a smooth local parametrization on any sufficiently small open subset of $\mathbb{R}^n$, and the induced metric is equal to the Euclidean metric in $(u^i)$ coordinates, and therefore the induced metric on $\mathbb{T}^n$ is flat.
\end{example}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:verify-surface-revolution-examples}
\dcref{ch2:2.22}
\uses{ex:metrics-in-graph-coordinates,ex:surfaces-of-revolution,ex:n-torus-riemannian-submanifold}

Verify the claims in Examples 2.19–2.21.
\end{exercise}

\section{Riemannian Products}

Next we consider products. If $(M_1, g_1)$ and $(M_2, g_2)$ are Riemannian manifolds, the product manifold $M_1 \times M_2$ has a natural Riemannian metric $g = g_1 \oplus g_2$, called the \emph{product metric}, defined by
\begin{equation}
g_{(p_1,p_2)}((v_1, v_2), (w_1, w_2)) = g_1\big|_{p_1}(v_1, w_1) + g_2\big|_{p_2}(v_2, w_2). \tag{2.12}
\end{equation}

where $(v_1, v_2)$ and $(w_1, w_2)$ are elements of $T_{p_1}M_1 \oplus T_{p_2}M_2$, which is naturally identified with $T_{(p_1,p_2)}(M_1 \times M_2)$. Smooth local coordinates $(x^1, \ldots, x^n)$ for $M_1$ and $(x^{n+1}, \ldots, x^{n+m})$ for $M_2$ give coordinates $(x^1, \ldots, x^{n+m})$ for $M_1 \times M_2$. In terms of these coordinates, the product metric has the local expression $g = g_{ij} \, dx^i \, dx^j$, where $(g_{ij})$ is the block diagonal matrix
\[
(g_{ij}) = \begin{pmatrix} (g_1)_{ab} & 0 \\ 0 & (g_2)_{cd} \end{pmatrix};
\]
here the indices $a, b$ run from $1$ to $n$, and $c, d$ run from $n+1$ to $n+m$. Product metrics on products of three or more Riemannian manifolds are defined similarly.

The two blocks of (2.12) are the pullbacks of $g_1$ and $g_2$ along the two projections. Indeed, $d(\pi_1)_{(p_1,p_2)}(v_1,v_2) = v_1$ and $d(\pi_2)_{(p_1,p_2)}(v_1,v_2) = v_2$ under the identification $T_{(p_1,p_2)}(M_1 \times M_2) \cong T_{p_1}M_1 \oplus T_{p_2}M_2$, so
\begin{equation}
g_1 \oplus g_2 = \pi_1^* g_1 + \pi_2^* g_2 . \tag{2.12'}
\end{equation}
Each summand is a smooth $2$-tensor field, being the pullback of one along a smooth map; neither is positive definite, since a projection is not an immersion; but the sum is, which is the content of Lemma \ref{lem:product-form-positive-definite}. Assembling (2.12') from its blocks uses only the two standard facts that sections of a smooth vector bundle may be added, and rescaled by a smooth function, without losing smoothness.

\begin{lemma}[Sums of smooth sections]
\label{lem:smooth-tensor-field-add}
\lean{ContMDiff.add_section}
\mathlibok
Let $V$ be a smooth vector bundle over a smooth manifold $B$, and let $s, t$ be smooth sections of $V$. Then $s + t$ is a smooth section of $V$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Smooth rescalings of smooth sections]
\label{lem:smooth-tensor-field-smul}
\lean{ContMDiff.smul_section}
\mathlibok
Let $V$ be a smooth vector bundle over a smooth manifold $B$, let $s$ be a smooth section of $V$, and let $c : B \to \mathbb{R}$ be smooth. Then $b \mapsto c(b)\,s_b$ is a smooth section of $V$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Positive definiteness of the product form]
\label{lem:product-form-positive-definite}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.prodForm_pos}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(M_1,g_1)$ and $(M_2,g_2)$ be Riemannian manifolds, and let $g_1 \oplus g_2$ be the symmetric $2$-tensor field on $M_1 \times M_2$ defined by (2.12). Then $g_1 \oplus g_2$ is positive definite at every point.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Fix $(p_1,p_2) \in M_1 \times M_2$ and let $v = (v_1,v_2) \in T_{p_1}M_1 \oplus T_{p_2}M_2$ be nonzero. Then $v_1 \neq 0$ or $v_2 \neq 0$. By (2.12),
\[
(g_1 \oplus g_2)_{(p_1,p_2)}(v,v) = g_1|_{p_1}(v_1,v_1) + g_2|_{p_2}(v_2,v_2),
\]
and both summands are nonnegative because $g_1$ and $g_2$ are positive definite. If $v_1 \neq 0$ the first summand is strictly positive and the second is nonnegative; if $v_2 \neq 0$ the second is strictly positive and the first is nonnegative. In either case the sum is strictly positive.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[The Product Metric]
\label{def:product-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.prodMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,lem:product-form-positive-definite,lem:smooth-tensor-field-add,lem:pullback-immersion-metric}
Let $(M_1,g_1)$ and $(M_2,g_2)$ be Riemannian manifolds. The \emph{product metric} $g_1 \oplus g_2$ on $M_1 \times M_2$ is the Riemannian metric defined by (2.12). It is symmetric because $g_1$ and $g_2$ are, it is positive definite by Lemma \ref{lem:product-form-positive-definite}, and it is a smooth $2$-tensor field by (2.12') together with Lemma \ref{lem:smooth-tensor-field-add}, each summand being a pullback of a smooth $2$-tensor field along a smooth map.
\end{definition}

\begin{exercise}[Induced Metric on $T^n$]
\label{exer:product-metric-torus}
\dcref{ch2:2.23}
\uses{ex:n-torus-riemannian-submanifold,def:product-metric}
Show that the induced metric on $T^n$ described in Exercise 2.21 is equal to the product metric obtained from the usual induced metric on $S^1 \subseteq \mathbb{R}^2$.
\end{exercise}

Here is an important generalization of product metrics. Suppose $(M_1, g_1)$ and $(M_2, g_2)$ are two Riemannian manifolds, and $f : M_1 \to \mathbb{R}^+$ is a strictly positive smooth function. The \emph{warped product} $M_1 \times_f M_2$ is the product manifold $M_1 \times M_2$ endowed with the Riemannian metric $g = g_1 \oplus f^2 g_2$, defined by
\begin{equation}
g_{(p_1,p_2)}((v_1, v_2), (w_1, w_2)) = g_1\big|_{p_1}(v_1, w_1) + f(p_1)^2 \, g_2\big|_{p_2}(v_2, w_2),
\end{equation}

where $(v_1, v_2), (w_1, w_2) \in T_{p_1}M_1 \oplus T_{p_2}M_2$ as before. (Despite the similarity with the notation for product metrics, $g_1 \oplus f^2 g_2$ is generally not a product metric unless $f$ is constant.) A wide variety of metrics can be constructed in this way; here are just a few examples.

\begin{lemma}[Positive definiteness of the warped product form]
\label{lem:warped-product-form-positive-definite}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.warpedProductForm_pos}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(M_1,g_1)$ and $(M_2,g_2)$ be Riemannian manifolds and let $f : M_1 \to \mathbb{R}$ be nowhere vanishing. Then $g_1 \oplus f^2 g_2$ is positive definite at every point.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,lem:product-form-positive-definite}
Fix $(p_1,p_2)$ and a nonzero $v = (v_1,v_2) \in T_{p_1}M_1 \oplus T_{p_2}M_2$, so that $v_1 \neq 0$ or $v_2 \neq 0$. Since $f(p_1) \neq 0$ we have $f(p_1)^2 > 0$, and
\[
(g_1 \oplus f^2 g_2)_{(p_1,p_2)}(v,v) = g_1|_{p_1}(v_1,v_1) + f(p_1)^2\, g_2|_{p_2}(v_2,v_2).
\]
Both summands are nonnegative, by positive definiteness of $g_1$ and $g_2$ and $f(p_1)^2 > 0$. If $v_1 \neq 0$ the first is strictly positive; if $v_2 \neq 0$ then $g_2|_{p_2}(v_2,v_2) > 0$ and hence the second is strictly positive, being a product of two strictly positive numbers. In either case the sum is strictly positive.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Warped Products]
\label{def:warped-product}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.warpedProductMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:product-metric,def:riemannian-metric,lem:warped-product-form-positive-definite,lem:smooth-tensor-field-add,lem:smooth-tensor-field-smul}
Let $(M_1,g_1)$ and $(M_2,g_2)$ be Riemannian manifolds and let $f : M_1 \to \mathbb{R}^+$ be a strictly positive smooth function. The \emph{warped product} $M_1 \times_f M_2$ is the product manifold $M_1 \times M_2$ endowed with the Riemannian metric $g_1 \oplus f^2 g_2$.

That $g_1 \oplus f^2 g_2$ is a Riemannian metric follows as for the product metric: it is symmetric because $g_1$ and $g_2$ are, positive definite by Lemma \ref{lem:warped-product-form-positive-definite}, and smooth because
\[
g_1 \oplus f^2 g_2 = \pi_1^* g_1 + (f \circ \pi_1)^2\, \pi_2^* g_2,
\]
whose first summand is smooth as in Definition \ref{def:product-metric} and whose second is the smooth function $(f \circ \pi_1)^2$ times a smooth $2$-tensor field, hence smooth by Lemma \ref{lem:smooth-tensor-field-smul}; the sum is then smooth by Lemma \ref{lem:smooth-tensor-field-add}.

Note that $f$ enters the metric only through $f^2$, so the definition makes sense for any nowhere-vanishing smooth $f$, and only $f(p_1) \neq 0$ is used.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Constant warping gives the product metric]
\label{prop:warped-product-constant-one}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.warpedProductMetric_one}
\leanok
\uses{def:warped-product,def:product-metric}
With $f \equiv 1$, the warped product $M_1 \times_f M_2$ is the product manifold $M_1 \times M_2$ with the product metric $g_1 \oplus g_2$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:warped-product,def:product-metric}
Both metrics are defined on the same underlying manifold $M_1 \times M_2$, so it suffices to compare them at each point. For every $(p_1,p_2)$ and all $(v_1,v_2), (w_1,w_2) \in T_{p_1}M_1 \oplus T_{p_2}M_2$,
\[
(g_1 \oplus f^2 g_2)_{(p_1,p_2)}((v_1,v_2),(w_1,w_2)) = g_1|_{p_1}(v_1,w_1) + 1^2\, g_2|_{p_2}(v_2,w_2),
\]
which is exactly the right-hand side of (2.12). A Riemannian metric is determined by the bilinear form it assigns to each point, so the two metrics are equal.
\end{proof}

\begin{example}[Warped Products]
\label{ex:warped-products}
\dcref{ch2:2.24}
\uses{ex:surfaces-of-revolution,REF:ch2-3,def:warped-product,prop:warped-product-constant-one}

\noindent (a) With $f \equiv 1$, the warped product $M_1 \times_f M_2$ is just the space $M_1 \times M_2$ with the product metric; this is Proposition \ref{prop:warped-product-constant-one}.

\noindent (b) Every surface of revolution can be expressed as a warped product, as follows. Let $H$ be the half-plane $\{(r,z) : r > 0\}$, let $C \subseteq H$ be an embedded smooth 1-dimensional submanifold, and let $S_C \subseteq \mathbb{R}^3$ denote the corresponding surface of revolution as in Example 2.20. Endow $C$ with the Riemannian metric induced from the Euclidean metric on $H$, and let $S^1$ be endowed with its standard metric. Let $f : C \to \mathbb{R}$ be the distance to the $z$-axis: $f(r,z) = r$. Then Problem 2-3 shows that $S_C$ is isometric to the warped product $C \times_f S^1$.

(c) If we let $\rho$ denote the standard coordinate function on $\mathbb{R}^+ \subset \mathbb{R}$, then the map $\Phi(\rho,\omega) = \rho\omega$ gives an isometry from the warped product $\mathbb{R}^+ \times_\rho S^{n-1}$ to $\mathbb{R}^n \setminus \{0\}$ with its Euclidean metric (see Problem 2-4). \hfill //
\end{example}

\section{Riemannian Submersions}

Unlike submanifolds and products of Riemannian manifolds, which automatically inherit Riemannian metrics of their own, quotients of Riemannian manifolds inherit Riemannian metrics only under very special circumstances. In this section, we see what those circumstances are.

Suppose $\tilde{M}$ and $M$ are smooth manifolds, $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ is a smooth submersion, and $\tilde{g}$ is a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. By the submersion level set theorem (Corollary A.25), each fiber $\tilde{M}_y = \pi^{-1}(y)$ is a properly embedded smooth submanifold of $\tilde{M}$. At each point $x \in \tilde{M}$, we define two subspaces of the tangent space $T_x \tilde{M}$ as follows: the \textbf{vertical tangent space at $x$} is
\[
V_x = \ker d\pi_x = T_x(\tilde{M}_{\pi(x)})
\]
