(that is, the tangent space to the fiber containing $x$), and the \textbf{horizontal tangent space at $x$} is its orthogonal complement:
\[
H_x = (V_x)^\perp.
\]
Then the tangent space $T_x \tilde{M}$ decomposes as an orthogonal direct sum $T_x \tilde{M} = H_x \oplus V_x$. Note that the vertical space is well defined for every submersion, because it does not refer to the metric; but the horizontal space depends on the metric.

A vector field on $\tilde{M}$ is said to be a \textbf{horizontal vector field} if its value at each point lies in the horizontal space at that point; a \textbf{vertical vector field} is defined similarly. Given a vector field $X$ on $M$, a vector field $\tilde{X}$ on $\tilde{M}$ is called a \textbf{horizontal lift of $X$} if $\tilde{X}$ is horizontal and $\pi$-related to $X$. (The latter property means that $d\pi_x(\tilde{X}_x) = X_{\pi(x)}$ for each $x \in \tilde{M}$.)

The next proposition is the principal tool for doing computations on Riemannian submersions.

Proposition \ref{prop:horizontal-vector-field-properties} below is part (a) of
Lee's Proposition 2.25; part (b) is Proposition
\ref{prop:horizontal-lift-of-vector-field} and part (c) is Proposition
\ref{prop:horizontal-lift-realizes-horizontal-vector}, both stated once the
horizontal lift has been constructed.

Lee derives all three parts from the rank theorem: it puts $\pi$ into the normal
form $(x^1,\ldots,x^m) \mapsto (x^1,\ldots,x^n)$, so that $V_q$ is spanned by
$\partial_{n+1}|_q,\ldots,\partial_m|_q$ on a chart, and Gram--Schmidt applied to
the reordered coordinate frame $(\partial_{n+1},\ldots,\partial_m,\partial_1,\ldots,\partial_n)$
produces a smooth orthonormal frame $(E_1,\ldots,E_m)$ whose first $m-n$ entries
span $V_q$ and whose last $n$ span $H_q$; the components of $W$ in that frame are
smooth, which gives the smoothness of $W^H$ and $W^V$. The treatment below
replaces that frame by an explicit formula for the horizontal lift. This costs
nothing in generality, and it buys the smoothness assertions directly: the lift is
a rational expression in $\tilde{g}$ and $d\pi$, and inversion of invertible
operators is smooth.

\begin{proposition}[The Smooth Horizontal--Vertical Splitting of a Vector Field]
\label{prop:horizontal-vector-field-properties}
\dcref{ch2:2.25}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.existsUnique_horizontal_add_vertical_field}
\leanok
\uses{def:submersion,def:vertical-space,def:horizontal-space,def:riemannian-metric,lem:tangent-space-horizontal-vertical-splitting,lem:horizontal-projection-field-smooth}

Let $\tilde{M}$ and $M$ be smooth manifolds, let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion, and let $\tilde{g}$ be a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. Every smooth vector field $W$ on $\tilde{M}$ can be expressed uniquely in the form $W = W^H + W^V$, where $W^H$ is horizontal, $W^V$ is vertical, and both $W^H$ and $W^V$ are smooth.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
At each point $x \in \tilde{M}$ the tangent vector $W_x$ splits uniquely as a horizontal plus a vertical vector, by Lemma \ref{lem:tangent-space-horizontal-vertical-splitting}; explicitly the two summands are $W^H_x = L_x(d\pi_x(W_x))$ and $W^V_x = W_x - W^H_x$, where $L_x$ is the horizontal lift of Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-tangent}. This already proves existence and uniqueness of the decomposition into rough vector fields, since uniqueness is a pointwise assertion and involves no smoothness.

It remains to show that the two rough fields so defined are smooth. The field $W^H$ is smooth by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-projection-field-smooth}, and $W^V = W - W^H$ is then smooth as a difference of smooth vector fields.
\end{proof}

The smoothness of $W^H$ is the only analytic content of the proposition, and it is
isolated in the next two lemmas. The first observes that $x \mapsto d\pi_x(W_x)$ is
a smooth section along $\pi$ — a section of $\pi^* TM$ — and the second lifts such
a section back to $\tilde{M}$. The generality matters: $d\pi_x(W_x)$ depends on $x$
and not merely on $\pi(x)$, since two points of a fibre may carry unrelated values
of $W$, so $W^H$ is \emph{not} the lift of any vector field on $M$ and part (b)
does not apply to it.

\begin{lemma}[The Pushforward of a Vector Field is a Smooth Section Along the Map]
\label{lem:pushforward-along-map-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiff_mfderivAlong}
\leanok
\uses{def:submersion}
Let $F : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth map and let $W$ be a smooth vector field on $\tilde{M}$. Then $x \mapsto dF_x(W_x)$ is a smooth section of $F^*TM$ along $F$, i.e.\ the map $x \mapsto (F(x), dF_x(W_x))$ is a smooth map $\tilde{M} \to TM$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
The bundled differential $dF : T\tilde{M} \to TM$, $(x, v) \mapsto (F(x), dF_x(v))$, is a smooth map of tangent bundles because $F$ is smooth. The map in question is its composite with the smooth map $x \mapsto (x, W_x)$, which is smooth because $W$ is a smooth vector field.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Horizontal Lift of a Smooth Section Along $\pi$ is Smooth]
\label{lem:horizontal-lift-along-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffAt_horizontalLiftAlong}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-tangent,lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data,def:submersion}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion and $\tilde{g}$ a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. Let $X$ be a smooth section along $\pi$, that is, a family $X_x \in T_{\pi(x)}M$ depending smoothly on $x \in \tilde{M}$. Then $x \mapsto L_x(X_x)$ is a smooth vector field on $\tilde{M}$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Smoothness is local, so fix $x_0 \in \tilde{M}$ and read both tangent bundles in trivializations around $x_0$ and $\pi(x_0)$. In those trivializations $\tilde{g}$ and $d\pi$ become smooth families $B(x)$ of positive definite bilinear forms and $A(x)$ of surjective linear maps, and $X$ becomes a smooth $E'$-valued function $u(x)$; by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec} the trivialized lift of the trivialized data is the trivialized lift, so near $x_0$ the field in question reads as $x \mapsto \mathcal{L}(B(x), A(x))\, u(x)$, where $\mathcal{L}(B,A)$ is the linear-algebraic horizontal lift of Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-linear}. This is smooth by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data} together with the smoothness of $u$, since evaluation of a linear map on a vector is a continuous bilinear operation.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Smoothness of the Horizontal Projection of a Vector Field]
\label{lem:horizontal-projection-field-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiff_horizontalProjField}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pushforward-along-map-smooth,lem:horizontal-lift-along-smooth,def:horizontal-space}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion, let $\tilde{g}$ be a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$, and let $W$ be a smooth vector field on $\tilde{M}$. Then the horizontal part $W^H$, defined pointwise by $W^H_x = L_x(d\pi_x(W_x))$, is a smooth vector field on $\tilde{M}$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
By Lemma \ref{lem:pushforward-along-map-smooth} the family $x \mapsto d\pi_x(W_x)$ is a smooth section along $\pi$, and by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-along-smooth} the horizontal lift of any such section is a smooth vector field. Composing the two gives the claim.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Smooth submersion]
\label{def:submersion}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsSubmersion}
\leanok
A smooth map $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ between smooth manifolds is a \emph{submersion} if its differential $d\pi_x : T_x\tilde{M} \to T_{\pi(x)}M$ is surjective at every $x \in \tilde{M}$.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[The horizontal lift of a surjection]
\label{def:horizontal-lift-linear}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.horizontalLift}
\leanok
Let $B$ be a positive definite symmetric bilinear form on a finite-dimensional real vector space $E$, let $E'$ be a finite-dimensional real vector space, and let $A : E \to E'$ be surjective. Writing $A^t : (E')^* \to E^*$ for the transpose and identifying $B$ with the isomorphism $E \to E^*$, $v \mapsto B(v, \cdot)$, the \emph{horizontal lift} of $A$ relative to $B$ is
\[
L \;=\; B^{-1}A^t\bigl(AB^{-1}A^t\bigr)^{-1} \;:\; E' \to E ,
\]
the $B$-weighted Moore--Penrose right inverse of $A$. Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec} shows that the two inverses exist and that $L$ is characterized by the properties appearing there.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The horizontal lift is the unique $B$-orthogonal right inverse]
\label{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.horizontalLift_unique}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-linear}
With $B$, $E$, $E'$ and $A$ as in Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-linear}, there is exactly one linear map $L : E' \to E$ such that
\begin{enumerate}
\item[(i)] $A(Lu) = u$ for every $u \in E'$, and
\item[(ii)] $B(Lu, w) = 0$ for every $u \in E'$ and every $w \in \ker A$,
\end{enumerate}
namely the map of Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-linear}.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-linear}
Since $B$ is positive definite it is nondegenerate, so $v \mapsto B(v,\cdot)$ is injective, hence an isomorphism $E \to E^*$ because $\dim E^* = \dim E$. Put $S = B^{-1}A^t : (E')^* \to E$, so that $S$ is characterized by
\begin{equation}
B(S\xi, w) = \xi(Aw) \qquad \text{for all } \xi \in (E')^*,\ w \in E. \tag{$*$}
\end{equation}

We claim $AS : (E')^* \to E'$ is an isomorphism. It is injective: if $A(S\xi) = 0$ then, taking $w = S\xi$ in $(*)$, $B(S\xi, S\xi) = \xi(A S\xi) = 0$, so $S\xi = 0$ by positive definiteness; then $(*)$ gives $\xi(Aw) = 0$ for every $w \in E$, and $A$ is surjective, so $\xi = 0$. Since $\dim (E')^* = \dim E'$, injectivity gives bijectivity.

Set $L = S(AS)^{-1}$. Then $A(Lu) = (AS)(AS)^{-1}u = u$, which is (i). For (ii), write $\xi = (AS)^{-1}u$, so $Lu = S\xi$; if $w \in \ker A$ then $(*)$ gives $B(Lu, w) = \xi(Aw) = 0$.

For uniqueness, suppose $L$ and $L'$ both satisfy (i) and (ii), and fix $u \in E'$. Put $d = Lu - L'u$. By (i), $Ad = u - u = 0$, so $d \in \ker A$; by (ii) applied to each of $L$ and $L'$ with $w = d$, we get $B(Lu, d) = B(L'u, d) = 0$, hence $B(d,d) = B(Lu,d) - B(L'u,d) = 0$. Positive definiteness forces $d = 0$, so $Lu = L'u$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The horizontal lift depends smoothly on the data]
\label{lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffAt_horizontalLift}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-linear}
Let $E$, $E'$ be finite-dimensional real vector spaces, let $N$ be a smooth manifold, and let $x \mapsto B_x$ and $x \mapsto A_x$ be smooth maps from $N$ into the bilinear forms on $E$ and into $\operatorname{Hom}(E,E')$ respectively. If $B_{x_0}$ is positive definite and $A_{x_0}$ is surjective, then $x \mapsto L_x$, the horizontal lift of $A_x$ relative to $B_x$, is smooth at $x_0$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-linear,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
By Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-linear}, $L_x = B_x^{-1}A_x^t(A_xB_x^{-1}A_x^t)^{-1}$. The map $A \mapsto A^t$ is linear, hence smooth. Inversion is smooth at every invertible operator, and composition of operators is bilinear, hence smooth. By Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec} both $B_{x_0}$ and $A_{x_0}B_{x_0}^{-1}A_{x_0}^t$ are invertible, so each inversion in the formula is applied at an invertible operator at $x = x_0$, and the composite is smooth at $x_0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[The vertical space]
\label{def:vertical-space}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.verticalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:submersion}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion. The \emph{vertical space} at $x \in \tilde{M}$ is $V_x = \ker d\pi_x \subseteq T_x\tilde{M}$. It refers to no metric.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[The horizontal space]
\label{def:horizontal-space}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.horizontalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:vertical-space,def:riemannian-metric}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion and $\tilde{g}$ a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. The \emph{horizontal space} at $x \in \tilde{M}$ is
\[
H_x \;=\; \{\, v \in T_x\tilde{M} \;:\; \tilde{g}_x(v,w) = 0 \text{ for every } w \in V_x \,\} ,
\]
the $\tilde{g}_x$-orthogonal complement of $V_x$. Unlike $V_x$, it depends on $\tilde{g}$.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[The horizontal lift of a tangent vector]
\label{def:horizontal-lift-tangent}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.horizontalLiftAt}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-linear,def:horizontal-space,def:submersion}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion and $\tilde{g}$ a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. For $x \in \tilde{M}$, the \emph{horizontal lift at $x$} is the map $L_x : T_{\pi(x)}M \to T_x\tilde{M}$ given by Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-linear} applied to $B = \tilde{g}_x$ and $A = d\pi_x$. By Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec} it is the unique linear map with $d\pi_x(L_x u) = u$ and $L_x u \in H_x$ for all $u$.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The tangent space splits]
\label{lem:tangent-space-horizontal-vertical-splitting}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isCompl_horizontalSpace_verticalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:vertical-space,def:horizontal-space,def:horizontal-lift-tangent}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion and $\tilde{g}$ a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. For every $x \in \tilde{M}$,
\[
T_x\tilde{M} = H_x \oplus V_x .
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:vertical-space,def:horizontal-space,def:horizontal-lift-tangent,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
The two subspaces intersect trivially: if $v \in H_x \cap V_x$ then, taking $w = v$ in the definition of $H_x$, we get $\tilde{g}_x(v,v) = 0$, hence $v = 0$ because $\tilde{g}_x$ is positive definite.

They span: given $v \in T_x\tilde{M}$, set $v^H = L_x(d\pi_x v)$ and $v^V = v - v^H$, where $L_x$ is the horizontal lift of Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-tangent}. Then $v^H \in H_x$ by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}(ii), and $d\pi_x(v^V) = d\pi_x v - d\pi_x(L_x(d\pi_x v)) = d\pi_x v - d\pi_x v = 0$ by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}(i), so $v^V \in V_x$. Hence $v = v^H + v^V \in H_x + V_x$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Existence and uniqueness of horizontal lifts]
\label{prop:horizontal-lift-of-vector-field}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_unique_horizontalLift}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-tangent,def:horizontal-space,def:submersion,def:riemannian-metric,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec,lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion and $\tilde{g}$ a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. Every $X \in \mathfrak{X}(M)$ has a horizontal lift $\tilde{X} \in \mathfrak{X}(\tilde{M})$: a smooth vector field with $\tilde{X}_x \in H_x$ and $d\pi_x(\tilde{X}_x) = X_{\pi(x)}$ for every $x \in \tilde{M}$. It is the only vector field with those two properties. This is part (b) of Proposition \ref{prop:horizontal-vector-field-properties}.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-tangent,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec,lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data,def:horizontal-space}
Define $\tilde{X}_x = L_x(X_{\pi(x)})$, with $L_x$ as in Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-tangent}. Then $\tilde{X}_x \in H_x$ and $d\pi_x(\tilde{X}_x) = X_{\pi(x)}$ by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}, so only smoothness and uniqueness remain.

Smoothness is local, so fix $x_0 \in \tilde{M}$ and work in local trivializations of $T\tilde{M}$ near $x_0$ and of $TM$ near $\pi(x_0)$; write $\theta_x : T_x\tilde{M} \to E$ and $\iota_y : T_yM \to E'$ for the resulting isomorphisms onto the model spaces. Set
\[
B_x(a,b) = \tilde{g}_x(\theta_x^{-1}a, \theta_x^{-1}b), \qquad A_x = \iota_{\pi(x)} \circ d\pi_x \circ \theta_x^{-1} .
\]
Then $x \mapsto B_x$ is smooth because $\tilde{g}$ is a smooth $2$-tensor field, and $x \mapsto A_x$ is smooth because $\pi$ is smooth and the differential of a smooth map is smooth in coordinates. Each $B_x$ is positive definite and each $A_x$ is surjective, both properties being carried across the isomorphisms $\theta_x$, $\iota_{\pi(x)}$ from $\tilde{g}_x$ and $d\pi_x$. Transporting the two defining properties of Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec} through $\theta_x$ and $\iota_{\pi(x)}$ shows that the horizontal lift of $A_x$ relative to $B_x$ is $\theta_x \circ L_x \circ \iota_{\pi(x)}^{-1}$; uniqueness in that lemma is what makes this an identity rather than a definition. Hence
\[
\theta_x(\tilde{X}_x) \;=\; \bigl(\theta_x \circ L_x \circ \iota_{\pi(x)}^{-1}\bigr)\bigl(\iota_{\pi(x)}(X_{\pi(x)})\bigr),
\]
which is smooth at $x_0$ by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data} together with smoothness of $x \mapsto \iota_{\pi(x)}(X_{\pi(x)})$, the latter because $X$ is smooth and $\pi$ is smooth. As $\theta$ is the trivialization, $\tilde{X}$ is a smooth section near $x_0$.

For uniqueness, let $Z$ be any vector field with $Z_x \in H_x$ and $d\pi_x(Z_x) = X_{\pi(x)}$ for all $x$. Then $Z_x$ and $\tilde{X}_x$ are both horizontal with the same image under $d\pi_x$, so they agree by the uniqueness in Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}. Hence $Z = \tilde{X}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Every Horizontal Vector is the Value of a Lift]
\label{prop:horizontal-lift-realizes-horizontal-vector}
\dcref{ch2:2.25}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_horizontalLift_eq}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-space,def:horizontal-lift-tangent,prop:horizontal-lift-of-vector-field,lem:vector-field-extension-point,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion and $\tilde{g}$ a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. For every $x \in \tilde{M}$ and every $v \in H_x$, there is a vector field $X \in \mathfrak{X}(M)$ whose horizontal lift $\tilde{X}$ satisfies $\tilde{X}_x = v$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Push $v$ down to the tangent vector $d\pi_x(v) \in T_{\pi(x)}M$, and use Lemma \ref{lem:vector-field-extension-point} to choose a smooth vector field $X \in \mathfrak{X}(M)$ with $X_{\pi(x)} = d\pi_x(v)$. Let $\tilde{X}$ be its horizontal lift, which exists and is smooth by Proposition \ref{prop:horizontal-lift-of-vector-field}. At the point $x$,
\[
\tilde{X}_x \;=\; L_x\bigl(X_{\pi(x)}\bigr) \;=\; L_x\bigl(d\pi_x(v)\bigr) \;=\; v,
\]
the last equality by the uniqueness clause of Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}: $L_x$ is a right inverse of $d\pi_x$ whose image is $H_x$, so it inverts $d\pi_x$ on horizontal vectors. This is the only place the hypothesis $v \in H_x$ is used, and it cannot be dropped: every horizontal lift is horizontal at every point, so a vector outside $H_x$ is not the value of any lift.
\end{proof}

The fact that every horizontal vector at a point of $\tilde{M}$ can be extended to a horizontal lift on all of $\tilde{M}$ (part (c) of Proposition \ref{prop:horizontal-vector-field-properties}) is highly useful for computations. It is important to be aware, though, that not every horizontal vector field on $\tilde{M}$ is a horizontal lift, as the next exercise shows.

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:horizontal-vector-field-not-lift}
\dcref{ch2:2.26}
Let $\pi: \mathbb{R}^2 \to \mathbb{R}$ be the projection map $\pi(x,y) = x$, and let $\tilde{W}$ be the smooth vector field $y\partial_x$ on $\mathbb{R}^2$. Show that $\tilde{W}$ is horizontal, but there is no vector field on $\mathbb{R}$ whose horizontal lift is equal to $\tilde{W}$.
\end{exercise}

Now we can identify some quotients of Riemannian manifolds that inherit metrics of their own. Let us begin by describing what such a metric should look like.

Suppose $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ and $(M,g)$ are Riemannian manifolds, and $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is a smooth submersion. Then $\pi$ is said to be a \textbf{Riemannian submersion} if for each $x \in \tilde{M}$, the differential $d\pi_x$ restricts to a linear isometry from $H_x$ onto $T_{\pi(x)}M$. In other words, $\tilde{g}_x(v,w) = g_{\pi(x)}(d\pi_x(v), d\pi_x(w))$ whenever $v,w \in H_x$.

\begin{example}[Riemannian Submersions]
\label{ex:riemannian-submersions}
\dcref{ch2:2.27}
\begin{enumerate}
\item[(a)] The projection $\pi: \mathbb{R}^{n+k} \to \mathbb{R}^n$ onto the first $n$ coordinates is a Riemannian submersion if $\mathbb{R}^{n+k}$ and $\mathbb{R}^n$ are both endowed with their Euclidean metrics.
\item[(b)] If $M$ and $N$ are Riemannian manifolds and $M \times N$ is endowed with the product metric, then both projections $\pi_M: M \times N \to M$ and $\pi_N: M \times N \to N$ are Riemannian submersions.
\item[(c)] If $M \times_f N$ is a warped product manifold, then the projection $\pi_M : M \times_f N \to M$ is a Riemannian submersion, but $\pi_N$ typically is not.
\end{enumerate}
\end{example}

Given a Riemannian manifold $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ and a surjective submersion $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$, it is almost never the case that there is a metric on $M$ that makes $\pi$ into a Riemannian submersion. It is not hard to see why: for this to be the case, whenever $p, q \in M$ are two points in the same fiber $\pi^{-1}(y)$, the linear maps $(d\pi_p|_{H_p})^{-1}: T_y M \to H_p$ both have to pull $\tilde{g}$ back to the same inner product on $T_y M$.

There is, however, an important special case in which there is such a metric. Suppose $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is a smooth surjective submersion, and $G$ is a group acting on $\tilde{M}$. (See Appendix C for a review of the basic definitions and terminology regarding group actions on manifolds.) We say that the action is \emph{vertical} if every element $\varphi \in G$ takes each fiber to itself, meaning that $\pi(\varphi \cdot p) = \pi(p)$ for all $p \in M$. The action is \emph{transitive on fibers} if for each $p, q \in \tilde{M}$ such that $\pi(p) = \pi(q)$, there exists $\varphi \in G$ such that $\varphi \cdot p = q$.

If in addition $\tilde{M}$ is endowed with a Riemannian metric, the action is said to be an \emph{isometric action} or an \emph{action by isometries}, and the metric is said to be \emph{invariant under $G$}, if the map $x \mapsto \varphi \cdot x$ is an isometry for each $\varphi \in G$. In that case, provided the action is effective (so that different elements of $G$ define different isometries of $\tilde{M}$), we can identify $G$ with a subgroup of $\text{Iso}(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$. Since an isometry is, in particular, a diffeomorphism, every isometric action is an action by diffeomorphisms.

\begin{definition}[Riemannian submersion]
\label{def:riemannian-submersion}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsRiemannianSubmersion}
\leanok
\uses{def:submersion,def:horizontal-space,def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ and $(M,g)$ be Riemannian manifolds. A smooth submersion $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ is a \emph{Riemannian submersion} if for every $x \in \tilde{M}$ and all $v,w \in H_x$,
\[
g_{\pi(x)}(d\pi_x v, d\pi_x w) = \tilde{g}_x(v,w),
\]
that is, $d\pi_x$ restricts to a linear isometry of $H_x$ onto $T_{\pi(x)}M$. Surjectivity of $d\pi_x|_{H_x}$ is automatic from Lemma \ref{lem:tangent-space-horizontal-vertical-splitting}.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[The inner product induced on the base]
\label{def:submersion-inner}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.submersionInner}
\leanok
\uses{def:horizontal-lift-tangent,def:riemannian-metric}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion and $\tilde{g}$ a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$. For $x \in \tilde{M}$, the \emph{induced form at $x$} is the bilinear form on $T_{\pi(x)}M$
\[
\beta_x(u,v) \;=\; \tilde{g}_x(L_x u, L_x v),
\]
where $L_x$ is the horizontal lift of Definition \ref{def:horizontal-lift-tangent}.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[A Riemannian submersion's base metric is the induced form]
\label{lem:riemannian-submersion-inner-eq}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsRiemannianSubmersion.inner_eq}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-submersion,def:submersion-inner}
If $\pi : (\tilde{M},\tilde{g}) \to (M,g)$ is a Riemannian submersion then $g_{\pi(x)} = \beta_x$ for every $x \in \tilde{M}$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-submersion,def:submersion-inner,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
Let $u,v \in T_{\pi(x)}M$. The vectors $L_x u$ and $L_x v$ are horizontal, so the Riemannian submersion condition gives $g_{\pi(x)}(d\pi_x L_x u, d\pi_x L_x v) = \tilde{g}_x(L_x u, L_x v) = \beta_x(u,v)$. But $d\pi_x L_x u = u$ and $d\pi_x L_x v = v$, so the left side is $g_{\pi(x)}(u,v)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Uniqueness of the submersion metric]
\label{prop:riemannian-submersion-metric-unique}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isRiemannianSubmersion_unique}
\leanok
\uses{lem:riemannian-submersion-inner-eq}
Let $\tilde{g}$ be a Riemannian metric on $\tilde{M}$ and let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a surjective smooth submersion. There is \emph{at most one} Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$ making $\pi$ a Riemannian submersion. This is the uniqueness half of Theorem \ref{thm:group-action-riemannian-submersion}, and it needs no group action.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:riemannian-submersion-inner-eq}
A Riemannian metric is determined by its inner products. Let $y \in M$ and, by surjectivity, write $y = \pi(x)$. If $g_1$ and $g_2$ both make $\pi$ a Riemannian submersion then Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-submersion-inner-eq} gives $(g_1)_y = \beta_x = (g_2)_y$.
\end{proof}

\noindent The existence half is where the group action enters, through the following three lemmas.

\begin{lemma}[The action's differential is invertible]
\label{lem:mfderiv-smul-inv-smul}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsIsometricAction.mfderiv_smul_inv_smul}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Let $G$ act on $\tilde{M}$ by smooth maps. For $a \in G$ and $x \in \tilde{M}$, the differentials of $a \cdot {-}$ at $x$ and of $a^{-1} \cdot {-}$ at $a \cdot x$ are mutually inverse.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Apply the chain rule to $(a \cdot {-}) \circ (a^{-1} \cdot {-}) = \mathrm{Id}$ at the point $a \cdot x$, noting $a^{-1} \cdot (a \cdot x) = x$, and use that the differential of the identity is the identity.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[A vertical action preserves the splitting]
\label{lem:horizontal-space-smul}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.horizontalSpace_smul}
\leanok
\uses{lem:mfderiv-smul-inv-smul,def:vertical-space,def:horizontal-space}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion and let $G$ act on $\tilde{M}$ by smooth maps preserving $\tilde{g}$, with $\pi(a \cdot x) = \pi(x)$ for all $a, x$. Then $d(a \cdot {-})_x$ carries $V_x$ onto $V_{a \cdot x}$, and carries $H_x$ into $H_{a \cdot x}$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:mfderiv-smul-inv-smul,def:vertical-space,def:horizontal-space}
Differentiating $\pi \circ (a \cdot {-}) = \pi$ gives $d\pi_{a \cdot x} \circ d(a \cdot {-})_x = d\pi_x$. Hence if $d\pi_x z = 0$ then $d\pi_{a\cdot x}(d(a\cdot{-})_x z) = 0$, so $d(a \cdot {-})_x$ maps $V_x$ into $V_{a \cdot x}$. Conversely, given $w \in V_{a \cdot x}$, put $z = d(a^{-1} \cdot {-})_{a \cdot x} w$; by Lemma \ref{lem:mfderiv-smul-inv-smul}, $d(a\cdot{-})_x z = w$, and then $d\pi_x z = d\pi_{a \cdot x} w = 0$, so $z \in V_x$. Thus $V_{a \cdot x}$ is the image of $V_x$.

Now let $v \in H_x$ and $w \in V_{a \cdot x}$. Write $w = d(a \cdot {-})_x z$ with $z \in V_x$, as just shown. Since the action preserves $\tilde{g}$,
\[
\tilde{g}_{a \cdot x}(d(a\cdot{-})_x v, w) = \tilde{g}_{a \cdot x}(d(a\cdot{-})_x v, d(a\cdot{-})_x z) = \tilde{g}_x(v,z) = 0,
\]
the last step because $v$ is horizontal and $z$ is vertical. So $d(a\cdot{-})_x v \in H_{a \cdot x}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The induced form is constant on fibers]
\label{lem:submersion-inner-smul}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.submersionInner_smul}
\leanok
\uses{lem:horizontal-space-smul,def:submersion-inner,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
With hypotheses as in Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-space-smul}, $\beta_{a \cdot x} = \beta_x$ for every $a \in G$ and $x \in \tilde{M}$ (an identity of bilinear forms on $T_{\pi(x)}M = T_{\pi(a \cdot x)}M$).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:horizontal-space-smul,def:submersion-inner,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
Write $\Phi = d(a \cdot {-})_x$. We claim $L_{a \cdot x} = \Phi \circ L_x$. By the uniqueness in Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec} it suffices to check that $\Phi \circ L_x$ is a horizontal right inverse of $d\pi_{a \cdot x}$. It is a right inverse: $d\pi_{a \cdot x}(\Phi(L_x u)) = d\pi_x(L_x u) = u$, using $d\pi_{a \cdot x} \circ \Phi = d\pi_x$ from the proof of Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-space-smul}. It is horizontal: $L_x u \in H_x$, so $\Phi(L_x u) \in H_{a \cdot x}$ by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-space-smul}.

Therefore, for $u,v \in T_{\pi(x)}M$,
\[
\beta_{a \cdot x}(u,v) = \tilde{g}_{a \cdot x}(\Phi(L_x u), \Phi(L_x v)) = \tilde{g}_x(L_x u, L_x v) = \beta_x(u,v),
\]
the middle step because the action preserves $\tilde{g}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The induced form depends only on the fiber]
\label{lem:submersion-inner-congr}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.submersionInner_congr}
\leanok
\uses{lem:submersion-inner-smul,def:submersion-inner,lem:horizontal-space-smul}
With hypotheses as in Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-space-smul}, suppose in addition that $G$ acts transitively on the fibers of $\pi$. If $\pi(x) = \pi(y)$ then $\beta_x = \beta_y$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:submersion-inner-smul,def:submersion-inner}
By transitivity on fibers there is $a \in G$ with $y = a \cdot x$, and then $\beta_y = \beta_{a \cdot x} = \beta_x$ by Lemma \ref{lem:submersion-inner-smul}.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The horizontal lift is smooth in tangent coordinates]
\label{lem:horizontal-lift-in-coordinates-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffAt_horizontalLiftAt_inTangentCoordinates}
\leanok
\uses{lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data,def:horizontal-lift-tangent,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
Let $\pi : \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion of a Riemannian manifold $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ and let $x_0 \in \tilde{M}$. The family of linear maps $x \mapsto L_x : T_{\pi(x)}M \to T_x\tilde{M}$, read in tangent coordinates around $x_0$ and around $\pi(x_0)$, is smooth at $x_0$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data,def:horizontal-lift-tangent,lem:horizontal-lift-linear-spec}
Read in the trivializations $\theta$ of $T\tilde{M}$ at $x_0$ and $\iota$ of $TM$ at $\pi(x_0)$, the data of $L_x$ becomes the pair $(B(x), A(x))$, where $B(x)$ is $\tilde{g}_x$ and $A(x)$ is $d\pi_x$ so read; $B$ is smooth at $x_0$ because $\tilde{g}$ is a smooth $2$-tensor field, and $A$ is smooth at $x_0$ because $\pi$ is smooth. Moreover $B(x_0)$ is positive definite and $A(x_0)$ is surjective, since $\theta$ and $\iota$ are isomorphisms and $\pi$ is a submersion. By the naturality of the lift under a pair of linear isomorphisms, the lift of the transported data is the transported lift, that is $\theta \circ L_x \circ \iota^{-1} = L(B(x), A(x))$, which is exactly $L_x$ read in tangent coordinates. Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-smooth-in-data} says the right-hand side is smooth at $x_0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}
\label{thm:group-action-riemannian-submersion}
\dcref{ch2:2.28}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.existsUnique_isRiemannianSubmersion_metric}
\leanok
\uses{REF:ch2-6,prop:riemannian-submersion-metric-unique,lem:submersion-inner-congr,prop:local-section-manifold,def:riemannian-submersion,lem:bilinear-comp-of-family-smooth,lem:horizontal-lift-in-coordinates-smooth}
Let $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ be a Riemannian manifold, let $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ be a surjective smooth submersion, and let $G$ be a group acting on $\tilde{M}$. If the action is isometric, vertical, and transitive on fibers, then there is a unique Riemannian metric on $M$ such that $\pi$ is a Riemannian submersion.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:riemannian-submersion-metric-unique,lem:submersion-inner-congr,prop:local-section-manifold,def:submersion-inner,def:riemannian-submersion,lem:bilinear-comp-of-family-smooth,lem:horizontal-lift-in-coordinates-smooth}
Uniqueness is Proposition \ref{prop:riemannian-submersion-metric-unique}. For existence, define $g_y = \beta_x$ for any $x$ with $\pi(x) = y$; this is legitimate because if $\pi(x) = \pi(x')$ then $\beta_x = \beta_{x'}$ by Lemma \ref{lem:submersion-inner-congr} (whose hypotheses hold: the action is isometric, vertical, and transitive on fibers).

$g_y$ is symmetric because $\tilde{g}_x$ is. It is positive definite: if $u \neq 0$ then $L_x u \neq 0$, since $d\pi_x(L_x u) = u \neq 0$, and hence $\beta_x(u,u) = \tilde{g}_x(L_x u, L_x u) > 0$.

For smoothness, fix $y_0 \in M$ and choose $x_0$ with $\pi(x_0) = y_0$. By Proposition \ref{prop:local-section-manifold} there is a smooth local section $\sigma$ of $\pi$ near $y_0$ with $\sigma(y_0) = x_0$. For $y$ near $y_0$ we have $\pi(\sigma(y)) = y$, so by well-definedness $g_y = \beta_{\sigma(y)}$, that is,
\[
g_y(u,v) = \tilde{g}_{\sigma(y)}(L_{\sigma(y)} u, L_{\sigma(y)} v).
\]
The right-hand side is the transport of the smooth field $\tilde{g}$ by the family of linear maps $y \mapsto L_{\sigma(y)} : T_yM \to T_{\sigma(y)}\tilde{M}$, in the sense of Definition \ref{def:bilinear-comp-of-family}, so Lemma \ref{lem:bilinear-comp-of-family-smooth} applies once its two hypotheses are checked. The base map $\sigma$ is smooth at $y_0$; and the family $y \mapsto L_{\sigma(y)}$ is smooth at $y_0$ in tangent coordinates, being the composite of $\sigma$ with $x \mapsto L_x$, which is smooth in tangent coordinates by Lemma \ref{lem:horizontal-lift-in-coordinates-smooth}. (Here the reference points match: $\sigma(y_0) = x_0$ upstairs and $\pi(x_0) = y_0$ downstairs.) Hence $g$ is smooth at $y_0$, and $y_0$ was arbitrary, so $g$ is a smooth $2$-tensor field.

Note that this is the step for which Lemma \ref{lem:bilinear-comp-of-family-smooth} is stated for an arbitrary family of linear maps rather than for a family of differentials: $L_{\sigma(y)}$ is not $d\sigma_y$, and is not the differential of any map --- $\sigma$ is a section of $\pi$, but not a horizontal one.

Finally $g$ makes $\pi$ a Riemannian submersion: for $v,w \in H_x$, $L_x(d\pi_x v) = v$ and $L_x(d\pi_x w) = w$, so $g_{\pi(x)}(d\pi_x v, d\pi_x w) = \beta_x(d\pi_x v, d\pi_x w) = \tilde{g}_x(v,w)$.
\end{proof}

The next corollary describes one important situation to which the preceding theorem applies.

\begin{corollary}
\label{cor:quotient-manifold-lie-group-riemannian-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.29}
\uses{thm:quotient-manifold-theorem,thm:group-action-riemannian-submersion}
Suppose $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ is a Riemannian manifold, and $G$ is a Lie group acting smoothly, freely, properly, and isometrically on $\tilde{M}$. Then the orbit space $M = \tilde{M}/G$ has a unique smooth manifold structure and Riemannian metric such that $\pi$ is a Riemannian submersion.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Under the given hypotheses, the quotient manifold theorem (Thm. C.17) shows that $M$ has a unique smooth manifold structure such that the quotient map $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is a smooth submersion. It follows easily from the definitions in that case that the given action of $G$ on $\tilde{M}$ is vertical and transitive on fibers. Since the action is also isometric, Theorem 2.28 shows that $M$ inherits a unique Riemannian metric making $\pi$ into a Riemannian submersion.
\end{proof}

Here is an important example of a Riemannian metric defined in this way. A larger class of such metrics is described in Problem 2-7.

\begin{example}[The Fubini--Study Metric]
\label{ex:fubini-study-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.30}
\uses{ex:complex-projective-spaces,thm:local-section-theorem,thm:group-action-riemannian-submersion}

Let $n$ be a positive integer, and consider the complex projective space $\mathbb{CP}^n$ defined in Example C.19. That example shows that the map $\pi : \mathbb{C}^{n+1} \setminus \{0\} \to \mathbb{CP}^n$ sending each point in $\mathbb{C}^{n+1} \setminus \{0\}$ to its span is a surjective smooth submersion. Identifying $\mathbb{C}^{n+1}$ with $\mathbb{R}^{2n+2}$ endowed with its Euclidean metric, we can view the unit sphere $S^{2n+1}$ with its round metric $\bar{g}$ as an embedded Riemannian submanifold of $\mathbb{C}^{n+1} \setminus \{0\}$. Let $p: S^{2n+1} \to \mathbb{CP}^n$ denote the restriction of the map $\pi$. Then $p$ is smooth, and it is surjective because every 1-dimensional complex subspace contains elements of unit norm. We need to show that it is a submersion. Let $z_0 \in S^{2n+1}$ and set $\zeta_0 = p(z_0) \in \mathbb{CP}^n$. Since $\pi$ is a smooth submersion, it has a smooth local section $\sigma : U \to \mathbb{C}^{n+1}$ defined on a neighborhood $U$ of $\zeta_0$ and satisfying $\sigma(\zeta_0) = z_0$ (Thm. A.17). Let $\nu: \mathbb{C}^{n+1} \setminus \{0\} \to S^{2n+1}$ be the radial projection onto the sphere:
\[
\nu(z) = \frac{z}{|z|}.
\]

Since dividing an element of $\mathbb{C}^{n+1}$ by a nonzero scalar does not change its span, it follows that $p \circ \nu = \pi$. Therefore, if we set $\bar{\sigma} = \nu \circ \sigma$, we have $p \circ \bar{\sigma} = p \circ \nu \circ \sigma = \pi \circ \sigma = \text{Id}_U$, so $\bar{\sigma}$ is a local section of $p$. By Theorem A.17, this shows that $p$ is a submersion.

Define an action of $S^1$ on $S^{2n+1}$ by complex multiplication:
\[
\lambda \cdot (z^1,\ldots,z^{n+1}) = (\lambda z^1,\ldots,\lambda z^{n+1}),
\]
for $\lambda \in S^1$ (viewed as a complex number of norm 1) and $z = (z^1,\ldots,z^{n+1}) \in S^{2n+1}$. This is easily seen to be isometric, vertical, and transitive on fibers of $p$. By Theorem 2.28, therefore, there is a unique metric on $\mathbb{CP}^n$ such that the map $p: S^{2n+1} \to \mathbb{CP}^n$ is a Riemannian submersion. This metric is called the \textbf{Fubini--Study metric}; you will have a chance to study its geometric properties in Problems 3-19 and 8-13.
\end{example}

\section{Riemannian Coverings}

Another important special case of Riemannian submersions occurs in the context of covering maps. Suppose $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ and $(M, g)$ are Riemannian manifolds. A smooth covering map $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is called a \textbf{Riemannian covering} if it is a local isometry.

\begin{definition}
\label{def:smooth-covering-map}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsSmoothCoveringMap}
\leanok
\uses{prop:elementary-covering-maps}
A smooth map $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is a \emph{smooth covering map} if it is surjective, is a topological covering map, and is a local diffeomorphism.

By Proposition A.49(c) a topological covering map is a smooth covering map, in the sense of Appendix A, exactly when it is a local diffeomorphism, so this description agrees with the sheetwise one.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
\label{def:covering-automorphism-group}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.CoveringAut}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-covering-map}
Let $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth covering map. A \emph{covering automorphism} of $\pi$ is a diffeomorphism $\varphi: \tilde{M} \to \tilde{M}$ satisfying $\pi \circ \varphi = \pi$. The covering automorphisms form a group $\operatorname{Aut}_\pi(\tilde{M})$ under composition, which acts on $\tilde{M}$ by evaluation.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
\label{def:normal-covering-fibre-transitive}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsNormalCovering}
\leanok
\uses{def:covering-automorphism-group,prop:normal-covering-transitive-action}
A smooth covering map $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is a \emph{normal covering} if $\operatorname{Aut}_\pi(\tilde{M})$ acts transitively on each fiber of $\pi$.

By Proposition C.21 this holds if and only if the image of $\pi_*: \pi_1(\tilde{M}, \tilde{x}) \to \pi_1(M, \pi(\tilde{x}))$ is a normal subgroup of $\pi_1(M, \pi(\tilde{x}))$, which is the condition the name refers to.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
\label{def:covering-automorphism-invariant-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsCoveringAutInvariant}
\leanok
\uses{def:covering-automorphism-group,def:metric-preserving}
Let $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth covering map. A metric $\tilde{g}$ on $\tilde{M}$ is \emph{invariant under all covering automorphisms} if $\varphi^*\tilde{g} = \tilde{g}$ for every $\varphi \in \operatorname{Aut}_\pi(\tilde{M})$.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
\label{def:riemannian-covering}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsRiemannianCovering}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-covering-map,def:metric-preserving}
Let $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ and $(M, g)$ be Riemannian manifolds. A smooth covering map $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is a \emph{Riemannian covering} if $\pi^*g = \tilde{g}$.
\end{definition}

Lee phrases the definition as ``a smooth covering map that is a local isometry''; the two agree, because a covering map is already a local diffeomorphism.

\begin{lemma}[A Riemannian Covering Is a Local Isometry]
\label{lem:riemannian-covering-local-isometry}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsRiemannianCovering.isLocalIsometry}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-covering,def:local-isometry,def:smooth-covering-map}
Every Riemannian covering is a local isometry.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:local-isometry,def:smooth-covering-map}
A smooth covering map is a local diffeomorphism, and a Riemannian covering satisfies $\pi^*g = \tilde{g}$ by definition; a local diffeomorphism that preserves the metric is a local isometry. No hypothesis on dimensions is needed: unlike in Exercise \ref{exer:characterize-local-isometry}, the local diffeomorphism is given rather than deduced, so the inverse function theorem does not enter.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:equidimensional-riemannian-submersion-local-isometry}
\dcref{ch2:2.31}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isRiemannianSubmersion_iff_isMetricPreserving}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-submersion,def:metric-preserving,def:submersion}
Let $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ be a smooth submersion whose differential $d\pi_x$ is injective at every $x \in \tilde{M}$, and let $\tilde{g}$ and $g$ be metrics on $\tilde{M}$ and $M$. Then $\pi$ is a Riemannian submersion if and only if $\pi^*g = \tilde{g}$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Suppose first that $\pi$ is a Riemannian submersion, and fix $x \in \tilde{M}$. The vertical space $V_x = \ker d\pi_x$ is trivial because $d\pi_x$ is injective, so the horizontal space $H_x$, consisting of the vectors $g$-orthogonal to every vertical vector, is all of $T_x\tilde{M}$. The defining condition of a Riemannian submersion therefore applies to every pair $v, w \in T_x\tilde{M}$ and reads $g_{\pi(x)}(d\pi_x v, d\pi_x w) = \tilde{g}_x(v, w)$, which is $(\pi^*g)_x = \tilde{g}_x$.

Conversely, suppose $\pi^*g = \tilde{g}$. Then $g_{\pi(x)}(d\pi_x v, d\pi_x w) = \tilde{g}_x(v, w)$ for all $v, w \in T_x\tilde{M}$, and in particular for horizontal $v$ and $w$; since $\pi$ is a submersion by hypothesis, $\pi$ is a Riemannian submersion.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:riemannian-covering-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.31}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.existsUnique_isRiemannianCovering_metric}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-covering-map,def:normal-covering-fibre-transitive,def:covering-automorphism-invariant-metric,def:riemannian-covering,prop:elementary-covering-maps,prop:normal-covering-transitive-action,thm:group-action-riemannian-submersion,prop:equidimensional-riemannian-submersion-local-isometry}

Suppose $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is a smooth normal covering map, and $\tilde{g}$ is any metric on $\tilde{M}$ that is invariant under all covering automorphisms. Then there is a unique metric $g$ on $M$ such that $\pi$ is a Riemannian covering.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Proposition A.49 shows that $\pi$ is a surjective smooth submersion. The automorphism group acts vertically by definition, and Proposition C.21 shows that it acts transitively on fibers when the covering is normal. It then follows from Theorem 2.28 that there is a unique metric $g$ on $M$ such that $\pi$ is a Riemannian submersion. Since $\pi$ is a local diffeomorphism, each $d\pi_x$ is injective, so by Proposition \ref{prop:equidimensional-riemannian-submersion-local-isometry} the metrics making $\pi$ a Riemannian submersion are exactly those making $\pi$ a Riemannian covering. Hence $g$ is the unique metric on $M$ for which $\pi$ is a Riemannian covering.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:riemannian-quotient-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.32}
\uses{prop:quotient-orbit-space-covering-map,prop:riemannian-covering-metric}
Suppose $(\widetilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ is a Riemannian manifold, and $\Gamma$ is a discrete Lie group acting smoothly, freely, properly, and isometrically on $\widetilde{M}$. Then $\widetilde{M}/\Gamma$ has a unique Riemannian metric such that the quotient map $\pi: \widetilde{M} \to \widetilde{M}/\Gamma$ is a normal Riemannian covering.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Proposition C.23 shows that $\pi$ is a smooth normal covering map, and Proposition 2.31 shows that $M = \widetilde{M}/\Gamma$ has a unique Riemannian metric such that $\pi$ is a Riemannian covering.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}
\label{cor:isometric-to-quotient}
\dcref{ch2:2.33}
\uses{prop:covering-automorphisms-discrete-lie-group,prop:quotient-orbit-space-covering-map,prop:uniqueness-smooth-quotients}
Suppose $(M, g)$ and $(\widetilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ are connected Riemannian manifolds, $\pi: \widetilde{M} \to M$ is a normal Riemannian covering map, and $\Gamma = \text{Aut}_\pi(\widetilde{M})$. Then $M$ is isometric to $\widetilde{M}/\Gamma$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Proposition C.20 shows that with the discrete topology, $\Gamma$ is a discrete Lie group acting smoothly, freely, and properly on $\widetilde{M}$, and then Proposition C.23 shows that $\widetilde{M}/\Gamma$ is a smooth manifold and the quotient map $q: \widetilde{M} \to \widetilde{M}/\Gamma$ is a smooth normal covering map. The fact that both $\pi$ and $q$ are normal coverings implies that $\Gamma$ acts transitively on the fibers of both maps, so the two maps are constant on each other's fibers. Proposition A.19 then implies that there is a diffeomorphism $F: M \to \widetilde{M}/\Gamma$ that satisfies $q \circ F = \pi$. Because both $q$ and $\pi$ are local isometries, $F$ is too, and because it is bijective it is a global isometry.
\end{proof}

\begin{example}
\label{ex:real-projective-space-metric}
\dcref{ch2:2.34}
\uses{ex:universal-covering-projective-space,prop:riemannian-quotient-metric}
The two-element group $\Gamma = \{\pm1\}$ acts smoothly, freely, properly, and isometrically on $S^n$ by multiplication. Example C.24 shows that the quotient space is diffeomorphic to the real projective space $\mathbb{RP}^n$ and the quotient map $q: S^n \to \mathbb{RP}^n$ is a smooth normal covering map. Because the action is isometric, Proposition 2.32 shows that there is a unique metric on $\mathbb{RP}^n$ such that $q$ is a Riemannian covering.
\end{example}

\begin{example}[The Open Möbius Band]
\label{ex:open-mobius-band}
\dcref{ch2:2.35}
\uses{REF:ch2-8}
The open Möbius band is the quotient space $M = \mathbb{R}^2/\mathbb{Z}$, where $\mathbb{Z}$ acts on $\mathbb{R}^2$ by $n \cdot (x,y) = (x+n, (-1)^n y)$. This action is smooth, free, proper, and isometric, and therefore $M$ inherits a flat Riemannian metric such that the quotient map is a Riemannian covering. (See Problem 2-8.)
\end{example}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:torus-covering}
\dcref{ch2:2.36}
\uses{ex:n-torus-riemannian-submanifold}
Let $T^n \subseteq \mathbb{R}^{2n}$ be the $n$-torus with its induced metric. Show that the map $X: \mathbb{R}^n \to T^n$ of Example 2.21 is a Riemannian covering.
\end{exercise}

\section{Basic Constructions on Riemannian Manifolds}

Every Riemannian metric yields an abundance of useful constructions on manifolds, besides the obvious ones of lengths of vectors and angles between them. In this section we describe the most basic ones. Throughout this section $M$ is a smooth manifold with or without boundary.

\section{Raising and Lowering Indices}

One elementary but important property of Riemannian metrics is that they allow us to convert vectors to covectors and vice versa. Given a Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$, we define a bundle homomorphism $\tilde{g} : TM \to T^* M$ by setting
\[
\tilde{g}(v)(w) = g_p(v,w)
\]
for all $p \in M$ and $v,w \in T_p M$. If $X$ and $Y$ are smooth vector fields on $M$, this yields
\[
\tilde{g}(X)(Y) = g(X,Y),
\]
which implies, first, that $\tilde{g}(X)(Y)$ is linear over $C^\infty(M)$ in $Y$ and thus $\tilde{g}(X)$ is a smooth covector field by the tensor characterization lemma (Lemma B.6); and second, that the covector field $\tilde{g}(X)$ is linear over $C^\infty(M)$ as a function of $X$, and thus $\tilde{g}$ is a smooth bundle homomorphism.

Given a smooth local frame $(E_i)$ and its dual coframe $(e^i)$, let $g = g_{ij} e^i \otimes e^j$ be the local expression for $g$. If $X = X^i E_i$ is a smooth vector field, the covector field $\tilde{g}(X)$ has the coordinate expression
\[
\tilde{g}(X) = (g_{ij}X^i)e^j.
\]
Thus the matrix of $\tilde{g}$ in any local frame is the same as the matrix of $g$ itself. Given a vector field $X$, it is standard practice to denote the components of the covector field $\tilde{g}(X)$ by
\[
X_j = g_{ij}X^i,
\]
so that
\[
\tilde{g}(X) = X_j e^j,
\]
and we say that $\tilde{g}(X)$ is obtained from $X$ by \textit{lowering an index}. With this in mind, the covector field $g(X)$ is denoted by $X^\flat$ and called $X$ \textit{flat}, borrowing from the musical notation for lowering a tone.

Because the matrix $(g_{ij})$ is nonsingular at each point, the map $\tilde{g}$ is invertible, and the matrix of $\tilde{g}^{-1}$ is just the inverse matrix of $(g_{ij})$. We denote this inverse matrix by $(g^{ij})$, so that $g^{ij}g_{jk} = g_{kj}g^{jl} = \delta_k^l$. The symmetry of $g_{ij}$ easily implies that $(g^{ij})$ is also symmetric in $i$ and $j$. In terms of a local frame, the inverse map $\tilde{g}^{-1}$ is given by
\[
\tilde{g}^{-1}(\omega) = \omega^i E_i,
\]
where
\begin{equation}
\omega^i = g^{ij}\omega_j. \tag{2.13}
\end{equation}

If $\omega$ is a covector field, the vector field $\tilde{g}^{-1}(\omega)$ is called (what else?) $\omega$ \textit{sharp} and denoted by $\omega^\sharp$, and we say that it is obtained from $\omega$ by \textit{raising an index}. The two inverse isomorphisms $\flat$ and $\sharp$ are known as the \textit{musical isomorphisms}.

We record the fibrewise content of the preceding discussion: at each point, $\flat$ is the map $v \mapsto g_p(v,\cdot)$, it is a bijection, and $\sharp$ is its inverse.

\begin{definition}[Lowering an index]
\label{def:flat-map}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.flat}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold and $p \in M$. The \emph{flat map} at $p$ is the linear map $\flat : T_p M \to T_p^* M$ given by $v^\flat = g_p(v, \cdot)$.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The flat map is a bijection]
\label{lem:flat-bijective}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.flat_bijective}
\leanok
\uses{def:flat-map,def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with $\dim M < \infty$ and $p \in M$. Then $\flat : T_p M \to T_p^* M$ is bijective.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:flat-map,def:riemannian-metric}
Since $g_p$ is an inner product on the finite-dimensional real vector space $T_p M$, the space $T_p M$ is a finite-dimensional real inner product space, hence complete. The Riesz representation theorem states that the map sending $v \in T_p M$ to the covector $g_p(v,\cdot)$ is a linear isometric isomorphism of $T_p M$ onto its dual $T_p^* M$. That map is precisely $\flat$, so $\flat$ is bijective.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Raising an index]
\label{def:sharp-map}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.sharp}
\leanok
\uses{def:flat-map,lem:flat-bijective}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with $\dim M < \infty$ and $p \in M$. The \emph{sharp map} at $p$ is the inverse $\sharp = \flat^{-1} : T_p^* M \to T_p M$, which exists by Lemma \ref{lem:flat-bijective}. Thus $\alpha^\sharp$ is the unique $v \in T_p M$ with $g_p(v,\cdot) = \alpha$, characterized by
\[
g_p(\alpha^\sharp, w) = \alpha(w) \qquad \text{for all } w \in T_p M .
\]
\end{definition}

Probably the most important application of the sharp operator is to extend the classical gradient operator to Riemannian manifolds. If $g$ is a Riemannian metric on $M$ and $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ is a smooth function, the \emph{gradient of $f$} is the vector field
$\operatorname{grad} f = (df)^\#$ obtained from $df$ by raising an index. Unwinding the definitions,
we see that $\operatorname{grad} f$ is characterized by the fact that
\begin{equation}
df_p(w) = \langle \operatorname{grad} f|_p, w \rangle \quad \text{for all } p \in M, w \in T_p M.
\tag{2.14}
\end{equation}
and has the local basis expression
\[
\operatorname{grad} f = (g^{ij} E_i f) E_j.
\]

Thus if $(E_i)$ is an orthonormal frame, then $\operatorname{grad} f$ is the vector field whose
components are the same as the components of $df$; but in other frames, this will
not be the case.

\begin{definition}[The gradient]
\label{def:gradient}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.grad}
\leanok
\uses{def:sharp-map,def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with $\dim M < \infty$ and $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ smooth. The \emph{gradient} of $f$ is $\operatorname{grad} f = (df)^\sharp$, so that $\operatorname{grad} f|_p = (df_p)^\sharp \in T_p M$ for each $p \in M$.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[Characterization of the gradient]
\label{lem:gradient-characterization}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.innerAt_grad}
\leanok
\uses{def:gradient,def:sharp-map}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with $\dim M < \infty$ and $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ smooth. Then (2.14) holds: $df_p(w) = \langle \operatorname{grad} f|_p, w\rangle$ for all $p \in M$ and $w \in T_p M$. Moreover $\operatorname{grad} f|_p$ is the unique vector with this property.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:gradient,def:sharp-map,lem:flat-bijective}
By Definition \ref{def:sharp-map}, $\alpha^\sharp$ is characterized by $g_p(\alpha^\sharp, w) = \alpha(w)$ for all $w$. Taking $\alpha = df_p$ gives $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_p, w\rangle = df_p(w)$, which is (2.14). For uniqueness, suppose $v \in T_p M$ also satisfies $\langle v, w\rangle = df_p(w)$ for all $w$. Then $v^\flat = (\operatorname{grad} f|_p)^\flat$, and $\flat$ is injective by Lemma \ref{lem:flat-bijective}, so $v = \operatorname{grad} f|_p$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The gradient is orthogonal to the kernel of the differential]
\label{lem:gradient-orthogonal-kernel}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.innerAt_grad_eq_zero_of_mem_ker}
\leanok
\uses{def:gradient,lem:gradient-characterization}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with $\dim M < \infty$, let $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ be smooth, and let $p \in M$. If $w \in T_p M$ satisfies $df_p(w) = 0$, then $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_p, w \rangle = 0$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gradient-characterization}
By (2.14), $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_p, w\rangle = df_p(w) = 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Critical points are the zeros of the gradient]
\label{lem:gradient-eq-zero-iff}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.grad_eq_zero_iff}
\leanok
\uses{def:gradient,lem:gradient-characterization,lem:flat-bijective}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with $\dim M < \infty$, let $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ be smooth, and let $p \in M$. Then $\operatorname{grad} f|_p = 0$ if and only if $df_p = 0$. Hence the regular points of $f$ are exactly the points where $\operatorname{grad} f$ does not vanish.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gradient-characterization,lem:flat-bijective}
If $\operatorname{grad} f|_p = 0$ then for every $w \in T_p M$, (2.14) gives $df_p(w) = \langle 0, w\rangle = 0$, so $df_p = 0$. Conversely, if $df_p = 0$ then for every $w$ we have $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_p, w\rangle = df_p(w) = 0 = \langle 0, w \rangle$, so $(\operatorname{grad} f|_p)^\flat = 0^\flat$, and $\flat$ is injective by Lemma \ref{lem:flat-bijective}, whence $\operatorname{grad} f|_p = 0$.
\end{proof}

Lemma \ref{lem:gradient-orthogonal-kernel} has a converse, which is what makes the gradient a faithful record of the differential: orthogonality to $\operatorname{grad} f|_p$ detects the kernel of $df_p$ exactly.

\begin{lemma}[Orthogonality to the gradient characterizes the kernel of the differential]
\label{lem:gradient-orthogonal-iff}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.innerAt_grad_eq_zero_iff}
\leanok
\uses{def:gradient,lem:gradient-characterization}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with $\dim M < \infty$, let $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ be smooth, let $p \in M$, and let $w \in T_p M$. Then $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_p, w\rangle = 0$ if and only if $df_p(w) = 0$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gradient-characterization}
Both directions are immediate from (2.14): $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_p, w\rangle = df_p(w)$, so one side vanishes precisely when the other does.
\end{proof}

The next proposition shows that the gradient has the same geometric interpretation
on a Riemannian manifold as it does in Euclidean space. If $f$ is a smooth
real-valued function on a smooth manifold $M$, recall that a point $p \in M$ is called a
\emph{regular point of $f$} if $df_p \neq 0$, and a \emph{critical point of $f$} otherwise; and a level set
$f^{-1}(c)$ is called a \emph{regular level set} if every point of $f^{-1}(c)$ is a regular point of $f$
(see Appendix A). Corollary A.26 shows that each regular level set is an embedded
smooth hypersurface in $M$.

\begin{definition}[The regular set]
\label{def:regular-set}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.regularSet}
\leanok
Let $M$ be a smooth manifold and $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ smooth. The \emph{regular set} of $f$ is $\mathcal{R} = \{x \in M : df_x \neq 0\}$, the set of regular points of $f$.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The regular set is where the gradient does not vanish]
\label{lem:regular-set-eq-gradient-nonvanishing}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.mem_regularSet_iff_grad_ne_zero}
\leanok
\uses{def:regular-set,def:gradient,lem:gradient-eq-zero-iff}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with $\dim M < \infty$ and $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ smooth. Then $x \in \mathcal{R}$ if and only if $\operatorname{grad} f|_x \neq 0$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gradient-eq-zero-iff}
By Lemma \ref{lem:gradient-eq-zero-iff}, $\operatorname{grad} f|_x = 0$ if and only if $df_x = 0$. Negating both sides gives the claim.
\end{proof}

The reduction of Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface} to the regular-value case rests on $\mathcal{R}$ being open, which is what makes it an open submanifold on which $f$ has $c$ as a regular value.

\begin{lemma}[The non-vanishing locus of a differential is open]
\label{lem:mfderiv-nonvanishing-open}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isOpen_setOf_mfderiv_ne_zero}
\leanok
Let $F : M \to N$ be a smooth map of smooth manifolds. Then $\{x \in M : dF_x \neq 0\}$ is open in $M$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Fix $x_0$ with $dF_{x_0} \neq 0$, and read $x \mapsto dF_x$ in tangent coordinates around $x_0$ and $F(x_0)$: choose trivializations $\Phi$ of $TM$ over a neighbourhood of $x_0$ and $\Psi$ of $TN$ over a neighbourhood of $F(x_0)$, and set
\[
  D(x) = \Psi_{F(x)} \circ dF_x \circ \Phi_x^{-1},
\]
a map into the fixed vector space $\operatorname{Hom}(E, E')$ of the two model spaces, defined for $x$ in the (open) set where both trivializations apply and $F(x)$ lies in the second base set. Since $F$ is smooth, $D$ is smooth, hence continuous, at $x_0$.

Because $\Phi_x$ and $\Psi_{F(x)}$ are linear isomorphisms on the relevant fibres, $D(x) = 0$ if and only if $dF_x = 0$. In particular $D(x_0) \neq 0$. As $\{L : L \neq 0\}$ is open in $\operatorname{Hom}(E,E')$ and $D$ is continuous at $x_0$, we get $D(x) \neq 0$, and hence $dF_x \neq 0$, for all $x$ in a neighbourhood of $x_0$. So every point of the set is interior to it.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The regular set is open]
\label{lem:regular-set-open}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isOpen_regularSet}
\leanok
\uses{def:regular-set,lem:mfderiv-nonvanishing-open}
Let $M$ be a smooth manifold and $f : M \to \mathbb{R}$ smooth. Then the regular set $\mathcal{R}$ of $f$ is open in $M$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:mfderiv-nonvanishing-open}
This is Lemma \ref{lem:mfderiv-nonvanishing-open} applied to $F = f$ with $N = \mathbb{R}$, since $\mathcal{R} = \{x : df_x \neq 0\}$ by definition.
\end{proof}

For a regular \emph{value} $c$ — one for which every point of $f^{-1}(c)$ is regular — the level set is a hypersurface by Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one}, and the gradient is normal to it.

\begin{proposition}[The gradient is normal to a regular level set]
\label{prop:gradient-normal-regular-level-set}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.innerAt_grad_mfderiv_levelSet_val_eq_zero}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one,prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel,def:gradient,lem:gradient-orthogonal-kernel}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold of dimension $n+1$ without boundary, let $f \in C^\infty(M)$, and let $c$ be a regular value of $f$. Then for every $x_0 \in f^{-1}(c)$ and every $w \in T_{x_0} f^{-1}(c)$,
\[
  \bigl\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_{x_0},\, d(\iota_c)_{x_0}(w) \bigr\rangle = 0 ,
\]
where $\iota_c : f^{-1}(c) \hookrightarrow M$ is the inclusion. That is, $\operatorname{grad} f$ is normal to $f^{-1}(c)$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel,lem:gradient-orthogonal-kernel}
Since $f \circ \iota_c$ is constant, the chain rule gives $df_{x_0}(d(\iota_c)_{x_0}(w)) = 0$ for every $w$, which is the inclusion $\subseteq$ of Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel}. So $d(\iota_c)_{x_0}(w) \in \ker df_{x_0}$, and Lemma \ref{lem:gradient-orthogonal-kernel} applies.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[The tangent space of a regular level set is the orthogonal complement of the gradient]
\label{prop:tangent-space-regular-level-set-gradient-orthogonal}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_mfderiv_levelSet_val_eq_of_innerAt_grad_eq_zero}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel,lem:gradient-orthogonal-iff,def:gradient}
In the situation of Proposition \ref{prop:gradient-normal-regular-level-set}, conversely, every $u \in T_{x_0} M$ with $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_{x_0}, u\rangle = 0$ lies in the image of $d(\iota_c)_{x_0}$. Hence
\[
  d(\iota_c)_{x_0}\bigl(T_{x_0} f^{-1}(c)\bigr) = \bigl(\operatorname{grad} f|_{x_0}\bigr)^{\perp} ,
\]
so the normal space of $f^{-1}(c)$ at $x_0$ is the line spanned by $\operatorname{grad} f|_{x_0}$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel,lem:gradient-orthogonal-iff}
If $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_{x_0}, u\rangle = 0$ then $df_{x_0}(u) = 0$ by Lemma \ref{lem:gradient-orthogonal-iff}, so $u \in \ker df_{x_0}$. By Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel} the image of $d(\iota_c)_{x_0}$ is all of $\ker df_{x_0}$, so $u$ is in that image. Combined with Proposition \ref{prop:gradient-normal-regular-level-set} this gives the displayed equality of subspaces; the normal space is then the orthogonal complement of a hyperplane, hence the line spanned by $\operatorname{grad} f|_{x_0}$, which is nonzero by Lemma \ref{lem:regular-set-eq-gradient-nonvanishing}.
\end{proof}

Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface} is the same statement for the possibly-critical level set $f^{-1}(c)$, of which only the regular part $M_c = f^{-1}(c) \cap \mathcal{R}$ is retained. It reduces to the regular-value case above once one knows that $\mathcal{R}$ is open, so that $M_c$ is a regular level set of the restriction $f|_{\mathcal{R}}$ on the open submanifold $\mathcal{R}$. Only the case $N = \mathbb{R}$ of the regular level set theorem is used, so Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one} suffices and the general Corollary \ref{cor:regular-level-set} is not needed.

\subsection*{Open submanifolds and restriction}

An open subset $U \subseteq M$ is a smooth manifold of the same dimension, its charts being those of $M$ restricted. The two lemmas below say that this identification is invisible to differentials, which is what makes the reduction to the regular-value case work.

\begin{lemma}[The differential of an open-submanifold inclusion]
\label{lem:opens-inclusion-differential}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.mfderiv_opens_subtypeVal}
\leanok
Let $M$ be a smooth manifold and $U \subseteq M$ open, regarded as an open submanifold. Then for each $x \in U$ the differential of the inclusion $\iota : U \hookrightarrow M$ is the identity,
\[
  d\iota_x = \operatorname{id} : T_x U \to T_x M ,
\]
under the identification of $T_x U$ with $T_x M$ induced by the charts. In particular $d\iota_x$ is an isomorphism.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
The chart of $U$ at $x$ is the chart of $M$ at $x$ restricted to $U$, so the two charts agree at every point of $U$ near $x$. The coordinate representation of $\iota$ is therefore $\kappa \circ \iota \circ \kappa^{-1}$ for one and the same chart $\kappa$, which is the identity on the chart's image. Differentiating the identity gives $d\iota_x = \operatorname{id}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Restriction to an open set preserves the differential]
\label{lem:opens-restrict-differential}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.mfderiv_opens_restrict}
\leanok
\uses{lem:opens-inclusion-differential}
Let $F : M \to M'$ be a smooth map, $U \subseteq M$ open, and $x \in U$. Then
\[
  d(F|_U)_x = dF_x .
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:opens-inclusion-differential}
$F|_U = F \circ \iota$ for the inclusion $\iota : U \hookrightarrow M$, so the chain rule gives $d(F|_U)_x = dF_x \circ d\iota_x$, and $d\iota_x = \operatorname{id}$ by Lemma \ref{lem:opens-inclusion-differential}.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Every value is a regular value of $f|_{\mathcal{R}}$]
\label{lem:regular-restrict-regular-value}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.regularRestrict_regular}
\leanok
\uses{def:regular-set,lem:regular-set-open,lem:opens-restrict-differential}
Let $M$ be a smooth manifold, $f \in C^\infty(M)$, and $\mathcal{R}$ the regular set of $f$, regarded as an open submanifold of $M$ (Lemma \ref{lem:regular-set-open}). Then $d(f|_{\mathcal{R}})_x \neq 0$ for every $x \in \mathcal{R}$; that is, every $c \in \mathbb{R}$ is a regular value of $f|_{\mathcal{R}}$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:opens-restrict-differential,def:regular-set}
By Lemma \ref{lem:opens-restrict-differential}, $d(f|_{\mathcal{R}})_x = df_x$, and $df_x \neq 0$ because $x \in \mathcal{R}$ by the definition of the regular set.
\end{proof}

\subsection*{The regular part of an arbitrary level set}

Throughout, $M_c$ denotes $(f|_{\mathcal{R}})^{-1}(c)$, the level set of the restriction inside the open submanifold $\mathcal{R}$; Lemma \ref{lem:regular-part-image} identifies it with Lee's subset $f^{-1}(c) \cap \mathcal{R}$ of $M$.

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isManifold_regularLevelSet}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.37}
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one,def:regular-set,lem:regular-set-open,lem:regular-restrict-regular-value}
Suppose $M$ is a smooth $(n+1)$-manifold without boundary, $f \in C^\infty(M)$, and
$\mathcal{R} \subseteq M$ is the set of regular points of $f$. For each $c \in \mathbb{R}$, the set $M_c = f^{-1}(c) \cap \mathcal{R}$ is a smooth $n$-manifold.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one,lem:regular-restrict-regular-value,lem:regular-set-open}
By Lemma \ref{lem:regular-set-open} the set $\mathcal{R}$ is open, so it is an open submanifold of $M$ of the same dimension $n+1$. Then $M_c = (f|_{\mathcal{R}})^{-1}(c)$, and $c$ is a regular value of $f|_{\mathcal{R}}$ by Lemma \ref{lem:regular-restrict-regular-value}. Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one}, applied to $f|_{\mathcal{R}}$ on the manifold $\mathcal{R}$, gives the slice-chart atlas making $M_c$ a smooth $n$-manifold.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The regular part, as a subset of $M$]
\label{lem:regular-part-image}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.range_regularLevelSetIncl}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface,def:regular-set}
In the situation of Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface}, the image of $M_c = (f|_{\mathcal{R}})^{-1}(c)$ under the inclusion $M_c \hookrightarrow \mathcal{R} \hookrightarrow M$ is exactly $f^{-1}(c) \cap \mathcal{R}$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
A point of $M$ lies in the image if and only if it lies in $\mathcal{R}$ and $f$ takes the value $c$ there, which is the definition of $f^{-1}(c) \cap \mathcal{R}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The regular part is embedded]
\label{lem:regular-part-embedded}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isEmbedding_regularLevelSetIncl}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface}
In the situation of Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface}, the inclusion $M_c \hookrightarrow M$ is a topological embedding.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
It is the composite of the inclusions $M_c \hookrightarrow \mathcal{R}$ and $\mathcal{R} \hookrightarrow M$, each of which carries the subspace topology and so is a topological embedding; a composite of embeddings is an embedding.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The inclusion of the regular part is smooth]
\label{lem:regular-part-inclusion-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiff_regularLevelSetIncl}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface,prop:regular-level-set-inclusion-smooth}
In the situation of Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface}, the inclusion $M_c \hookrightarrow M$ is smooth.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-inclusion-smooth}
It is the composite of $M_c \hookrightarrow \mathcal{R}$, smooth by Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-inclusion-smooth} applied to $f|_{\mathcal{R}}$, with the inclusion $\mathcal{R} \hookrightarrow M$ of an open submanifold, which is smooth.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The inclusion of the regular part is an immersion]
\label{lem:regular-part-inclusion-immersion}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.injective_mfderiv_regularLevelSetIncl}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface,lem:opens-inclusion-differential,prop:regular-level-set-inclusion-smooth}
In the situation of Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface}, the differential of the inclusion $M_c \hookrightarrow M$ is injective at every point. With Lemma \ref{lem:regular-part-embedded}, $M_c$ is an embedded smooth hypersurface in $M$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:opens-inclusion-differential,prop:regular-level-set-inclusion-smooth}
The differential of the composite $M_c \hookrightarrow \mathcal{R} \hookrightarrow M$ is $d(\mathcal{R} \hookrightarrow M) \circ d(M_c \hookrightarrow \mathcal{R})$, and the first factor is the identity by Lemma \ref{lem:opens-inclusion-differential}. So the differential equals that of $M_c \hookrightarrow \mathcal{R}$, which is injective by Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-inclusion-smooth} applied to $f|_{\mathcal{R}}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The tangent space of the regular part]
\label{lem:regular-part-tangent-kernel}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.range_mfderiv_regularLevelSetIncl}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface,prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel,lem:opens-restrict-differential}
In the situation of Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface}, for every $x \in M_c$ the image of the differential of the inclusion $M_c \hookrightarrow M$ is exactly $\ker df_x$; that is, $T_x M_c = \ker df_x$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel,lem:opens-restrict-differential,lem:opens-inclusion-differential}
By Lemma \ref{lem:opens-inclusion-differential} the differential of $M_c \hookrightarrow M$ equals that of $M_c \hookrightarrow \mathcal{R}$, whose image is $\ker d(f|_{\mathcal{R}})_x$ by Proposition \ref{prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel} applied to $f|_{\mathcal{R}}$. Finally $d(f|_{\mathcal{R}})_x = df_x$ by Lemma \ref{lem:opens-restrict-differential}, so the two kernels coincide.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[$\operatorname{grad} f$ is normal to the regular part]
\label{prop:gradient-normal-regular-part}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.innerAt_grad_mfderiv_regularLevelSetIncl_eq_zero}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface,lem:regular-part-tangent-kernel,def:gradient,lem:gradient-orthogonal-kernel}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold of dimension $n+1$ without boundary, $f \in C^\infty(M)$, and $c \in \mathbb{R}$. Then for every $x \in M_c$ and every $w \in T_x M_c$,
\[
  \bigl\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_{x},\, d\iota_{x}(w) \bigr\rangle = 0 ,
\]
where $\iota : M_c \hookrightarrow M$ is the inclusion. That is, $\operatorname{grad} f$ is everywhere normal to $M_c$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:regular-part-tangent-kernel,lem:gradient-orthogonal-kernel}
Since $f$ is constant on $M_c$, the chain rule gives $df_x(d\iota_x(w)) = 0$ for every $w \in T_x M_c$, so $d\iota_x(w) \in \ker df_x$. Lemma \ref{lem:gradient-orthogonal-kernel} then gives $\langle \operatorname{grad} f|_x, d\iota_x(w)\rangle = 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[$\operatorname{grad} f$ does not vanish on the regular part]
\label{lem:gradient-nonvanishing-regular-part}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.grad_ne_zero_regularLevelSet}
\leanok
\uses{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface,lem:regular-set-eq-gradient-nonvanishing,def:gradient}
In the situation of Proposition \ref{prop:gradient-normal-regular-part}, $\operatorname{grad} f|_x \neq 0$ for every $x \in M_c$. Together with Proposition \ref{prop:gradient-normal-regular-part} and Lemma \ref{lem:regular-part-tangent-kernel}, $\operatorname{grad} f|_x$ spans the normal line of the hypersurface $M_c$ at $x$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:regular-set-eq-gradient-nonvanishing}
Every point of $M_c$ lies in $\mathcal{R}$, so this is Lemma \ref{lem:regular-set-eq-gradient-nonvanishing}.
\end{proof}

The flat and sharp operators can be applied to tensors of any rank, in any index
position, to convert tensors from covariant to contravariant or vice versa. Formally,
this operation is defined as follows: if $F$ is any $(k,l)$-tensor and $i \in \{1, \ldots, k + l\}$ is
any covariant index position for $F$ (meaning that the $i$th argument is a vector, not a
covector), we can form a new tensor $F^\#$ of type $(k + 1, l - 1)$ by setting
\[
F^\#(\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_{k+l}) = F(\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_{i-1}, \alpha_i^*, \alpha_{i+1}, \ldots, \alpha_{k+l})
\]
whenever $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_{k+l}$ are vectors or covectors as appropriate. In any local frame,
the components of $F^\#$ are obtained by multiplying the components of $F$ by $g^{kl}$ and
contracting one of the indices of $g^{kl}$ with the $i$th index of $F$. Similarly, if $i$ is a
contravariant index position, we can define a $(k - 1, l + 1)$-tensor $F^b$ by
\[
F^b(\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_{k+l}) = F(\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_{i-1}, \alpha_i^*, \alpha_{i+1}, \ldots, \alpha_{k+l}).
\]

In components, it is computed by multiplying by $g_{kl}$ and contracting.

For example, if $A$ is a mixed 3-tensor given in terms of a local frame by
\[
A = A_i^{jk} \varepsilon^i \otimes E_j \otimes \varepsilon^k
\]
(see (B.6)), we can lower its middle index to obtain a covariant 3-tensor $A^b$ with components
\[
A_{ijk} = g_{jl} A^l_{ik}.
\]

To avoid overly cumbersome notation, we use the symbols $F^\#$ and $F^b$ without explicitly specifying which index position the sharp or flat operator is to be applied to; when there is more than one choice, we will always stipulate in words what is meant.

Another important application of the flat and sharp operators is to extend the trace operator introduced in Appendix B to covariant tensors. If $h$ is any covariant $k$-tensor field on a Riemannian manifold with $k \ge 2$, we can raise one of its indices (say the last one for definiteness) and obtain a $(1, k-1)$-tensor $h^\#$. The trace of $h^\#$ is thus a well-defined covariant $(k-2)$-tensor field (see Exercise B.3). We define the \textbf{trace of $h$ with respect to $g$} as
\[
\text{tr}_g h = \text{tr}(h^\#).
\]

Sometimes we may wish to raise an index other than the last, or to take the trace on a pair of indices other than the last covariant and contravariant ones. In each such case, we will say in words what is meant.

The most important case is that of a covariant 2-tensor field. In this case, $h^\#$ is a $(1,1)$-tensor field, which can equivalently be regarded as an endomorphism field, and $\text{tr}_g h$ is just the ordinary trace of this endomorphism field. In terms of a basis, this is
\[
\text{tr}_g h = h_i^i = g^{ij} h_{ij}.
\]

In particular, in an orthonormal frame this is the ordinary trace of the matrix $(h_{ij})$ (the sum of its diagonal entries); but if the frame is not orthonormal, then this trace is different from the ordinary trace.

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:inverse-metric-ambiguity}
\dcref{ch2:2.38}
If $g$ is a Riemannian metric on $M$ and $(E_i)$ is a local frame on $M$, there is a potential ambiguity about what the expression $(g^{ij})$ represents: we have defined it to mean the inverse matrix of $(g_{ij})$, but one could also interpret it as the components of the contravariant 2-tensor field $g^{\#\#}$ obtained by raising both of the indices of $g$. Show that these two interpretations lead to the same result.
\end{exercise}

\section{Inner Products of Tensors}

A Riemannian metric yields, by definition, an inner product on tangent vectors at each point. Because of the musical isomorphisms between vectors and covectors, it is easy to carry the inner product over to covectors as well.

Suppose $g$ is a Riemannian metric on $M$, and $x \in M$. We can define an inner product on the cotangent space $T_x^* M$ by
\[
\langle \omega, \eta \rangle_g = \langle \omega^\#, \eta^\# \rangle_g.
\]
(Just as with inner products of vectors, we might sometimes omit $g$ from the notation when the metric is understood.) To see how to compute this, we just use the basis formula (2.13) for the sharp operator, together with the relation $g_{kl}g^{kl} = g_{ik}g^{kl} = \delta_l^i$, to obtain
\begin{align*}
\langle\omega, \eta\rangle &= g_{kl}(g^{ki}\omega_i)(g^{lj}\eta_j)\\
&= \delta_l^i g^{lj}\omega_i\eta_j\\
&= g^{ij}\omega_i\eta_j.
\end{align*}

In other words, the inner product on covectors is represented by the inverse matrix $(g^{ij})$. Using our conventions for raising and lowering indices, this can also be written
\begin{align*}
\langle\omega, \eta\rangle &= \omega_i\eta^i = \omega^j\eta_j.
\end{align*}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:orthonormal-frames-and-coframes}
\dcref{ch2:2.39}
Let $(M, g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary, let $(E_i)$ be a local frame for $M$, and let $(e^i)$ be its dual coframe. Show that the following are equivalent:
\begin{enumerate}
\item[(a)] $(E_i)$ is orthonormal.
\item[(b)] $(e^i)$ is orthonormal.
\item[(c)] $(e^i)^{\sharp} = E_i$ for each $i$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

This construction can be extended to tensor bundles of any rank, as the following proposition shows. First a bit of terminology: if $E \to M$ is a smooth vector bundle, a \textbf{smooth fiber metric} on $E$ is an inner product on each fiber $E_p$ that varies smoothly, in the sense that for any (local) smooth sections $\sigma, \tau$ of $E$, the inner product $\langle\sigma, \tau\rangle$ is a smooth function.

\begin{proposition}[Inner Products of Tensors]
\label{prop:inner-products-of-tensors}
\dcref{ch2:2.40}
Let $(M, g)$ be an $n$-dimensional Riemannian manifold with or without boundary. There is a unique smooth fiber metric on each tensor bundle $T^{(k,l)}TM$ with the property that if $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_{k+l}, \beta_1, \ldots, \beta_{k+l}$ are vector or covector fields as appropriate, then
\begin{equation}
\langle\alpha_1 \otimes \cdots \otimes \alpha_{k+l}, \beta_1 \otimes \cdots \otimes \beta_{k+l}\rangle = \langle\alpha_1, \beta_1\rangle \cdots \langle\alpha_{k+l}, \beta_{k+l}\rangle. \tag{2.15}
\end{equation}
