and $dV_g$ is its volume form. If $X$ is a smooth vector field on $M$, then $X \lrcorner dV_g$ is
an $(n-1)$-form. The exterior derivative of this $(n-1)$-form is a smooth $n$-form, so
it can be expressed as a smooth function multiplied by $dV_g$. That function is called
the \emph{divergence of } $X$, and denoted by $\operatorname{div} X$; thus it is characterized by the following
formula:
\begin{equation}
d(X \lrcorner dV_g) = (\operatorname{div} X)dV_g. \tag{2.19}
\end{equation}

Even if $M$ is nonorientable, in a neighborhood of each point we can choose an
orientation and define the divergence by (2.19), and then note that reversing the orientation changes the sign of $dV_g$ on both sides of the equation, so $\operatorname{div} X$ is well
defined, independently of the choice of orientation. In this way, we can define the
divergence operator on \emph{any} Riemannian manifold with or without boundary, by
requiring that it satisfy (2.19) for any choice of orientation in a neighborhood of
each point.

The most important application of the divergence operator is the \emph{divergence theorem}, which you will be asked to prove in Problem 2-22.

Using the divergence operator, we can define another important operator, this one
acting on real-valued functions. The \emph{Laplacian} (or \emph{Laplace--Beltrami operator}) is
the linear operator $\Delta: C^\infty(M) \to C^\infty(M)$ defined by
\begin{equation}
\Delta u = \operatorname{div}(\operatorname{grad} u). \tag{2.20}
\end{equation}

(Note that many books, including [LeeSM] and the first edition of this book, define
the Laplacian as $-\operatorname{div}(\operatorname{grad} u)$. The main reason for choosing the negative sign is
so that the operator will have nonnegative eigenvalues; see Problem 2-24. But the
definition we give here is much more common in Riemannian geometry.)

The next proposition gives alternative formulas for these operators.

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:coordinate-representations-divergence-laplacian}
\dcref{ch2:2.46}

Let $(M, g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary,
and let $(x^i)$ be any smooth local coordinates on an open set $U \subseteq M$. The coordinate
representations of the divergence and Laplacian are as follows:
\[
\operatorname{div}\left(X^i \frac{\partial}{\partial x^i}\right) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{\det g}} \frac{\partial}{\partial x^i}\left(\sqrt{\det g}\, X^i\right),
\]
\[
\Delta u = \frac{1}{\sqrt{\det g}} \frac{\partial}{\partial x^i}\left(\sqrt{\det g}\, g^{ij} \frac{\partial u}{\partial x^j}\right),
\]
where $\det g = \det(g_{kl})$ is the determinant of the component matrix of $g$ in these coordinates. On $\mathbb{R}^n$ with the Euclidean metric and standard coordinates, these reduce to

\[
\operatorname{div}\left(X^i \frac{\partial}{\partial x^i}\right) = \sum_{i=1}^n \frac{\partial X^i}{\partial x^i},
\]

\[
\Delta u = \sum_{i=1}^n \frac{\partial^2 u}{(\partial x^i)^2}.
\]
\end{proposition}

\textit{Proof.} Problem 2-21. \hfill $\square$

\section{Lengths and Distances}

Perhaps the most important tool that a Riemannian metric gives us is the ability to measure lengths of curves and distances between points. Throughout this section, $(M, g)$ denotes a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary.

Without further qualification, a \textbf{curve} in $M$ always means a \textbf{parametrized curve}, that is, a continuous map $\gamma: I \to M$, where $I \subseteq \mathbb{R}$ is some interval. Unless otherwise specified, we will not worry about whether the interval is bounded or unbounded, or whether it includes endpoints or not. To say that $\gamma$ is a \textbf{smooth curve} is to say that it is smooth as a map from the manifold (with boundary) $I$ to $M$. If $I$ has one or two endpoints and $M$ has empty boundary, then $\gamma$ is smooth if and only if it extends to a smooth curve defined on some open interval containing $I$. (If $\partial M \neq \emptyset$, then smoothness of $\gamma$ has to be interpreted as meaning that each coordinate representation of $\gamma$ has a smooth extension to an open interval.) A \textbf{curve segment} is a curve whose domain is a compact interval.

A smooth curve $\gamma: I \to M$ has a well-defined velocity $\gamma'(t) \in T_{\gamma(t)} M$ for each $t \in I$. We say that $\gamma$ is a \textbf{regular curve} if $\gamma'(t) \neq 0$ for $t \in I$. This implies that $\gamma$ is an immersion, so its image has no \textquotedblleft corners\textquotedblright\ or \textquotedblleft kinks.\textquotedblright

We wish to use curve segments as \textquotedblleft measuring tapes\textquotedblright\ to define distances between points in a connected Riemannian manifold. Many aspects of the theory become technically much simpler if we work with a slightly larger class of curve segments instead of just the regular ones. We now describe the appropriate class of curves.

If $[a, b] \subseteq \mathbb{R}$ is a closed bounded interval, a \textbf{partition of $[a,b]$} is a finite sequence $(a_0, \ldots, a_k)$ of real numbers such that $a = a_0 < a_1 < \cdots < a_k = b$. Each interval $[a_{i-1}, a_i]$ is called a \textbf{subinterval of the partition}. If $M$ is a smooth manifold with or without boundary, a (continuous) curve segment $\gamma: [a, b] \to M$ is said to be
\emph{piecewise regular} if there exists a partition $(a_0,\ldots,a_k)$ of $[a,b]$ such that $\gamma|_{[a_{i-1},a_i]}$ is a regular curve segment (meaning it is smooth with nonvanishing velocity) for $i=1,\ldots,k$. For brevity, we refer to a piecewise regular curve segment as an \textbf{admissible curve}, and any partition $(a_0,\ldots,a_k)$ such that $\gamma|_{[a_{i-1},a_i]}$ is smooth for each $i$ an \textbf{admissible partition for $\gamma$}. (There are many admissible partitions for a given admissible curve, because we can always add more points to the partition.) It is also convenient to consider any map $\gamma: \{a\} \to M$ whose domain is a single real number to be an admissible curve.

Suppose $\gamma$ is an admissible curve and $(a_0,\ldots,a_k)$ is an admissible partition for it. At each of the intermediate partition points $a_1,\ldots,a_{k-1}$, there are two one-sided velocity vectors, which we denote by
\[
\gamma'(a_i^-) = \lim_{t \nearrow a_i} \gamma'(t),
\]
\[
\gamma'(a_i^+) = \lim_{t \searrow a_i} \gamma'(t).
\]
They are both nonzero, but they need not be equal.

If $\gamma: I \to M$ is a smooth curve, we define a \emph{reparametrization} of $\gamma$ to be a curve of the form $\tilde{\gamma} = \gamma \circ \varphi: I' \to M$, where $I' \subseteq \mathbb{R}$ is another interval and $\varphi: I' \to I$ is a diffeomorphism. Because intervals are connected, $\varphi$ is either strictly increasing or strictly decreasing on $I'$. We say that $\tilde{\gamma}$ is a \emph{forward reparametrization} if $\varphi$ is increasing, and a \emph{backward reparametrization} if it is decreasing.

For an admissible curve $\gamma: [a,b] \to M$, we define a \emph{reparametrization of $\gamma$} a little more broadly, as a curve of the form $\tilde{\gamma} = \gamma \circ \varphi$, where $\varphi: [c,d] \to [a,b]$ is a homeomorphism for which there is a partition $(c_0,\ldots,c_k)$ of $[c,d]$ such that the restriction of $\varphi$ to each subinterval $[c_{i-1},c_i]$ is a diffeomorphism onto its image.

If $\gamma: [a,b] \to M$ is an admissible curve, we define the \emph{length of $\gamma$} to be
\[
L_g(\gamma) = \int_a^b |\gamma'(t)|_g \, dt.
\]
The integrand is bounded and continuous everywhere on $[a,b]$ except possibly at the finitely many points where $\gamma$ is not smooth, so this integral is well defined.

\begin{definition}[The Length of a Curve, extended form]
\label{def:length-of-curve}
\lean{Manifold.pathELength}
\mathlibok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
For a curve $\gamma$ into $M$ and $a \le b$, the \emph{length} of $\gamma$ on $[a,b]$ is
\[
  L_g(\gamma|_{[a,b]}) \;=\; \int_{[a,b]} |\gamma'(t)|_g \, dt \;\in\; [0,\infty],
\]
the integral being taken in the Lebesgue sense, so that it is defined for every curve and takes values in $[0,\infty]$. For an admissible curve on a compact interval the integrand is bounded, so the value is finite and agrees with $L_g(\gamma)$ above.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Additivity of Length]
\label{prop:length-additive}
\lean{Manifold.pathELength_add}
\mathlibok
\uses{def:length-of-curve}
\dcref{ch2:2.47}
Suppose $(M,g)$ is a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary and $\gamma$ is an admissible curve. If $a \le c \le b$, then
\[
  L_g(\gamma|_{[a,c]}) + L_g(\gamma|_{[c,b]}) = L_g(\gamma|_{[a,b]}).
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}[Parameter Independence of Length]
\label{prop:length-reparam-invariant}
\lean{Manifold.pathELength_comp_of_monotoneOn}
\mathlibok
\uses{def:length-of-curve}
\dcref{ch2:2.47}
Suppose $(M,g)$ is a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary and $\gamma$ is an admissible curve on $[a,b]$. If $\tilde{\gamma} = \gamma \circ \varphi$ is a forward reparametrization of $\gamma$, then $L_g(\tilde{\gamma}) = L_g(\gamma)$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}[Isometry Invariance of Length]
\label{prop:length-isometry-invariant}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsMetricPreserving.pathELength_comp}
\leanok
\uses{def:length-of-curve,def:metric-preserving}
\dcref{ch2:2.47}
Suppose $(M,g)$ and $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ are Riemannian manifolds with or without boundary, $\varphi: M \to \tilde{M}$ is a smooth metric-preserving map, and $\gamma : [a,b] \to M$ is an admissible curve. Then
\[
  L_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi \circ \gamma) = L_g(\gamma).
\]
In particular this holds when $\varphi$ is a local isometry, since a local isometry preserves the metric.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:metric-preserving,def:length-of-curve}
Since $\varphi$ preserves the metric, each differential $d\varphi_p$ is a linear isometry, so for every $p \in M$ and $v \in T_p M$,
\[
  |d\varphi_p(v)|_{\tilde{g}}^2 = \tilde{g}_{\varphi(p)}(d\varphi_p v, d\varphi_p v) = g_p(v,v) = |v|_g^2 .
\]
At every $t$ in the open interval $(a,b)$ the curve $\gamma$ is differentiable, so the chain rule gives $(\varphi \circ \gamma)'(t) = d\varphi_{\gamma(t)}(\gamma'(t))$, and therefore
\[
  |(\varphi \circ \gamma)'(t)|_{\tilde{g}} = |d\varphi_{\gamma(t)}(\gamma'(t))|_{\tilde{g}} = |\gamma'(t)|_g .
\]
The two integrands agree on $(a,b)$, which differs from $[a,b]$ by a set of measure zero, so the integrals agree.
\end{proof}

Lee's Proposition 2.47 collects three properties of the length functional: additivity, parameter independence, and isometry invariance. They are recorded above as Propositions \ref{prop:length-additive}, \ref{prop:length-reparam-invariant} and \ref{prop:length-isometry-invariant} respectively, each stated separately because each is proved by a different argument: the first two are properties of the integral defining the length and hold on any manifold whose tangent spaces are normed, while only the third refers to a metric at all.

\begin{exercise}[Prove Proposition 2.47]
\label{exer:prove-prop-247}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.48}
\uses{prop:length-additive,prop:length-reparam-invariant,prop:length-isometry-invariant}
\end{exercise}

Suppose $\gamma : [a, b] \to M$ is an admissible curve. The \textbf{arc-length function} of $\gamma$ is the function $s : [a, b] \to \mathbb{R}$ defined by
\[s(t) = L_g(\gamma|_{[a,t]}) = \int_a^t |\gamma'(u)|_g \, du.\]

It is continuous everywhere, and it follows from the fundamental theorem of calculus that it is smooth wherever $\gamma$ is, with derivative $s'(t) = |\gamma'(t)|$.

For this reason, if $\gamma: I \to M$ is any smooth curve (not necessarily a curve segment), we define the \textbf{speed} of $\gamma$ at any time $t \in I$ to be the scalar $|\gamma'(t)|$. We say that $\gamma$ is a \textbf{unit-speed curve} if $|\gamma'(t)| = 1$ for all $t$, and a \textbf{constant-speed curve} if $|\gamma'(t)|$ is constant. If $\gamma$ is a piecewise smooth curve, we say that $\gamma$ has \textbf{unit speed} if $|\gamma'(t)| = 1$ wherever $\gamma$ is smooth.

If $\gamma : [a, b] \to M$ is a unit-speed admissible curve, then its arc-length function has the simple form $s(t) = t - a$. If, in addition, its parameter interval is of the form $[0, b]$ for some $b > 0$, then the arc-length function is $s(t) = t$. For this reason, a unit-speed admissible curve whose parameter interval is of the form $[0, b]$ is said to be \textbf{parametrized by arc length}.

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:reparametrization-by-arc-length}
\dcref{ch2:2.49}
Suppose $(M, g)$ is a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary.
\begin{enumerate}
\item[(a)] Every regular curve in $M$ has a unit-speed forward reparametrization.
\item[(b)] Every admissible curve in $M$ has a unique forward reparametrization by arc length.
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Suppose $\gamma : I \to M$ is a regular curve. Choose an arbitrary $t_0 \in I$, and define $s : I \to \mathbb{R}$ by
\[s(t) = \int_{t_0}^t |\gamma'(u)|_g \, du.\]

Since $s'(t) = |\gamma'(t)|_g > 0$, it follows that $s$ is a strictly increasing local diffeomorphism, and thus maps $I$ diffeomorphically onto an interval $I' \subseteq \mathbb{R}$. If we let $\varphi = s^{-1} : I' \to I$, then $\tilde{\gamma} = \gamma \circ \varphi$ is a forward reparametrization of $\gamma$, and the chain rule gives
\[|\tilde{\gamma}'(t)|_g = |\varphi'(t)\gamma'(\varphi(t))|_g = \frac{1}{s'(\varphi(t))}|\gamma'(\varphi(t))|_g = 1.\]

Thus $\tilde{\gamma}$ is a unit-speed reparametrization of $\gamma$.

Now let $\gamma : [a, b] \to M$ be an admissible curve. We prove the existence statement in part (b) by induction on the number of smooth segments in an admissible partition. If $\gamma$ has only one smooth segment, then it is a regular curve segment, and (b) follows by applying (a) in the special case $I = [a, b]$ and choosing $t_0 = a$. Assuming that the result is true for admissible curves with $k$ smooth segments, suppose $\gamma$
has an admissible partition $(a_0, \ldots, a_{k+1})$. The inductive hypothesis gives piecewise regular homeomorphisms $\varphi : [0,c] \to [a, a_k]$ and $\psi : [0,d] \to [a_k, b]$ such that $\gamma \circ \varphi$ is an arc-length reparametrization of $\gamma|_{[a,a_k]}$ and $\gamma \circ \psi$ is an arc-length reparametrization of $\gamma|_{[a_k,b]}$. If we define $\bar{\varphi}: [0, c+d] \to [a,b]$ by
\[
\bar{\varphi}(s) = \begin{cases}
\varphi(s), & s \in [0,c], \\
\psi(s-c), & s \in [c, c+d],
\end{cases}
\]
then $\gamma \circ \bar{\varphi}$ is a reparametrization of $\gamma$ by arc length.

To prove uniqueness, suppose that $\tilde{\gamma} = \gamma \circ \varphi$ and $\hat{\gamma} = \gamma \circ \psi$ are both forward reparametrizations of $\gamma$ by arc length. Since both have the same length, it follows that $\varphi$ and $\psi$ both have the same parameter domain $[0,c]$, and thus both are piecewise regular homeomorphisms from $[0,c]$ to $[a,b]$. If we define $\eta = \varphi^{-1} \circ \psi: [0,c] \to [0,c]$, then $\eta$ is a piecewise regular increasing homeomorphism satisfying $\tilde{\gamma} = \gamma \circ \varphi \circ \eta = \tilde{\gamma} \circ \eta$. The fact that both $\tilde{\gamma}$ and $\hat{\gamma}$ are of unit speed implies the following equality for all $s \in [0,c]$ except the finitely many values at which $\tilde{\gamma}$ or $\eta$ is not smooth:
\[
1 = |\dot{\tilde{\gamma}}(s)|_g = |\dot{\tilde{\gamma}}(\eta(s))\eta'(s)|_g = |\dot{\tilde{\gamma}}(\eta(s))|_g \eta'(s) = \eta'(s).
\]

Since $\eta$ is continuous and $\eta(0) = 0$, it follows that $\eta(s) = s$ for all $s \in [0,c]$, and thus $\tilde{\gamma} = \hat{\gamma}$.
\end{proof}

\section{The Riemannian Distance Function}

We are now in a position to introduce one of the most important concepts from classical geometry into the Riemannian setting: distances between points.

Suppose $(M, g)$ is a connected Riemannian manifold with or without boundary. For each pair of points $p, q \in M$, we define the \textbf{Riemannian distance from $p$ to $q$}, denoted by $d_g(p,q)$, to be the infimum of the lengths of all admissible curves from $p$ to $q$. The following proposition guarantees that $d_g(p,q)$ is a well-defined nonnegative real number for each $p, q \in M$.

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:connected-manifold-admissible-curve}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_contMDiffOn_path_of_preconnected}
\leanok
\uses{prop:existence-riemannian-metric,prop:riemannian-distance-finite,def:riemannian-distance}
\dcref{ch2:2.50}
If $M$ is a connected smooth manifold (with or without boundary), then any two points of $M$ can be joined by an admissible curve.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:existence-riemannian-metric,prop:riemannian-distance-finite,def:riemannian-distance}
Lee argues directly: since a connected manifold is path-connected, $p$ and $q$ are joined by a continuous path $c: [a,b] \to M$; by compactness there is a partition of $[a,b]$ such that each $c([a_{i-1}, a_i])$ lies in a single smooth coordinate ball (or half-ball if $\partial M \neq \emptyset$), and replacing each segment by a straight-line path in coordinates yields an admissible curve between the same points (Fig.~2.2).

The following route reaches the same conclusion from results already established. The statement mentions no metric, but nothing prevents introducing one: by Proposition \ref{prop:existence-riemannian-metric} choose a Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$. By Proposition \ref{prop:riemannian-distance-finite}, $d_g(p,q) < \infty$, so $d_g(p,q) < d_g(p,q) + 1$. Since $d_g(p,q)$ is by definition the infimum of the lengths of admissible curves from $p$ to $q$, a bound strictly exceeding the infimum is attained by some competitor: there is an admissible curve $\gamma$ from $p$ to $q$ with $L_g(\gamma) < d_g(p,q) + 1$. In particular such a $\gamma$ exists, which is the assertion; the metric $g$ and the length bound play no part in it and are discarded.
\end{proof}

For convenience, if $(M, g)$ is a disconnected Riemannian manifold, we also let $d_g(p,q)$ denote the Riemannian distance from $p$ to $q$, provided that $p$ and $q$ lie in the same connected component of $M$. (See also Problem 2-30.)

It is convenient to have a form of the definition that makes sense before one knows
that $p$ and $q$ can be joined at all, and that ranges over a class of curves closed
under the operations used below.  We therefore take the following as the working
definition, with values in $[0,\infty]$.

\begin{definition}[The Riemannian Distance, extended form]
\label{def:riemannian-distance}
\lean{Manifold.riemannianEDist}
\mathlibok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,def:length-of-curve}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold. For $p, q \in M$ set
\[
  d_g(p,q) \;=\; \inf \bigl\{\, L_g(\gamma) \;:\; \gamma : [0,1] \to M \text{ is } C^1,\;
    \gamma(0) = p,\; \gamma(1) = q \,\bigr\} \;\in\; [0, \infty],
\]
the infimum of the empty set being $\infty$.
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}
\label{rem:riemannian-distance-c1-vs-admissible}
\uses{def:riemannian-distance}
Definition \ref{def:riemannian-distance} takes the infimum over $C^1$ curves, whereas
Lee takes it over admissible (piecewise regular) curves.  Neither class contains the
other: a $C^1$ curve need not be regular, and an admissible curve need not be $C^1$ at
its corners.  The two infima nevertheless agree, because an admissible curve may be
reparametrized so as to become $C^1$ with vanishing velocity at the corners without
changing its length, and conversely a $C^1$ curve may be approximated in length by
regular ones.  That equality is not needed for anything below and is not proved here;
every statement in this section refers to Definition \ref{def:riemannian-distance}.
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[Connected Riemannian Manifolds Are $C^1$-Path Connected]
\label{prop:connected-manifold-c1-curve}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_contMDiffOn_path}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance,prop:riemannian-distance-finite}
Let $(M,g)$ be a connected Riemannian manifold. Then any two points of $M$ can be joined
by a $C^1$ curve $\gamma : [0,1] \to M$.

This is Proposition \ref{prop:connected-manifold-admissible-curve} in the $C^1$ formulation
of Definition \ref{def:riemannian-distance}. Lee assumes only that $M$ be a connected smooth
manifold, with no metric; the statement here carries a metric because the proof measures with
it. That costs no generality, since every smooth manifold admits a Riemannian metric by
Proposition \ref{prop:existence-riemannian-metric}.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance,prop:riemannian-distance-finite}
Let $p, q \in M$. By Proposition \ref{prop:riemannian-distance-finite}, $d_g(p,q) < \infty$,
so $d_g(p,q) < d_g(p,q) + 1 < \infty$. By Definition \ref{def:riemannian-distance},
$d_g(p,q)$ is the infimum of $L_g(\gamma)$ over $C^1$ curves $\gamma$ from $p$ to $q$; since
$d_g(p,q) + 1$ is strictly greater than that infimum, it is not a lower bound for the set of
such lengths, so the set is nonempty and contains an element $L_g(\gamma) < d_g(p,q)+1$. The
corresponding $\gamma$ is a $C^1$ curve from $p$ to $q$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Symmetry of the Riemannian Distance]
\label{lem:riemannian-distance-symm}
\lean{Manifold.riemannianEDist_comm}
\mathlibok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance}
$d_g(p,q) = d_g(q,p)$ for all $p, q$ in a Riemannian manifold $(M,g)$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Triangle Inequality for the Riemannian Distance]
\label{lem:riemannian-distance-triangle}
\lean{Manifold.riemannianEDist_triangle}
\mathlibok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance}
$d_g(p,r) \le d_g(p,q) + d_g(q,r)$ for all $p, q, r$ in a Riemannian manifold $(M,g)$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Nearby Points Are at Small Distance]
\label{lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small}
\lean{eventually_riemannianEDist_lt}
\mathlibok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold, let $p \in M$ and let $c > 0$. Then $p$ has a
neighborhood $U$ such that $d_g(p,q) < c$ for every $q \in U$.

This is the content of Lemma \ref{lem:distance-comparison-neighborhood}(a): a point
close to $p$ in a coordinate neighborhood is joined to $p$ by the coordinate segment,
whose $g$-length is controlled by its Euclidean length.
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Points at Small Distance Are Nearby]
\label{lem:riemannian-distance-small-implies-nearby}
\lean{setOf_riemannianEDist_lt_subset_nhds'}
\mathlibok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold and let $U$ be a neighborhood of $p \in M$. Then
there is $c > 0$ such that $\{q \in M : d_g(p,q) < c\} \subseteq U$.

This is the content of Lemma \ref{lem:distance-comparison-neighborhood}(b): a curve
from $p$ that leaves $U$ must cross a coordinate annulus around $p$, and so has
$g$-length bounded below.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proposition}[Finiteness of the Riemannian Distance]
\label{prop:riemannian-distance-finite}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.riemannianEDist_ne_top}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance,lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small,lem:riemannian-distance-symm,lem:riemannian-distance-triangle}
Let $(M,g)$ be a connected Riemannian manifold. Then $d_g(p,q) < \infty$ for all
$p, q \in M$.

This is the conclusion that Proposition \ref{prop:connected-manifold-admissible-curve}
exists to supply: it is exactly the assertion that some curve of finite length joins
$p$ to $q$, so that the infimum defining $d_g(p,q)$ is taken over a nonempty set of
finite numbers.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small,lem:riemannian-distance-symm,lem:riemannian-distance-triangle}
Fix $p \in M$ and put $S = \{q \in M : d_g(p,q) < \infty\}$. We show $S$ is open, closed
and nonempty.

\emph{$S$ is open.} Let $q_0 \in S$. By Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small}
applied at $q_0$ with $c = 1$, there is a neighborhood $U$ of $q_0$ with $d_g(q_0, q) < 1$
for every $q \in U$. For such $q$, Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-triangle} gives
\[
  d_g(p,q) \;\le\; d_g(p,q_0) + d_g(q_0,q) \;<\; \infty,
\]
both summands being finite. Hence $U \subseteq S$.

\emph{$S$ is closed.} We show $M \setminus S$ is open. Let $q_0 \in M \setminus S$, so
$d_g(p,q_0) = \infty$. Again there is a neighborhood $U$ of $q_0$ with $d_g(q_0,q) < 1$
for every $q \in U$. Suppose some $q \in U$ had $d_g(p,q) < \infty$. Then by
Lemmas \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-triangle} and \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-symm},
\[
  d_g(p,q_0) \;\le\; d_g(p,q) + d_g(q,q_0) \;=\; d_g(p,q) + d_g(q_0,q) \;<\; \infty,
\]
contradicting $d_g(p,q_0) = \infty$. Hence $U \subseteq M \setminus S$.

\emph{$S$ is nonempty.} The constant curve at $p$ has length $0$, so $d_g(p,p) = 0 < \infty$
and $p \in S$.

Since $M$ is connected and $S$ is a nonempty subset that is both open and closed, $S = M$.
As $p \in M$ was arbitrary, $d_g(p,q) < \infty$ for all $p, q \in M$.
\end{proof}

% [FIGURE: Any two points can be connected by an admissible curve]

\begin{lemma}[Metric-Preserving Maps Do Not Increase Distance]
\label{lem:metric-preserving-distance-le}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsMetricPreserving.riemannianEDist_le}
\leanok
\uses{def:metric-preserving,def:riemannian-distance,prop:length-isometry-invariant}
Suppose $(M,g)$ and $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ are Riemannian manifolds with or without boundary and $\varphi : M \to \tilde{M}$ is a smooth metric-preserving map. Then
\[
  d_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi(x), \varphi(y)) \;\le\; d_g(x,y) \qquad \text{for all } x, y \in M.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:length-isometry-invariant,def:riemannian-distance}
Let $r > d_g(x,y)$. By definition of $d_g$ as an infimum there is an admissible curve $\gamma : [0,1] \to M$ from $x$ to $y$ with $L_g(\gamma) < r$. Then $\varphi \circ \gamma$ is an admissible curve from $\varphi(x)$ to $\varphi(y)$, and by Proposition \ref{prop:length-isometry-invariant} it has $L_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi \circ \gamma) = L_g(\gamma) < r$. Since $d_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi(x),\varphi(y))$ is an infimum over such curves, $d_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi(x),\varphi(y)) < r$. As $r > d_g(x,y)$ was arbitrary and the order is dense, $d_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi(x),\varphi(y)) \le d_g(x,y)$.
\end{proof}

The inequality cannot be improved to an equality for metric-preserving maps in general: $\varphi$ carries curves of $M$ to curves of the same length in $\tilde{M}$, but $\tilde{M}$ may contain shorter curves between $\varphi(x)$ and $\varphi(y)$ that do not lie in the image of $\varphi$. Equality requires $\varphi$ to be invertible.

\begin{proposition}[Isometry Invariance of the Riemannian Distance Function]
\label{prop:isometry-invariance-riemannian-distance}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsIsometry.riemannianEDist_comp}
\leanok
\uses{def:isometry,def:riemannian-distance,lem:metric-preserving-distance-le,lem:isometry-symm}
\dcref{ch2:2.51}

Suppose $(M, g)$ and $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ are connected Riemannian manifolds with or without boundary, and $\varphi: M \to \tilde{M}$ is an isometry. Then $d_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi(x), \varphi(y)) = d_g(x, y)$ for all $x, y \in M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:metric-preserving-distance-le,lem:isometry-symm}
Applying Lemma \ref{lem:metric-preserving-distance-le} to $\varphi$ gives $d_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi(x),\varphi(y)) \le d_g(x,y)$. By Lemma \ref{lem:isometry-symm}, $\varphi^{-1}$ is an isometry, so the same lemma applied to $\varphi^{-1}$ at the points $\varphi(x), \varphi(y)$ gives
\[
  d_g(x,y) = d_g\bigl(\varphi^{-1}(\varphi(x)), \varphi^{-1}(\varphi(y))\bigr) \le d_{\tilde{g}}(\varphi(x), \varphi(y)).
\]
The two inequalities give equality.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:prove-isometry-invariance}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.52}
\uses{prop:isometry-invariance-riemannian-distance}

Prove the preceding proposition.
\end{exercise}

\begin{proposition}[Linear Isometries of Euclidean Space]
\label{prop:euclidean-linear-isometry-distance}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isIsometry_euclideanMetric_riemannianEDist}
\leanok
\uses{ex:euclidean-metric,def:isometry,prop:isometry-invariance-riemannian-distance,prop:euclidean-riemannian-distance}
Let $F$ and $\tilde{F}$ be inner product spaces with their Euclidean metrics, and let $e : F \to \tilde{F}$ be a linear isometry of $F$ onto $\tilde{F}$. Then $e$ is an isometry of the corresponding Riemannian manifolds, and
\[
  d_{\bar{g}}(e(x), e(y)) = |x - y| \qquad \text{for all } x, y \in F.
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:isometry-invariance-riemannian-distance,prop:euclidean-riemannian-distance,ex:euclidean-metric}
A linear map is its own differential, so $de_x = e$ for every $x$, and the condition $e^* \bar{g} = \bar{g}$ reads $\langle e(v), e(w)\rangle = \langle v, w \rangle$, which holds because $e$ is a linear isometry. Being in addition a linear homeomorphism, $e$ is a diffeomorphism, so it is an isometry. Proposition \ref{prop:isometry-invariance-riemannian-distance} then gives $d_{\bar{g}}(e(x), e(y)) = d_{\bar{g}}(x,y)$, and $d_{\bar{g}}(x,y) = |x-y|$ by Proposition \ref{prop:euclidean-riemannian-distance}.
\end{proof}

(Note that unlike lengths of curves, Riemannian distances are not necessarily preserved by \textit{local} isometries; see Problem 2-31.)
\uses{prob:local-isometry-distance}

We wish to show that the Riemannian distance function turns $M$ into a metric space, whose metric topology is the same as its original manifold topology. To do so, we need the following lemmas.

The comparison below rests on two ingredients, isolated here. The first is the joint
smoothness of the metric in the base point and the tangent vector together, which is what
makes the function $(x,v) \mapsto |v|_g$ on the tangent bundle continuous.

\begin{lemma}[Smoothness of the Energy Function]
\label{lem:metric-energy-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric.contMDiff_innerAt_totalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold. The energy function $E : TM \to \mathbb{R}$, defined on
the total space of the tangent bundle by
\[
E(x,v) = \langle v, v \rangle_g,
\]
is smooth. Consequently $(x,v) \mapsto |v|_g = E(x,v)^{1/2}$ is continuous on $TM$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric}
Smoothness of $g$ says that for any smooth map $b : N \to M$ and any two smooth sections
$X, Y$ of the pullback $b^*TM$, the function $p \mapsto \langle X_p, Y_p \rangle_{g_{b(p)}}$ is
smooth on $N$. Apply this with $N = TM$, with $b : TM \to M$ the bundle projection, and with
$X = Y$ the tautological section of $b^*TM$ assigning to a point $(x,v) \in TM$ the vector
$v \in T_x M$ itself. The resulting function is $E$. The final claim holds because
$t \mapsto t^{1/2}$ is continuous on $[0,\infty)$ and $E \geq 0$.
\end{proof}

The second ingredient is the analytic heart of the matter, stated with no reference to
manifolds: a family of inner products that varies continuously over a compact set is
comparable to a fixed norm, \emph{uniformly} over that set. The hypotheses are imposed only
on $K$, so the lemma applies to a family defined on any set containing $K$ --- which is what
Lemma \ref{lem:distance-comparison-neighborhood} needs, since there the family is a metric
read in a chart and is undefined outside the chart's image.

\begin{lemma}[Uniform Comparison over a Compact Set]
\label{lem:norm-comparison-compact-core}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_forall_sqrt_comparison}
\leanok
Let $V$ be a finite-dimensional real normed vector space, let $q$ assign to each $x \in V$ a
bilinear form $q_x$ on $V$, and let $K \subseteq V$ be compact. Write $S = \{v \in V : |v| = 1\}$
and suppose
\begin{enumerate}
\item[(i)] $(x,v) \mapsto q_x(v,v)$ is continuous on $K \times S$;
\item[(ii)] $q_x(v,v) > 0$ for every $x \in K$ and every $v \neq 0$.
\end{enumerate}
Then there are positive constants $c, C$ such that
\[
c|v| \leq q_x(v,v)^{1/2} \leq C|v| \qquad \text{for all } x \in K \text{ and all } v \in V.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
The set $K \times S$ is compact, being a product of compact sets ($S$ is compact because $V$ is
finite-dimensional). Every $v \in S$ is nonzero, so by (ii) the function $(x,v) \mapsto q_x(v,v)$
is strictly positive on $K \times S$; being continuous there by (i), it is bounded below by some
$c_0 > 0$ and above by some $C_0$. Set $c = c_0^{1/2}$ and $C = \max(C_0,1)^{1/2}$, both positive.

Let $x \in K$ and $v \in V$. If $v = 0$ both sides vanish. Otherwise put $u = v/|v| \in S$, so
that $v = |v|\,u$. Bilinearity gives the homogeneity relation $q_x(\lambda u, \lambda u) =
\lambda^2 q_x(u,u)$, whence $q_x(v,v) = |v|^2 q_x(u,u)$ and therefore
\[
q_x(v,v)^{1/2} = |v| \, q_x(u,u)^{1/2}.
\]
Since $c_0 \leq q_x(u,u) \leq C_0 \leq \max(C_0,1)$, taking square roots and multiplying by
$|v| \geq 0$ gives $c|v| \leq q_x(v,v)^{1/2} \leq C|v|$.

If $K$ or $S$ is empty the two bounds are produced vacuously, so no nondegeneracy assumption is
needed beyond (ii).
\end{proof}

Lee's lemma follows for a metric defined on all of $\mathbb{R}^n$; this is the case in which
the tangent bundle is canonically a product, so that the compact set $L$ appearing in the
argument literally is $K \times S^{n-1}$.

\begin{lemma}[Norm Comparison on $\mathbb{R}^n$]
\label{lem:riemannian-euclidean-norm-comparison-flat}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric.exists_forall_normAt_le_euclidean}
\leanok
\uses{lem:norm-comparison-compact-core, lem:metric-energy-smooth, def:riemannian-metric, ex:euclidean-metric}
Let $g$ be a Riemannian metric on $\mathbb{R}^n$ and let $\tilde{g}$ denote the Euclidean
metric. For every compact subset $K \subseteq \mathbb{R}^n$, there are positive constants
$c, C$ such that for all $x \in K$ and all $v \in T_x \mathbb{R}^n$,
\[
c|v|_{\tilde{g}} \leq |v|_g \leq C|v|_{\tilde{g}}.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:norm-comparison-compact-core, lem:metric-energy-smooth, ex:euclidean-metric}
Under the canonical identification of $T\mathbb{R}^n$ with $\mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R}^n$,
Lemma \ref{lem:metric-energy-smooth} says exactly that $(x,v) \mapsto \langle v,v\rangle_g$ is
continuous on $\mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R}^n$, so hypothesis (i) of Lemma
\ref{lem:norm-comparison-compact-core} holds for $q_x = g_x$ on $K \times S^{n-1}$; hypothesis
(ii) is the positive definiteness of $g$. That lemma yields $c, C > 0$ with
$c|v| \leq |v|_g \leq C|v|$ for $x \in K$, and $|v|_{\tilde{g}} = |v|$ because the Euclidean
metric assigns to a tangent vector its norm.
\end{proof}

Lee states the comparison for a metric defined only on an open subset, which is the form in
which it is applied to a metric read in a chart.

\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:riemannian-euclidean-norm-comparison}
\dcref{ch2:2.53}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric.exists_forall_normAt_le_euclidean_opens}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric, ex:euclidean-metric, lem:norm-comparison-compact-core, lem:metric-energy-smooth, lem:riemannian-euclidean-norm-comparison-flat}

Let $g$ be a Riemannian metric on an open subset $W \subseteq \mathbb{R}^n$ or $\mathbb{R}^n_+$, and let $\tilde{g}$ denote the Euclidean metric on $W$. For every compact subset $K \subseteq W$, there are positive constants $c, C$ such that for all $x \in K$ and all $v \in T_x \mathbb{R}^n$,
\begin{equation}
c|v|_{\tilde{g}} \leq |v|_g \leq C|v|_{\tilde{g}}.
\tag{2.21}
\end{equation}

\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:norm-comparison-compact-core, lem:metric-energy-smooth, ex:euclidean-metric}
Define a continuous function $F: TW \to \mathbb{R}$ by $F(x, v) = |v|_g = g_x(v, v)^{1/2}$ for $x \in W$ and $v \in T_x \mathbb{R}^n$; it is continuous by Lemma \ref{lem:metric-energy-smooth}. Let $K \subseteq W$ be any compact subset, and define $L \subseteq TW$ by
\[
L = \{(x, v) \in TW : x \in K, |v|_{\tilde{g}} = 1\}.
\]

Because $W$ is open in $\mathbb{R}^n$, every chart of $W$ is the inclusion $W \hookrightarrow \mathbb{R}^n$; the tangent bundle $TW$ therefore admits the single global trivialization $TW \cong W \times \mathbb{R}^n$, under which $L$ is the product set $K \times S^{n-1}$, which is compact. Since $F$ is positive on $L$, there are positive constants $c, C$ such that
\[
c \leq |v|_g \leq C \quad \text{for all } (x, v) \in L.
\]

For each $x \in K$ and $v \in T_x \mathbb{R}^n$ with $v \neq 0$, we can write $v = \lambda \tilde{v}$, where $\lambda = |v|_{\tilde{g}}$ and $\tilde{v} = v/\lambda$. Then $(x, \tilde{v}) \in L$, and it follows from the homogeneity of $|\cdot|_g$ that
\[
|v|_g = \lambda |\tilde{v}|_g \leq \lambda C = C|v|_{\tilde{g}}.
\]

The same inequality holds trivially when $v = 0$. Arguing similarly in the other direction, we conclude that (2.21) holds for all $x \in K$ and $v \in T_x \mathbb{R}^n$. The two displayed bounds on $L$, together with the homogeneity step, are the content of Lemma \ref{lem:norm-comparison-compact-core} applied to $q_x = g_x$ and the compact set $K$.
\end{proof}

% [FIGURE: Two overlapping regions with one labeled V, a point p inside, and a point q with an arrow labeled  \gamma (t₀), illustrating the neighborhood structure for the distance comparison proof]

% [FIGURE: A curved path with labeled points p,  \gamma ₁, q,  \gamma ₂, and r, demonstrating the triangle inequality concept]

The next lemma shows how to transfer this result to Riemannian manifolds.

\begin{lemma}\label{lem:distance-comparison-neighborhood}\dcref{ch2:2.54}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric.exists_coordinate_nbhd_riemannianEDist_comparison}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,def:riemannian-distance,lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small,lem:riemannian-distance-small-implies-nearby}
Let $(M, g)$ be a Riemannian manifold with or without boundary and
let $d_g$ be its Riemannian distance function. Suppose $U$ is an open subset of $M$ and
$p \in U$. Then $p$ has a coordinate neighborhood $V \subset U$ with the property that there
are positive constants $C, D$ satisfying the following inequalities:
\begin{enumerate}
\item[(a)] If $q \in V$, then $d_g(p,q) \leq Cd_{\bar{g}}(p,q)$, where $\bar{g}$ is the Euclidean metric in the
given coordinates on $\mathbb{R}^n$ or $\mathbb{R}^n_+$.
\item[(b)] If $q \in M \setminus V$, then $d_g(p,q) \geq D$.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Let $W$ be any neighborhood of $p$ contained in $U$ on which there exist smooth
coordinates $(x^i)$. Using these coordinates, we can identify $W$ with an open subset
of $\mathbb{R}^n$ or $\mathbb{R}^n_+$. Let $K$ be a compact subset of $W$ containing a neighborhood $V$ of
$p$, chosen as follows: If $p$ is an interior point of $M$, let $K$ be the closed Euclidean
ball $\bar{B}_\epsilon(0)$, with $\epsilon > 0$ chosen small enough that $K \subseteq W$, and let $V =
B_\epsilon(0)$. If $p \in \partial M$, let $K$ be a closed half-ball $\bar{B}_\epsilon(0) \cap \mathbb{R}^n_+$ with $\epsilon$ chosen similarly, and let $V =
B_\epsilon(0) \cap \mathbb{R}^n_+$. Let $\bar{g} = \sum_i (dx^i)^2$ denote the Euclidean metric in these coordinates,
and let $c, C$ be constants satisfying (2.21). If $\gamma$ is any admissible curve whose image
lies entirely in $V$, it follows that

$$cL_{\bar{g}}(\gamma) \leq L_g(\gamma) \leq CL_{\bar{g}}(\gamma). \quad (2.22)$$

To prove (a), suppose $q \in V$. Letting $\gamma : [a,b] \to V$ be a parametrization of the
line segment from $p$ to $q$, we conclude from (2.22) that

$$d_g(p,q) \leq L_g(\gamma) \leq CL_{\bar{g}}(\gamma) = Cd_{\bar{g}}(p,q).$$

To prove (b), suppose $q \in M \setminus V$. If $\gamma : [a,b] \to M$ is any admissible curve from
$p$ to $q$, let $t_0$ denote the infimum of times $t \in [a,b]$ such that $\gamma(t) \notin V$. It follows
that $\gamma([a, t_0]) \subseteq K$ and $d_{\bar{g}}(p, \gamma(t_0)) = \epsilon$ by continuity (Fig. 2.3), so (2.22) implies

$$L_g(\gamma) \geq L_g(\gamma|_{[a,t_0]}) \geq cL_{\bar{g}}(\gamma|_{[a,t_0]}) \geq cd_{\bar{g}}(p, \gamma(t_0)) = c\epsilon.$$

Taking the infimum over all such curves $\gamma$, we obtain (b) with $D = c\varepsilon$. \qed
\end{proof}
\end{lemma}

\begin{theorem}[Riemannian Manifolds as Metric Spaces]
\label{thm:riemannian-manifolds-metric-spaces}
\dcref{ch2:2.55}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.MetricSpace.ofRiemannianMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance,prop:riemannian-distance-finite,lem:riemannian-distance-symm,lem:riemannian-distance-triangle,lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small,lem:riemannian-distance-small-implies-nearby}

Let $(M,g)$ be a connected Riemannian manifold with or without boundary. Then $M$ carries a
metric space structure.

That its distance function is $d_g$ is Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-metric-space-dist}, and that
its topology is the manifold topology is Theorem \ref{thm:riemannian-distance-topology}; the
three together are Lee's Theorem 2.55.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:riemannian-distance-finite,lem:riemannian-distance-symm,lem:riemannian-distance-triangle,lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small,lem:riemannian-distance-small-implies-nearby}
It is immediate from the definition of $d_g$ that $d_g(p,q) \ge 0$ and $d_g(p,p) = 0$, and
$d_g(p,q) = d_g(q,p)$ is Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-symm}. The triangle inequality
is Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-triangle}; it holds because a curve from $p$ to $q$
can be concatenated with one from $q$ to $r$ to yield one from $p$ to $r$ whose length is
the sum of the two lengths.

For separation, suppose $p, q \in M$ are distinct. Since $M$ is Hausdorff, there is an open
set $U$ containing $p$ but not $q$. By Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-small-implies-nearby}
there is $c > 0$ with $\{r : d_g(p,r) < c\} \subseteq U$. As $q \notin U$, we get
$d_g(p,q) \ge c > 0$.

Finally, $d_g$ takes finite values by Proposition \ref{prop:riemannian-distance-finite},
which is where connectedness of $M$ is used, so $d_g$ is a genuine real-valued metric rather
than merely an extended one. Together these say that $d_g$ turns $M$ into a metric space.
\end{proof}

The theorem as stated asserts only that some metric exists. What makes it Lee's Theorem 2.55
is that the metric in question is $d_g$, which is the following lemma.

\begin{lemma}[The Metric Is the Riemannian Distance]
\label{lem:riemannian-metric-space-dist}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.MetricSpace.ofRiemannianMetric_dist}
\leanok
\uses{thm:riemannian-manifolds-metric-spaces,def:riemannian-distance,prop:riemannian-distance-finite}
The distance function of the metric space of Theorem \ref{thm:riemannian-manifolds-metric-spaces}
is the Riemannian distance: for all $p, q \in M$,
\[
  \operatorname{dist}(p,q) \;=\; d_g(p,q),
\]
where the right-hand side, a priori an element of $[0,\infty]$ by
Definition \ref{def:riemannian-distance}, is read as a real number using
Proposition \ref{prop:riemannian-distance-finite}.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance,prop:riemannian-distance-finite}
The metric space of Theorem \ref{thm:riemannian-manifolds-metric-spaces} is obtained from the
extended distance $d_g$ of Definition \ref{def:riemannian-distance} by taking real values, which
is legitimate because $d_g$ is finite by Proposition \ref{prop:riemannian-distance-finite}. Its
distance function is therefore $d_g$ itself, and the identity holds by construction.
\end{proof}

The remaining assertion of Lee's Theorem 2.55 is that this metric induces the topology one
started with; it is separated out here because it is a statement about the metric
constructed above rather than part of its construction.

\begin{theorem}[The Metric Topology Is the Manifold Topology]
\label{thm:riemannian-distance-topology}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.MetricSpace.ofRiemannianMetric_topology}
\leanok
\uses{thm:riemannian-manifolds-metric-spaces,lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small,lem:riemannian-distance-small-implies-nearby}
Let $(M,g)$ be a connected Riemannian manifold with or without boundary. The topology
induced by the metric $d_g$ of Theorem \ref{thm:riemannian-manifolds-metric-spaces} is the
given manifold topology.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small,lem:riemannian-distance-small-implies-nearby}
Suppose first that $U \subseteq M$ is open in the manifold topology and let $p \in U$. By
Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-small-implies-nearby} there is $c > 0$ with
$\{q : d_g(p,q) < c\} \subseteq U$, i.e. the $d_g$-ball of radius $c$ about $p$ is contained
in $U$. Thus $U$ is open in the metric topology.

Conversely, suppose $U'$ is open in the metric topology and let $p \in U'$. Choose $\delta > 0$
with the $d_g$-ball of radius $\delta$ about $p$ contained in $U'$. By
Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-distance-locally-small} applied at $p$ with $c = \delta$, there is a
neighborhood $V$ of $p$ in the manifold topology with $d_g(p,q) < \delta$ for every $q \in V$;
hence $V \subseteq U'$. So $U'$ is a neighborhood of each of its points in the manifold
topology, and is therefore open in it.

The two inclusions of neighborhood filters say exactly that the two topologies coincide.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[The Euclidean Distance]
\label{prop:euclidean-riemannian-distance}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.euclideanMetric_riemannianEDist}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance,ex:euclidean-metric}
On $\mathbb{R}^n$ with the Euclidean metric $\bar g$ of Example \ref{ex:euclidean-metric},
the Riemannian distance is the Euclidean distance:
\[
  d_{\bar g}(x,y) \;=\; |x - y| \qquad \text{for all } x, y \in \mathbb{R}^n .
\]
This is the one case in which the answer is known in advance, so it is the natural check on
Definition \ref{def:riemannian-distance}. The real-valued form $d_{\bar g}(x,y) = |x-y|$ is
Lemma \ref{lem:euclidean-riemannian-distance-real}.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-distance,ex:euclidean-metric}
For the upper bound, let $\gamma(t) = x + t(y-x)$ for $t \in [0,1]$. This curve is $C^1$, joins
$x$ to $y$, and has $\gamma'(t) = y - x$, so under the identification of each tangent space of
$\mathbb{R}^n$ with $\mathbb{R}^n$ its $\bar g$-length is
\[
  L_{\bar g}(\gamma) \;=\; \int_0^1 |\gamma'(t)| \, dt \;=\; \int_0^1 |y-x| \, dt \;=\; |y-x| .
\]
Hence $d_{\bar g}(x,y) \le |x-y|$.

For the lower bound, let $\gamma : [0,1] \to \mathbb{R}^n$ be any $C^1$ curve with
$\gamma(0) = x$ and $\gamma(1) = y$. By the fundamental theorem of calculus and the triangle
inequality for the integral,
\[
  |y - x| \;=\; \Bigl| \int_0^1 \gamma'(t) \, dt \Bigr| \;\le\; \int_0^1 |\gamma'(t)| \, dt
    \;=\; L_{\bar g}(\gamma) .
\]
So $|x-y|$ is a lower bound for the set of lengths whose infimum is $d_{\bar g}(x,y)$, giving
$|x-y| \le d_{\bar g}(x,y)$.

The two inequalities give $d_{\bar g}(x,y) = |x-y|$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Euclidean Distance, real-valued form]
\label{lem:euclidean-riemannian-distance-real}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.euclideanMetric_riemannianEDist_toReal}
\leanok
\uses{prop:euclidean-riemannian-distance}
With the notation of Proposition \ref{prop:euclidean-riemannian-distance}, $d_{\bar g}(x,y)$ is
finite and equals $|x-y|$ as a real number.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:euclidean-riemannian-distance}
Immediate from Proposition \ref{prop:euclidean-riemannian-distance}, since $|x-y|$ is a real
number and hence $d_{\bar g}(x,y) \ne \infty$.
\end{proof}

Thanks to the preceding theorem, it makes sense to apply all the concepts of the theory of metric spaces to a connected Riemannian manifold $(M,g)$. For example, we say that $M$ is \textit{(metrically) complete} if every Cauchy sequence in $M$ converges. A subset $A \subseteq M$ is \textit{bounded} if there is a constant $C$ such that $d_g(p,q) \leq C$ for all $p,q \in A$; if this is the case, the \textit{diameter of } $A$ is the smallest such constant:
\[
\operatorname{diam}(A) = \sup\{d_g(p,q) : p,q \in A\}.
\]

Since every compact metric space is bounded, every compact connected Riemannian manifold with or without boundary has finite diameter. (Note that the unit sphere with the Riemannian distance determined by the round metric has diameter $\pi$, not $2$, since the Riemannian distance between antipodal points is $\pi$. See Problem 6-2.)

\section{Pseudo-Riemannian Metrics}

From the point of view of geometry, Riemannian metrics are by far the most important structures that manifolds carry. However, there is a generalization of Riemannian metrics that has become especially important because of its application to physics.

Before defining this generalization, we begin with some linear-algebraic preliminaries. Suppose $V$ is a finite-dimensional vector space, and $q$ is a symmetric covariant 2-tensor on $V$ (also called a \textbf{symmetric bilinear form}). Just as for an inner product, there is a linear map $\tilde{q}: V \to V^*$ defined by
\[
\tilde{q}(v)(w) = q(v,w) \text{ for all } v, w \in V.
\]

We say that $q$ is \textbf{nondegenerate} if $\tilde{q}$ is an isomorphism.

\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.nondegenerate_tfae}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.56}
Suppose $q$ is a symmetric covariant 2-tensor on a finite-dimensional vector space $V$. The following are equivalent:
\begin{enumerate}
\item[(a)] $q$ is nondegenerate.
\item[(b)] For every nonzero $v \in V$, there is some $w \in V$ such that $q(v,w) \neq 0$.
\item[(c)] If $q = q_{ij} e^i e^j$ in terms of some basis $(e^j)$ for $V^*$, then the matrix $(q_{ij})$ is invertible.
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
(a) $\Rightarrow$ (b): if $\tilde q$ is an isomorphism and $v \neq 0$, then $\tilde q(v) \neq 0$, so there is some $w \in V$ with $q(v,w) = \tilde q(v)(w) \neq 0$.

(b) $\Rightarrow$ (a): condition (b) says exactly that $\tilde q$ is injective, since $\tilde q(v) = 0$ means $q(v,w) = 0$ for every $w$, which by (b) forces $v = 0$. As $\dim V^* = \dim V < \infty$, an injective linear map $V \to V^*$ is an isomorphism.

(a) $\Leftrightarrow$ (c): let $(e_i)$ be the basis of $V$ dual to $(e^j)$. Then $\tilde q(e_i)(e_j) = q(e_i, e_j) = q_{ij}$, so $(q_{ij})$ is precisely the matrix of $\tilde q : V \to V^*$ with respect to the bases $(e_i)$ and $(e^j)$. A linear map between spaces of equal finite dimension is an isomorphism if and only if its matrix in any pair of bases is invertible.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:prove-nondegenerate-equivalent}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.57}
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Prove the preceding lemma.
\end{exercise}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
This is the content of Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}, proved above.
\end{proof}

Every inner product is a nondegenerate symmetric bilinear form, as is every symmetric bilinear form that is \textbf{negative definite} (which is defined by obvious analogy with positive definite). But there are others that are neither positive definite nor negative definite, as we will see below.

We use the term \textbf{scalar product} to denote any nondegenerate symmetric bilinear form on a finite-dimensional vector space $V$, and reserve the term \textbf{inner product} for the special case of a positive definite scalar product. A \textbf{scalar product space} is a finite-dimensional vector space endowed with a scalar product. When convenient, we will often use a notation like $\langle \cdot, \cdot \rangle$ to denote a scalar product. We say that vectors $v, w \in V$ are \textbf{orthogonal} if $\langle v, w \rangle = 0$, just as in the case of an inner product. Given a vector $v \in V$, we define the \textbf{norm} of $v$ to be $|v| = \sqrt{\langle v, v \rangle}$. Note that in the indefinite case, it is possible for a nonzero vector to be orthogonal to itself, and thus to have norm zero. Thus $|v|$ is not technically a norm in the sense defined on page 47 below, but it is customary to call it ``the norm of $v$'' anyway.

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:polarization-scalar-product}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.BilinForm.IsSymm.inner_eq_apply_add_sub_apply_sub_div_four}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.58}
\uses{exer:polarization-proof}
Prove that the polarization identity (2.2) holds for every scalar product.
\end{exercise}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
The proof of Lemma \ref{lem:polarization-identity} used only bilinearity and symmetry of $\langle \cdot, \cdot \rangle$, never positive definiteness: expanding $\langle v \pm w, v \pm w \rangle = \langle v,v \rangle \pm 2\langle v,w \rangle + \langle w,w \rangle$ and subtracting gives $4\langle v,w \rangle$. Since a scalar product is by definition a symmetric bilinear form, the identity holds verbatim.
\end{proof}

If $S \subseteq V$ is any linear subspace, the set of vectors in $V$ that are orthogonal to every vector in $S$ is a linear subspace denoted by $S^{\perp}$.

\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:orthogonal-complement}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.finrank_add_finrank_orthogonal_eq_finrank}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.59}
Suppose $(V, q)$ is a finite-dimensional scalar product space, and $S \subseteq V$ is a linear subspace. Then $\dim S + \dim S^{\perp} = \dim V$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Define a linear map $\Phi : V \to S^*$ by $\Phi(v) = \tilde{q}(v)|_S$. Note that $v \in \Ker\Phi$ if and only if $q(v,x) = \tilde{q}(v)(x) = 0$ for all $x \in S$, so $\Ker\Phi = S^\perp$. If $\varphi \in S^*$ is arbitrary, there is a covector $\tilde{\varphi} \in V^*$ whose restriction to $S$ is equal to $\varphi$. (For example, such a covector is easily constructed after choosing a basis for $S$ and extending it to a basis for $V$.) Since $\tilde{q}$ is an isomorphism, there exists $v \in V$ such that $\tilde{q}(v) = \tilde{\varphi}$. It follows that $\Phi(v) = \varphi$, and therefore $\Phi$ is surjective. By the rank--nullity theorem, the dimension of $S^\perp = \Ker\Phi$ is equal to $\dim V - \dim S^* = \dim V - \dim S$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:orthogonal-complement-involution}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.orthogonal_orthogonal_eq_self}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.59}
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement}
Suppose $(V, q)$ is a finite-dimensional scalar product space, and $S \subseteq V$ is a linear subspace. Then $(S^{\perp})^{\perp} = S$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement}
Every $v \in S$ is orthogonal to every element of $S^\perp$ by definition, so $S \subseteq (S^\perp)^\perp$. Applying Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement} twice gives $\dim (S^\perp)^\perp = \dim V - \dim S^\perp = \dim S$. Because these finite-dimensional vector spaces are nested and have the same dimension, they are equal.
\end{proof}

An ordered $k$-tuple $(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$ of elements of $V$ is said to be \textbf{orthonormal} if $|v_i| = 1$ for each $i$ and $\langle v_i, v_j \rangle = 0$ for $i \neq j$, or equivalently, if $\langle v_i, v_j \rangle = \pm\delta_{ij}$ for each $i$ and $j$. We wish to prove that every scalar product space has an orthonormal basis. Note that the usual Gram--Schmidt algorithm does not always work in this situation, because the vectors that appear in the denominators in (2.5)--(2.6) might have vanishing norms. In order to get around this problem, we introduce the following definitions. If $(V,q)$ is a finite-dimensional scalar product space, a subspace $S \subseteq V$ is said to be \textbf{nondegenerate} if the restriction of $q$ to $S \times S$ is nondegenerate. An ordered $k$-tuple of vectors $(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$ in $V$ is said to be \textbf{nondegenerate} if for each $j = 1,\ldots,k$, the vectors $(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$ span a nondegenerate $j$-dimensional subspace of $V$. For example, every orthonormal basis is nondegenerate.

\begin{lemma}\label{lem:nondegenerate-subspace}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.restrict_nondegenerate_tfae}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.60}
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement-involution}
Suppose $(V,q)$ is a finite-dimensional scalar product space, and $S \subseteq V$ is a linear subspace. The following are equivalent:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item $S$ is nondegenerate.
\item $S^\perp$ is nondegenerate.
\item $S \cap S^\perp = \{0\}$.
\item $V = S \oplus S^\perp$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement,lem:orthogonal-complement-involution}
(c) $\Rightarrow$ (a): suppose $S \cap S^\perp = \{0\}$ and let $x \in S$ satisfy $q(x,y) = 0$ for all $y \in S$. Then $x \in S^\perp$, so $x \in S \cap S^\perp = \{0\}$; thus $q|_{S \times S}$ is nondegenerate by Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}.

(a) $\Rightarrow$ (d): suppose $S$ is nondegenerate. Then $S \cap S^\perp = \{0\}$, since any $x$ in the intersection is orthogonal to all of $S$ and lies in $S$, hence vanishes by nondegeneracy of $q|_{S \times S}$. By Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement}, $\dim S + \dim S^\perp = \dim V$, so $S + S^\perp$ has dimension $\dim S + \dim S^\perp - \dim(S \cap S^\perp) = \dim V$, whence $S + S^\perp = V$ and the sum is direct.

(d) $\Rightarrow$ (c): immediate, since a direct sum decomposition has trivial intersection.

(a) $\Leftrightarrow$ (b): by Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement-involution}, $(S^\perp)^\perp = S$, so applying the equivalence (a) $\Leftrightarrow$ (d) to the subspace $S^\perp$ shows that $S^\perp$ is nondegenerate if and only if $V = S^\perp \oplus (S^\perp)^\perp = S^\perp \oplus S$. Since a direct sum decomposition is symmetric in its two summands, this holds if and only if $V = S \oplus S^\perp$, which by (a) $\Leftrightarrow$ (d) holds if and only if $S$ is nondegenerate.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}\label{exer:preceding-lemma-nondegenerate}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.61}
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-subspace}
Prove the preceding lemma.
\end{exercise}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
This is the content of Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-subspace}, proved above.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Completion of Nondegenerate Bases]\label{lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isNondegenerateTuple_basis}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.62}
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-subspace}
Suppose $(V,q)$ is an $n$-dimensional scalar product space, and $(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$ is a nondegenerate $k$-tuple in $V$ with $0 \leq k < n$. Then there exist vectors $v_{k+1},\ldots,v_n \in V$ such that $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ is a nondegenerate basis for $V$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-subspace,lem:orthogonal-complement,exer:polarization-scalar-product}
It suffices to adjoin one vector, and then to repeat.

Let $S = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_k) \subseteq V$, a nondegenerate subspace of dimension $k$. Because $k < n$, Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement} gives $\dim S^\perp = n - k > 0$, so $S^\perp$ is a nontrivial subspace of $V$, and Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-subspace} shows that $S^\perp$ is nondegenerate and $V = S \oplus S^\perp$.

There must be a vector in $S^\perp$ with nonzero length. Otherwise $\langle x,x\rangle = 0$ for every $x \in S^\perp$, and then the polarization identity of Exercise \ref{exer:polarization-scalar-product},
\[
\langle x,y \rangle = \tfrac14\left(\langle x+y,x+y\rangle - \langle x-y,x-y\rangle\right),
\]
applied to $x, y \in S^\perp$ (which is a subspace, so $x \pm y \in S^\perp$) would give $\langle x,y\rangle = 0$ for all $x,y \in S^\perp$; as $S^\perp \neq \{0\}$, this contradicts the nondegeneracy of $S^\perp$.

Let $v_{k+1} \in S^\perp$ have $\langle v_{k+1},v_{k+1}\rangle \neq 0$. Then $v_{k+1} \notin S$, since $S \cap S^\perp = \{0\}$ and $v_{k+1} \neq 0$; hence $\dim(S \oplus \langle v_{k+1}\rangle) = k+1$. This span is nondegenerate: suppose $y = s + c\,v_{k+1}$ with $s \in S$ is orthogonal to all of $S \oplus \langle v_{k+1}\rangle$. Testing against any $w \in S$ gives $\langle s,w\rangle + c\langle v_{k+1},w\rangle = \langle s,w\rangle = 0$, since $v_{k+1} \in S^\perp$; as $S$ is nondegenerate, $s = 0$. Testing $y = c\,v_{k+1}$ against $v_{k+1}$ gives $c\langle v_{k+1},v_{k+1}\rangle = 0$, and $\langle v_{k+1},v_{k+1}\rangle \neq 0$ forces $c = 0$. So $y = 0$.

Thus $(v_1,\ldots,v_{k+1})$ is a nondegenerate $(k+1)$-tuple. Repeating this argument for $v_{k+2},\ldots,v_n$, which terminates after $n-k$ steps, completes the proof.
\end{proof}


\begin{proposition}[Gram--Schmidt Algorithm for Scalar Products]
\label{prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_basis_isOrthonormal_prefixSpan_eq}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.63}
Suppose $(V, q)$ is an $n$-dimensional scalar product space. If $(v_i)$ is a nondegenerate basis for $V$, then there is an orthonormal basis $(b_i)$ with the property that $\operatorname{span}(b_1, \ldots, b_k) = \operatorname{span}(v_1, \ldots, v_k)$ for each $k = 1, \ldots, n$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
As in the positive definite case the tuple is constructed recursively, the inductive hypothesis at stage $k$ being that $(b_1,\ldots,b_k)$ is orthonormal and satisfies $\operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_j) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$ for every $j \leq k$. For $k = 0$ both conditions are vacuous.

Assume $(b_1,\ldots,b_k)$ has been constructed and write $S = \operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_k) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$. Set
\[
z = v_{k+1} - \sum_{i=1}^k \frac{\langle v_{k+1}, b_i \rangle}{\langle b_i, b_i \rangle} b_i.
\]
Each denominator is $\langle b_i, b_i \rangle = \pm 1 \neq 0$, so $z$ is well defined; this is the step at which positive definiteness is dispensable but nondegeneracy of the prefixes is not. For each $j \leq k$, orthogonality of the $b_i$ leaves a single surviving term in the sum:
\[
\langle z, b_j \rangle = \langle v_{k+1}, b_j \rangle - \frac{\langle v_{k+1}, b_j \rangle}{\langle b_j, b_j \rangle}\langle b_j, b_j \rangle = 0,
\]
so $z$ is orthogonal to every element of $S$.

Since $z - v_{k+1} \in S$, adjoining $z$ and adjoining $v_{k+1}$ to $S$ give the same subspace:
\[
S + \langle z \rangle = S + \langle v_{k+1} \rangle = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_{k+1}) =: T .
\]
Moreover $z \neq 0$; otherwise $v_{k+1} \in S$ and hence $T = S$, contradicting $\dim T = k+1 > k = \dim S$, both dimensions being part of the hypothesis that $(v_i)$ is nondegenerate.

Next, $\langle z, z \rangle \neq 0$. This is the one point at which the indefinite case genuinely differs: $z \neq 0$ no longer suffices. Suppose $\langle z,z\rangle = 0$. Every $w \in T$ can be written $w = s + cz$ with $s \in S$ and $c \in \mathbb{R}$, whence
\[
\langle z, w \rangle = \langle z, s \rangle + c\langle z, z \rangle = 0 ,
\]
so $z$ is orthogonal to all of $T$. As $T$ is nondegenerate, Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear} forces $z = 0$, a contradiction.

We may therefore complete the inductive step by putting
\[
b_{k+1} = \frac{z}{\sqrt{|\langle z, z \rangle|}} ,
\]
dividing by $\sqrt{|\langle z,z\rangle|}$ rather than by $|z|$, which need not be a real number in the indefinite case. Then $\langle b_{k+1}, b_{k+1} \rangle = \langle z,z\rangle/|\langle z,z\rangle| = \pm 1$, and $b_{k+1}$ is still orthogonal to $b_1,\ldots,b_k$, so $(b_1,\ldots,b_{k+1})$ is orthonormal. Rescaling by the nonzero scalar $|\langle z,z\rangle|^{-1/2}$ does not change the span, so
\[
\operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_{k+1}) = S + \langle z \rangle = T = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_{k+1}),
\]
which completes the induction.

Taking $k = n$: the tuple $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ is orthonormal, hence linearly independent, and it spans $\operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_n) = V$. So it is an orthonormal basis with the required property.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:symmetric-bilinear-orthogonal-basis}
\lean{LinearMap.BilinForm.exists_orthogonal_basis}
\mathlibok
Let $q$ be a symmetric bilinear form on a finite-dimensional vector space $V$ over a field in which $2$ is invertible. Then $V$ has a basis $(b_i)$ that is $q$-orthogonal, that is, $q(b_i, b_j) = 0$ whenever $i \neq j$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:scalar-product-orthonormal-basis}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_basis_isOrthonormal}
\leanok
\uses{prop:symmetric-bilinear-orthogonal-basis}
Every scalar product space $(V,q)$ has an orthonormal basis.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:symmetric-bilinear-orthogonal-basis,lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Since $q$ is symmetric and $\dim V < \infty$, Proposition \ref{prop:symmetric-bilinear-orthogonal-basis} provides a $q$-orthogonal basis $(b_i)$ of $V$. No $b_i$ has vanishing norm: if $\langle b_i, b_i \rangle = 0$, then, $b_i$ being orthogonal to every $b_j$ with $j \neq i$ as well, $b_i$ would be orthogonal to a basis of $V$ and hence to all of $V$, contradicting nondegeneracy by Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}. Set
\[
e_i = \frac{b_i}{\sqrt{|\langle b_i, b_i \rangle|}}.
\]
This is legitimate because $|\langle b_i, b_i \rangle| > 0$; note that one divides by $\sqrt{|\langle b_i,b_i\rangle|}$ rather than by $|b_i|$, which need not be a real number in the indefinite case. Rescaling each vector of a basis by a nonzero scalar again yields a basis and preserves orthogonality, and
\[
\langle e_i, e_i \rangle = \frac{\langle b_i, b_i \rangle}{|\langle b_i, b_i \rangle|} = \pm 1 .
\]
Hence $(e_i)$ is an orthonormal basis.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}
\label{cor:scalar-product-signature-form}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_basis_isOrthonormal_signature}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.64}
\uses{prop:scalar-product-orthonormal-basis}
Suppose $(V, q)$ is an $n$-dimensional scalar product space. There is a basis $(\beta^i)$ for $V^*$ with respect to which $q$ has the expression
\[
q = (\beta^1)^2 + \cdots + (\beta^r)^2 - (\beta^{r+1})^2 - \cdots - (\beta^{r+s})^2,
\]
for some nonnegative integers $r, s$ with $r + s = n$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:scalar-product-orthonormal-basis}
Let $(b_i)$ be an orthonormal basis for $V$, which exists by Proposition \ref{prop:scalar-product-orthonormal-basis}, and let $(\beta^i)$ be the dual basis for $V^*$. For $v \in V$ write $v = \sum_i \beta^i(v) \, b_i$; bilinearity and orthogonality give
\[
q(v,v) = \sum_{i,j} \beta^i(v)\beta^j(v) \langle b_i, b_j\rangle = \sum_i \langle b_i, b_i \rangle \, (\beta^i(v))^2 ,
\]
so $q = \sum_i \langle b_i, b_i\rangle (\beta^i)^2$ with each coefficient $\langle b_i, b_i \rangle = \pm 1$. This is a basis expression of the required form, but perhaps with the positive and negative terms in a different order. Let $r$ be the number of indices with $\langle b_i, b_i \rangle = 1$ and $s$ the number with $\langle b_i, b_i \rangle = -1$; then $r + s = n$, since every index is of exactly one of the two kinds. Permuting the index set so that the $r$ indices of the first kind come first leaves the basis orthonormal, since orthonormality constrains only the pairwise values $\langle b_i, b_j \rangle$, and yields the stated expression.
\end{proof}

It turns out that the numbers $r$ and $s$ in (2.23) are independent of the choice of basis. The key to proving this is the following classical result from linear algebra.

\begin{proposition}[Sylvester's Law of Inertia]
\label{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsOrthonormal.isGreatest_ncard}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.65}
\uses{cor:scalar-product-signature-form}
Suppose $(V, q)$ is a finite-dimensional scalar product space. If $q$ has the representation (2.23) in some basis, then the number $r$ is the maximum dimension among all subspaces on which the restriction of $q$ is positive definite, and thus $r$ and $s$ are independent of the choice of basis.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{cor:scalar-product-signature-form}
Let $(b_i)$ be the orthonormal basis dual to $(\beta^i)$, so that $\langle b_i, b_i\rangle = 1$ for $i \leq r$ and $\langle b_i, b_i \rangle = -1$ for $i > r$, and $q(v,v) = \sum_i \langle b_i,b_i\rangle (\beta^i(v))^2$.

First, $r$ is attained. On $P = \operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_r)$ every $v \neq 0$ has $q(v,v) = \sum_{i \leq r} (\beta^i(v))^2 > 0$, since some coordinate $\beta^i(v)$ with $i \le r$ is nonzero. So $q|_P$ is positive definite and $\dim P = r$.

Second, $r$ is an upper bound. Let $N = \operatorname{span}(b_{r+1},\ldots,b_{r+s})$, so $\dim N = s$ and every $v \in N$ has $q(v,v) = -\sum_{i > r} (\beta^i(v))^2 \leq 0$. Let $W \subseteq V$ be any subspace on which $q$ is positive definite. Then $W \cap N = \{0\}$: a nonzero $v$ in the intersection would satisfy both $q(v,v) > 0$ and $q(v,v) \leq 0$. Hence $\dim W + \dim N \leq \dim V$, that is, $\dim W + s \leq r + s$, so $\dim W \leq r$.

Therefore $r$ is the maximum of $\dim W$ over all subspaces $W$ on which $q$ is positive definite. This characterization makes no reference to the basis, so $r$ is independent of the choice of basis; and then so is $s = n - r$.
\end{proof}

Proposition \ref{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia} characterizes $r$, and hence $s = n - r$, without reference to a basis. It is worth recording the relation $r + s = n$ of Corollary \ref{cor:scalar-product-signature-form} in those basis-free terms as well, since that is the form in which a signature can be manipulated arithmetically. The radical of a scalar product is the obstruction to doing so, and it vanishes.

\begin{lemma}[A Scalar Product Has Trivial Radical]
\label{lem:radical-scalar-product-trivial}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.radical_toQuadraticMap_eq_bot}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space. The radical of $q$, that is, the set of $v \in V$ with $q(v,v) = 0$ whose associated polar form $w \mapsto q(v,w) + q(w,v)$ also vanishes identically, is $\{0\}$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Let $v$ lie in the radical. Since $q$ is symmetric, the polar form at $v$ is $w \mapsto 2\langle v,w\rangle$, so its vanishing gives $2\langle v,w\rangle = 0$, hence $\langle v,w \rangle = 0$, for every $w \in V$. Thus $v$ is orthogonal to all of $V$, and Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear} forces $v = 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[$r + s = n$, without a basis]
\label{cor:scalar-product-signature-sum}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.sigPos_add_sigNeg_eq_finrank}
\leanok
\uses{lem:radical-scalar-product-trivial,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
Let $(V,q)$ be an $n$-dimensional scalar product space of signature $(r,s)$. Then $r + s = n$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:radical-scalar-product-trivial,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
For any quadratic form on a finite-dimensional space, the maximal dimension of a subspace on which it is positive definite, plus that on which it is negative definite, plus the dimension of its radical, is $\dim V$. By Proposition \ref{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia} the first two quantities are $r$ and $s$, and by Lemma \ref{lem:radical-scalar-product-trivial} the radical is trivial, so its dimension is $0$. Hence $r + s = n$.
\end{proof}

The integer $s$ in the expression (2.23) (the number of negative terms) is called the \textit{index of $q$}, and the ordered pair $(r, s)$ is called the \textit{signature of $q$}.

Now suppose $M$ is a smooth manifold. The generalization we are after replaces the inner product on each tangent space by a scalar product; that is, it drops positivity and keeps nondegeneracy. It costs nothing to state the underlying structure for an arbitrary smooth vector bundle, and there is a reason to do so: the orthonormalization results below are proved in that generality, and the normal bundle of Corollary \ref{cor:normal-bundle-pseudo-riemannian-submanifold} is not a tangent bundle.

\begin{definition}[Smooth Field of Scalar Products]
\label{def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
\lean{Bundle.ContMDiffPseudoMetric}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Let $\pi : V \to B$ be a smooth real vector bundle over a smooth manifold $B$. A \emph{smooth field of scalar products} on $V$ is a choice, for each $b \in B$, of a bilinear form $g_b$ on the fibre $V_b$ such that
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item $g_b$ is symmetric: $g_b(v,w) = g_b(w,v)$ for all $v,w \in V_b$;
\item $g_b$ is nondegenerate: for every nonzero $v \in V_b$ there is a $w \in V_b$ with $g_b(v,w) \neq 0$;
\item $b \mapsto g_b$ is a smooth section of the bundle of bilinear forms on $V$.
\end{enumerate}
Condition (b) is stated in the witness form (b) of Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}, which is the form a construction can discharge directly.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The Fibres are Scalar Product Spaces]
\label{lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
\lean{Bundle.ContMDiffPseudoMetric.bilin_nondegenerate}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-field-scalar-products,lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on a vector bundle $V \to B$ whose fibres are finite-dimensional. Then $(V_b, g_b)$ is a scalar product space for every $b \in B$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-field-scalar-products,lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
The form $g_b$ is a symmetric bilinear form on the finite-dimensional space $V_b$ by conditions (a) and (c) of Definition \ref{def:smooth-field-scalar-products}. Condition (b) of that definition is verbatim condition (b) of Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}, which is there shown equivalent to nondegeneracy of $g_b$. So $g_b$ is a nondegenerate symmetric bilinear form on a finite-dimensional vector space, which is what a scalar product is.
\end{proof}

The pairing of two smooth sections against such a field is a smooth function. This is the analytic workhorse of everything below, and the point to notice is that its proof appeals only to the smoothness of $b \mapsto g_b$: positivity plays no part, so Lemma \ref{lem:inner-product-vector-fields-smooth} carries over unchanged.

\begin{lemma}[Smoothness of the Pairing of Smooth Sections]
\label{lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth}
\lean{Bundle.ContMDiffPseudoMetric.contMDiffOn_pairing, Bundle.ContMDiffPseudoMetric.contMDiff_pairing}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on a vector bundle $V \to B$, let $N$ be a smooth manifold, and let $s \subseteq N$. Let $f : N \to B$ be smooth on $s$, and let $v, w$ be sections of $V$ along $f$ — that is, assignments $x \mapsto v_x \in V_{f(x)}$ — that are smooth on $s$. Then
\[
x \longmapsto g_{f(x)}(v_x, w_x)
\]
is a smooth real-valued function on $s$. In particular, taking $s = N$, the pairing of two smooth sections along a smooth map is smooth.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
Smoothness on $s$ is checked at each point of $s$, so fix $x_0 \in s$ and put $b_0 = f(x_0)$. Choose a local trivialization $\Phi$ of $V$ over an open set $U \ni b_0$, with fibrewise linear isomorphisms $\Phi_b : V_b \to F$ onto the model fibre. It induces a trivialization of the bundle of bilinear forms on $V$ over $U$, carrying a bilinear form $\beta$ on $V_b$ to the bilinear form
\[
\hat\beta = \beta\bigl(\Phi_b^{-1}\,\cdot\,, \Phi_b^{-1}\,\cdot\,\bigr)
\]
on $F$; this is again a linear isomorphism on each fibre. Since $f$ is continuous at $x_0$ there is a neighbourhood $W$ of $x_0$ in $N$ with $f(W) \subseteq U$, and on $W$
\[
g_{f(x)}(v_x,w_x) = \hat g_{f(x)}\bigl(\Phi_{f(x)}v_x,\ \Phi_{f(x)}w_x\bigr).
\]
Read in the trivialization, the three maps
\[
x \mapsto \hat g_{f(x)}, \qquad x \mapsto \Phi_{f(x)}v_x, \qquad x \mapsto \Phi_{f(x)}w_x
\]
are smooth on $W \cap s$ with values in the fixed spaces of bilinear forms on $F$, in $F$, and in $F$: the first because $b \mapsto g_b$ is a smooth section and $f$ is smooth on $s$, the second and third because $v$ and $w$ are smooth sections along $f$ on $s$. Finally, evaluation $(\beta, y, z) \mapsto \beta(y,z)$ is a continuous multilinear map, hence smooth. The displayed function is the composite of these, so it is smooth at $x_0$ within $s$. As $x_0 \in s$ was arbitrary, it is smooth on $s$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Pseudo-Riemannian Metric]
\label{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.PseudoRiemannianMetric}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.7}
\uses{def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
Let $M$ be a smooth manifold. A \emph{pseudo-Riemannian metric} on $M$ (called by some authors a \emph{semi-Riemannian metric}) is a smooth field of scalar products on the tangent bundle of $M$; that is, a smooth symmetric $2$-tensor field $g$ whose value $g_p$ at each $p \in M$ is nondegenerate on $T_pM$, so that $(T_pM, g_p)$ is a scalar product space at every point. A \emph{pseudo-Riemannian manifold} is a pair $(M,g)$ consisting of a smooth manifold together with a pseudo-Riemannian metric on it.
\end{definition}

Lee's definition asks in addition that $g$ have \emph{the same signature everywhere}. That requirement is carried separately here, by Definition \ref{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature} below, and a ``pseudo-Riemannian manifold of signature $(r,s)$'' is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold together with the assertion that its signature is constantly $(r,s)$. The two are kept apart because Propositions \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} and \ref{cor:pseudo-riemannian-embedding-criterion} each exhibit a metric first and then \emph{conclude} that its signature is a particular constant; that conclusion is only sayable if a metric can be spoken of before its signature is known to be constant.

\begin{proposition}[Every Riemannian Metric is Pseudo-Riemannian]
\label{prop:riemannian-is-pseudo-riemannian}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.RiemannianMetric.toPseudoRiemannianMetric}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.7}
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric}
Every Riemannian metric on a smooth manifold $M$ is a pseudo-Riemannian metric on $M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Let $g$ be a Riemannian metric. By Definition \ref{def:riemannian-metric} it is a smooth symmetric $2$-tensor field, so conditions (a) and (c) of Definition \ref{def:smooth-field-scalar-products} hold and only nondegeneracy at each point requires argument. Fix $p \in M$ and let $v \in T_pM$ be nonzero. Take $w = v$: positive definiteness of $g_p$ gives $g_p(v,v) > 0$, so in particular $g_p(v,w) \neq 0$. This is condition (b) of Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}, hence $g_p$ is nondegenerate.
\end{proof}

The witness $w = v$ used in that proof is exactly the step that fails for an indefinite scalar product: there a nonzero vector may be null, that is, orthogonal to itself, so $\langle v,v\rangle \neq 0$ does not follow from $v \neq 0$. Every difficulty in this section traces back to that one line.

\begin{definition}[Signature of a Pseudo-Riemannian Metric at a Point]
\label{def:pseudo-metric-signature-at}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.PseudoRiemannianMetric.sigPosAt, LeeLib.Ch02.PseudoRiemannianMetric.sigNegAt}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product,cor:scalar-product-signature-form,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
Let $(M,g)$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold and let $p \in M$. By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product} the pair $(T_pM, g_p)$ is a scalar product space, so by Corollary \ref{cor:scalar-product-signature-form} it has a signature $(r_p, s_p)$, which by Proposition \ref{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia} does not depend on the basis used to compute it. We call $(r_p,s_p)$ the \emph{signature of $g$ at $p$}, and $s_p$ the \emph{index of $g$ at $p$}. Equivalently, by Proposition \ref{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}, $r_p$ is the largest dimension of a subspace of $T_pM$ on which $g_p$ is positive definite, and $s_p$ the largest dimension of one on which $g_p$ is negative definite.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[The Signature at a Point Sums to the Dimension]
\label{prop:pseudo-metric-signature-sum}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.PseudoRiemannianMetric.sigPosAt_add_sigNegAt}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-signature-at,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product,cor:scalar-product-signature-sum}
Let $(M,g)$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold, let $p \in M$, and let $(r_p,s_p)$ be the signature of $g$ at $p$. Then $r_p + s_p = \dim T_pM$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product,cor:scalar-product-signature-sum}
