By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product} the pair $(T_pM, g_p)$ is a scalar product space, and $(r_p,s_p)$ is by Definition \ref{def:pseudo-metric-signature-at} its signature. Corollary \ref{cor:scalar-product-signature-sum} applied to it gives $r_p + s_p = \dim T_pM$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Constant Signature]
\label{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.HasSignature}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-signature-at}
Let $(M,g)$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold and let $r,s \geq 0$ be integers. We say that $g$ \emph{has signature $(r,s)$} if the signature of $g$ at $p$ is $(r,s)$ for every $p \in M$. A \emph{pseudo-Riemannian manifold of signature $(r,s)$} is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold $(M,g)$ whose metric has signature $(r,s)$.
\end{definition}

By Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-metric-signature-sum}, a metric of signature $(r,s)$ on a manifold of dimension $n$ satisfies $r + s = n$, so on a fixed manifold a signature has only one degree of freedom.

\subsection{The Gram--Schmidt Algorithm as an Explicit Recursion}

Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products} orthonormalizes a nondegenerate basis, but it does so as an \emph{existence} statement: its induction produces \emph{some} orthonormal basis, and asserts nothing about how that basis depends on the input. For the frames of Proposition \ref{prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian} that is not enough. There the input tuple varies with a point of a manifold and the output must be shown to vary \emph{smoothly} with it; smoothness is a property of a function, and there is no function to speak of until the orthonormalization is exhibited as a formula in its input. We therefore rerun the algorithm of Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products} as an explicit recursion and reprove its properties for that formula.

\begin{definition}[The Gram--Schmidt Recursion for a Bilinear Form]
\label{def:gram-schmidt-bilin}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.gramSchmidtBilin}
\leanok
Let $V$ be a real vector space, let $B$ be a bilinear form on $V$, and let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be an $n$-tuple in $V$. Define the $n$-tuple $u = \mathrm{GS}_B(v)$ by the recursion
\[
u_j = v_j - \sum_{i < j} \frac{B(u_i, v_j)}{B(u_i,u_i)} \, u_i .
\]
This is well founded, $u_j$ depending only on those $u_i$ with $i < j$. The formula is used only under the hypothesis of Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec}, which guarantees that no denominator $B(u_i,u_i)$ vanishes.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[The Normalized Gram--Schmidt Recursion]
\label{def:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.gramSchmidtBilinNormed}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin}
With $V$, $B$ and $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ as in Definition \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin} and $u = \mathrm{GS}_B(v)$, define the $n$-tuple $b = \widehat{\mathrm{GS}}_B(v)$ by
\[
b_j = \frac{u_j}{\sqrt{\,|B(u_j,u_j)|\,}} .
\]
As in Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products}, one divides by $\sqrt{|B(u_j,u_j)|}$ rather than by $|u_j|$, which need not be a real number in the indefinite case. The effect is that $B(b_j,b_j) = \pm 1$ rather than $= 1$.
\end{definition}

The next lemma is the computation at the heart of the algorithm, isolated from the induction that uses it.

\begin{lemma}[The New Vector is Orthogonal to its Predecessors]
\label{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthogonal-earlier}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.gramSchmidtBilin_apply_of_lt}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin}
Let $B$ be a bilinear form on a real vector space $V$, let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be an $n$-tuple in $V$, and write $u = \mathrm{GS}_B(v)$. Fix $k$ and suppose
\[
B(u_i,u_i) \neq 0 \quad\text{for every } i \leq k, \qquad B(u_i,u_{i'}) = 0 \quad\text{for all } i \neq i' \text{ with } i,i' \leq k .
\]
Then, writing $j = k+1$, we have $B(u_c, u_j) = 0$ for every $c < j$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin}
Let $c < j$, so that $c \leq k$. Expanding the recursion of Definition \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin} at $j$ and using linearity of $B$ in its second argument,
\[
B(u_c, u_j) = B(u_c, v_j) - \sum_{i < j} \frac{B(u_i,v_j)}{B(u_i,u_i)} \, B(u_c, u_i) .
\]
Every index $i < j$ satisfies $i \leq k$, as does $c$; so by the second hypothesis $B(u_c,u_i) = 0$ for every such $i$ other than $i = c$, and the sum collapses to its single term $i = c$. Hence
\[
B(u_c,u_j) = B(u_c,v_j) - \frac{B(u_c,v_j)}{B(u_c,u_c)}\, B(u_c,u_c) = B(u_c,v_j) - B(u_c,v_j) = 0 ,
\]
the cancellation being legitimate because $B(u_c,u_c) \neq 0$ by the first hypothesis.
\end{proof}

The nonvanishing of the denominators, the pairwise orthogonality of the $u_j$, and the reproduction of the flag are proved together, by a single induction. They cannot be separated: orthogonality of $u_j$ against its predecessors needs their denominators to be nonzero, the denominator $B(u_j,u_j) \neq 0$ needs $\operatorname{span}(u_1,\ldots,u_j) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$, and that identity needs $u_j \equiv v_j$ modulo the earlier span.

\begin{proposition}[The Gram--Schmidt Recursion: Simultaneous Induction]
\label{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.gramSchmidtBilin_spec}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthogonal-earlier,lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Let $V$ be a real vector space, let $B$ be a symmetric bilinear form on $V$, and let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be a nondegenerate $n$-tuple in $V$. Write $u = \mathrm{GS}_B(v)$. Then for every $k \leq n$ the following three statements hold:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item $B(u_i,u_i) \neq 0$ for every $i \leq k$;
\item $B(u_i,u_{i'}) = 0$ for all $i \neq i'$ with $i,i' \leq k$;
\item $\operatorname{span}(u_1,\ldots,u_j) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$ for every $j \leq k$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthogonal-earlier,lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Induct on $k$. For $k = 0$, statements (a) and (b) are vacuous, and (c) holds for the only admissible index $j = 0$, both spans being the span of the empty family, namely $\{0\}$.

Assume (a), (b), (c) hold for some $k$ with $k + 1 \leq n$, and put $j = k+1$ and $S = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$. By the inductive hypothesis (c), $S = \operatorname{span}(u_1,\ldots,u_k)$, and $\dim S = k$ because the tuple $(v_i)$ is nondegenerate.

\emph{The vector $u_j$ is orthogonal to $S$.} The inductive hypotheses (a) and (b) are exactly the hypotheses of Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthogonal-earlier}, which therefore gives $B(u_c,u_j) = 0$ for every $c < j$, that is, for every $c \leq k$. By symmetry of $B$ also $B(u_j,u_c) = 0$ for every $c \leq k$; since $B$ is linear in its second argument and the $u_c$ with $c \leq k$ span $S$, it follows that $B(u_j, y) = 0$ for every $y \in S$.

\emph{The vector $u_j$ agrees with $v_j$ modulo $S$.} Write $\sigma = \sum_{i<j} \frac{B(u_i,v_j)}{B(u_i,u_i)} u_i$, so that $u_j = v_j - \sigma$ by Definition \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin}. Each index $i < j$ satisfies $i \leq k$, so $\sigma$ is a linear combination of $u_1,\ldots,u_k$ and hence lies in $S$.

\emph{The vector $v_j$ is not in $S$.} If it were, then $\operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j) \subseteq S$; the reverse inclusion always holds, so $\operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j) = S$. But nondegeneracy of the tuple $(v_i)$ gives $\dim \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j) = j = k+1$, while $\dim S = k$, a contradiction.

\emph{Hence $u_j \neq 0$}: otherwise $v_j = \sigma \in S$, contrary to the previous paragraph.

\emph{The span identity.} Put $T = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$. Since $u_j = v_j - \sigma$ with $\sigma \in S \subseteq T$ and $v_j \in T$, we get $u_j \in T$, whence $S + \langle u_j\rangle \subseteq T$. Conversely $v_j = u_j + \sigma \in S + \langle u_j \rangle$ and $v_1,\ldots,v_k \in S$, so $T \subseteq S + \langle u_j\rangle$. Therefore
\[
T = S + \langle u_j \rangle .
\]

\emph{The denominator does not vanish:} $B(u_j,u_j) \neq 0$. This is the one point at which the indefinite case genuinely differs, $u_j \neq 0$ no longer sufficing. Suppose $B(u_j,u_j) = 0$. By the span identity every $w \in T$ can be written $w = y + c\,u_j$ with $y \in S$ and $c \in \mathbb{R}$, and then
\[
B(u_j, w) = B(u_j, y) + c\,B(u_j,u_j) = 0 ,
\]
the first term vanishing because $u_j$ is orthogonal to $S$. So $u_j$ is an element of $T$ orthogonal to all of $T$. As $T$ is nondegenerate, being a member of the flag of the nondegenerate tuple $(v_i)$, Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear} forces $u_j = 0$, contradicting the previous paragraph.

\emph{Assembling the three statements at $k+1$.} For (a), let $i \leq k+1$: if $i \leq k$ this is the inductive hypothesis, and if $i = j$ it is the previous paragraph. For (b), let $i \neq i'$ with $i,i' \leq k+1$; not both can equal $j$, so after using the symmetry of $B$ we may assume $i' \leq k$. If also $i \leq k$ this is the inductive hypothesis; if $i = j$ it is the first paragraph. For (c), let $m \leq k+1$: if $m \leq k$ this is the inductive hypothesis, and if $m = k+1$ then, by the inductive hypothesis (c) and the span identity,
\[
\operatorname{span}(u_1,\ldots,u_{k+1}) = \operatorname{span}(u_1,\ldots,u_k) + \langle u_j\rangle = S + \langle u_j \rangle = T = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_{k+1}) . \qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

Taking $k = n$ in Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec} gives the three statements in the form in which they are used.

\begin{lemma}[The Denominators Never Vanish]
\label{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.gramSchmidtBilin_apply_self_ne_zero}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec}
Let $B$ be a symmetric bilinear form on a real vector space $V$, let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be a nondegenerate $n$-tuple in $V$, and let $u = \mathrm{GS}_B(v)$. Then $B(u_j,u_j) \neq 0$ for every $j$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec}
This is part (a) of Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec} with $k = n$.
\end{proof}

This statement has no positive definite counterpart: for an inner product, $B(u_j,u_j) \neq 0$ follows from $u_j \neq 0$ alone, and $u_j \neq 0$ follows from linear independence. It is the reason nondegeneracy of the whole flag, and not mere linear independence, is the right hypothesis for an indefinite Gram--Schmidt.

\begin{lemma}[The Unnormalized Vectors are Orthogonal]
\label{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthogonal}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.gramSchmidtBilin_apply_ne}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec}
With $B$, $(v_i)$ and $u$ as in Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing}, $B(u_i,u_j) = 0$ whenever $i \neq j$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec}
This is part (b) of Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec} with $k = n$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Unnormalized Vectors Reproduce the Flag]
\label{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-flag}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.prefixSpan_gramSchmidtBilin}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec}
With $B$, $(v_i)$ and $u$ as in Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing}, $\operatorname{span}(u_1,\ldots,u_j) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$ for every $j \leq n$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec}
This is part (c) of Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-spec} with $k = n$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Normalizing Scalar is Nonzero]
\label{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normalizer-ne-zero}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.gramSchmidtBilin_normalizer_ne_zero}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing,def:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed}
With $B$, $(v_i)$ and $u$ as in Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing}, the scalar $\bigl(\sqrt{|B(u_j,u_j)|}\bigr)^{-1}$ is nonzero for every $j$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing}
By Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing}, $B(u_j,u_j) \neq 0$, so $|B(u_j,u_j)| > 0$ and therefore $\sqrt{|B(u_j,u_j)|} > 0$. The inverse of a nonzero real number is nonzero.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[The Recursion Produces an Orthonormal Tuple]
\label{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthonormal}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.gramSchmidtBilin_isOrthonormal}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthogonal}
Let $B$ be a symmetric bilinear form on a real vector space $V$, let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be a nondegenerate $n$-tuple in $V$, and let $b = \widehat{\mathrm{GS}}_B(v)$. Then $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ is orthonormal: $B(b_i,b_j) = 0$ for $i \neq j$, and $B(b_j,b_j) = \pm 1$ for every $j$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthogonal}
Write $u = \mathrm{GS}_B(v)$ and $\lambda_j = \bigl(\sqrt{|B(u_j,u_j)|}\bigr)^{-1}$, so that $b_j = \lambda_j u_j$ by Definition \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed}. By Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing} we have $B(u_j,u_j) \neq 0$, hence $|B(u_j,u_j)| > 0$ and $\lambda_j^2 = |B(u_j,u_j)|^{-1}$.

For $i \neq j$, bilinearity and Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthogonal} give
\[
B(b_i,b_j) = \lambda_i \lambda_j \, B(u_i,u_j) = 0 .
\]
For each $j$, bilinearity gives
\[
B(b_j,b_j) = \lambda_j^2 \, B(u_j,u_j) = \frac{B(u_j,u_j)}{|B(u_j,u_j)|} = \pm 1 ,
\]
the sign being $+1$ if $B(u_j,u_j) > 0$ and $-1$ if $B(u_j,u_j) < 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Normalized Tuple Reproduces the Flag]
\label{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed-flag}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.prefixSpan_gramSchmidtBilinNormed}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-flag,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normalizer-ne-zero}
With $B$, $(v_i)$ and $b = \widehat{\mathrm{GS}}_B(v)$ as in Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthonormal}, $\operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_j) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$ for every $j \leq n$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-flag,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normalizer-ne-zero}
By Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-flag} it suffices to show $\operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_j) = \operatorname{span}(u_1,\ldots,u_j)$, where $u = \mathrm{GS}_B(v)$. Each $b_i = \lambda_i u_i$ with $\lambda_i \neq 0$ by Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normalizer-ne-zero}, so $b_i \in \operatorname{span}(u_1,\ldots,u_j)$ for $i \leq j$, giving one inclusion; and $u_i = \lambda_i^{-1} b_i \in \operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_j)$ for $i \leq j$, giving the other. Rescaling the members of a family by nonzero scalars therefore leaves every span unchanged.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products}, Recovered from the Recursion]
\label{cor:gram-schmidt-bilin-recovers}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isOrthonormal_prefixSpan_eq_of_gramSchmidt}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthonormal,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed-flag}
Let $B$ be a symmetric bilinear form on a real vector space $V$ and let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be a nondegenerate $n$-tuple in $V$. Then there is an orthonormal $n$-tuple $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ in $V$ with $\operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_j) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$ for every $j \leq n$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthonormal,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed-flag}
Take $b = \widehat{\mathrm{GS}}_B(v)$. It is orthonormal by Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthonormal} and reproduces the flag by Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed-flag}.
\end{proof}

This confirms that the recursion computes what the existence proof of Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products} asserts; the point of the recursion is that, unlike that proof, it has an output one can differentiate.

\subsection{Smoothness of the Fibrewise Gram--Schmidt Algorithm}

Two ingredients are needed to run the recursion of Definition \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin} fibrewise and conclude that the output is smooth. The first is Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth}, which makes the pairings appearing in the recursion smooth functions of the base point. The second concerns normalization: the Riemannian proof of Proposition \ref{prop:existence-orthonormal-frames} divides by $\sqrt{\langle u_j,u_j\rangle}$, whose argument is positive, whereas here one divides by $\sqrt{|B(u_j,u_j)|}$, and $|\cdot|$ is not differentiable at $0$. It does not need to be.

\begin{lemma}[$(\sqrt{|\cdot|})^{-1}$ is Smooth Away from the Origin]
\label{lem:inv-sqrt-abs-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contDiffAt_inv_sqrt_abs}
\leanok
Let $t \in \mathbb{R}$ with $t \neq 0$. Then $s \mapsto \bigl(\sqrt{|s|}\bigr)^{-1}$ is smooth at $t$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
First, $s \mapsto |s|$ is smooth at $t$. If $t < 0$ then $|s| = -s$ for every $s$ in the neighbourhood $(-\infty,0)$ of $t$; if $t > 0$ then $|s| = s$ for every $s$ in the neighbourhood $(0,\infty)$ of $t$. In either case $|\cdot|$ agrees near $t$ with a linear function, and smoothness at a point depends only on the germ there.

Next, $|t| > 0$ and $r \mapsto \sqrt{r}$ is smooth on $(0,\infty)$, hence smooth at $|t|$; so the composite $s \mapsto \sqrt{|s|}$ is smooth at $t$.

Finally $\sqrt{|t|} > 0$, in particular $\sqrt{|t|} \neq 0$, and $r \mapsto r^{-1}$ is smooth at every nonzero real number. Composing once more, $s \mapsto (\sqrt{|s|})^{-1}$ is smooth at $t$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Pointwise Nondegenerate Smooth Family]
\label{def:local-nondegenerate-family}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsLocalNondegenerateOn}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-field-scalar-products,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on a vector bundle $V \to B$ with finite-dimensional fibres, and let $u \subseteq B$. A family $(X_1,\ldots,X_m)$ of sections of $V$ is \emph{nondegenerate on $u$} if
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item each $X_i$ is smooth on $u$; and
\item for every $x \in u$ the tuple $(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_m|_x)$ is a nondegenerate $m$-tuple in the scalar product space $(V_x, g_x)$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{definition}

Condition (b) is strictly stronger than pointwise linear independence: it asks each $\operatorname{span}(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_k|_x)$ to be a nondegenerate subspace of $V_x$, not merely of dimension $k$. This is the indefinite replacement for the hypothesis ``$(X_i)$ is a local frame'' of Proposition \ref{prop:existence-orthonormal-frames}. The strengthening is forced: a linearly independent tuple of null vectors is impossible when $g_x$ is an inner product but routine for an indefinite scalar product, and on such a tuple the recursion of Definition \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin} divides by zero.

\begin{definition}[The Fibrewise Gram--Schmidt Frame]
\label{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoGramSchmidtFrameAux, LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoGramSchmidtFrame}
\leanok
\uses{def:gram-schmidt-bilin,def:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed,def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on a vector bundle $V \to B$ and let $(X_1,\ldots,X_m)$ be a family of sections of $V$. Applying Definitions \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin} and \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed} in each fibre against the form $g_x$, define families $(U_1,\ldots,U_m)$ and $(E_1,\ldots,E_m)$ of sections of $V$ by
\[
U_j|_x = \mathrm{GS}_{g_x}\bigl(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_m|_x\bigr)_j , \qquad
E_j|_x = \widehat{\mathrm{GS}}_{g_x}\bigl(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_m|_x\bigr)_j = \frac{U_j|_x}{\sqrt{\,|g_x(U_j|_x, U_j|_x)|\,}} .
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[Nonvanishing of the Fibrewise Denominators]
\label{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoGramSchmidtFrameAux_apply_self_ne_zero}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame,def:local-nondegenerate-family,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on $V \to B$ with finite-dimensional fibres, and let $(X_1,\ldots,X_m)$ be nondegenerate on $u \subseteq B$. Then $g_x(U_j|_x, U_j|_x) \neq 0$ for every $x \in u$ and every $j$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
Fix $x \in u$. By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product} the form $g_x$ is a symmetric bilinear form on $V_x$, and by condition (b) of Definition \ref{def:local-nondegenerate-family} the tuple $(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_m|_x)$ is nondegenerate. Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-nonvanishing}, applied in $V_x$ to $B = g_x$ and this tuple, gives the claim.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Orthonormality of the Gram--Schmidt Frame]
\label{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-orthonormal}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoGramSchmidtFrame_isOrthonormal}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame,def:local-nondegenerate-family,prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthonormal,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
With $g$ and $(X_i)$ as in Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}, the tuple $(E_1|_x,\ldots,E_m|_x)$ is orthonormal in $(V_x, g_x)$ for every $x \in u$; that is, $g_x(E_i|_x, E_j|_x) = \pm\delta_{ij}$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthonormal,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
Fix $x \in u$. As in the proof of Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}, $g_x$ is a symmetric bilinear form on $V_x$ and $(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_m|_x)$ is a nondegenerate tuple. By Definition \ref{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame}, $(E_1|_x,\ldots,E_m|_x)$ is exactly the tuple $\widehat{\mathrm{GS}}_{g_x}(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_m|_x)$, which Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-bilin-orthonormal} shows to be orthonormal.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Gram--Schmidt Frame Reproduces the Flag]
\label{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-flag}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.prefixSpan_pseudoGramSchmidtFrame}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame,def:local-nondegenerate-family,lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed-flag,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
With $g$ and $(X_i)$ as in Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}, for every $x \in u$ and every $k \leq m$,
\[
\operatorname{span}\bigl(E_1|_x,\ldots,E_k|_x\bigr) = \operatorname{span}\bigl(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_k|_x\bigr) .
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed-flag,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
Fix $x \in u$. As above, $g_x$ is a symmetric bilinear form on $V_x$ and $(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_m|_x)$ is a nondegenerate tuple, and $(E_j|_x)_j$ is $\widehat{\mathrm{GS}}_{g_x}$ of it. Lemma \ref{lem:gram-schmidt-bilin-normed-flag} applied in $V_x$ gives the claim.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Smoothness of the Unnormalized Gram--Schmidt Sections]
\label{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-aux-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffOn_pseudoGramSchmidtFrameAux}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame,def:local-nondegenerate-family,lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth,lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}
With $g$ and $(X_i)$ as in Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}, each section $U_j$ is smooth on $u$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame,lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth,lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}
By strong induction on $j$: assume $U_i$ is smooth on $u$ for every $i < j$. By Definition \ref{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame} and Definition \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin},
\[
U_j|_x = X_j|_x - \sum_{i<j} \frac{g_x(U_i|_x, X_j|_x)}{g_x(U_i|_x, U_i|_x)} \, U_i|_x .
\]
Fix $i < j$. The functions
\[
x \mapsto g_x(U_i|_x, X_j|_x) \qquad\text{and}\qquad x \mapsto g_x(U_i|_x, U_i|_x)
\]
are smooth on $u$ by Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth}, applied with $f$ the identity of $B$: its hypotheses hold because $U_i$ is smooth on $u$ by the inductive hypothesis and $X_j$ is smooth on $u$ by condition (a) of Definition \ref{def:local-nondegenerate-family}. By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators} the second of them is nowhere zero on $u$, so the quotient
\[
c_i : x \mapsto \frac{g_x(U_i|_x, X_j|_x)}{g_x(U_i|_x, U_i|_x)}
\]
is smooth on $u$, a quotient of smooth real-valued functions with nonvanishing denominator.

Therefore each $c_i \cdot U_i$ is a smooth section on $u$, being a smooth scalar multiple of a smooth section; the finite sum $\sum_{i<j} c_i U_i$ is a smooth section on $u$; and $U_j = X_j - \sum_{i<j} c_i U_i$ is smooth on $u$, being a difference of smooth sections.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Smoothness of the Gram--Schmidt Frame]
\label{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffOn_pseudoGramSchmidtFrame}
\leanok
\uses{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-aux-smooth,lem:inv-sqrt-abs-smooth,lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth,lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators,def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame}
With $g$ and $(X_i)$ as in Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}, each section $E_j$ is smooth on $u$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-aux-smooth,lem:inv-sqrt-abs-smooth,lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth,lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}
By Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-aux-smooth} the section $U_j$ is smooth on $u$, so by Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth} the real-valued function
\[
q : x \mapsto g_x(U_j|_x, U_j|_x)
\]
is smooth on $u$, and by Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators} it is nowhere zero there. Lemma \ref{lem:inv-sqrt-abs-smooth} shows that $s \mapsto (\sqrt{|s|})^{-1}$ is smooth at every nonzero real number, hence at $q(x)$ for every $x \in u$. Therefore the composite
\[
x \mapsto \bigl(\sqrt{|q(x)|}\bigr)^{-1}
\]
is smooth on $u$. By Definition \ref{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame}, $E_j$ is the product of this smooth real-valued function with the smooth section $U_j$, hence is a smooth section on $u$.
\end{proof}

Note where positivity was and was not missed. The pairings are smooth without it, by Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth}; the quotients are smooth because their denominators are \emph{nonvanishing}, which is Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-denominators}, and not because they are positive. Only the normalization notices the difference, and Lemma \ref{lem:inv-sqrt-abs-smooth} absorbs it.

\subsection{Orthonormal Frames}

The results above orthonormalize a family that is \emph{given} to be nondegenerate at every point of $u$. Proposition \ref{prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian} additionally requires such a family to exist near each point, which the Riemannian case obtains for free — any local frame will do there — but which here is a genuine step. It rests on three lemmas.

\begin{lemma}[Nondegeneracy of a Tuple via its Gram Matrix]
\label{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-gram-matrix}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isNondegenerate_span_iff_det_gramMatrix_ne_zero}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space and let $(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$ be a $k$-tuple in $V$. Then $\operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$ is a nondegenerate subspace of dimension $k$ if and only if the Gram matrix $G = \bigl(\langle v_i, v_j\rangle\bigr)_{i,j=1}^{k}$ is invertible.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Suppose $G$ is invertible, and write $S = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$. If $\sum_i a_i v_i = 0$ then pairing with $v_j$ gives $\sum_i a_i \langle v_j,v_i\rangle = 0$ for each $j$, that is, $Ga = 0$, whence $a = 0$; so the $v_i$ are linearly independent and $\dim S = k$. If $y = \sum_i a_i v_i \in S$ is orthogonal to all of $S$, then in particular $\langle v_j, y\rangle = 0$ for each $j$, which is again $Ga = 0$, so $a = 0$ and $y = 0$. By Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear} the restriction $q|_{S\times S}$ is therefore nondegenerate.

Conversely, suppose $S$ is nondegenerate of dimension $k$. Then $(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$ is a basis of $S$, and $G$ is precisely the matrix of the map $\widetilde{q|_S} : S \to S^*$ with respect to that basis and its dual, since $\widetilde{q|_S}(v_i)(v_j) = \langle v_i,v_j\rangle$. Nondegeneracy of $q|_{S \times S}$ says that this map is an isomorphism, so by Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear} its matrix $G$ is invertible.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Nondegeneracy of a Tuple via its Leading Principal Minors]
\label{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-leading-minors}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isNondegenerateTuple_iff_forall_det_gramMatrix_ne_zero}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-gram-matrix}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space and let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be an $n$-tuple in $V$. For $j \leq n$ write $G_j = \bigl(\langle v_i, v_l\rangle\bigr)_{i,l=1}^{j}$ for the Gram matrix of the prefix $(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$. Then $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ is a nondegenerate tuple if and only if $\det G_j \neq 0$ for every $j \leq n$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-gram-matrix}
By definition $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ is a nondegenerate tuple exactly when, for every $j \leq n$, the subspace $\operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$ is nondegenerate of dimension $j$. For each fixed $j$ that span is the span of the $j$-tuple $(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$, whose Gram matrix is $G_j$, so Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-gram-matrix} applied to that $j$-tuple says precisely that the condition at $j$ holds if and only if $G_j$ is invertible, that is, $\det G_j \neq 0$. Conjoining over $j \leq n$ gives the claim.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[A Nondegenerate Tuple is Linearly Independent]
\label{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-independent}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsNondegenerateTuple.linearIndependent}
\leanok
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space and let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be a nondegenerate $n$-tuple in $V$. Then $v_1,\ldots,v_n$ are linearly independent.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
By definition the last step of the flag, $\operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$, has dimension $n$. A spanning set of $n$ vectors for an $n$-dimensional space is a basis, hence linearly independent.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[A Nondegenerate Tuple of Full Length is a Basis]
\label{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-basis}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsNondegenerateTuple.basis}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-independent}
Let $(V,q)$ be an $n$-dimensional scalar product space and let $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ be a nondegenerate $n$-tuple in $V$. Then $(v_1,\ldots,v_n)$ is a basis of $V$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-independent}
By Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-independent} the tuple is linearly independent, and it consists of $n = \dim V$ vectors; a linearly independent family of $\dim V$ vectors spans, hence is a basis.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[An Orthonormal Tuple is Nondegenerate]
\label{lem:orthonormal-tuple-nondegenerate}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsOrthonormal.isNondegenerateTuple}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-leading-minors}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space and let $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ be an orthonormal $n$-tuple in $V$. Then $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ is a nondegenerate tuple.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-leading-minors}
Fix $j \leq n$. The prefix $(b_1,\ldots,b_j)$ is again orthonormal, orthonormality being a condition on pairs of members. Its Gram matrix $G_j$ therefore satisfies $(G_j)_{il} = \langle b_i, b_l \rangle = 0$ for $i \neq l$, so $G_j$ is diagonal, with diagonal entries $\langle b_i,b_i\rangle = \pm 1$. Hence $\det G_j = \prod_{i=1}^{j} \langle b_i, b_i \rangle$ is a product of numbers $\pm 1$ and so is nonzero. As $j \leq n$ was arbitrary, Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-leading-minors} shows the tuple is nondegenerate.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Smoothness of the Leading Gram Determinants]
\label{lem:gram-det-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffOn_det_gramMatrix}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on a vector bundle $V \to B$, let $u \subseteq B$, and let $Y_1,\ldots,Y_k$ be sections of $V$ that are smooth on $u$. Then
\[
x \longmapsto \det \bigl(g_x(Y_i|_x, Y_l|_x)\bigr)_{i,l=1}^{k}
\]
is a smooth real-valued function on $u$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth}
By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-pairing-smooth} each entry $x \mapsto g_x(Y_i|_x, Y_l|_x)$ is smooth on $u$. The determinant of a $k \times k$ matrix is the finite sum, over the permutations $\sigma$ of $\{1,\ldots,k\}$, of $\operatorname{sgn}(\sigma)$ times the finite product of the entries $(\sigma(i), i)$; finite sums and finite products of smooth real-valued functions are smooth, so the composite is smooth on $u$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Nondegeneracy of a Tuple of Smooth Sections is an Open Condition]
\label{lem:open-nondegenerate-tuple}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isLocalNondegenerateOn_nhds}
\leanok
\uses{def:local-nondegenerate-family,lem:nondegenerate-tuple-leading-minors,lem:gram-det-smooth,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on a vector bundle $V \to B$ with finite-dimensional fibres, let $u \subseteq B$ be open, and let $X_1,\ldots,X_m$ be sections of $V$ that are smooth on $u$. If $(X_1|_{x_0},\ldots,X_m|_{x_0})$ is a nondegenerate $m$-tuple in $(V_{x_0}, g_{x_0})$ for some $x_0 \in u$, then there is an open set $w$ with $x_0 \in w \subseteq u$ such that $(X_1,\ldots,X_m)$ is nondegenerate on $w$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-leading-minors,lem:gram-det-smooth,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
For $k \leq m$ and $x \in u$ let $G_k(x) = \bigl(g_x(X_i|_x, X_j|_x)\bigr)_{i,j=1}^{k}$ be the $k$-th leading principal submatrix of the Gram matrix of the tuple at $x$. By Lemma \ref{lem:gram-det-smooth} the function $x \mapsto \det G_k(x)$ is smooth, in particular continuous, on $u$.

By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product} each $(V_x, g_x)$ is a scalar product space, so Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-leading-minors} applies in each fibre. Nondegeneracy of the tuple $(X_1|_{x_0},\ldots,X_m|_{x_0})$ therefore says exactly that $\det G_k(x_0) \neq 0$ for every $k \leq m$.

For each $k \leq m$ continuity therefore yields an open set $w_k$ with $x_0 \in w_k \subseteq u$ on which $\det G_k$ does not vanish. Put $w = \bigcap_{k \leq m} w_k$, an intersection of finitely many open sets, so open, and containing $x_0$. For $x \in w$ every $\det G_k(x)$ is nonzero, so by Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-leading-minors} again the tuple $(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_m|_x)$ is nondegenerate. Together with the smoothness of the $X_i$ on $w \subseteq u$, this is Definition \ref{def:local-nondegenerate-family}.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Smooth Sections Through a Prescribed Basis]
\label{lem:sections-through-prescribed-basis}
\lean{LeeLib.AppendixA.exists_contMDiffOn_section_eq_basis}
\leanok
Let $V \to B$ be a smooth vector bundle of rank $m$, let $x_0 \in B$, and let $(w_1,\ldots,w_m)$ be a basis of the fibre $V_{x_0}$. Then there are an open set $v \ni x_0$ and sections $Y_1,\ldots,Y_m$ of $V$, smooth on $v$, with $Y_j|_{x_0} = w_j$ for every $j$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Choose a local trivialization $\Phi$ of $V$ over an open set $v \ni x_0$, with fibrewise linear isomorphisms $\Phi_x : V_x \to F$, and set
\[
Y_j|_x = \Phi_x^{-1}\bigl(\Phi_{x_0}(w_j)\bigr), \qquad x \in v .
\]
Each $Y_j$ is smooth on $v$, being constant in the trivialization $\Phi$, and $Y_j|_{x_0} = \Phi_{x_0}^{-1}(\Phi_{x_0}(w_j)) = w_j$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Orthonormalization of a Nondegenerate Family of Vector Fields]
\label{prop:orthonormalize-nondegenerate-family}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_pseudo_orthonormalFrame}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:local-nondegenerate-family,def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame,prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-smooth,prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-orthonormal,lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-flag}
Let $(M,g)$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold, let $u \subseteq M$, and let $(X_1,\ldots,X_m)$ be a family of vector fields that is nondegenerate on $u$. Then there is a family $(Y_1,\ldots,Y_m)$ of vector fields, smooth on $u$, such that for every $x \in u$ the tuple $(Y_1|_x,\ldots,Y_m|_x)$ is orthonormal in $(T_xM,g_x)$ and
\[
\operatorname{span}(Y_1|_x,\ldots,Y_k|_x) = \operatorname{span}(X_1|_x,\ldots,X_k|_x) \qquad \text{for every } k \leq m .
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame,prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-smooth,prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-orthonormal,lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-flag}
Take $(Y_1,\ldots,Y_m)$ to be the fibrewise Gram--Schmidt frame of $(X_1,\ldots,X_m)$ of Definition \ref{def:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame}. Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-smooth} gives its smoothness on $u$, Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-orthonormal} its orthonormality at each point of $u$, and Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-flag} the equality of spans.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Orthonormal Frames for Pseudo-Riemannian Manifolds]
\label{prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_pseudo_orthonormalFrame_nhds}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.66}
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases,lem:sections-through-prescribed-basis,lem:open-nondegenerate-tuple,prop:orthonormalize-nondegenerate-family}
Let $(M,g)$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold. For each $p \in M$, there exists a smooth orthonormal frame on a neighborhood of $p$ in $M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases,lem:sections-through-prescribed-basis,lem:open-nondegenerate-tuple,prop:orthonormalize-nondegenerate-family,lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}
Write $n = \dim M$ and fix $p \in M$. By Definition \ref{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric} and Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-metric-fibre-scalar-product}, $(T_pM, g_p)$ is a scalar product space.

\emph{A nondegenerate basis at $p$.} The empty tuple is nondegenerate, so Lemma \ref{lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases}, applied with $k = 0$, produces a nondegenerate basis $(w_1,\ldots,w_n)$ of $T_pM$.

\emph{Extension to smooth vector fields.} By Lemma \ref{lem:sections-through-prescribed-basis} applied to the tangent bundle there are an open set $v \ni p$ and vector fields $Y_1,\ldots,Y_n$, smooth on $v$, with $Y_j|_p = w_j$.

\emph{Shrinking to a nondegenerate family.} The tuple $(Y_1|_p,\ldots,Y_n|_p) = (w_1,\ldots,w_n)$ is nondegenerate in $(T_pM,g_p)$, so Lemma \ref{lem:open-nondegenerate-tuple} yields an open set $v'$ with $p \in v' \subseteq v$ such that $(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n)$ is nondegenerate on $v'$ in the sense of Definition \ref{def:local-nondegenerate-family}.

\emph{Orthonormalization.} Proposition \ref{prop:orthonormalize-nondegenerate-family}, applied to $(Y_1,\ldots,Y_n)$ on $v'$, yields vector fields $(E_1,\ldots,E_n)$, smooth on $v'$, with $(E_1|_x,\ldots,E_n|_x)$ orthonormal in $(T_xM, g_x)$ for every $x \in v'$. An orthonormal tuple is linearly independent, and $n$ linearly independent vectors in the $n$-dimensional space $T_xM$ form a basis; so $(E_1,\ldots,E_n)$ is a smooth orthonormal frame on the neighborhood $v'$ of $p$.
\end{proof}

The argument splits into two halves of unequal cost. The orthonormalization half — Propositions \ref{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-smooth} and \ref{prop:pseudo-gram-schmidt-frame-orthonormal}, resting on the recursion of Definition \ref{def:gram-schmidt-bilin} and its properties — smoothly orthonormalizes any family that is nondegenerate on a set, and is the substance of the proposition. The other half produces such a family near a given point: Lemma \ref{lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases} supplies a nondegenerate basis of the single tangent space $T_pM$, Lemma \ref{lem:sections-through-prescribed-basis} spreads it to smooth vector fields near $p$, and Lemma \ref{lem:open-nondegenerate-tuple} shrinks the neighborhood until nondegeneracy holds at every point of it, not only at $p$. That last step is where the Riemannian case is free and this one is not: there, linear independence is both what Gram--Schmidt needs and what an arbitrary local frame already supplies, whereas here nondegeneracy of the whole flag must be propagated off the point $p$ by an open condition on the leading principal minors of the Gram matrix.

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:prove-orthonormal-frames}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.67}
\uses{prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian}
Prove the preceding proposition.
\end{exercise}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian}
This is the content of Proposition \ref{prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian}, proved above.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}
\label{exer:product-pseudo-riemannian}
\dcref{ch2:2.68}
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:pseudo-metric-has-signature,def:product-metric}

Suppose $(M_1, g_1)$ and $(M_2, g_2)$ are pseudo-Riemannian manifolds of signatures $(r_1, s_1)$ and $(r_2, s_2)$, respectively. Show that $(M_1 \times M_2, g_1 \oplus g_2)$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold of signature $(r_1 + r_2, s_1 + s_2)$.
\end{exercise}

For nonnegative integers $r$ and $s$, we define the \textbf{pseudo-Euclidean space of signature $(r,s)$}, denoted by $\mathbb{R}^{r,s}$, to be the manifold $\mathbb{R}^{r+s}$, with standard coordinates denoted by $(\xi^1, \ldots, \xi^r, \tau^1, \ldots, \tau^s)$, and with the pseudo-Riemannian metric $\bar{g}^{(r,s)}$ defined by
\begin{equation}
\bar{g}^{(r,s)} = (d\xi^1)^2 + \cdots + (d\xi^r)^2 - (d\tau^1)^2 - \cdots - (d\tau^s)^2.
\tag{2.24}
\end{equation}

By far the most important pseudo-Riemannian metrics (other than the Riemannian ones) are the Lorentz metrics.

\begin{definition}[Lorentz Metric]
\label{def:lorentz-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsLorentzMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature}
A \emph{Lorentz metric} is a pseudo-Riemannian metric of index $1$; that is, one having signature $(r,1)$ for some $r \geq 0$. A \emph{Lorentz manifold} is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold whose metric is a Lorentz metric.
\end{definition}

By Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-metric-signature-sum} the integer $r$ is determined by the manifold: a Lorentz metric on an $n$-manifold has signature $(n-1,1)$. (Some authors, especially in the physics literature, prefer to use signature $(1, r)$; either one can be converted to the other by multiplying the metric by $-1$, so there is no significant difference.)

The pseudo-Euclidean metric $\bar{g}^{(r,1)}$ is a Lorentz metric called the \textbf{Minkowski metric}, and the Lorentz manifold $\mathbb{R}^{r,1}$ is called $(r+1)$-\textbf{dimensional Minkowski space}. If we denote standard coordinates on $\mathbb{R}^{r,1}$ by $(\xi^1, \ldots, \xi^r, \tau)$, then the Minkowski metric is given by
\begin{equation}
\bar{g}^{(r,1)} = (d\xi^1)^2 + \cdots + (d\xi^r)^2 - (d\tau)^2.
\tag{2.25}
\end{equation}

In the special case of $\mathbb{R}^{3,1}$, the Minkowski metric is the fundamental invariant of Albert Einstein's \textit{special theory of relativity}, which can be expressed succinctly by saying that if gravity is ignored, then spacetime is accurately modeled by $(3+1)$-dimensional Minkowski space, and the laws of physics have the same form in every coordinate system in which the Minkowski metric has the expression (2.25). The differing physical characteristics of ``space'' (the $\xi^i$ directions) and ``time'' (the $\tau$ direction) arise from the fact that they are subspaces on which the metric is positive definite and negative definite, respectively. The \textit{general theory of relativity} includes gravitational effects by allowing the Lorentz metric to vary from point to point.

Many, but not all, results from the theory of Riemannian metrics apply equally well to pseudo-Riemannian metrics. Throughout this book, we will attempt to point out which results carry over directly to pseudo-Riemannian metrics, which ones can be adapted with minor modifications, and which ones do not carry over at all. As a rule of thumb, proofs that depend only on the nondegeneracy of the metric tensor, such as properties of the musical isomorphisms and existence and uniqueness of geodesics, work fine in the pseudo-Riemannian setting, while proofs that use positivity in an essential way, such as those involving lengths of curves, do not.

One notable result that does \emph{not} carry over to the pseudo-Riemannian case is Proposition~2.4, about the existence of metrics. For example, the following result characterizes those manifolds that admit Lorentz metrics.

The two halves of Theorem \ref{thm:lorentz-metrics} are of very different character, so they are recorded separately. Sufficiency is constructive: a rank-1 distribution and an auxiliary Riemannian metric produce a Lorentz metric by flipping the sign of the metric along the distribution. Necessity runs the other way: recovering a distribution from a Lorentz metric $\bar g$ means selecting, smoothly in $x$, the negative-eigenvalue eigenline of $\bar g_x$ relative to an auxiliary Riemannian metric.

That such a selection is smooth is usually quoted as a perturbation-theoretic fact about simple eigenvalues. It need not be. The eigen-equation $Sv = \lambda v$, together with a normalization $\langle v_0, v\rangle = 1$ pinning down the scale, is a system of $\dim V + 1$ equations in the $\dim V + 1$ unknowns $(v,\lambda)$, depending on the operator $S$ as a parameter; Definition \ref{def:augmented-eigen-equation} packages it as a single map $\Phi_{v_0}$ carrying $S$ along unchanged. Simplicity of the eigenvalue is exactly the invertibility of the derivative of that map (Lemma \ref{lem:eigen-linearisation-injective}), so the inverse function theorem alone yields the selection and its smoothness (Proposition \ref{prop:smooth-eigen-selection}). No spectral projection is constructed, and no operator square root, contour integral or functional calculus is needed.

The pointwise content of sufficiency is the following sign flip, which is Lee's own recipe in Problem \ref{prob:lorentz-metric-existence}(a) written invariantly: for a $g$-orthonormal frame with $E_1$ spanning $D$ and dual coframe $(\varepsilon^i)$, the form $-(\varepsilon^1)^2 + (\varepsilon^2)^2 + \cdots + (\varepsilon^n)^2$ equals $\langle v,w\rangle - 2\langle v,E_1\rangle\langle w,E_1\rangle$.

\begin{lemma}[Flipping the sign of a scalar product along a unit vector]
\label{lem:lorentz-form-pointwise}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.lorentzForm_isSymm_nondegenerate_signature}
\leanok
\uses{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia,cor:scalar-product-signature-sum}
Let $(V, \langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle)$ be an inner product space with $\dim V = n+1$, and let $u \in V$ be a unit vector. Then
\[
  B(v,w) = \langle v,w\rangle - 2\langle v,u\rangle\langle w,u\rangle
\]
is symmetric and nondegenerate, and has signature $(n,1)$. Moreover $B$ depends on $u$ only through $\operatorname{span}(u)$: replacing $u$ by $-u$ leaves $B$ unchanged.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia,cor:scalar-product-signature-sum}
Symmetry is immediate from symmetry of $\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle$, and the invariance under $u \mapsto -u$ holds because $u$ occurs exactly twice in the formula, so the two sign changes cancel.

For nondegeneracy, write $R w = w - 2\langle w,u\rangle u$, so that $B(v,w) = \langle v, Rw\rangle$. Since $\langle u,u\rangle = 1$, we get $\langle Rw, u\rangle = \langle w,u\rangle - 2\langle w,u\rangle = -\langle w,u\rangle$, whence
\[
  R(Rw) = Rw - 2\langle Rw,u\rangle u = w - 2\langle w,u\rangle u + 2\langle w,u\rangle u = w,
\]
so $R$ is an involution. If $B(v,w) = 0$ for all $w$, then taking $w = Rv$ gives $0 = \langle v, R(Rv)\rangle = \langle v,v\rangle$, so $v = 0$.

For the signature, extend $u$ to an orthonormal basis $(e_0, \ldots, e_n)$ of $V$ with $e_0 = u$. Then $\langle e_i, u\rangle = \delta_{i0}$, so
\[
  B(e_i, e_j) = \delta_{ij} - 2\delta_{i0}\delta_{j0},
\]
which is $-1$ when $i = j = 0$, is $1$ when $i = j \neq 0$, and vanishes when $i \neq j$. So $(e_i)$ is an orthonormal basis for $B$ in the indefinite sense, with exactly one diagonal entry equal to $-1$. By Sylvester's law of inertia (Proposition \ref{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}) the index of $B$ is therefore $1$, and since $B$ is nondegenerate its radical is trivial, so the number of positive terms is $\dim V - 1 = n$. Hence $B$ has signature $(n,1)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Rank-1 tangent distribution]
\label{def:rank-one-distribution}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsRankOneDistribution}
\leanok
A \emph{rank-1 tangent distribution} on a smooth manifold $M$ is a family $D = (D_x)_{x \in M}$ of subspaces $D_x \subseteq T_xM$ such that every point of $M$ has a neighbourhood $U$ carrying a smooth vector field $X$ with $X_x \neq 0$ and $D_x = \operatorname{span}(X_x)$ for every $x \in U$. Each $D_x$ is then a line, so $D$ is a field of lines in $TM$.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[A rank-1 distribution is a rank-1 subbundle]
\label{prop:rank-one-distribution-is-subbundle}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.hasLocalSubframes_of_isRankOneDistribution}
\leanok
\uses{def:rank-one-distribution,lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles,prop:existence-riemannian-metric}
Let $D$ be a rank-1 tangent distribution on a smooth manifold $M$. Then $D$ satisfies the hypothesis of the subbundle criterion (Lemma \ref{lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles}) with $k = 1$, and is therefore a smooth rank-1 subbundle of $TM$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:rank-one-distribution,lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles}
The criterion asks that every point have a neighbourhood on which $D$ is spanned by $k$ smooth sections of $TM$ that are linearly independent at each point. Take $k=1$ and let $U$ and $X$ be as in Definition \ref{def:rank-one-distribution}. A family consisting of a single vector is linearly independent exactly when that vector is nonzero, which holds on $U$; and the span of the range of the one-element family $(X)$ is $\operatorname{span}(X_x) = D_x$ for $x \in U$. So the hypothesis holds, and the criterion makes $D$ a smooth rank-1 vector bundle over $M$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Tensor product of two $1$-forms]
\label{def:tensor-product-one-forms}
\lean{Bundle.formProduct}
\leanok
For covectors $\alpha, \beta \in V^*$, the bilinear form $\alpha \otimes \beta$ on $V$ is $(\alpha \otimes \beta)(v,w) = \alpha(v)\,\beta(w)$. For $1$-forms $\alpha, \beta$ on $M$ this is applied fibrewise, giving a field of bilinear forms $x \mapsto \alpha_x \otimes \beta_x$.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The tensor product of two smooth $1$-forms is smooth]
\label{lem:tensor-product-one-forms-smooth}
\lean{Bundle.contMDiffAt_formProduct}
\leanok
\uses{def:tensor-product-one-forms}
Let $E \to M$ be a smooth vector bundle and let $\alpha, \beta$ be smooth sections of its dual bundle. Then $x \mapsto \alpha_x \otimes \beta_x$ is a smooth section of the bundle of bilinear forms on $E$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:tensor-product-one-forms}
Smoothness is local, so it may be checked in a local trivialization $\Lambda$ of $E$ over a neighbourhood of a given point. In that trivialization a bilinear form $\varphi_x$ on $E_x$ is represented by $(\xi,\eta) \mapsto \varphi_x(\Lambda_x^{-1}\xi, \Lambda_x^{-1}\eta)$ and a covector $\alpha_x$ by $\hat\alpha_x \colon \xi \mapsto \alpha_x(\Lambda_x^{-1}\xi)$. Since
\[
  (\alpha_x \otimes \beta_x)(\Lambda_x^{-1}\xi, \Lambda_x^{-1}\eta) = \alpha_x(\Lambda_x^{-1}\xi)\,\beta_x(\Lambda_x^{-1}\eta) = (\hat\alpha_x \otimes \hat\beta_x)(\xi,\eta),
\]
the representation of $\alpha \otimes \beta$ is the tensor product $\hat\alpha \otimes \hat\beta$ of the representations of $\alpha$ and $\beta$. By hypothesis $\hat\alpha$ and $\hat\beta$ are smooth maps into the fixed model dual space, and the tensor product $(a,b) \mapsto a \otimes b$ is a bounded bilinear map between fixed normed spaces, hence smooth; so $\hat\alpha \otimes \hat\beta$ is smooth, and therefore so is $\alpha \otimes \beta$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[The metric with its sign flipped along a vector field]
\label{def:sign-flipped-form}
\lean{Bundle.flipFormOf}
\leanok
\uses{def:tensor-product-one-forms}
Let $b$ be a field of bilinear forms on a vector bundle $E \to M$ and $u$ a section of $E$. Write $b(u,\cdot)$ for the $1$-form $v \mapsto b(u,v)$. The \emph{sign flip of $b$ along $u$} is the field of bilinear forms
\[
  \flip(b,u) = b - 2\, b(u,\cdot) \otimes b(u,\cdot), \qquad \flip(b,u)_x(v,w) = b_x(v,w) - 2\,b_x(u_x,v)\,b_x(u_x,w).
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The sign flip of a smooth form along a smooth section is smooth]
\label{lem:sign-flipped-form-smooth}
\lean{Bundle.contMDiffAt_flipFormOf}
\leanok
\uses{def:sign-flipped-form,lem:tensor-product-one-forms-smooth}
If $b$ is a smooth field of bilinear forms on $E \to M$ and $u$ is a smooth section of $E$, then $\flip(b,u)$ is a smooth field of bilinear forms.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:tensor-product-one-forms-smooth}
The $1$-form $b(u,\cdot)$ is smooth, being a smooth field of bilinear forms applied to a smooth section. Its tensor square is smooth by Lemma \ref{lem:tensor-product-one-forms-smooth}, and the smooth sections of a vector bundle are closed under subtraction and multiplication by constants, so $b - 2\,b(u,\cdot)\otimes b(u,\cdot)$ is smooth.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[A line contains exactly two unit vectors]
\label{lem:two-unit-vectors-of-a-line}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.eq_or_eq_neg_of_span_eq}
\leanok
Let $(V, \langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle)$ be an inner product space and let $u, u' \in V$ satisfy $\langle u,u\rangle = \langle u',u'\rangle = 1$ and $\operatorname{span}(u') = \operatorname{span}(u)$. Then $u' = u$ or $u' = -u$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Since $u' \in \operatorname{span}(u)$ we have $u' = cu$ for some $c \in \mathbb{R}$. Then $1 = \langle u',u'\rangle = c^2\langle u,u\rangle = c^2$, so $c = \pm 1$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Smooth local unit sections of a rank-1 distribution]
\label{lem:local-unit-section}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsRankOneDistribution.exists_localUnitSection}
\leanok
\uses{def:rank-one-distribution,def:riemannian-metric}
Let $(M,g)$ be a Riemannian manifold and $D$ a rank-1 tangent distribution on $M$. Every $p \in M$ has a neighbourhood $U$ carrying a smooth vector field $E$ with $\langle E_x, E_x\rangle_g = 1$ and $\operatorname{span}(E_x) = D_x$ for every $x \in U$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:rank-one-distribution}
Let $U$ and $X$ be as in Definition \ref{def:rank-one-distribution}, so $X$ is smooth and nowhere zero on $U$ and spans $D$ there. The function $x \mapsto \langle X_x, X_x\rangle_g$ is smooth on $U$, and strictly positive because $g$ is positive definite and $X$ is nowhere zero; hence $x \mapsto \langle X_x, X_x\rangle_g^{-1/2}$ is smooth on $U$. Therefore $E = X/|X|_g$ is smooth on $U$, and $\langle E,E\rangle_g = \langle X,X\rangle_g / |X|_g^2 = 1$. Finally $E_x$ is a nonzero scalar multiple of $X_x$, so $\operatorname{span}(E_x) = \operatorname{span}(X_x) = D_x$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Sufficiency in Theorem \ref{thm:lorentz-metrics}]
\label{prop:lorentz-metric-of-rank-one-distribution}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isLorentzMetric_of_isRankOneDistribution}
\leanok
\uses{def:lorentz-metric,def:rank-one-distribution,prop:existence-riemannian-metric,lem:lorentz-form-pointwise}
If a smooth manifold $M$ admits a rank-1 tangent distribution $D \subseteq TM$, then $M$ admits a Lorentz metric.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:lorentz-form-pointwise,prop:existence-riemannian-metric,def:sign-flipped-form,lem:sign-flipped-form-smooth,lem:two-unit-vectors-of-a-line,lem:local-unit-section}
Choose a Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$ (Proposition \ref{prop:existence-riemannian-metric}). For each $x \in M$ the fibre $D_x$ is a line in $T_xM$, so it contains exactly two $g$-unit vectors, $\pm u_x$ (Lemma \ref{lem:two-unit-vectors-of-a-line}); choose one of them for each $x$, with no continuity required of the choice. Define
\[
  \bar{g}_x(v,w) = g_x(v,w) - 2\,g_x(v,u_x)\,g_x(w,u_x),
\]
that is, $\bar g = \flip(g,u)$ in the notation of Definition \ref{def:sign-flipped-form}.

By Lemma \ref{lem:lorentz-form-pointwise} the value of $\bar g_x$ does not depend on which of the two unit vectors is called $u_x$, so $\bar{g}$ is independent of the choice; and each $\bar{g}_x$ is symmetric, nondegenerate, and of signature $(n,1)$, where $n+1 = \dim M$. Note $\dim M \geq 1$, since $T_xM$ contains the line $D_x$. In particular $\bar{g}$ has index $1$ at every point.

It remains to check that $\bar{g}$ is smooth, which is a local question. Near any $x_0$, Lemma \ref{lem:local-unit-section} provides a smooth $g$-unit section $E$ of $D$ on a neighbourhood $U$ of $x_0$. For every $x \in U$ both $E_x$ and $u_x$ are $g$-unit vectors spanning the line $D_x$, so $E_x = \pm u_x$ by Lemma \ref{lem:two-unit-vectors-of-a-line}, and hence $\bar g_x = \flip(g,E)_x$ by the sign-independence just established. Thus $\bar g$ agrees on $U$ with $\flip(g,E)$, which is smooth by Lemma \ref{lem:sign-flipped-form-smooth}; so $\bar g$ is smooth at $x_0$. Hence $\bar{g}$ is a Lorentz metric.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Simple eigenpair]
\label{def:simple-eigenpair}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsSimpleEigenpair}
\leanok
Let $(V,\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle)$ be a finite-dimensional real inner product space, $S : V \to V$ linear, $v \in V$ and $\lambda \in \mathbb{R}$. The triple $(S,v,\lambda)$ is a \emph{simple eigenpair} if $S$ is symmetric, $v \neq 0$, $Sv = \lambda v$, and every $w \in V$ with $Sw = \lambda w$ lies in $\operatorname{span}(v)$; that is, $\ker(S - \lambda\,\mathrm{id}) = \operatorname{span}(v)$.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Linearisation of the eigen-equation]
\label{def:eigen-linearisation}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.eigenLin}
\leanok
For $S : V \to V$ linear, $v, v_0 \in V$ and $\lambda \in \mathbb{R}$, let $L_{S,v,\lambda,v_0} : V \times \mathbb{R} \to V \times \mathbb{R}$ be the linear map
\[
  L_{S,v,\lambda,v_0}(w,\mu) = \bigl(Sw - \lambda w - \mu v,\ \langle v_0, w\rangle\bigr).
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[Simplicity makes the linearisation injective]
\label{lem:eigen-linearisation-injective}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.eigenLin_injective}
\leanok
\uses{def:simple-eigenpair,def:eigen-linearisation}
Let $(S,v,\lambda)$ be a simple eigenpair and let $v_0 \in V$ satisfy $\langle v_0, v\rangle = 1$. Then $L_{S,v,\lambda,v_0}$ is injective, hence bijective.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:simple-eigenpair,def:eigen-linearisation}
Suppose $L_{S,v,\lambda,v_0}(w,\mu) = 0$, that is $Sw - \lambda w = \mu v$ and $\langle v_0, w\rangle = 0$. Pair the first equation with $v$: since $S$ is symmetric and $Sv = \lambda v$,
\[
  \langle Sw - \lambda w, v\rangle = \langle w, Sv\rangle - \lambda\langle w, v\rangle = \lambda\langle w,v\rangle - \lambda\langle w,v\rangle = 0 ,
\]
while the right-hand side gives $\langle \mu v, v\rangle = \mu\langle v,v\rangle$. As $v \neq 0$ we have $\langle v,v\rangle \neq 0$, so $\mu = 0$. Then $Sw = \lambda w$, so simplicity puts $w = cv$ for some $c \in \mathbb{R}$, and $0 = \langle v_0, w\rangle = c\langle v_0, v\rangle = c$. Hence $w = 0$ and $(w,\mu) = 0$. Injectivity of a linear self-map of the finite-dimensional space $V \times \mathbb{R}$ gives bijectivity.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Augmented eigen-equation]
\label{def:augmented-eigen-equation}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.eigenAug}
\leanok
For $v_0 \in V$ let $\Phi_{v_0} : \operatorname{End}(V) \times V \times \mathbb{R} \to \operatorname{End}(V) \times V \times \mathbb{R}$ be
\[
  \Phi_{v_0}(S, v, \lambda) = \bigl(S,\ Sv - \lambda v,\ \langle v_0, v\rangle - 1\bigr).
\]
Thus $\Phi_{v_0}(S,v,\lambda) = (S,0,0)$ says exactly that $v$ is an eigenvector of $S$ with eigenvalue $\lambda$, normalized by $\langle v_0, v\rangle = 1$.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The augmented equation has an invertible derivative at a simple eigenpair]
\label{lem:augmented-eigen-equation-derivative}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.hasFDerivAt_eigenAug}
\leanok
\uses{def:augmented-eigen-equation,def:eigen-linearisation,def:simple-eigenpair,lem:eigen-linearisation-injective}
$\Phi_{v_0}$ is smooth, and its derivative at $(S,v,\lambda)$ is
\[
  D\Phi_{v_0}(S,v,\lambda)\,(T,w,\mu) = \bigl(T,\ Tv + Sw - \lambda w - \mu v,\ \langle v_0, w\rangle\bigr) .
\]
If $(S,v,\lambda)$ is a simple eigenpair and $\langle v_0, v\rangle = 1$, this derivative is a linear isomorphism.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:eigen-linearisation-injective}
Each component of $\Phi_{v_0}$ is a polynomial expression in $(S,v,\lambda)$ built from the bilinear evaluation map $(S,v) \mapsto Sv$, the scalar multiplication $(\lambda,v)\mapsto \lambda v$ and the linear functional $v \mapsto \langle v_0,v\rangle$, so $\Phi_{v_0}$ is smooth and the displayed formula is its derivative.

For invertibility, note the derivative is block lower triangular with respect to the splitting $\operatorname{End}(V) \oplus (V \times \mathbb{R})$: it is the identity on the first block, it is $L_{S,v,\lambda,v_0}$ on the second, and the remaining block is the map $T \mapsto (Tv, 0)$. A block triangular map with invertible diagonal blocks is invertible, and $L_{S,v,\lambda,v_0}$ is bijective by Lemma \ref{lem:eigen-linearisation-injective}.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Smooth selection of a simple eigenpair]
\label{prop:smooth-eigen-selection}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_eigenSelection}
\leanok
\uses{def:simple-eigenpair,def:augmented-eigen-equation,lem:augmented-eigen-equation-derivative}
Let $(S_0, v_0, \lambda_0)$ be a simple eigenpair with $\langle v_0, v_0\rangle = 1$. Then there are an open set $W \subseteq \operatorname{End}(V)$ containing $S_0$ and maps $\mathcal{V} : W \to V$, $\Lambda : W \to \mathbb{R}$ such that
\begin{enumerate}
  \item $\mathcal{V}(S_0) = v_0$ and $\Lambda(S_0) = \lambda_0$;
  \item $S\,\mathcal{V}(S) = \Lambda(S)\,\mathcal{V}(S)$ and $\langle v_0, \mathcal{V}(S)\rangle = 1$ for every $S \in W$;
  \item $\mathcal{V}$ and $\Lambda$ are continuous on $W$;
  \item $\mathcal{V}$ and $\Lambda$ are smooth at every $S \in W$ for which $(S, \mathcal{V}(S), \Lambda(S))$ is again a simple eigenpair.
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:augmented-eigen-equation-derivative,def:augmented-eigen-equation}
Since $S_0 v_0 = \lambda_0 v_0$ and $\langle v_0,v_0\rangle = 1$, we have $\Phi_{v_0}(S_0,v_0,\lambda_0) = (S_0,0,0)$. By Lemma \ref{lem:augmented-eigen-equation-derivative}, $\Phi_{v_0}$ is smooth with invertible derivative at $(S_0,v_0,\lambda_0)$, so by the inverse function theorem it restricts to a diffeomorphism $\Phi$ from an open neighbourhood $\mathcal{U}$ of $(S_0,v_0,\lambda_0)$ onto an open neighbourhood $\mathcal{W}$ of $(S_0,0,0)$.

Let $W = \{S : (S,0,0) \in \mathcal{W}\}$, an open set containing $S_0$ because $S \mapsto (S,0,0)$ is continuous, and define $(\mathcal{V}(S), \Lambda(S))$ to be the last two components of $\Phi^{-1}(S,0,0)$.

For (2), apply $\Phi_{v_0}$ to $\Phi^{-1}(S,0,0)$: the result is $(S,0,0)$, and reading off the three components of the definition of $\Phi_{v_0}$ gives that the first component of $\Phi^{-1}(S,0,0)$ is $S$ itself, that $S\,\mathcal{V}(S) - \Lambda(S)\,\mathcal{V}(S) = 0$, and that $\langle v_0, \mathcal{V}(S)\rangle - 1 = 0$. For (1), $\Phi^{-1}(S_0,0,0) = (S_0,v_0,\lambda_0)$ because $\Phi(S_0,v_0,\lambda_0) = (S_0,0,0)$ and $\Phi$ is injective on $\mathcal{U}$. Claim (3) is continuity of $\Phi^{-1}$ composed with the continuous map $S \mapsto (S,0,0)$.

For (4), fix $S \in W$ such that $(S,\mathcal{V}(S),\Lambda(S))$ is a simple eigenpair. By (2) we have $\langle v_0, \mathcal{V}(S)\rangle = 1$, so Lemma \ref{lem:augmented-eigen-equation-derivative} applies at the point $\Phi^{-1}(S,0,0) = (S, \mathcal{V}(S), \Lambda(S))$ and shows the derivative of $\Phi_{v_0}$ there is invertible. Since $\Phi_{v_0}$ is smooth, $\Phi^{-1}$ is therefore smooth at $(S,0,0)$; composing with $S \mapsto (S,0,0)$ and projecting gives smoothness of $\mathcal{V}$ and $\Lambda$ at $S$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The index counts the negative eigenvalues]
\label{lem:signature-and-eigenvalue-signs}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.sigNeg_eq_ncard_neg_eigenvalues}
\leanok
\uses{cor:scalar-product-signature-form,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
Let $(V,\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle)$ be a finite-dimensional real inner product space and let $B$ be a symmetric nondegenerate bilinear form on $V$, so that $(V,B)$ is a scalar product space of some signature $(r,s)$. Let $A : V \to V$ be a linear operator with $B(v,w) = \langle Av, w\rangle$ for all $v,w$; such an $A$ exists and is unique because $\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle$ is nondegenerate, and it is symmetric. Then the index $s$ is the number of negative eigenvalues of $A$, counted with multiplicity.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{cor:scalar-product-signature-form,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
Symmetry of $A$ is immediate: $\langle Av,w\rangle = B(v,w) = B(w,v) = \langle Aw,v\rangle = \langle v, Aw\rangle$. By the spectral theorem for symmetric operators there is a $\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle$-orthonormal basis $u_1,\dots,u_n$ of $V$ with $Au_i = \mu_i u_i$, the eigenvalues listed with multiplicity. Then
\[
  B(u_i,u_j) = \langle Au_i, u_j\rangle = \mu_i\langle u_i,u_j\rangle = \mu_i\delta_{ij},
\]
so $B$ is diagonal in this basis with the eigenvalues on the diagonal. Each $\mu_i \neq 0$: otherwise $B(u_i, w) = \langle \mu_i u_i, w\rangle = 0$ for every $w$, putting $u_i \neq 0$ in the radical of $B$ and contradicting nondegeneracy.

Set $e_i = |\mu_i|^{-1/2}u_i$, which is again a basis since the scalars are nonzero. Then $B(e_i,e_j) = |\mu_i|^{-1/2}|\mu_j|^{-1/2}\mu_i\delta_{ij}$, which vanishes for $i \neq j$ and equals $\mu_i/|\mu_i| = \pm 1$ for $i = j$. So $(e_i)$ is an orthonormal basis for $B$ in the indefinite sense of Corollary \ref{cor:scalar-product-signature-form}, and $B(e_i,e_i) = -1$ exactly at the indices with $\mu_i < 0$. By Sylvester's law of inertia (Proposition \ref{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}) the number of $-1$'s is the index $s$, so $s$ is the number of indices with $\mu_i < 0$, which is the number of negative eigenvalues counted with multiplicity.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Index one yields a simple eigenpair]
\label{lem:simple-eigenpair-of-index-one}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isSimpleEigenpair_of_sigNeg_eq_one}
\leanok
\uses{lem:signature-and-eigenvalue-signs,def:simple-eigenpair}
In the situation of Lemma \ref{lem:signature-and-eigenvalue-signs}, suppose the index of $B$ is $1$. Then $A$ has exactly one negative eigenvalue $\lambda$; the eigenspace $\ker(A - \lambda\,\mathrm{id})$ is a line, spanned by an eigenvector $u$; and $(A, u, \lambda)$ is a simple eigenpair.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:signature-and-eigenvalue-signs,def:simple-eigenpair}
Keep the eigenbasis $(u_i)$ and eigenvalues $(\mu_i)$ of the previous proof. By Lemma \ref{lem:signature-and-eigenvalue-signs} exactly one index $i_0$ has $\mu_{i_0} < 0$; write $\lambda = \mu_{i_0}$ and $u = u_{i_0}$, so $u \neq 0$ and $Au = \lambda u$.

For a symmetric operator the dimension of the $\lambda$-eigenspace equals the number of indices $i$ with $\mu_i = \lambda$. If $\mu_i = \lambda$ then $\mu_i < 0$, so $i = i_0$ by uniqueness of that index; hence there is exactly one such index and $\dim\ker(A - \lambda\,\mathrm{id}) = 1$. Now $\operatorname{span}(u) \subseteq \ker(A-\lambda\,\mathrm{id})$ because $Au = \lambda u$, and $\dim\operatorname{span}(u) = 1$ because $u \neq 0$; two subspaces of the same finite dimension with one contained in the other are equal, so $\operatorname{span}(u) = \ker(A - \lambda\,\mathrm{id})$. Thus every $w$ with $Aw = \lambda w$ lies in $\operatorname{span}(u)$, which together with symmetry of $A$ and $u \neq 0$ says exactly that $(A,u,\lambda)$ is a simple eigenpair.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Necessity in Theorem \ref{thm:lorentz-metrics}]
\label{prop:rank-one-distribution-of-lorentz-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isRankOneDistribution_of_isLorentzMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:lorentz-metric,def:rank-one-distribution,prop:existence-riemannian-metric,lem:simple-eigenpair-of-index-one,prop:smooth-eigen-selection,prop:existence-orthonormal-frames}
If a smooth manifold $M$ admits a Lorentz metric, then $M$ admits a rank-1 tangent distribution.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:existence-riemannian-metric,lem:simple-eigenpair-of-index-one,prop:smooth-eigen-selection,prop:existence-orthonormal-frames,def:rank-one-distribution}
Let $\bar{g}$ be a Lorentz metric and choose an auxiliary Riemannian metric $g$, which exists by Proposition \ref{prop:existence-riemannian-metric}. For each $x$ let $A_x : T_xM \to T_xM$ be the $g$-self-adjoint endomorphism determined by $\bar{g}_x(v,w) = g_x(A_xv, w)$. Define
\[
  D_x = \bigsqcup_{\mu < 0} \ker\bigl(A_x - \mu\,\mathrm{id}\bigr),
\]
the sum of all the negative eigenspaces of $A_x$. This mentions no eigenvalue of $A_x$, so $D$ is a well-defined field of subspaces depending on nothing but $g$.

We claim $D_x$ is a line, spanned by any nonzero $v$ with $A_xv = \mu v$ and $\mu < 0$. Since $\bar{g}$ is Lorentz, its index at every $x$ is $1$, so by Lemma \ref{lem:simple-eigenpair-of-index-one} the operator $A_x$ has exactly one negative eigenvalue $\lambda(x)$, and its eigenspace is the line spanned by the corresponding spectral eigenvector. Any negative eigenvalue of $A_x$ occurs in the spectrum of $A_x$, hence equals $\lambda(x)$; so every summand $\ker(A_x - \mu)$ with $\mu < 0$ is either $0$ or that same line, and $D_x$ is exactly it. In particular $(A_x, v, \lambda(x))$ is a simple eigenpair for every nonzero $v \in D_x$. It remains to produce smooth local spanning sections, which is a local question.

Fix $x_0 \in M$. By Proposition \ref{prop:existence-orthonormal-frames} there is a smooth $g$-orthonormal frame $E_1,\dots,E_n$ on a neighbourhood $U$ of $x_0$. Let $\iota_x : \mathbb{R}^n \to T_xM$, $\iota_x(c) = \sum_i c_i E_i(x)$, be its coefficient map; it is linear and bijective because a frame is a pointwise basis. Let $S(x) \in \operatorname{End}(\mathbb{R}^n)$ be the operator with matrix $S(x)_{ij} = \bar{g}_x(E_i, E_j)$. These entries are $\bar{g}$-pairings of smooth sections, hence smooth functions of $x$ on $U$, so $x \mapsto S(x)$ is a smooth map from $U$ into $\operatorname{End}(\mathbb{R}^n)$, and each $S(x)$ is symmetric because $\bar g$ is. Because the frame is $g$-orthonormal, $S(x)$ is conjugate to $A_x$:
\[
  A_x \circ \iota_x = \iota_x \circ S(x),
\]
since both sides have the same $g$-inner products against every $E_j(x)$, namely $\sum_i c_i \bar{g}_x(E_i,E_j)$. Consequently $\iota_x$ matches the $\mu$-eigenvectors of $S(x)$ with those of $A_x$; combined with the claim above, $(S(x), c, \mu)$ is a simple eigenpair whenever $c \neq 0$, $S(x)c = \mu c$ and $\mu < 0$. Note only the injectivity of $\iota_x$ is used here, not that it is an isometry.

Let $v_0$ be a unit vector spanning the $\lambda(x_0)$-eigenspace of $S(x_0)$, obtained by pulling a negative eigenvector of $A_{x_0}$ back through $\iota_{x_0}$ and normalizing. Proposition \ref{prop:smooth-eigen-selection} applied to the simple eigenpair $(S(x_0), v_0, \lambda(x_0))$ gives an open $W \ni S(x_0)$ and maps $\mathcal{V}, \Lambda$ on $W$ selecting an eigenpair of each $S \in W$. Since $\Lambda$ is continuous and $\Lambda(S(x_0)) = \lambda(x_0) < 0$, the set
\[
  U' = \{x \in U : S(x) \in W \text{ and } \Lambda(S(x)) < 0\}
\]
is open and contains $x_0$. Set $X_x = \iota_x\,\mathcal{V}(S(x))$.

For $x \in U'$ the vector $\mathcal{V}(S(x))$ is an eigenvector of $S(x)$ with eigenvalue $\Lambda(S(x)) < 0$, and it is nonzero because $\langle v_0, \mathcal{V}(S(x))\rangle = 1$; so $X_x \neq 0$ by injectivity of $\iota_x$, and $X_x$ is an eigenvector of $A_x$ with the negative eigenvalue $\Lambda(S(x))$ by the conjugation relation. Hence $\operatorname{span}(X_x) = D_x$ by the claim.

Smoothness of $X$ on $U'$ is smoothness of $x \mapsto \mathcal{V}(S(x))$ together with smoothness of the frame, since $X_x = \sum_i \mathcal{V}(S(x))_i\,E_i(x)$. By the previous paragraph $(S(x), \mathcal{V}(S(x)), \Lambda(S(x)))$ is a simple eigenpair for each $x \in U'$. So clause (4) of Proposition \ref{prop:smooth-eigen-selection} applies at every $S(x)$, making $\mathcal{V}$ smooth there; composing with the smooth map $x \mapsto S(x)$ shows the coefficients of $X$ are smooth on $U'$, hence so is $X$.

Thus every $x_0 \in M$ has a neighbourhood carrying a smooth nowhere-zero section of $D$, so $D$ is a rank-1 tangent distribution in the sense of Definition \ref{def:rank-one-distribution}.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}
\label{thm:lorentz-metrics}
\dcref{ch2:2.69}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isLorentzMetric_iff_exists_isRankOneDistribution}
\leanok
\uses{def:lorentz-metric,prop:existence-riemannian-metric,prop:lorentz-metric-of-rank-one-distribution,prop:rank-one-distribution-of-lorentz-metric}
A smooth manifold $M$ admits a Lorentz metric if and only if it admits a rank-1 tangent distribution (i.e., a rank-1 subbundle of $TM$).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:lorentz-metric-of-rank-one-distribution,prop:rank-one-distribution-of-lorentz-metric}
Sufficiency is Proposition \ref{prop:lorentz-metric-of-rank-one-distribution} and necessity is Proposition \ref{prop:rank-one-distribution-of-lorentz-metric}.
\end{proof}

\emph{Proof.} Problem~2-34. \qed

With some more sophisticated tools from algebraic topology, it can be shown that every noncompact connected smooth manifold admits a Lorentz metric, and a compact connected smooth manifold admits a Lorentz metric if and only if its Euler characteristic is zero (see \cite{ON83}, p.~149). It follows that no even-dimensional sphere admits a Lorentz metric, because $S^{2n}$ has Euler characteristic equal to 2. For a thorough treatment of pseudo-Riemannian metrics from a mathematical point of view, see the excellent book \cite{ON83}; a more physical treatment can be found in \cite{HE73}.

\section{Pseudo-Riemannian Submanifolds}

The theory of submanifolds is only slightly more complicated in the pseudo-Riemannian case. If $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold, a smooth submanifold $\iota: M \hookrightarrow \tilde{M}$ is called a \emph{pseudo-Riemannian submanifold} of $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ if $\iota^* \tilde{g}$ is nondegenerate with constant signature. If this is the case, we always consider $M$ to be endowed with the induced pseudo-Riemannian metric $\iota^* \tilde{g}$. In the special case in which $\iota^* \tilde{g}$ is positive definite, $M$ is called a \emph{Riemannian submanifold}.

The nondegeneracy hypothesis is not automatically satisfied; for example, if $M \subseteq \mathbb{R}^{1,1}$ is the submanifold $\{(\xi, \tau) : \xi = \tau\}$ and $\iota: M \to \mathbb{R}^{1,1}$ is inclusion, then the pullback tensor $\iota^* \tilde{g}^{(1,1)}$ is identically zero on $M$.

For hypersurfaces (submanifolds of codimension 1), the nondegeneracy condition is easy to check. If $M \subseteq \tilde{M}$ is a smooth submanifold and $p \in M$, then a vector $v \in T_p \tilde{M}$ is said to be \emph{normal} to $M$ if $(v,x) = 0$ for all $x \in T_p M$, just as in the Riemannian case. The space of all normal vectors at $p$ is a subspace of $T_p \tilde{M}$ denoted by $N_p M$.

The proofs of Propositions \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} and \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-adapted-frame} both begin with the same purely linear-algebraic step, carried out pointwise on a tangent space: a nondegenerate tuple is absorbed into an orthonormal basis inducing the same flag. We isolate it once.

\begin{lemma}[Orthonormal Bases Adapted to a Nondegenerate Flag]
\label{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isOrthonormal_prefixSpan_eq_of_isNondegenerateTuple}
\leanok
\uses{lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases,prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products}
Suppose $(V,q)$ is an $n$-dimensional scalar product space and $(v_1,\ldots,v_k)$ is a nondegenerate $k$-tuple in $V$, with $k \leq n$. Then there is an orthonormal basis $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ for $V$ such that $\operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_j) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$ for every $j \leq k$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases,prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products}
By Lemma \ref{lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases} the tuple extends to a nondegenerate basis $(w_1,\ldots,w_n)$ of $V$ with $w_i = v_i$ for every $i \leq k$. Proposition \ref{prop:gram-schmidt-algorithm-scalar-products} applied to $(w_i)$ yields an orthonormal basis $(b_i)$ with $\operatorname{span}(b_1,\ldots,b_j) = \operatorname{span}(w_1,\ldots,w_j)$ for every $j \leq n$. For $j \leq k$ the tuples $(w_i)$ and $(v_i)$ have the same first $j$ entries, so $\operatorname{span}(w_1,\ldots,w_j) = \operatorname{span}(v_1,\ldots,v_j)$, which gives the claim.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Orthonormal Basis Adapted to a Non-Null Line]
\label{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis-line}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isOrthonormal_prefixSpan_one_eq_span}
\leanok
\uses{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis}
Suppose $(V,q)$ is an $n$-dimensional scalar product space with $n \geq 1$, and $x \in V$ satisfies $\langle x, x \rangle \neq 0$. Then there is an orthonormal basis $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ for $V$ with $\operatorname{span}(b_1) = \operatorname{span}(x)$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis}
The line $\operatorname{span}(x)$ is $1$-dimensional, since $\langle x,x\rangle \neq 0$ forces $x \neq 0$. It is nondegenerate: an element $cx$ orthogonal to every element of $\operatorname{span}(x)$ satisfies in particular $c\langle x,x \rangle = \langle cx, x\rangle = 0$, and $\langle x,x\rangle \neq 0$ gives $c = 0$. Hence the $1$-tuple $(x)$ is nondegenerate, and Lemma \ref{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis} applied with $k = 1$ produces an orthonormal basis $(b_i)$ with $\operatorname{span}(b_1) = \operatorname{span}(x)$.
\end{proof}

The rest of the linear algebra behind Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} is pointwise as well, and involves the manifold nowhere: it takes place entirely in the scalar product space $(T_p\tilde M, \tilde g_p)$, in which the normal space $N_pM$ is a line and the tangent space $T_pM$ is that line's orthogonal complement. We isolate it here, in four steps: nondegeneracy is decided on the normal line; the tail of an adapted orthonormal basis spans the complement of its head; that tail is an orthonormal basis of the complement; and counting its signs gives the signature.

\begin{lemma}[Nondegeneracy of a Line]
\label{lem:nondegenerate-line}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.restrict_nondegenerate_iff_of_finrank_eq_one}
\leanok
Let $q$ be a bilinear form on a finite-dimensional vector space $V$ and let $L \subseteq V$ be a subspace with $\dim L = 1$. Then $q|_{L \times L}$ is nondegenerate if and only if $\langle v,v \rangle \neq 0$ for every nonzero $v \in L$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Suppose first that $q|_{L\times L}$ is nondegenerate, and let $v \in L$ be nonzero with $\langle v,v\rangle = 0$. Then $\operatorname{span}(v) \subseteq L$ and both are $1$-dimensional, so $\operatorname{span}(v) = L$. Every $w \in L$ is therefore of the form $cv$, whence $\langle v,w\rangle = c\langle v,v\rangle = 0$. Thus $v$ is orthogonal to all of $L$, and nondegeneracy forces $v = 0$, a contradiction.

Conversely, suppose every nonzero $v \in L$ satisfies $\langle v,v\rangle \neq 0$, and let $v \in L$ be orthogonal to all of $L$ on either side. Taking the other argument to be $v$ itself gives $\langle v,v \rangle = 0$, so $v = 0$ by hypothesis.
\end{proof}

This equivalence is special to dimension $1$. In dimension $\geq 2$ the condition that no nonzero vector be null says that the restriction is \emph{definite}, which is strictly stronger than nondegenerate: the form $(\xi,\tau) \mapsto \xi^2 - \tau^2$ on $\mathbb{R}^2$ is nondegenerate, yet every vector with $\xi = \pm\tau$ is null.
