This is why Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} is stated for hypersurfaces, whose normal spaces are lines.

\begin{lemma}[Nondegeneracy of a Hyperplane]
\label{lem:hyperplane-nondegenerate}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.restrict_nondegenerate_iff_forall_orthogonal}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-subspace,lem:nondegenerate-line,lem:orthogonal-complement}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space and let $W \subseteq V$ be a subspace with $\dim W + 1 = \dim V$. Then $q|_{W\times W}$ is nondegenerate if and only if $\langle v,v\rangle \neq 0$ for every nonzero $v \in W^\perp$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-subspace,lem:nondegenerate-line,lem:orthogonal-complement}
By Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-subspace}, $q|_{W\times W}$ is nondegenerate if and only if $q|_{W^\perp \times W^\perp}$ is. By Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement}, $\dim W^\perp = \dim V - \dim W = 1$, so Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-line} applies to $W^\perp$ and identifies the latter condition with the requirement that every nonzero $v \in W^\perp$ satisfy $\langle v,v \rangle \neq 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Tail of an Orthonormal Basis Spans the Complement of its Head]
\label{lem:orthonormal-tail-spans-orthogonal}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.span_range_succ_eq_orthogonal}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement}
Let $q$ be a nondegenerate bilinear form on a finite-dimensional vector space $V$ and let $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ be an orthonormal basis for $V$. Then $\operatorname{span}(b_2,\ldots,b_n) = \operatorname{span}(b_1)^\perp$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement}
Each $b_j$ with $j \geq 2$ is orthogonal to $b_1$, hence to every element $cb_1$ of $\operatorname{span}(b_1)$; as $\operatorname{span}(b_1)^\perp$ is a subspace, this gives $\operatorname{span}(b_2,\ldots,b_n) \subseteq \operatorname{span}(b_1)^\perp$. The reverse inclusion is a matter of dimension. The vectors $b_2,\ldots,b_n$ are linearly independent, being part of a basis, so $\dim\operatorname{span}(b_2,\ldots,b_n) = n-1$. On the other hand $\langle b_1,b_1\rangle = \pm 1 \neq 0$ forces $b_1 \neq 0$, so $\dim \operatorname{span}(b_1) = 1$, and Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement} gives $\dim \operatorname{span}(b_1)^\perp = n-1$. A subspace contained in another of the same finite dimension equals it.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Orthonormal Basis of the Complement of a Line]
\label{lem:orthonormal-basis-orthogonal-complement}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_basis_isOrthonormal_orthogonal_span_singleton}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthonormal-tail-spans-orthogonal}
Let $q$ be a nondegenerate bilinear form on a finite-dimensional vector space $V$ and let $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ be an orthonormal basis for $V$. Then $(b_2,\ldots,b_n)$ is an orthonormal basis for $\operatorname{span}(b_1)^\perp$ equipped with the restriction of $q$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthonormal-tail-spans-orthogonal}
By Lemma \ref{lem:orthonormal-tail-spans-orthogonal} the vectors $b_2,\ldots,b_n$ lie in $\operatorname{span}(b_1)^\perp$ and span it. They are linearly independent in $V$, being part of a basis, and hence linearly independent as a family in the subspace, the inclusion being injective; so they form a basis of $\operatorname{span}(b_1)^\perp$. Orthonormality is inherited, the restricted form taking the same values on them as $q$: $\langle b_i,b_j\rangle = 0$ for $i \neq j$, and $\langle b_i,b_i\rangle = \pm 1$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Complement of a Non-Null Line is a Hyperplane]
\label{lem:orthogonal-complement-non-null-line-hyperplane}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.finrank_orthogonal_span_singleton}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space of dimension $n$ and let $x \in V$ be nonzero. Then $\dim \operatorname{span}(x)^\perp = n - 1$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement}
Since $x \neq 0$, the line $\operatorname{span}(x)$ has dimension $1$, and Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement} gives $\dim \operatorname{span}(x) + \dim \operatorname{span}(x)^\perp = n$.
\end{proof}

This is the pointwise form of $\dim T_pM = \dim T_p\tilde M - 1$, and it is what makes Lemma \ref{lem:hyperplane-nondegenerate} applicable to the tangent space of a hypersurface.

\begin{lemma}[The Complement of a Non-Null Line is a Scalar Product Space]
\label{lem:orthogonal-complement-non-null-line-nondegenerate}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.restrict_orthogonal_span_singleton_nondegenerate}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-subspace,lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis-line}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space and let $x \in V$ satisfy $\langle x,x\rangle \neq 0$. Then the restriction of $q$ to $\operatorname{span}(x)^\perp$ is nondegenerate.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-subspace,lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis-line}
The line $\operatorname{span}(x)$ is nondegenerate, as recorded in the proof of Lemma \ref{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis-line}: an element $cx$ orthogonal to all of $\operatorname{span}(x)$ satisfies $c\langle x,x\rangle = 0$, and $\langle x,x \rangle \neq 0$ gives $c = 0$. Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-subspace} then shows that $\operatorname{span}(x)^\perp$ is nondegenerate as well.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Signature of the Complement of a Non-Null Line]
\label{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.sigPos_sigNeg_restrict_orthogonal_span_singleton}
\leanok
\uses{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis-line,lem:orthonormal-basis-orthogonal-complement,lem:orthogonal-complement-non-null-line-nondegenerate,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space of signature $(r,s)$ and let $x \in V$ satisfy $\langle x,x\rangle \neq 0$. Let $(r',s')$ be the signature of $\operatorname{span}(x)^\perp$ with the restriction of $q$, which is a scalar product space by Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement-non-null-line-nondegenerate}. If $\langle x,x\rangle > 0$, then $r' + 1 = r$ and $s' = s$. If $\langle x,x \rangle < 0$, then $r' = r$ and $s' + 1 = s$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis-line,lem:orthonormal-basis-orthogonal-complement,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
By Lemma \ref{lem:adapted-orthonormal-basis-line} there is an orthonormal basis $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ for $V$ with $\operatorname{span}(b_1) = \operatorname{span}(x)$, so that $\operatorname{span}(x)^\perp = \operatorname{span}(b_1)^\perp$; and by Lemma \ref{lem:orthonormal-basis-orthogonal-complement} the tail $(b_2,\ldots,b_n)$ is an orthonormal basis of that complement.

By Proposition \ref{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}, $r$ is the number of indices $i$ with $\langle b_i,b_i\rangle = 1$ and $s$ the number with $\langle b_i,b_i \rangle = -1$; likewise $r'$ and $s'$ are the corresponding counts over $i \geq 2$. The two pairs of counts therefore differ exactly by the contribution of the index $1$, which contributes to $r$ if $\langle b_1,b_1\rangle = 1$ and to $s$ if $\langle b_1,b_1 \rangle = -1$.

It remains to determine the sign of $\langle b_1, b_1 \rangle$. Since $\operatorname{span}(b_1) = \operatorname{span}(x)$, we may write $b_1 = cx$ with $c \neq 0$, and then $\langle b_1,b_1\rangle = c^2 \langle x,x\rangle$ has the same sign as $\langle x,x \rangle$. So if $\langle x,x\rangle > 0$ then $\langle b_1,b_1\rangle = 1$, giving $r' + 1 = r$ and $s' = s$; and if $\langle x,x\rangle < 0$ then $\langle b_1,b_1\rangle = -1$, giving $r' = r$ and $s' + 1 = s$.
\end{proof}

Proposition \ref{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise} is stated about the complement of a line, whereas Lee states his conclusion about the hyperplane $T_pM$ itself, and Lemma \ref{lem:hyperplane-nondegenerate} is about that hyperplane too. The two are identified by Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement-involution}: a hyperplane is the orthogonal complement of its own normal line. Making that identification puts both halves of the proposition in the same terms.

\begin{lemma}[A Hyperplane is the Complement of its Normal Line]
\label{lem:hyperplane-eq-complement-normal-line}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.orthogonal_span_singleton_eq}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement,lem:orthogonal-complement-involution}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space, let $W \subseteq V$ satisfy $\dim W + 1 = \dim V$, and let $x \in W^\perp$ be nonzero. Then $\operatorname{span}(x)^\perp = W$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:orthogonal-complement,lem:orthogonal-complement-involution}
By Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement}, $\dim W^\perp = \dim V - \dim W = 1$. Since $x$ is a nonzero element of $W^\perp$, the line $\operatorname{span}(x)$ is a $1$-dimensional subspace of the $1$-dimensional space $W^\perp$, so $\operatorname{span}(x) = W^\perp$. Hence $\operatorname{span}(x)^\perp = (W^\perp)^\perp = W$, the last step by Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement-involution}.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Signature of a Hypersurface's Tangent Space]
\label{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise-hyperplane}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.sigPos_sigNeg_restrict_of_mem_orthogonal}
\leanok
\uses{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise,lem:hyperplane-eq-complement-normal-line}
Let $(V,q)$ be a scalar product space of signature $(r,s)$, let $W \subseteq V$ satisfy $\dim W + 1 = \dim V$, and let $x \in W^\perp$ be nonzero. Let $(r',s')$ be the signature of $W$ with the restriction of $q$. If $\langle x,x\rangle > 0$, then $r' + 1 = r$ and $s' = s$. If $\langle x,x\rangle < 0$, then $r' = r$ and $s' + 1 = s$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise,lem:hyperplane-eq-complement-normal-line}
By Lemma \ref{lem:hyperplane-eq-complement-normal-line}, $W = \operatorname{span}(x)^\perp$, so the claim is exactly Proposition \ref{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise} applied to $x$.
\end{proof}

Lemma \ref{lem:hyperplane-nondegenerate} and Proposition \ref{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise-hyperplane} together are Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} at a single point: taking $V = T_p\tilde M$, $q = \tilde g_p$ and $W = T_pM$, whose orthogonal complement is $N_pM$, the first says that $\iota^*\tilde g$ is nondegenerate at $p$ exactly when every nonzero normal vector at $p$ is non-null, and the second says that the signature of $T_pM$ is then $(r-1,s)$ or $(r,s-1)$ according to the sign of the normal vectors. What Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} adds is the passage from a point to the manifold: that these pointwise conditions, holding at every $p$, make $\iota^*\tilde g$ a pseudo-Riemannian metric of constant signature.

That passage has exactly three steps. First, $T_pM$ and $N_pM$ must be a hyperplane and its complement \emph{in one and the same space}; they already are, both being subspaces of the ambient tangent space $T_{F(p)}\tilde{M}$, which is the fibre at $p$ of the pullback bundle $F^*T\tilde{M}$, so nothing need be transported. Second, $N_pM$, defined by orthogonality against the tangent space, must be recognized as the orthogonal complement of that tangent space. Third, the signature of $g$ at $p$ is that of a form on $T_pM$, whereas the pointwise results compute the signature of $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}$ restricted to $dF_p(T_pM)$, a form on a subspace of the ambient tangent space; these are different vector spaces, and $dF_p$ is an isometry between them.

\begin{lemma}[The Normal Space is an Orthogonal Complement]
\label{lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoNormalSpace_eq_orthogonal}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold and let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be smooth. For every $x \in M$,
\[
  N_xM \;=\; \bigl(dF_x(T_xM)\bigr)^{\perp},
\]
the orthogonal complement being taken in the scalar product space $(T_{F(x)}\tilde{M}, \tilde{g}_{F(x)})$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric}
Definition \ref{def:pseudo-normal-space} places $v$ in $N_xM$ when $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}(v,w) = 0$ for every $w \in dF_x(T_xM)$, whereas $v$ lies in $(dF_x(T_xM))^\perp$ when $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}(w,v) = 0$ for every such $w$. The two conditions differ only in which argument the tested vector occupies, and $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}$ is symmetric, so each is equivalent to the other term by term.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[A Hypersurface's Tangent Space is a Hyperplane]
\label{lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.finrank_tangentRange_add_one}
\leanok
Let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be smooth, let $x \in M$ be a point at which $dF_x$ is injective, and suppose $\dim M + 1 = \dim \tilde{M}$. Then
\[
  \dim dF_x(T_xM) + 1 \;=\; \dim T_{F(x)}\tilde{M} .
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Since $dF_x$ is injective, it is a linear isomorphism onto its image, so $\dim dF_x(T_xM) = \dim T_xM = \dim M$. The ambient tangent space $T_{F(x)}\tilde{M}$ has dimension $\dim\tilde{M}$. Substituting both into the hypothesis $\dim M + 1 = \dim \tilde{M}$ gives the claim.
\end{proof}

This is the codimension hypothesis in the form the pointwise results of this section ask for it, and it is the only place where $\dim M = \dim\tilde{M} - 1$ is used.

\begin{lemma}[The Normal Space of a Hypersurface is a Line]
\label{lem:pseudo-normal-space-line}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.finrank_pseudoNormalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal,lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane,lem:orthogonal-complement}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold, let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be smooth with $\dim M + 1 = \dim\tilde{M}$, and let $x \in M$ be a point at which $dF_x$ is injective. Then $\dim N_xM = 1$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal,lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane,lem:orthogonal-complement}
Write $W = dF_x(T_xM)$. By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal}, $N_xM = W^\perp$, so Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement} applied in the scalar product space $(T_{F(x)}\tilde{M}, \tilde{g}_{F(x)})$ gives $\dim W + \dim N_xM = \dim T_{F(x)}\tilde{M}$. Lemma \ref{lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane} gives $\dim W + 1 = \dim T_{F(x)}\tilde{M}$. Cancelling $\dim W$ leaves $\dim N_xM = 1$.
\end{proof}

This is why a \emph{single} non-null normal vector decides Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature}: any nonzero $v \in N_xM$ spans the line, so the sign of $\tilde{g}(v,v)$ cannot depend on which nonzero normal is chosen, rescaling $v \mapsto cv$ multiplying that number by $c^2 > 0$. We record this reduction, first as a statement about an arbitrary line and then in the form Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} consumes.

\begin{lemma}[The Sign of a Line is Decided by One of its Vectors]
\label{lem:line-sign-one-vector}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pos_of_mem_of_finrank_eq_one}
\leanok
Let $V$ be a real vector space, $B$ a bilinear form on $V$, and $N \leq V$ a subspace with
$\dim N = 1$. Suppose $x \in N$ is nonzero with $B(x,x) > 0$. Then $B(v,v) > 0$ for every
nonzero $v \in N$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
Since $\dim N = 1$ and $x \in N$ is nonzero, the line $\mathbb{R}x$ is contained in $N$ and has the
same dimension, so $N = \mathbb{R}x$. A nonzero $v \in N$ is therefore $v = ax$ for some
$a \in \mathbb{R}$, and $a \neq 0$ because $v \neq 0$. Bilinearity gives
$B(v,v) = B(ax, ax) = a^2 B(x,x)$, a product of the positive numbers $a^2$ and $B(x,x)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[One Positive Normal Makes Every Normal Positive]
\label{lem:one-normal-decides-sign}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.forall_pos_of_pos_mem_pseudoNormalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,lem:pseudo-normal-space-line,lem:line-sign-one-vector}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$, $F$ and $x$ be as in Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-line}, and
suppose some nonzero $v_0 \in N_xM$ satisfies $\tilde{g}(v_0,v_0) > 0$. Then
$\tilde{g}(v,v) > 0$ for every nonzero $v \in N_xM$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pseudo-normal-space-line,lem:line-sign-one-vector}
By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-line}, $\dim N_xM = 1$. Apply Lemma
\ref{lem:line-sign-one-vector} to the subspace $N_xM$ of the ambient tangent space, the form
$\tilde{g}_{F(x)}$, and the vector $v_0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[One Negative Normal Makes Every Normal Negative]
\label{lem:one-normal-decides-sign-neg}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.forall_neg_of_neg_mem_pseudoNormalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,lem:pseudo-normal-space-line,lem:line-sign-one-vector}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$, $F$ and $x$ be as in Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-line}, and
suppose some nonzero $v_0 \in N_xM$ satisfies $\tilde{g}(v_0,v_0) < 0$. Then
$\tilde{g}(v,v) < 0$ for every nonzero $v \in N_xM$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:one-normal-decides-sign}
Apply Lemma \ref{lem:one-normal-decides-sign} to the form $-\tilde{g}$, for which $v_0$ and every
nonzero normal satisfy the positivity hypothesis and conclusion respectively; note $N_xM$ is the
same subspace for $\tilde{g}$ and $-\tilde{g}$.
\end{proof}

This is the form in which the hypothesis of Proposition
\ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} is met in practice: a caller does not verify a
sign for every normal vector, it exhibits one distinguished normal — for a regular level set, the
gradient of the defining function — and checks the sign there.

\begin{lemma}[A Hypersurface has a Nonzero Normal Vector at Every Point]
\label{lem:exists-nonzero-normal}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_ne_zero_mem_pseudoNormalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,lem:pseudo-normal-space-line}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$, $F$ and $x$ be as in Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-line}. Then there is a nonzero $v \in N_xM$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pseudo-normal-space-line}
By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-line}, $\dim N_xM = 1 > 0$, so $N_xM$ is not the zero subspace and therefore contains a nonzero vector.
\end{proof}

Slight as it is, this is what allows the hypothesis ``$\tilde{g}(v,v) > 0$ for every nonzero normal vector $v$'' to be \emph{used} rather than merely assumed: were $N_xM$ trivial, that hypothesis would hold vacuously and yield nothing.

\begin{lemma}[The Differential is an Isometry onto the Tangent Space]
\label{lem:differential-isometry-tangent-range}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isometryEquivRestrictTangentRange}
\leanok
\uses{def:induced-tensor-along-map,lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
Let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be smooth and suppose the pseudo-Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$ is induced by $\tilde{g}$ along $F$. Then for every $x \in M$ the differential $dF_x$ is an isometry of scalar product spaces from $(T_xM, g_x)$ onto $\bigl(dF_x(T_xM),\, \tilde{g}_{F(x)}|_{dF_x(T_xM)}\bigr)$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:induced-tensor-along-map,lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion} the map $dF_x$ is injective, hence a linear isomorphism onto its image $dF_x(T_xM)$. It preserves the forms: for $v,w \in T_xM$, Definition \ref{def:induced-tensor-along-map} gives $g_x(v,w) = \tilde{g}_{F(x)}(dF_xv,\, dF_xw)$, and since $dF_xv$ and $dF_xw$ both lie in $dF_x(T_xM)$, the right-hand side is the restriction of $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}$ to that subspace evaluated at them. A form-preserving linear isomorphism is an isometry.
\end{proof}

This is the formal content of Lee's ``we usually identify $T_pM$ with its image in $T_p\tilde{M}$ under $d\iota_p$''. The identification is not free of charge: $g_x$ is a form on $T_xM$, whereas the pointwise results compute the signature of $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}$ restricted to $dF_x(T_xM)$, a form on a subspace of a different vector space. The isometry is what makes the two signatures the same number.

\begin{lemma}[The Signature of a Submanifold is Computed in the Ambient Tangent Space]
\label{lem:submanifold-signature-in-ambient-fibre}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.sigPosAt_eq_sigPos_restrict_tangentRange, LeeLib.Ch02.sigNegAt_eq_sigNeg_restrict_tangentRange}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-signature-at,lem:differential-isometry-tangent-range,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
Let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be smooth and suppose the pseudo-Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$ is induced by $\tilde{g}$ along $F$. Then for every $x \in M$ the signature of $g$ at $x$ is the signature of the scalar product space $\bigl(dF_x(T_xM),\, \tilde{g}_{F(x)}|_{dF_x(T_xM)}\bigr)$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-signature-at,lem:differential-isometry-tangent-range,prop:sylvesters-law-inertia}
By Definition \ref{def:pseudo-metric-signature-at} the signature of $g$ at $x$ is the signature of the scalar product space $(T_xM, g_x)$, and by Lemma \ref{lem:differential-isometry-tangent-range} the map $dF_x$ is an isometry from that space onto the displayed one. It therefore suffices that isometric scalar product spaces have the same signature. An isometry carries an orthonormal basis $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ of the source to a basis of the target, being a linear isomorphism, and that image basis is orthonormal with $\langle dF_xb_i, dF_xb_i\rangle = \langle b_i,b_i\rangle$ for every $i$, the forms being preserved. So the two bases have the same number of members of square $+1$ and the same number of square $-1$, and by Proposition \ref{prop:sylvesters-law-inertia} those two counts are the signature of the respective space.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Nondegeneracy of a Hypersurface]
\label{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-nondegenerate}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.restrict_tangentRange_nondegenerate_iff}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.70}
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:pseudo-normal-space,lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal,lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane,lem:hyperplane-nondegenerate}
Suppose $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold and $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ is smooth with $\dim M + 1 = \dim\tilde{M}$, and let $x \in M$ be a point at which $dF_x$ is injective. Then the restriction of $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}$ to $dF_x(T_xM)$ is nondegenerate if and only if $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}(v,v) \neq 0$ for every nonzero $v \in N_xM$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal,lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane,lem:hyperplane-nondegenerate}
Apply Lemma \ref{lem:hyperplane-nondegenerate} in the scalar product space $V = (T_{F(x)}\tilde{M}, \tilde{g}_{F(x)})$ with $W = dF_x(T_xM)$. Its hypothesis $\dim W + 1 = \dim V$ is Lemma \ref{lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane}, available because $dF_x$ is injective. Its conclusion is that $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}|_W$ is nondegenerate if and only if every nonzero $v \in W^\perp$ satisfies $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}(v,v) \neq 0$; and $W^\perp = N_xM$ by Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal}.
\end{proof}

Here the induced tensor appears as $\tilde{g}$ restricted to $dF_x(T_xM)$ rather than as a metric on $M$, and $F$ is assumed only to be an immersion. This is forced: Definition \ref{def:induced-tensor-along-map} presupposes a \emph{nondegenerate} $g$ on $M$, and nondegeneracy of the induced tensor is precisely what this proposition decides, so it cannot appear among its hypotheses.

\begin{proposition}[A Single Normal Vector Decides the Signature at a Point]
\label{prop:hypersurface-signature-at-a-point}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.sigPosAt_sigNegAt_of_mem_pseudoNormalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature,def:pseudo-normal-space,def:induced-tensor-along-map,lem:submanifold-signature-in-ambient-fibre,prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise-hyperplane,lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal,lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane,lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
Suppose $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold of signature $(r,s)$ and $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ presents $M$ as a pseudo-Riemannian submanifold with induced metric $g$, where $\dim M + 1 = \dim\tilde{M}$. Let $x \in M$, let $(r_x,s_x)$ be the signature of $g$ at $x$, and let $v \in N_xM$ be nonzero. If $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}(v,v) > 0$, then $r_x + 1 = r$ and $s_x = s$. If $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}(v,v) < 0$, then $r_x = r$ and $s_x + 1 = s$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature,lem:submanifold-signature-in-ambient-fibre,prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise-hyperplane,lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal,lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane,lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
Put $V = (T_{F(x)}\tilde{M}, \tilde{g}_{F(x)})$ and $W = dF_x(T_xM)$. By Definition \ref{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature} applied at the point $F(x)$, the scalar product space $V$ has signature $(r,s)$. By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion} the differential $dF_x$ is injective, so Lemma \ref{lem:hypersurface-tangent-hyperplane} gives $\dim W + 1 = \dim V$; and $v$ is a nonzero element of $W^\perp$ by Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-eq-orthogonal}. Proposition \ref{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise-hyperplane}, applied to $V$, $W$ and $v$, therefore computes the signature $(r',s')$ of $W$ with the restriction of $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}$: it satisfies $r' + 1 = r$ and $s' = s$ when $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}(v,v) > 0$, and $r' = r$ and $s' + 1 = s$ when $\tilde{g}_{F(x)}(v,v) < 0$. Finally $(r',s') = (r_x,s_x)$ by Lemma \ref{lem:submanifold-signature-in-ambient-fibre}.
\end{proof}

The conclusion is recorded as $r_x + 1 = r$ rather than $r_x = r - 1$. The two are equivalent, but the first carries the additional information that $r \geq 1$, which is what makes the subtraction in the statement below meaningful.

\begin{proposition}
\label{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.hasSignature_of_forall_normal_pos, LeeLib.Ch02.hasSignature_of_forall_normal_neg}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.70}
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:pseudo-metric-has-signature,def:induced-tensor-along-map,def:pseudo-normal-space,lem:exists-nonzero-normal,prop:hypersurface-signature-at-a-point,lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
Suppose $(\tilde{M}, \tilde{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold of signature $(r,s)$, and $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ presents $M$ as a pseudo-Riemannian submanifold with induced metric $g$, where $\dim M + 1 = \dim\tilde{M}$; the case Lee has in mind is that of a smooth hypersurface $M \subseteq \tilde{M}$ and its inclusion $\iota$, with $g = \iota^*\tilde{g}$. If $\tilde{g}(v,v) > 0$ for every $p \in M$ and every nonzero normal vector $v \in N_pM$, then $g$ has signature $(r-1,s)$; and if $\tilde{g}(v,v) < 0$ for every such vector, then $g$ has signature $(r,s-1)$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature,lem:exists-nonzero-normal,prop:hypersurface-signature-at-a-point,lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
Take the first case, and fix $p \in M$; let $(r_p,s_p)$ be the signature of $g$ at $p$. By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion} the differential $dF_p$ is injective, so Lemma \ref{lem:exists-nonzero-normal} supplies a nonzero $v \in N_pM$. By hypothesis $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}(v,v) > 0$, so Proposition \ref{prop:hypersurface-signature-at-a-point} gives $r_p + 1 = r$ and $s_p = s$; in particular $r \geq 1$ and $(r_p,s_p) = (r-1,s)$. As $p$ was arbitrary, $g$ has signature $(r-1,s)$ in the sense of Definition \ref{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature}. The second case is identical, using instead the alternative $r_p = r$, $s_p + 1 = s$ of Proposition \ref{prop:hypersurface-signature-at-a-point}, which gives $s \geq 1$ and $(r_p,s_p) = (r,s-1)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Induced Tensor of a Non-Null Hypersurface is Nondegenerate]
\label{lem:pseudo-hypersurface-induced-tensor-nondegenerate}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_pseudoPullbackForm_ne_zero}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-pullback-form,def:pseudo-normal-space,prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-nondegenerate}
Suppose $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold and $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ is a smooth immersion with $\dim M + 1 = \dim\tilde{M}$, and suppose $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}(v,v) \neq 0$ for every $p \in M$ and every nonzero $v \in N_pM$. Then the pullback tensor $F^*\tilde{g}$ of Definition \ref{def:pseudo-pullback-form} is nondegenerate at every point: for every $p \in M$ and every nonzero $v \in T_pM$ there is a $w \in T_pM$ with $(F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,w) \neq 0$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-pullback-form,prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-nondegenerate}
Fix $p \in M$ and a nonzero $v \in T_pM$, and put $W = dF_p(T_pM)$. By Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-nondegenerate} the restriction of $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}$ to $W$ is nondegenerate. The vector $u = dF_p(v)$ lies in $W$, and $u \neq 0$ because $dF_p$ is injective and $v \neq 0$. Nondegeneracy of the restriction therefore supplies a $u' \in W$ with $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}(u,u') \neq 0$. Since $W$ is the image of $dF_p$, we may write $u' = dF_p(w)$ for some $w \in T_pM$, and then
\[
  (F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,w) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}\bigl(dF_pv,\, dF_pw\bigr) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}(u,u') \neq 0 .
\]
\end{proof}

This is what turns Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-nondegenerate} from a criterion into a construction, and with it the two halves of Lee's proposition combine into a single statement in which the induced metric is produced rather than presupposed.

\begin{corollary}[Signature of a Pseudo-Riemannian Hypersurface]
\label{cor:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-submanifold}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.hasSignature_pseudoPullbackMetric_of_forall_normal_pos, LeeLib.Ch02.hasSignature_pseudoPullbackMetric_of_forall_normal_neg}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.70}
\uses{def:pseudo-metric-has-signature,def:pseudo-normal-space,def:pseudo-induced-metric,lem:pseudo-hypersurface-induced-tensor-nondegenerate,lem:pseudo-riemannian-submanifold-induced-tensor,prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature}
Suppose $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold of signature $(r,s)$ and $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ is a smooth immersion with $\dim M + 1 = \dim\tilde{M}$. If $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}(v,v) > 0$ for every $p \in M$ and every nonzero $v \in N_pM$, then the pullback tensor $F^*\tilde{g}$ is a pseudo-Riemannian metric on $M$ of signature $(r-1,s)$, so that $F$ presents $M$ as a pseudo-Riemannian submanifold of that signature. If instead $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}(v,v) < 0$ for every such $v$, then $F^*\tilde{g}$ is a pseudo-Riemannian metric of signature $(r,s-1)$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-induced-metric,lem:pseudo-hypersurface-induced-tensor-nondegenerate,lem:pseudo-riemannian-submanifold-induced-tensor,prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature}
In either case every nonzero normal vector is non-null, so Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-hypersurface-induced-tensor-nondegenerate} shows that $F^*\tilde{g}$ is nondegenerate at every point of $M$, and Definition \ref{def:pseudo-induced-metric} accordingly makes $g = F^*\tilde{g}$ a pseudo-Riemannian metric on $M$. By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-riemannian-submanifold-induced-tensor} this $g$ is induced by $\tilde{g}$ along $F$, so $F$ presents $M$ as a pseudo-Riemannian submanifold. Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} applied to $g$ then gives signature $(r-1,s)$ in the positive case and $(r,s-1)$ in the negative one.
\end{proof}

Every hypothesis of this corollary is one a reader has in hand: an ambient metric, a codimension-one immersion, and the sign of the normal vectors. Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} takes the induced metric $g$ as given, and Definition \ref{def:pseudo-induced-metric} cannot produce it without nondegeneracy; for an indefinite $\tilde{g}$ that nondegeneracy is available from no source other than the non-nullity of the normals.

If $(\bar{M}, \bar{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold and $f \in C^\infty(M)$, then the \emph{gradient} of $f$ is defined as the smooth vector field $\text{grad} f = (df)^\#$ just as in the Riemannian case.

The gradient of the preceding paragraph makes sense on any pseudo-Riemannian manifold, and its fibrewise construction is the following. On a vector space carrying a nondegenerate symmetric bilinear form there is no Riesz representation to appeal to, since the form need not be positive definite; nondegeneracy alone suffices.

\begin{definition}[The gradient of a functional relative to a scalar product]
\label{def:bilinear-gradient}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.bilinGrad}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Let $V$ be a finite-dimensional real vector space, let $B$ be a nondegenerate symmetric bilinear form on $V$, and let $a \in V^*$. The \emph{$B$-gradient} $a^\sharp$ of $a$ is the unique $v \in V$ with $B(v, w) = a(w)$ for all $w \in V$.
\end{definition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:nondegenerate-symmetric-bilinear}
Consider the linear map $\Phi : V \to V^*$, $\Phi(v) = B(v,\cdot)$. Its kernel is $\{v : B(v,w) = 0 \text{ for all } w\}$, which is trivial exactly because $B$ is nondegenerate, so $\Phi$ is injective. Since $\dim V^* = \dim V < \infty$, $\Phi$ is therefore bijective, and $a^\sharp := \Phi^{-1}(a)$ is the unique vector with $B(a^\sharp, w) = a(w)$ for all $w$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The $B$-gradient is orthogonal to the kernel of the functional]
\label{lem:bilinear-gradient-orthogonal-ker}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.bilinGrad_mem_orthogonal_ker}
\leanok
\uses{def:bilinear-gradient}
With $V$, $B$ and $a$ as in Definition \ref{def:bilinear-gradient}, $a^\sharp \in (\ker a)^\perp$, where $\perp$ is taken with respect to $B$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:bilinear-gradient}
Let $w \in \ker a$. By symmetry of $B$ and the defining property of $a^\sharp$, $B(w, a^\sharp) = B(a^\sharp, w) = a(w) = 0$. As $w$ was arbitrary, $a^\sharp \in (\ker a)^\perp$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Signature drop on the kernel of a functional]
\label{prop:embedding-criterion-pointwise}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.sigPos_sigNeg_restrict_ker_bilinGrad}
\leanok
\uses{def:bilinear-gradient,lem:bilinear-gradient-orthogonal-ker,prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise-hyperplane}
Let $V$ be a finite-dimensional real vector space, let $B$ be a nondegenerate symmetric bilinear form on $V$ of signature $(r,s)$, and let $a \in V^*$ be nonzero. If $B(a^\sharp, a^\sharp) > 0$ then $B|_{\ker a}$ has signature $(r-1, s)$; and if $B(a^\sharp, a^\sharp) < 0$ then $B|_{\ker a}$ has signature $(r, s-1)$.

This is the fibrewise content of Corollary \ref{cor:pseudo-riemannian-embedding-criterion}, with $V = T_p \bar{M}$, $B = \bar{g}_p$, $a = df_p$, $a^\sharp = \operatorname{grad} f|_p$ and $\ker a = T_p M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:bilinear-gradient,lem:bilinear-gradient-orthogonal-ker,prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise-hyperplane}
Since $a \neq 0$ it is surjective onto $\mathbb{R}$, so by rank--nullity $\dim \ker a + 1 = \dim V$; that is, $W = \ker a$ is a hyperplane. The vector $x = a^\sharp$ lies in $W^\perp$ by Lemma \ref{lem:bilinear-gradient-orthogonal-ker}, and $x \neq 0$: if $a^\sharp = 0$ then $a(w) = B(a^\sharp, w) = B(0,w) = 0$ for every $w$, contradicting $a \neq 0$. Proposition \ref{prop:hypersurface-signature-pointwise-hyperplane} applied to $W$ and $x$ now gives exactly the two claimed signature drops, according to the sign of $B(x,x) = B(a^\sharp, a^\sharp)$.
\end{proof}

The signature computation above does not by itself say that $B|_{\ker a}$ is \emph{nondegenerate}, which is what "pseudo-Riemannian submanifold" asserts and what constructing the induced metric requires. It comes from the same source.

\begin{lemma}[Nondegeneracy on the kernel of a functional]
\label{lem:embedding-criterion-nondegenerate-pointwise}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.nondegenerate_restrict_ker_bilinGrad}
\leanok
\uses{def:bilinear-gradient,lem:bilinear-gradient-orthogonal-ker,lem:hyperplane-eq-complement-normal-line,lem:orthogonal-complement-non-null-line-nondegenerate}
Let $V$ be a finite-dimensional real vector space, let $B$ be a nondegenerate symmetric bilinear form on $V$, and let $a \in V^*$ be nonzero. If $B(a^\sharp, a^\sharp) \neq 0$ then $B|_{\ker a}$ is nondegenerate.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:bilinear-gradient-orthogonal-ker,lem:hyperplane-eq-complement-normal-line,lem:orthogonal-complement-non-null-line-nondegenerate}
Since $a \neq 0$, rank--nullity makes $\ker a$ a hyperplane, and $a^\sharp$ is a nonzero element of $(\ker a)^\perp$ by Lemma \ref{lem:bilinear-gradient-orthogonal-ker}. Hence $\ker a = \langle a^\sharp \rangle^{\perp}$ by Lemma \ref{lem:hyperplane-eq-complement-normal-line}. The hypothesis $B(a^\sharp, a^\sharp) \neq 0$ says the line $\langle a^\sharp\rangle$ is non-null, and the $B$-orthogonal complement of a non-null line is nondegenerate by Lemma \ref{lem:orthogonal-complement-non-null-line-nondegenerate}.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}
\label{cor:pseudo-riemannian-embedding-criterion}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.hasSignature_pseudoPullbackMetric_levelSet_of_pseudoGrad_pos, LeeLib.Ch02.hasSignature_pseudoPullbackMetric_levelSet_of_pseudoGrad_neg, LeeLib.Ch02.form_pseudoGrad_mfderiv_levelSet_val_eq_zero}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.71}
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:pseudo-metric-has-signature,prop:embedding-criterion-pointwise,def:bilinear-gradient,prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one,prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel}
Suppose $(\bar{M}, \bar{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold of signature $(r, s)$, $f \in C^\infty(\bar{M})$, and $M = f^{-1}(c)$ for some $c \in \mathbb{R}$. If $\bar{g}(\text{grad} f, \text{grad} f) > 0$ everywhere on $M$, then $M$ is an embedded pseudo-Riemannian submanifold of $\bar{M}$ of signature $(r-1, s)$; and if $\bar{g}(\text{grad} f, \text{grad} f) < 0$ everywhere on $M$, then $M$ is an embedded pseudo-Riemannian submanifold of $\bar{M}$ of signature $(r, s-1)$. In either case, $\text{grad} f$ is everywhere normal to $M$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{prop:embedding-criterion-pointwise,prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one,prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel}
The hypothesis $\bar{g}(\operatorname{grad} f, \operatorname{grad} f) \neq 0$ on $M$ forces $df_p \neq 0$ at each $p \in M$, so $c$ is a regular value of $f$; since $f$ is real-valued, the codimension-one case suffices, and Propositions \ref{prop:regular-level-set-hypersurface-codim-one} and \ref{prop:regular-level-set-tangent-kernel} show that $M = f^{-1}(c)$ is an embedded smooth hypersurface with $T_p M = \ker df_p$ for each $p \in M$. Fix such a $p$ and apply Proposition \ref{prop:embedding-criterion-pointwise} with $V = T_p \bar{M}$, $B = \bar{g}_p$ and $a = df_p$: since $\operatorname{grad} f|_p = (df_p)^\sharp$, the signature of $\bar{g}_p|_{T_p M}$ is $(r-1,s)$ when $\bar{g}_p(\operatorname{grad} f|_p, \operatorname{grad} f|_p) > 0$ and $(r,s-1)$ when it is negative. In particular $\bar{g}_p|_{T_p M}$ is nondegenerate at every $p$, so $M$ is a pseudo-Riemannian submanifold of the stated signature. That $\operatorname{grad} f$ is normal to $M$ is Lemma \ref{lem:bilinear-gradient-orthogonal-ker} applied at each $p$, since $T_p M = \ker df_p$.
\end{proof}

As in the Riemannian case (Proposition \ref{prop:adapted-orthonormal-frames-immersed}), a submanifold is presented by a smooth immersion $F : M \to \bar{M}$, and the bundle in which an adapted frame lives is the ambient tangent bundle along $M$, that is, the pullback $F^* T\bar{M}$, whose fibre at $p$ is $T_{F(p)}\bar{M}$. The ambient metric restricts to each such fibre, making $F^* T\bar{M}$ a bundle of scalar product spaces.

\begin{lemma}[The Ambient Metric Along a Submanifold]
\label{lem:pullback-pseudo-metric-bundle}
\lean{Bundle.ContMDiffPseudoMetric.pullback}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on a vector bundle $V \to B$ and let $F : B' \to B$ be smooth. Then $(F^* g)_x := g_{F(x)}$ is a smooth field of scalar products on the pullback bundle $F^* V \to B'$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
Fibrewise there is nothing to prove: $(F^*V)_x$ \emph{is} $V_{F(x)}$, so $g_{F(x)}$ is already a bilinear form on it, and symmetry and nondegeneracy are those of $g$ at $F(x)$ verbatim. It remains to see that the form varies smoothly with $x \in B'$. Read in a trivialization of $F^*V$ at $x_0$, it is the field $g$ read in the trivialization of $V$ at $F(x_0)$ and precomposed with $F$; the first is smooth because $g$ is, the second because $F$ is, and a composite of smooth maps is smooth.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[The Tensor Induced Along a Map]
\label{def:induced-tensor-along-map}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.IsPullbackAlong}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric}
Let $F : M \to \bar{M}$ be smooth, let $\bar{g}$ be a pseudo-Riemannian metric on $\bar{M}$, and let $g$ be a pseudo-Riemannian metric on $M$. We say $g$ is \emph{induced by $\bar{g}$ along $F$}, and write $g = F^*\bar{g}$, if
\[
  g_p(v,w) \;=\; \bar{g}_{F(p)}\bigl(dF_p(v), dF_p(w)\bigr)
  \qquad\text{for all } p \in M \text{ and } v,w \in T_pM.
\]
That such a $g$ exists is precisely Lee's condition that $M$ be a pseudo-Riemannian submanifold: it says the pullback tensor $F^*\bar{g}$ is itself nondegenerate.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[A Pseudo-Riemannian Submanifold is Immersed]
\label{lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.injective_mfderiv_of_isPullbackAlong}
\leanok
\uses{def:induced-tensor-along-map}
Let $F : M \to \bar{M}$ be smooth and suppose the pseudo-Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$ is induced by $\bar{g}$ along $F$. Then $dF_p$ is injective for every $p \in M$; that is, $F$ is an immersion.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:induced-tensor-along-map}
Let $v \in T_pM$ satisfy $dF_p(v) = 0$. For every $w \in T_pM$,
\[
  g_p(v,w) = \bar{g}_{F(p)}\bigl(dF_p(v), dF_p(w)\bigr) = \bar{g}_{F(p)}\bigl(0, dF_p(w)\bigr) = 0 .
\]
So $v$ is orthogonal to all of $T_pM$ with respect to $g_p$, and nondegeneracy of $g_p$ forces $v = 0$. Since $dF_p$ is linear with trivial kernel, it is injective.
\end{proof}

This is the precise sense in which nondegeneracy of the induced tensor, and not immersivity, is the substantive hypothesis: it implies the latter. It does not come for free — the diagonal $\{\xi = \tau\}$ in $\mathbb{R}^{1,1}$ is an embedded line on which the induced tensor vanishes identically — whereas in the Riemannian case the two conditions coincide, by Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-immersion-metric}.

\begin{lemma}[A Riemannian Submanifold is a Pseudo-Riemannian Submanifold]
\label{lem:riemannian-submanifold-induced-tensor}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isPullbackAlong_pullbackMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:induced-tensor-along-map,lem:pullback-immersion-metric,def:induced-metric}
Let $F : M \to \bar{M}$ be a smooth immersion and let $\bar{g}$ be a Riemannian metric on $\bar{M}$. Then the induced metric $F^*\bar{g}$ of Definition \ref{def:induced-metric} is induced by $\bar{g}$ along $F$ in the sense of Definition \ref{def:induced-tensor-along-map}.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:induced-tensor-along-map,lem:pullback-immersion-metric,def:induced-metric}
By Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-immersion-metric} the tensor $F^*\bar{g}$ is positive definite, hence nondegenerate, so it is a pseudo-Riemannian metric on $M$. Its defining formula is $(F^*\bar{g})_p(v,w) = \bar{g}_{F(p)}(dF_p v, dF_p w)$, which is the condition of Definition \ref{def:induced-tensor-along-map}.
\end{proof}

For an indefinite ambient metric that route is closed, and the pullback must be built by hand. It is worth isolating first what pulling back actually uses, which is nothing beyond bilinearity --- and indeed nothing beyond \emph{linearity of the transporting maps}, which need not be differentials at all.

\begin{definition}[Transport of a Field of Bilinear Forms by a Family of Linear Maps]
\label{def:bilinear-comp-of-family}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.bilinearCompOf}
\leanok
Let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be a map, let $b$ assign to each $y \in \tilde{M}$ a bilinear form $b_y$ on $T_y\tilde{M}$, and let $A$ assign to each $p \in M$ a linear map $A_p : T_pM \to T_{F(p)}\tilde{M}$. The \emph{transport of $b$ by $A$} is the field of bilinear forms on $M$ defined by
\[
  (A^*b)_p(v,w) \;=\; b_{F(p)}\bigl(A_p v,\, A_p w\bigr), \qquad v,w \in T_pM .
\]
Each $(A^*b)_p$ is indeed bilinear, being the composite of the bilinear $b_{F(p)}$ with the linear map $A_p$ in each of its two arguments.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[Transport by a Smooth Family of Linear Maps is Smooth]
\label{lem:bilinear-comp-of-family-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffAt_bilinearCompOf}
\leanok
\uses{def:bilinear-comp-of-family}
Let $b$ be a smooth field of bilinear forms on $T\tilde{M}$, let $p_0 \in M$, let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be smooth at $p_0$, and let $A_p : T_pM \to T_{F(p)}\tilde{M}$ be a family of linear maps whose representation in tangent coordinates is smooth at $p_0$. Then the section $p \mapsto (A^*b)_p$ is smooth at $p_0$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:bilinear-comp-of-family}
Smoothness is local, so read the section $p \mapsto (A^*b)_p$ in tangent coordinates around $p_0$ and around $F(p_0)$. It becomes the map
\[
  p \;\longmapsto\; B(F(p))\bigl(D(p)\,\cdot,\, D(p)\,\cdot\bigr),
\]
where $D(p)$ is $A_p$ read in tangent coordinates and $B$ is $b$ read in coordinates, both with values in fixed model spaces. Now $p \mapsto D(p)$ is smooth at $p_0$ by hypothesis, and $p \mapsto B(F(p))$ is smooth at $p_0$ as a composite of the smooth section $b$ with $F$. Finally the map $(\beta, A) \mapsto \beta(A\,\cdot, A\,\cdot)$, from pairs of a form on the model space of $T\tilde{M}$ and an operator into it, to forms on the model space of $TM$, is continuous and polynomial in its arguments --- linear in $\beta$ and quadratic in $A$ --- hence smooth. The coordinate representation is the composite of these, so it is smooth at $p_0$.
\end{proof}

The transporting family is left arbitrary because the two consumers need exactly that. Pulling back along $F$ is the case $A_p = dF_p$, immediately below; but the quotient metric of Theorem \ref{thm:group-action-riemannian-submersion} is the case where $A_p$ is the horizontal lift $L_{\sigma(p)}$ of a Riemannian submersion along a local section, and that family is \emph{not} the differential of any map.

\begin{definition}[The Pullback of a Field of Bilinear Forms]
\label{def:pullback-form-of-family}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pullbackFormOf}
\leanok
\uses{def:bilinear-comp-of-family}
Let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be a map and let $b$ assign to each $y \in \tilde{M}$ a bilinear form $b_y$ on $T_y\tilde{M}$. The \emph{pullback of $b$ along $F$} is the transport of $b$ by the family of differentials $A_p = dF_p$ in the sense of Definition \ref{def:bilinear-comp-of-family}:
\[
  (F^*b)_p(v,w) \;=\; b_{F(p)}\bigl(dF_p v,\, dF_p w\bigr), \qquad v,w \in T_pM .
\]
Each $(F^*b)_p$ is indeed bilinear, being the composite of the bilinear $b_{F(p)}$ with the linear map $dF_p$ in each of its two arguments.
\end{definition}

Neither positivity, nor nondegeneracy, nor even symmetry of $b$ plays any part here, so both the Riemannian pullback of Definition \ref{def:induced-metric} and the indefinite one of Definition \ref{def:pseudo-pullback-form} below are instances of this definition, and both inherit its smoothness.

\begin{lemma}[The Pullback of a Smooth Field of Forms is Smooth]
\label{lem:pullback-form-of-family-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiff_pullbackFormOf}
\leanok
\uses{def:pullback-form-of-family,lem:bilinear-comp-of-family-smooth}
Let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be smooth and let $b$ be a smooth field of bilinear forms on $T\tilde{M}$, that is, a smooth section $y \mapsto b_y$ of the bundle of bilinear forms. Then $F^*b$ is a smooth field of bilinear forms on $TM$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pullback-form-of-family,lem:bilinear-comp-of-family-smooth}
This is the case $A_p = dF_p$ of Lemma \ref{lem:bilinear-comp-of-family-smooth}, applied at each $p_0 \in M$. Its hypothesis on $A$ holds: the differential of a smooth map, read in tangent coordinates, is smooth in the base point, which is the standard smoothness of $x \mapsto dF_x$ as a section of the bundle $\mathrm{Hom}(TM, F^*T\tilde{M})$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[The Pullback of an Indefinite Metric]
\label{def:pseudo-pullback-form}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoPullbackForm}
\leanok
\uses{def:pullback-form-of-family,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold and let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be a map. The \emph{pullback tensor} $F^*\tilde{g}$ is the pullback of the field $y \mapsto \tilde{g}_y$ along $F$ in the sense of Definition \ref{def:pullback-form-of-family}:
\[
  (F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,w) \;=\; \tilde{g}_{F(p)}\bigl(dF_p v,\, dF_p w\bigr), \qquad v,w \in T_pM .
\]
\end{definition}

This is Lee's $\iota^*\tilde{g}$. It is a field of bilinear forms for every $F$ whatever; whether it is a \emph{metric} is a separate question, and the one Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} answers.

\begin{lemma}[The Pullback Tensor is Symmetric]
\label{lem:pseudo-pullback-form-symm}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoPullbackForm_symm}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-pullback-form,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric}
With $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ and $F$ as in Definition \ref{def:pseudo-pullback-form}, $(F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,w) = (F^*\tilde{g})_p(w,v)$ for every $p \in M$ and $v,w \in T_pM$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-pullback-form,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric}
Unwinding Definition \ref{def:pseudo-pullback-form} twice and using symmetry of $\tilde{g}_{F(p)}$ in the middle step,
\[
  (F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,w) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}(dF_pv, dF_pw) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}(dF_pw, dF_pv) = (F^*\tilde{g})_p(w,v) .
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[The Pullback Tensor is Smooth]
\label{lem:pseudo-pullback-form-smooth}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoPullbackForm_contMDiff}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-pullback-form,lem:pullback-form-of-family-smooth,def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
With $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ as in Definition \ref{def:pseudo-pullback-form} and $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ smooth, the field $p \mapsto (F^*\tilde{g})_p$ is smooth.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-pullback-form,lem:pullback-form-of-family-smooth,def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
By condition (c) of Definition \ref{def:smooth-field-scalar-products}, $y \mapsto \tilde{g}_y$ is a smooth section of the bundle of bilinear forms on $T\tilde{M}$, so Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-form-of-family-smooth} applies to it verbatim. Nondegeneracy of $\tilde{g}$ plays no part.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[The Metric Induced by an Indefinite Ambient Metric]
\label{def:pseudo-induced-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoPullbackMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-pullback-form,lem:pseudo-pullback-form-symm,lem:pseudo-pullback-form-smooth,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$ be a pseudo-Riemannian manifold and let $F : M \to \tilde{M}$ be smooth. Suppose the pullback tensor $F^*\tilde{g}$ is nondegenerate at every point, that is, for every $p \in M$ and every nonzero $v \in T_pM$ there is a $w \in T_pM$ with $(F^*\tilde{g})_p(v,w) \neq 0$. Then $F^*\tilde{g}$ is a pseudo-Riemannian metric on $M$, the \emph{metric induced by $F$}.
\end{definition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{lem:pseudo-pullback-form-symm,lem:pseudo-pullback-form-smooth,def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:smooth-field-scalar-products}
The three conditions of Definition \ref{def:smooth-field-scalar-products} for the tangent bundle of $M$ are met in turn: symmetry at each point is Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-pullback-form-symm}, nondegeneracy at each point is the hypothesis, in exactly the witness form (b) requires, and smoothness of the section is Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-pullback-form-smooth}. So $F^*\tilde{g}$ is a smooth field of scalar products on $TM$, which by Definition \ref{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric} is a pseudo-Riemannian metric.
\end{proof}

Nondegeneracy is a genuine hypothesis here, and this is where the indefinite case parts company with the Riemannian one. By Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-immersion-metric}, positive definiteness of a pullback is \emph{equivalent} to $F$ being an immersion, so in the Riemannian case the immersion hypothesis alone builds the metric, as Definition \ref{def:induced-metric} does. Nondegeneracy of a pullback is not implied by immersion: a nondegenerate form can restrict to a degenerate one on a subspace, as the diagonal $\{\xi = \tau\}$ in $\mathbb{R}^{1,1}$ already shows. Deciding when it does not is the content of Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature}, and that is why the proposition exists at all.

\begin{lemma}[A Pseudo-Riemannian Pullback is an Induced Tensor]
\label{lem:pseudo-riemannian-submanifold-induced-tensor}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isPullbackAlong_pseudoPullbackMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-induced-metric,def:pseudo-pullback-form,def:induced-tensor-along-map}
Let $(\tilde{M},\tilde{g})$, $F$ and the nondegeneracy hypothesis be as in Definition \ref{def:pseudo-induced-metric}. Then the induced metric $F^*\tilde{g}$ is induced by $\tilde{g}$ along $F$ in the sense of Definition \ref{def:induced-tensor-along-map}.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-induced-metric,def:pseudo-pullback-form,def:induced-tensor-along-map}
By Definition \ref{def:pseudo-induced-metric} the tensor $g = F^*\tilde{g}$ is a pseudo-Riemannian metric on $M$, so it is eligible. The condition of Definition \ref{def:induced-tensor-along-map} is $g_p(v,w) = \tilde{g}_{F(p)}(dF_pv, dF_pw)$ for all $p$, $v$ and $w$, and for $g = F^*\tilde{g}$ that is the defining formula of Definition \ref{def:pseudo-pullback-form}.
\end{proof}

This is the indefinite counterpart of Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-submanifold-induced-tensor}, and it is what makes the hypothesis of Definition \ref{def:induced-tensor-along-map} --- assumed by Propositions \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} and \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-adapted-frame} and by Corollary \ref{cor:normal-bundle-pseudo-riemannian-submanifold} alike --- available for a genuinely indefinite ambient metric. Lemma \ref{lem:riemannian-submanifold-induced-tensor} supplies that hypothesis only when $\tilde{g}$ is Riemannian, and in that case Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-hypersurface-signature} says no more than that a hypersurface of a Riemannian manifold is Riemannian.

\begin{lemma}[Extension of a Nondegenerate Family to a Nondegenerate Frame]
\label{lem:extend-nondegenerate-family}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_isLocalNondegenerateOn_extend}
\leanok
\uses{def:local-nondegenerate-family,lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases,lem:nondegenerate-tuple-independent,lem:nondegenerate-tuple-basis,lem:sections-through-prescribed-basis,lem:open-nondegenerate-tuple}
Let $g$ be a smooth field of scalar products on a rank-$m$ vector bundle $V \to B$, let $u \subseteq B$ be open, let $x_0 \in u$, and let $X_1,\ldots,X_k$ be sections of $V$ that are nondegenerate on $u$. Then $k \leq m$, and there are an open set $v$ with $x_0 \in v \subseteq u$ and sections $Y_1,\ldots,Y_m$ of $V$, nondegenerate on $v$, with $Y_i = X_i$ on $v$ for every $i \leq k$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:local-nondegenerate-family,lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases,lem:nondegenerate-tuple-independent,lem:nondegenerate-tuple-basis,lem:sections-through-prescribed-basis,lem:open-nondegenerate-tuple}
The tuple $(X_1|_{x_0},\ldots,X_k|_{x_0})$ is nondegenerate in the $m$-dimensional scalar product space $(V_{x_0}, g_{x_0})$, hence linearly independent by Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-independent}; so $k \leq m$. By Lemma \ref{lem:completion-nondegenerate-bases} it extends to a nondegenerate $m$-tuple $(w_1,\ldots,w_m)$ of $V_{x_0}$ with $w_i = X_i|_{x_0}$ for $i \leq k$, and by Lemma \ref{lem:nondegenerate-tuple-basis} that tuple is a basis of $V_{x_0}$.

Lemma \ref{lem:sections-through-prescribed-basis} applied to this basis yields an open $v_1 \ni x_0$ and sections $W_1,\ldots,W_m$, smooth on $v_1$, with $W_j|_{x_0} = w_j$. Define
\[
  Y_j \;=\; \begin{cases} X_j, & j \leq k,\\ W_j, & j > k,\end{cases}
\]
so that $Y_j$ is smooth on $u \cap v_1$ for every $j$, and $Y_j|_{x_0} = w_j$ for every $j$: for $j \leq k$ because $w_j = X_j|_{x_0}$, and for $j > k$ by the choice of $W_j$. Hence $(Y_1|_{x_0},\ldots,Y_m|_{x_0}) = (w_1,\ldots,w_m)$ is a nondegenerate $m$-tuple, and Lemma \ref{lem:open-nondegenerate-tuple} produces an open $v$ with $x_0 \in v \subseteq u \cap v_1$ on which $(Y_1,\ldots,Y_m)$ is nondegenerate. Finally $Y_i = X_i$ on $v$ for $i \leq k$ by construction.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Pseudo-Riemannian Adapted Orthonormal Frames]
\label{prop:pseudo-riemannian-adapted-frame}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_adapted_pseudo_orthonormalFrame}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.72}
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:induced-tensor-along-map,lem:pullback-pseudo-metric-bundle,prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian,lem:extend-nondegenerate-family,prop:orthonormalize-nondegenerate-family,lem:orthonormal-tuple-nondegenerate,lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
Suppose $(\bar{M}, \bar{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian manifold, and $F : M \to \bar{M}$ presents $M$ as a pseudo-Riemannian or Riemannian submanifold, the induced metric being $g$. For each $p \in M$, there exists a smooth orthonormal frame for $F^* T\bar{M}$ on a neighborhood of $p$ that is adapted to $M$, in the sense that its first $n = \dim M$ members span $dF_x(T_xM)$ at every point $x$ of that neighborhood.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:induced-tensor-along-map,lem:pullback-pseudo-metric-bundle,prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian,lem:extend-nondegenerate-family,prop:orthonormalize-nondegenerate-family,lem:orthonormal-tuple-nondegenerate,lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}
Write $m = \dim\bar{M}$ and $n = \dim M$, and equip $F^*T\bar{M}$ with the ambient metric $F^*\bar{g}$ of Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-pseudo-metric-bundle}. By Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-submanifold-immersion}, $F$ is an immersion, so $dF_x$ is injective and $\dim dF_x(T_xM) = n$ for every $x$.

\emph{The tangential half.} By Proposition \ref{prop:orthonormal-frames-pseudo-riemannian} applied to $(M,g)$ there are an open $u \ni p$ and a smooth orthonormal frame $(E_1,\ldots,E_n)$ for $TM$ on $u$. Put $Z_i = dF(E_i)$, a smooth section of $F^*T\bar{M}$ on $u$. Because $g$ is induced by $\bar{g}$ along $F$,
\[
  (F^*\bar{g})_x(Z_i|_x, Z_j|_x) = \bar{g}_{F(x)}\bigl(dF_x(E_i|_x), dF_x(E_j|_x)\bigr) = g_x(E_i|_x, E_j|_x),
\]
so $(Z_1,\ldots,Z_n)$ is orthonormal at every $x \in u$, and hence nondegenerate there by Lemma \ref{lem:orthonormal-tuple-nondegenerate}. This is the step the Riemannian argument does not need and cannot be replaced by a coordinate frame: an arbitrary frame of $\bar{M}$ pushed to the fibres need not be nondegenerate.

\emph{Extension and orthonormalization.} By Lemma \ref{lem:extend-nondegenerate-family} there are an open $v$ with $p \in v \subseteq u$ and sections $(Y_1,\ldots,Y_m)$ of $F^*T\bar{M}$, nondegenerate on $v$, with $Y_i = Z_i$ on $v$ for $i \leq n$. Proposition \ref{prop:orthonormalize-nondegenerate-family} applied to $(Y_j)$ yields sections $(b_1,\ldots,b_m)$, smooth and orthonormal at every point of $v$, with $\operatorname{span}(b_1|_x,\ldots,b_k|_x) = \operatorname{span}(Y_1|_x,\ldots,Y_k|_x)$ for every $k \leq m$ and $x \in v$. Being orthonormal, $(b_1|_x,\ldots,b_m|_x)$ is linearly independent, and $m$ independent vectors in the $m$-dimensional fibre form a basis; so $(b_j)$ is a frame on $v$.

\emph{Adaptedness.} Fix $x \in v$ and $k \leq n$. Then
\[
  \operatorname{span}(b_1|_x,\ldots,b_k|_x) = \operatorname{span}(Y_1|_x,\ldots,Y_k|_x) = \operatorname{span}(Z_1|_x,\ldots,Z_k|_x) \subseteq dF_x(T_xM),
\]
the last inclusion because each $Z_i|_x = dF_x(E_i|_x)$ lies in the image of $dF_x$, which is a subspace. Taking $k = n$, the left-hand side is spanned by $n$ linearly independent vectors, so has dimension $n$; as it is contained in $dF_x(T_xM)$, which also has dimension $n$, the two are equal. Thus $(b_1,\ldots,b_n)$ spans $dF_x(T_xM)$ at every $x \in v$, which is the assertion that $(b_1,\ldots,b_m)$ is adapted to $M$.
\end{proof}

The next corollary is proved in the same way as Proposition 2.16.

\begin{definition}[The Normal Space of a Pseudo-Riemannian Submanifold]
\label{def:pseudo-normal-space}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.pseudoNormalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,lem:pullback-pseudo-metric-bundle}
Let $(\bar{M},\bar{g})$ be a pseudo-Riemannian $m$-manifold and let $F : M \to \bar{M}$ present an $n$-manifold $M$ as a smooth immersion. For $x \in M$, the \emph{normal space to $M$ at $x$} is the subspace of the ambient tangent space $T_{F(x)}\bar{M}$ given by
\[
  N_x M \;=\; \bigl\{\, v \in T_{F(x)}\bar{M} \;:\; \bar{g}_{F(x)}(v,w) = 0 \text{ for every } w \in dF_x(T_xM) \,\bigr\}.
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[Local Frames for the Normal Space]
\label{lem:pseudo-normal-space-frame}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_pseudo_localFrame_normalSpace}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,prop:pseudo-riemannian-adapted-frame,lem:orthonormal-tuple-nondegenerate}
Let $(\bar{M},\bar{g})$ be a pseudo-Riemannian $m$-manifold and let $F : M \to \bar{M}$ present $M$ as an $n$-dimensional pseudo-Riemannian or Riemannian submanifold. Every $p \in M$ has a neighborhood $v$ carrying $m-n$ sections of $F^* T\bar{M}$, smooth on $v$, whose values at each $x \in v$ are orthonormal and span $N_x M$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,prop:pseudo-riemannian-adapted-frame,lem:orthonormal-tuple-nondegenerate}
Let $(b_1,\ldots,b_m)$ be an adapted orthonormal frame on a neighborhood $v$ of $p$, as furnished by Proposition \ref{prop:pseudo-riemannian-adapted-frame}, and take $N_j = b_{n+j}$ for $j = 1,\ldots,m-n$. These are smooth on $v$, and orthonormal at each $x \in v$, both being inherited from $(b_i)$.

\emph{They are normal.} Fix $x \in v$ and $j$. For every $i \leq n$ we have $\langle N_j|_x, b_i|_x \rangle = 0$, the indices $n+j$ and $i$ being distinct; so $N_j|_x$ is orthogonal to each member of $(b_1|_x,\ldots,b_n|_x)$, and hence to their span, which is $dF_x(T_xM)$ by adaptedness. Thus $N_j|_x \in N_xM$.

\emph{They span.} The previous paragraph gives $\operatorname{span}(N_1|_x,\ldots,N_{m-n}|_x) \subseteq N_xM$. For the reverse, let $w \in N_xM$. Being orthonormal, $(b_1|_x,\ldots,b_m|_x)$ is linearly independent and so a basis of the $m$-dimensional fibre; write $w = \sum_a c_a b_a|_x$. Pairing with $b_a|_x$ and using orthogonality of the distinct members,
\[
  \langle b_a|_x, w\rangle = c_a \langle b_a|_x, b_a|_x\rangle .
\]
For $a \leq n$ the vector $b_a|_x$ lies in $dF_x(T_xM)$, so $\langle b_a|_x, w \rangle = 0$ because $w$ is normal; and $\langle b_a|_x,b_a|_x\rangle = \pm 1 \neq 0$ by orthonormality, whence $c_a = 0$. (It is here that the indefinite case needs orthonormality rather than mere independence: a null $b_a$ would make the displayed equation vacuous and leave $c_a$ undetermined.) So $w = \sum_{a > n} c_a b_a|_x$ lies in $\operatorname{span}(N_1|_x,\ldots,N_{m-n}|_x)$.
\end{proof}

Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-frame} is the hypothesis of Lemma \ref{lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles}, but that lemma additionally requires the ambient bundle to carry a Riemannian fibre metric, which it uses only to normalize the given frames. An indefinite $\bar{g}$ is not such a metric, and Proposition \ref{prop:normal-bundle}'s move of supplying the ambient metric itself is therefore unavailable. The remedy is that the fibre metric of Lemma \ref{lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles} is required to bear no relation to the family of subspaces: an arbitrary Riemannian metric on $\bar{M}$ serves, and one exists by Proposition \ref{prop:existence-riemannian-metric}.

\begin{corollary}[Normal Bundle to a Pseudo-Riemannian Submanifold]
\label{cor:normal-bundle-pseudo-riemannian-submanifold}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.contMDiffVectorBundle_pseudoNormalSpace}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2.73}
\uses{def:pseudo-riemannian-metric,def:pseudo-normal-space,prop:pseudo-riemannian-adapted-frame,lem:pseudo-normal-space-frame,lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles,prop:existence-riemannian-metric}
Suppose $(\bar{M}, \bar{g})$ is a pseudo-Riemannian $m$-manifold, and $F : M \to \bar{M}$ presents $M$ as an $n$-dimensional embedded or immersed pseudo-Riemannian or Riemannian submanifold. The set of vectors normal to $M$ is a smooth rank-$(m-n)$ vector subbundle of $T\bar{M}|_M$, called the \emph{normal bundle} to $M$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:pseudo-normal-space,lem:pseudo-normal-space-frame,lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles,prop:existence-riemannian-metric}
By Proposition \ref{prop:existence-riemannian-metric} choose a Riemannian metric $h$ on $\bar{M}$; by Lemma \ref{lem:ambient-tangent-bundle-riemannian} it makes $F^*T\bar{M}$ a Riemannian vector bundle over $M$. Lemma \ref{lem:pseudo-normal-space-frame} provides, near every point of $M$, sections of $F^*T\bar{M}$ smooth there and forming a basis of $N_xM$ at each point, orthonormality for $\bar{g}$ giving in particular linear independence. This is exactly the hypothesis of Lemma \ref{lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles} applied to the bundle $F^*T\bar{M}$ with fibre metric $h$ and the family of subspaces $D_x = N_xM$, which therefore exhibits $NM$ as a smooth rank-$(m-n)$ subbundle of $F^*T\bar{M}$. The metric $h$ enters only through Lemma \ref{lem:local-frame-criterion-for-subbundles}'s normalization of frames and is unrelated to $\bar{g}$; the subspaces $N_xM$ and the frames spanning them are defined by $\bar{g}$ alone.
\end{proof}

\section{Other Generalizations of Riemannian Metrics}

Pseudo-Riemannian metrics are obtained by relaxing the positivity requirement for Riemannian metrics. In addition, there are other useful generalizations that result when we relax other requirements. In this section we touch briefly on two of those generalizations. We will not treat these anywhere else in the book, but it is useful to know the definitions.

\subsection{Sub-Riemannian Metrics}

The first generalization arises when we relax the requirement that the metric be defined on the whole tangent space.

A \emph{sub-Riemannian metric} (also sometimes known as a \emph{singular Riemannian metric} or \emph{Carnot--Carathéodory metric}) on a smooth manifold $M$ is a smooth fiber metric on a smooth tangent distribution $S \subseteq TM$ (i.e., a vector subbundle of $TM$). Since lengths make sense only for vectors in $S$, the only curves whose lengths can be measured are those whose velocity vectors lie everywhere in $S$. Therefore, one usually imposes some condition on $S$ that guarantees that any two nearby points can be connected by such a curve. This is, in a sense, the opposite of the Frobenius integrability condition, which would restrict every such curve to lie in a single leaf of a foliation.

Sub-Riemannian metrics arise naturally in the study of the abstract models of real submanifolds of complex space $\mathbb{C}^n$, called \emph{CR manifolds} (short for \emph{Cauchy--Riemann manifolds}). CR manifolds are real manifolds endowed with a tangent distribution $S \subseteq TM$ whose fibers carry the structure of complex vector spaces (with an additional integrability condition that need not concern us here). In the model case of a submanifold $M \subseteq \mathbb{C}^n$, $S$ is the set of vectors tangent to $M$ that remain tangent after multiplication by $i = \sqrt{-1}$ in the ambient complex coordinates. If $S$ is sufficiently far from being integrable, choosing a fiber metric on $S$ results in a sub-Riemannian metric whose geometric properties closely reflect the complex-analytic properties of $M$ as a subset of $\mathbb{C}^n$.

Another motivation for studying sub-Riemannian metrics arises from \emph{control theory}. In this subject, one is given a manifold with a vector field depending on parameters called \emph{controls}, with the goal being to vary the controls so as to obtain a solution curve with desired properties, often one that minimizes some function such as arc length. If the vector field is constrained to be everywhere tangent to a distribution $S$ on the manifold (for example, in the case of a robot arm whose motion is restricted by the orientations of its hinges), then the function can often be modeled as a sub-Riemannian metric and optimal solutions modeled as sub-Riemannian geodesics.

A useful introduction to the geometry of sub-Riemannian metrics is provided in the article [Str86].

\subsection{Finsler Metrics}

Another important generalization arises from relaxing the requirement that norms of vectors be defined in terms of an inner product on each tangent space.

In general, a \emph{norm} on a vector space $V$ is a real-valued function on $V$, usually written $v \mapsto |v|$, that satisfies
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textsc{Homogeneity}: $|cv| = |c||v|$ for $v \in V$ and $c \in \mathbb{R}$;
\item \textsc{Positivity}: $|v| \geq 0$ for $v \in V$, with equality if and only if $v = 0$;
\item \textsc{Triangle Inequality}: $|v + w| \leq |v| + |w|$ for $v, w \in V$.
\end{enumerate}

For example, the length function associated with an inner product is a norm. Now suppose $M$ is a smooth manifold. A \emph{Finsler metric} on $M$ is a continuous function $F: TM \to \mathbb{R}$, smooth on the set of nonzero vectors, whose restriction to each tangent space $T_pM$ is a norm. Again, the norm function associated with a Riemannian metric is a special case.

The inventor of Riemannian geometry himself, Bernhard Riemann, clearly envisioned an important role in $n$-dimensional geometry for what we now call Finsler metrics; he restricted his investigations to the ``Riemannian'' case purely for simplicity (see [Spi79, Vol. 2]). However, it was not until the late twentieth century that Finsler metrics began to be studied seriously from a geometric point of view.

The recent upsurge of interest in Finsler metrics has been motivated in part by the fact that two different Finsler metrics appear very naturally in the theory of several complex variables. For certain bounded open sets in $\mathbb{C}^n$ (the ones with smooth, strictly convex boundaries, for example), the \emph{Kobayashi metric} and the \emph{Carathéodory metric} are intrinsically defined, biholomorphically invariant Finsler metrics. Combining differential-geometric and complex-analytic methods has led to striking new insights into both the function theory and the geometry of such domains. We do not treat Finsler metrics further in this book, but you can consult one of the recent books on the subject [AP94, BCS00, JP13].

\section{Problems}

\begin{exercise}\label{prob:riemannian-1-manifold-flat}\dcref{ch2:2-1}
Show that every Riemannian $1$-manifold is flat. (Used on pp. 13, 193.)
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}\label{prob:distance-preserving-map-linear-isometry}\dcref{ch2:2-2}
Suppose $V$ and $W$ are finite-dimensional real inner product spaces of the same dimension, and $F: V \to W$ is any map (not assumed to be linear or even continuous) that preserves the origin and all distances: $F(0) = 0$ and $|F(x) - F(y)| = |x - y|$ for all $x, y \in V$. Prove that $F$ is a linear isometry. [Hint: First show that $F$ preserves inner products, and then show that it is linear.] (Used on p. 187.)
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}\label{prob:surface-of-revolution-warped-product}\dcref{ch2:2-3}
\uses{ex:warped-products}
Given a smooth embedded $1$-dimensional submanifold $C \subseteq H$ as in Example 2.24(b), show that the surface of revolution $S_C \subseteq \mathbb{R}^3$ with its induced metric is isometric to the warped product $C \times_a S^1$, where $a: C \to \mathbb{R}$ is the distance to the $z$-axis.
\end{exercise}

\begin{definition}[The open submanifold metric]
\label{def:open-submanifold-metric}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.openSubmanifoldMetric}
\leanok
\uses{def:riemannian-metric,def:induced-metric,lem:mfderiv-open-inclusion}
Let $W \subseteq \mathbb{R}^n$ be open and let $g$ be a Riemannian metric on $\mathbb{R}^n$. The metric \emph{induced on $W$} is $\iota^* g$, where $\iota : W \hookrightarrow \mathbb{R}^n$ is the inclusion. By Lemma \ref{lem:mfderiv-open-inclusion} the inclusion is an immersion, so Lemma \ref{lem:pullback-immersion-metric} applies and $\iota^* g$ is a Riemannian metric.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The differential of the inclusion of an open subset]
\label{lem:mfderiv-open-inclusion}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.mfderiv_opens_subtypeVal}
\leanok
An open set $W \subseteq \mathbb{R}^n$ is a smooth manifold whose single chart is the inclusion $\iota : W \hookrightarrow \mathbb{R}^n$, and every tangent space $T_x W$ is canonically $\mathbb{R}^n$. Under these identifications $d\iota_x = \operatorname{id}$ for every $x \in W$. In particular $\iota$ is an immersion.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
The differential is computed from the coordinate representation of $\iota$, which is $\iota \circ \iota^{-1}$ read through the chart. Since the chart of $W$ is the inclusion itself, this composite is the identity map of $\mathbb{R}^n$ \emph{restricted to $W$} — it is undefined outside $W$, so the two maps are not equal globally. As $W$ is open it is a neighborhood of $x$, and a derivative depends only on the germ at the point, so $d\iota_x$ is the derivative of the identity, namely $\operatorname{id}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Tangent directions to the sphere are orthogonal to the radius]
\label{lem:sphere-tangent-orthogonal-radius}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.inner_coe_sphere_mfderiv_eq_zero}
\leanok
\uses{ex:spheres}
Let $\iota : S^{n-1} \hookrightarrow \mathbb{R}^n$ be the inclusion. For every $\omega \in S^{n-1}$ and every $w \in T_\omega S^{n-1}$,
\[
\langle \omega, d\iota_\omega(w)\rangle = 0.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
The image of $d\iota_\omega$ is the orthogonal complement $(\mathbb{R}\omega)^\perp$ of the line spanned by $\omega$. Since $\omega$ lies in $\mathbb{R}\omega$, every element of the image is orthogonal to $\omega$.
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[The polar map]
\label{def:polar-map}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.polarMap}
\leanok
\uses{def:warped-product,ex:spheres,def:open-submanifold-metric}
$\Phi : \mathbb{R}^+ \times S^{n-1} \to \mathbb{R}^n$ is defined by $\Phi(\rho, \omega) = \rho\,\omega$.
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[The differential of the polar map]
\label{lem:mfderiv-polar-map}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.mfderiv_polarMap_apply}
\leanok
\uses{def:polar-map,lem:mfderiv-open-inclusion}
For $(r, \omega) \in \mathbb{R}^+ \times S^{n-1}$ and $(a, w) \in T_r\mathbb{R}^+ \oplus T_\omega S^{n-1}$,
\[
d\Phi_{(r,\omega)}(a, w) = a\,\omega + r\,d\iota_\omega(w).
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
$\Phi$ is a map on a product manifold, so its differential is the sum of its two partial differentials. Holding $\omega$ fixed, $r \mapsto r\,\omega$ is linear in $r$ precomposed with the inclusion $\mathbb{R}^+ \hookrightarrow \mathbb{R}$, whose differential is the identity by Lemma \ref{lem:mfderiv-open-inclusion}; its differential therefore sends $a$ to $a\,\omega$. Holding $r$ fixed, $\omega \mapsto r\,\omega$ is the inclusion $\iota$ followed by the fixed scaling $u \mapsto r\,u$, which is linear, so its differential sends $w$ to $r\,d\iota_\omega(w)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}\label{prob:warped-product-isometry}
\dcref{ch2:2-4}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.isMetricPreserving_polarMap}
\leanok
\uses{ex:warped-products,def:polar-map,lem:mfderiv-polar-map,lem:sphere-tangent-orthogonal-radius,def:metric-preserving,def:open-submanifold-metric,prop:warped-product-constant-one}
Let $\rho: \mathbb{R}^+ \to \mathbb{R}$ be the restriction of the standard coordinate function, and let $\mathbb{R}^+ \times_\rho S^{n-1}$ denote the resulting warped product (see Example 2.24(c)). Define $\Phi: \mathbb{R}^+ \times_\rho S^{n-1} \to \mathbb{R}^n \setminus \{0\}$ by $\Phi(\rho,\omega) = \rho\omega$. Show that $\Phi$ is an isometry between the warped product metric and the Euclidean metric on $\mathbb{R}^n \setminus \{0\}$. (Used on p.~293.)
\end{exercise}

\begin{proof}
\leanok
\uses{def:polar-map,lem:mfderiv-polar-map,lem:sphere-tangent-orthogonal-radius,def:metric-preserving}
We verify $\Phi^*\bar{g} = g_{\mathbb{R}^+} \oplus \rho^2 \mathring{g}$ at each point; that $\Phi$ is a bijection onto $\mathbb{R}^n \setminus \{0\}$ is checked separately below.

Fix $(r,\omega)$ and let $(a,w), (a',w')$ be tangent vectors there. By Lemma \ref{lem:mfderiv-polar-map},
\[
\langle d\Phi(a,w), d\Phi(a',w')\rangle
 = \langle a\omega + r\,d\iota(w),\ a'\omega + r\,d\iota(w')\rangle,
\]
which expands into four terms:
\[
a a' \langle\omega,\omega\rangle + a r \langle \omega, d\iota(w')\rangle + a' r \langle d\iota(w), \omega\rangle + r^2 \langle d\iota(w), d\iota(w')\rangle.
\]
Now $\langle\omega,\omega\rangle = |\omega|^2 = 1$ because $\omega \in S^{n-1}$, and the two middle terms vanish by Lemma \ref{lem:sphere-tangent-orthogonal-radius}. Since $\mathring{g}_\omega(w,w') = \langle d\iota(w), d\iota(w')\rangle$ by the definition of the round metric as $\iota^*\bar{g}$, what remains is
\[
a a' + r^2\,\mathring{g}_\omega(w,w'),
\]
which is exactly the warped product metric $g_{\mathbb{R}^+} \oplus \rho^2\mathring{g}$ evaluated at $(r,\omega)$, the radial factor $g_{\mathbb{R}^+}$ being $\iota^*\bar{g} = d\rho^2$ by Lemma \ref{lem:mfderiv-open-inclusion} and the warping function being $\rho(r) = r$.

For the bijection: $|\Phi(r,\omega)| = r|\omega| = r > 0$, so $\Phi$ never takes the value $0$; if $\Phi(r,\omega) = \Phi(r',\omega')$ then taking norms gives $r = r'$ and then cancelling the nonzero scalar $r$ gives $\omega = \omega'$, so $\Phi$ is injective; and any $x \neq 0$ equals $\Phi(|x|, x/|x|)$, so $\Phi$ is onto $\mathbb{R}^n \setminus \{0\}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}\label{prob:horizontal-vector-fields}
\dcref{ch2:2-5}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.exists_horizontalLift_eq}
\leanok
\uses{prop:horizontal-lift-of-vector-field,prop:horizontal-lift-realizes-horizontal-vector}
Prove parts (b) and (c) of Proposition 2.25 (properties of horizontal vector fields). (Used on p.~146.)
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}\label{prob:riemannian-metric-submersion}
\lean{LeeLib.Ch02.existsUnique_isRiemannianSubmersion_metric}
\leanok
\dcref{ch2:2-6}
\uses{thm:group-action-riemannian-submersion}
Prove Theorem 2.28 (if $\pi: \tilde{M} \to M$ is a surjective smooth submersion, and a group acts on $\tilde{M}$ isometrically, vertically, and transitively on fibers, then $M$ inherits a unique Riemannian metric such that $\pi$ is a Riemannian submersion).
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}\label{prob:grassmann-stiefel-manifolds}
\dcref{ch2:2-7}
For $0 \le k \le n$, the set $G_k(\mathbb{R}^n)$ of $k$-dimensional linear subspaces of $\mathbb{R}^n$ is called a \textbf{Grassmann manifold} or \textbf{Grassmannian}. The group $GL(n,\mathbb{R})$ acts transitively on $G_k(\mathbb{R}^n)$ in an obvious way, and $G_k(\mathbb{R}^n)$ has a unique smooth manifold structure making this action smooth (see [LeeSM, Example 21.21]).

(a) Let $V_k(\mathbb{R}^n)$ denote the set of orthonormal ordered $k$-tuples of vectors in $\mathbb{R}^n$. By arranging the vectors in $k$ columns, we can view $V_k(\mathbb{R}^n)$ as a subset of the vector space $M(n \times k, \mathbb{R})$ of all $n \times k$ real matrices. Prove that $V_k(\mathbb{R}^n)$ is a smooth submanifold of $M(n \times k, \mathbb{R})$ of dimension $k(2n - k - 1)/2$, called a \textbf{Stiefel manifold}. [Hint: Consider the map $\Phi: M(n \times k, \mathbb{R}) \to M(k \times k, \mathbb{R})$ given by $\Phi(A) = A^T A$.]

(b) Show that the map $\pi: V_k(\mathbb{R}^n) \to G_k(\mathbb{R}^n)$ that sends a $k$-tuple to its span is a surjective smooth submersion.

(c) Give $V_k(\mathbb{R}^n)$ the Riemannian metric induced from the Euclidean metric on $M(n \times k, \mathbb{R})$.
